# THE SALT PREACHER

### A novel

*by [Author Name]*

---

*In a drought-cracked West Texas county in 1893, a woman who has been struck by lightning three times and survived is hired by desperate ranchers to "pull the water up" — and, walking the dying land with copper rods she filed herself, begins to believe she can.*

---

\newpage

# CHAPTER 1 · Movement I — The Hiring

### "Copper Because It Looks Like It Means Something"

*Cheyne County, on the Texas / New Mexico Territory line — late May, 1893.*
*Third dry year. No rain in nine weeks. The Rueda down two feet at the ford. Hauled water: a nickel the barrel. Buzzards standing all day in the dead cottonwood by the crossing, in no hurry, because nothing was.*

---

She heard the horse before she saw it, and she knew from the walk of it that the man on top was worth money.

A poor man's horse comes to water fast and greedy, whatever the man wants. This one came slow, picking its feet over the caliche like the ground was a floor it did not care to scuff, and that told Odessa two things before she stood up from the seep she had been kneeling at. It told her the horse was grain-fed in a country where grass was gone. And it told her the man had all the time there was, which in Cheyne County that spring was the surest sign of a man who owned enough water that other men's thirst was his and not his own.

She stayed on one knee a moment longer with her ruined hand flat on the mud. The mud was the last of it. A week ago the seep had made a hand's-breadth of standing water by dawn and given it up by noon; now it made only this — a dark stain the size of a saddle blanket, cool to the palm, going gray at the edges where the salt was starting to draw up out of the ground the way it always did last, the way the county drew up its meanness last, when everything sweeter was spent.

She smelled the gyp coming into it. Bitter, faint, the far edge of rotten egg. Another few days and the seep would be poison, and the two nanny goats she kept would not drink it, and she would be hauling from the Rueda like the rest.

She did not tell the horse any of that. She wiped the hand on her skirt and got up.

Emory Cade sat his good horse at the lip of the draw with the sun behind him so she had to look into it to see him, which she took to be on purpose, because everything a man like Cade did was on purpose or he would not have lived to be as heavy and as clean-booted as he was. He did not get down. He looked at her dugout — a half-cave dug into the cutbank with a plank front and a stovepipe and a hide door — and then he looked at her, and she watched him do the thing they all did, the quick flick down her right arm to the sleeve, hunting the scar, disappointed to find it covered. The whole county wanted to see the scar. She kept it covered. That was the first lesson: what they wanted, you kept behind cloth, and you charged for the looking.

"Miz Pike," he said.

"Mr. Cade."

He was surprised she knew him. He shouldn't have been. Everyone in the county knew Cade the way they knew the courthouse — you might never go inside, but you knew where it stood and who it stood for.

"You'll know why I've come," he said.

"I d-don't." The block caught the *don't* and turned it into a small stone in her mouth, and she saw his eyes catch it too, catalog it, file it. She hated the stammer worst in front of men who filed things. She let the hate settle and made her voice go flat and slow, the way she could when she wanted to, spacing the words so there was no gap for the block to sit in. "You've never once come to see me, Mr. Cade. Ten years I've been on this ground and you've ridden past it a hundred times. Something's changed the price of me."

That got a look out of him. Not a smile. Cade did not spend smiles. But a settling in the shoulders, a man deciding the horse was better than he'd hoped.

"The head-springs are failing," he said.

He said it the way another man would confess a sin. The head-springs of the Rueda fed the Bar EM and, through the Bar EM, fed the Association's grip on the whole county, because Cade leased the depot water-tank besides, and between the springs and the tank he owned nearly every drop of live water a man could count on. If the springs were failing, then the county was closer to the bone than even she had reckoned, and she had reckoned it close.

"Failing how much."

"That's Association business."

"Then it isn't m-mine." She turned back toward the seep as if she had somewhere to be, which she did not, and she felt him not move behind her, felt the horse shift its weight, felt him decide.

"The free-grass men cut the Dobie fence Tuesday night," he said to her back. "Twelve rods of it. Left a note nailed to the post. My riders have been sleeping with rifles since. The Waybournes are telling the little cowmen that I've been holding water back to break them, which I have not, and it doesn't matter that I have not, because they believe it. There's going to be killing over the last live water in this county, Miz Pike, and it's going to be soon, and when it starts it will not stop at one."

She turned around.

"And you want me to find you more water," she said.

"No." And here he did smile, finally, a thin cold thing that cost him nothing. "I know what's under this county about as well as any man living. There's no more water to find. Not enough. Not in time." He leaned on the saddle horn. "I want you to *look* for it."

---

She understood him then, all the way down, faster than she let her face show.

He did not want water. He wanted the *waiting* for water. He wanted armed men who were an inch from cutting each other over the head-springs to stand instead in a field with their hats in their hands, watching a lightning-struck woman walk over the ground with a stick, and to keep standing there, hoping, as long as she could make the hoping last — because a man who is hoping is a man who is not yet shooting.

She wanted to laugh. She did not. She had learned young that laughing where they could see it was a door, and doors got you killed.

"You want a show," she said.

"I want peace," he said. "The show is how I pay for it."

"And when I don't find your water?"

"Then you'll have looked, and looked hard, and the Lord will have withheld, and no man can be angry at the Lord with a gun. You'll have bought me a season. That's worth money to me." He straightened. "It's the only thing left in this county that's worth money to me."

She let the silence run. She was good at silence; it was the one thing the strikes had left her more of, not less. And in the silence she did the arithmetic she always did, the only arithmetic she trusted, which was: *what keeps me and mine alive, and off other men's mercy, one more year.*

A nickel a barrel now. Two bits by August, if it went the way it was going, and it was going that way. Her goats. The seep gone to gyp inside the week. The deed she did not have and wanted more than she wanted the breath in her — a fenced quarter-section somewhere with a well in it that no Cade and no Waybourne and no man who had ever pointed at her in the road could put her off of. That was the whole of it. That was the only god she had.

"What does it pay," she said.

"Twenty dollars the walk," Cade said. "Whether you find or not. I'll want you at the Bar EM range in three days. There'll be men there — mine, Waybourne's, a couple of the Dobie crowd if the sheriff can get them to come without shooting on the road. You'll walk where I tell you and you'll take your time about it."

Twenty dollars the walk. A top hand made thirty in a month, and rode all thirty days for it, and slept in his boots. She kept her face still with an effort that cost her in the jaw.

"I'll want it in silver," she said. "Before I walk. Not after."

"Half before."

"All before, or find yourself another woman the Lord won't kill."

She had not meant to say it that plain. It came up out of her hard and she watched it land, watched Cade take the measure of the thing he was buying — not a diviner, whatever the county thought, but a woman who had been struck by God's own artillery three separate times and had the gall to still be standing in the road charging admission. That was what he was paying for. Not the water. The nerve of a woman lightning could not finish.

"All before," Cade said. Something in him had settled into liking her, which was worse than the other thing, and she knew it, and she filed it.

He turned the horse. Then he stopped it.

"They say I can smell the storm on you," he said, not quite a question. "The men. They say a body can smell scorch."

"Then they've a keen nose for something that isn't there," she said.

He looked at her a long moment, and she knew he'd smelled it anyway — the ozone that was not on her, that had never been on her, that lived only in the space between what a frightened man expected and what his own nose reported, and always, always, the fright won. He touched his hat to her, which no man in the county had done in ten years, and rode his grain-fed horse back up out of the draw at the same unhurried walk, a man who owned the time.

She stood in the dead seep until he was gone. Then she went and got the rods.

---

The rods were copper because copper looked like it meant something.

She had cut them from a length of tube a peddler had left for dead behind the livery, and she had bent each one to an L over the anvil at the smithy when the smith was drunk, and she had filed the long ends bright herself, sitting in the dugout by the coal-oil lamp, filing until the metal took the light and threw it back, because a thing that catches the light is a thing men will watch. She had tried them in willow first, the way the old books had it, a green forked wand held palm-up. But willow was a poor man's magic and it looked poor, and half the county already thought her poor and cursed both, and you could not build anything on a thing that looked poor.

Copper looked like it meant something. That was the whole of the science of it.

She could not close her right hand. The fingers had curled to a claw the night of the third strike and stayed that way, the nerve in them dead as fence wire, and no amount of working it in the years since had brought it back. It would hold nothing on its own. So she took a length of baling wire and lashed the right rod to her wrist the way she did before every walk, wrapping it tight enough to bite, because the ruined hand could not grip but the wrist could bear, and a rod wired to a wrist looks, to a man twenty feet off with the sun in his eyes, exactly like a rod held in a hand.

The wire was the truth of it. The copper was the lie. She wound the one under the other and pushed her sleeve down over both.

Then, because she was alone, and because she had learned the hardest lesson three separate times and did not need to learn it a fourth, she walked her own dying draw with the rods raised. Not to find water. There was no water to find; the seep behind her was the last of it and it was turning to poison as she stood there. She walked it to *practice the waiting.* She raised the rods slow, the way you'd lift a dying man's head to help him drink, and she moved down the draw a step at a time, and she made herself hold the pronouncement she was not going to make anyway, held it and held it, until she could feel in her own body the exact weight of the silence a crowd would strain against — that pull, that leaning-in, that terrible readiness of frightened people to be told.

And here was the thing she never let herself think all the way through, the thing she pushed down under the money and the deed and the arithmetic every time it tried to rise.

The ground *told her things.*

Not through the copper. The copper was tube from a dead peddler. But she could not stop the reading any more than she could stop her hand from having died. She had knelt at a thousand seeps and dry tanks and cutbanks in ten years of walking worthless ground and she had watched what grew and what didn't and what the ants did before weather and where the doves flew at dusk, and it had gone into her the way water goes into caliche, slow, and against her will, and it stayed. She knew, standing in her own dead draw, that there was nothing under it. She would have known it with her eyes shut. And she knew — the way you know a man is lying before he finishes the sentence — that on the Bar EM range in three days, on ground Cade swore was dry, she would very likely find water, because Cade did not know how to look and she did, and in this country if you truly knew how to look there was, God help them all, more water than there was any right to be.

She did not call it a skill. To call it a skill was to admit she had it, and a thing you have can be taken, and a thing you have can be *believed in*, and she wanted no man believing in anything about her but the twenty dollars and the copper and the fear.

She lowered the rods. Her right wrist ached where the wire cut. The seep behind her had gone quiet and sour, the last live water on her own poor ground giving up the ghost while she practiced pretending to find it elsewhere for silver.

---

That night the wind came up out of the southwest, hot and steady, the wind that in a wet year would have carried the smell of rain from a hundred miles off and this year carried only more of the same — dust, and the sweet-rot of dead stock from three ranches over, and the mineral bitterness of the flats to the west where nothing lived and the salt lay on the ground like a lie about snow. She sat in the mouth of the dugout with the hide door tied back and did not light the lamp, to save the coal oil, and she watched the horizon where the buzzards had been. They were gone to roost. In their place, low on the edge of the world, she saw the small orange stars of other people's fires — the nesters' fires, out in the dry-land belt to the east, families on their doomed quarter-sections burning what they had left to burn, waiting on a rain that the whole county now understood, in the private place where a man does his true reckoning, was not coming.

She counted them the way she counted everything. Eleven fires tonight. There had been fourteen a month ago. The three that were dark had either loaded a wagon and gone, or had stopped being able to afford the loading, which came, in the end, to the same darkness.

She thought about the twenty dollars. She thought about the deed. She let herself think, once, briefly, with the flat clear cruelty she trusted more than any warmth, that a killing among the free-grass men and the Bar EM would be no loss to her either way — that Cade was paying her to prevent a thing that did not touch her, and that she would take his silver to prevent it not because she cared whether they slaughtered one another over the head-springs but because a county at peace paid better and longer than a county at war, and a woman alone did well to be paid.

That was true. She held onto it because it was true, and because the other thing was starting, already, underneath it, and she did not want the other thing.

The other thing was this: that three days from now, men who would otherwise be shooting were going to stand in a field with their hats off and *wait on her.* Wait on her word the way this whole burnt county waited on the sky. And she had learned young what it was to be waited on by frightened people — she had lain three times on the ground with her hair smoking and her hand dead and the church-shaped county standing over her in a ring, waiting to see whether she would rise up cursed or rise up chosen, wanting her to be one or the other because a woman lightning cannot kill is unbearable as merely a woman. She had felt them wanting her to be a sign. She had felt the terrible pull of all that wanting, that leaning-in, that readiness.

And she was, in three days, for twenty dollars in silver, going to raise a copper rod slow, the way you'd lift a dying man's head to help him drink, and she was going to make them wait, and she was going to say the least that would do the job.

She did not tell herself yet what she already knew about how it would feel. You never told yourself yet. You made yourself wait, so that when the knowing came it landed like weather.

Out east, while she watched, a twelfth fire went dark, and did not come back.


---

# CHAPTER 2 · Movement I — The Hiring

### "The First Walk"

*The Bar EM range, Cheyne County — three days on. Still no rain. The Rueda down two and a half feet at the ford now, half a foot lost in three days. Forty head of Bar EM cattle dead at the Aguja tank, a cracked pan of dried mud with the print of a hundred hooves baked into it hard as a wagon rut. The hides already stripped and salted and loaded for the depot, because in the third dry year a dead cow was worth more skinned than alive.*

---

She came to the Aguja on foot, leading the mule, because a woman who rode in on a good horse invited the wrong kind of looking and a woman who rode in on a bad one invited the other kind, and Odessa had learned there was no horse a woman could sit that a hundred men would not use to price her. So she walked, and she led the mule with the rods in a flour sack across its withers, and she came over the last rise into the smell of the dead ones before she saw the men.

The smell got there first. It always did now. Sweet-rot laid over the dust, thick where the air pooled in the low ground by the tank, and under it the dry-mud smell, and under that, faint, the mineral edge she had smelled at her own seep — the county drawing up its salt, its last and meanest water, out of the ground it had left. Forty head. She counted them because she counted everything. They lay where they had gone down at the rim of the cracked pan, hunting the last of the mud, and the mud had held them like fly-paper until the sun finished them, and now they were hides on the wagon and the wagon was already gone and only the dark stains of them were left, drying to the color of old blood in the caliche.

The men stood upwind of it, which told her most of what she needed about the men.

There were nine of them and she read them the way she read the ground, at a glance, without letting the reading show on her face. Cade's men she knew by the boots. Three Bar EM riders, hard young men gone lean, with the particular held stillness of men who had been sleeping with rifles and had not decided yet whether this was a rest or a trick. Two of the Slash W she knew by the opposite — a looseness that was working too hard, Waybourne's men grinning at the Bar EM men the way you grin at a man you have already imagined killing. And two others apart from both, standing close together with their hats low, older, with the burnt patience of men who owned little and meant to keep it: the free-grass men. The Dobie crowd. The sheriff had got them there without shooting on the road, then. That was something. The sheriff himself sat his horse a little off, fat in the saddle, sweating through his hatband, wanting to be anywhere the county's money was and knowing exactly whose money the county's was.

Cade was not there. Cade did not come to the walk. Cade paid for the walk and stood in his own cool rooms while other men stood in the sun and waited, and that was the whole art of Cade, and she respected it the way you respect weather.

"You're the Pike woman," one of the Slash W men said. It was not a question and it was not friendly. He was young and had good teeth for the country and she disliked him at once, on instinct, the animal instinct she trusted, because a man that pleased with his own mouth was a man who talked to fill a silence, and she needed the silence.

"I am." She set the sack down off the mule and did not hurry. She never hurried. Hurry was a poor man's tell; she had spent ten years training it out of herself the way Cade had been born without it.

"They say lightning wouldn't have you," the Slash W man said. "Three times. That right?"

"Ask the lightning."

One of the Dobie men made a sound that in a wetter year might have been a laugh. The Slash W man's teeth went away.

She knelt and unknotted the sack. She took her time with the knot. She let them watch her do it, the nine of them and the fat sheriff, let the not-talking build the way you build a fire, small at first. Then she drew out the rods and the light caught the filed copper the way she had filed it to, and every man there looked at the shine of it, and she felt the day change its weight.

---

There was water at the Aguja. She had known it before she came over the rise, and she had known it better once the smell reached her, and she had known it past all doubting the moment she stood at the rim of the cracked tank and looked at what the men had already given up on.

They had given up on the *tank.* The tank was a dirt pan a man had scraped out twenty years ago to catch the runoff, and the runoff had stopped coming and the pan had cracked and the cattle had died in the last of its mud. Any man could read the tank. The tank said dead.

But the tank was the wrong thing to read.

> **ASSAY.**
> Tank dead — surface catch, no feed. Cattle down at rim, N and NE, hunting mud. Wrong sign; ignore.
> Draw comes in from the W, old cut, deep-shouldered. Outside bend swings hard S below the tank, cutbank ten feet, undercut at the base.
> One cottonwood standing in the whole draw. Below the bend, S side, forty paces off the dead pan. Green in the crown — green, not gray, not shedding early. Alone. Nothing else green for a mile.
> Ground falls a hand toward it. Gravel in the cut, rounded, a lens of it under the caliche shelf where the water runs when it runs.
> Cattle trails: three, converging, worn to the caliche, running past the dead tank and on down the draw to the bend. Stock quit the tank; stock still walks the bend.
> Dawn: cool air standing in the low ground below the cutbank, an hour past sunup, pooled where it should have burned off.
> Cutbank base, broke with the heel: caliche damp under the crust. No gyp smell here. Cool. Sweet.
> Water lay old at the bend. Under the gravel, off the caliche. Not deep. The tank is a corpse forty paces from a live thing nobody dug.

She read all of it in the time it took to raise the rods, and told none of it, and raised the rods.

---

She raised them slow, the way you'd lift a dying man's head to help him drink, and she saw the nine men go still the way she needed them to go still. That was the whole science. Not the copper. The copper only gave their eyes a place to fasten while she took from them the thing she was really after, which was their attention entire, every man's, all at once, turned away from every other man.

She walked the dead pan first. She walked it on purpose, over the ground the cattle had died on, over the stains, and she let the rods do nothing there because there was nothing there for them to do, and she let the men see the rods do nothing, so that when the rods did something later the difference would be a thing they had witnessed and not a thing she had told them. She stammered nowhere. On the walk the stammer left her the way sweat leaves a man in a dry wind, without a trace, and the leaving of it was itself a kind of power she did not question and did not name — that the broken woman who could not say *don't* to Emory Cade could walk over dead ground in front of armed men and be, for as long as the rods were up, whole.

"Nothing here," the Slash W man said. "Told you. It's dead ground." He said it to the Dobie men, working, always working, planting the idea that Cade had brought them all out to a corpse.

She did not answer him. Answering was a door. She walked off the dead pan and down toward the bend, unhurried, and the men followed her at a distance the way a herd follows a thing it is not sure of, drifting, and she felt them drift, felt the two Slash W men and the three Bar EM men and the two Dobie men all pulled along after the copper in her wired-up hand, nine men who three nights ago had been oiling rifles over the head-springs, walking now in a loose slow line behind a lightning-struck woman with a stick, because she was the one thing in the county none of them could shoot without first finding out what she was.

That was Cade's twenty dollars, right there. That was the thing he was buying, and it was working, and she took a hard flat pleasure in the working of it that she did not examine.

At the bend she stopped.

She stopped because the reading said stop, but she did not tell them the reading. She let the rods do the telling. She had learned the trick of it in her own dugout by lamplight — a small turn of the wrists, so small no man could catch it, the ruined right one bearing on the baling wire, the good left one rolling a quarter-inch — and the two copper L's swung in toward each other slow, slow, and crossed.

A sound went through the men. Not words. A sound, an intake, the sound a congregation makes.

"They're moving," one of the Bar EM men said, low, to no one.

"She's moving them," the Slash W man said, but he said it quieter than before, and there was a thing under his voice now that had not been there, and she knew that thing, she had heard it her whole life. It was the sound of a man wanting very badly to be right about a thing he had just stopped being sure of.

She held the crossed rods over the bend and she waited.

---

This was the part she had practiced. Not the finding. The finding was easy and it was real and it was the one thing she would not claim. The *waiting* was the art.

She held the rods down and she made them wait, and she counted in her head the way she counted everything, and she let it run long past the point where it was comfortable, long past the point where the Slash W man shifted his feet, long past the point where the fat sheriff took his hat off without seeming to know he had done it and held it against his chest like a man at a graveside. She waited until the waiting had a floor under it, until she could feel the nine of them leaning, that pull, that terrible readiness of people to be told a thing, and she knew — she felt it come into her body the way the storm-dread came, unwanted, physical — that she could tell them anything now. That in the held silence at the bottom of the draw she had them the way the lightning had once had her, laid out on the ground with a ring of faces above wanting her to be a sign. She had them wanting her to be a sign.

The knowing of it turned in her like something swallowed wrong.

She had come for the twenty dollars. She held onto the twenty dollars. But under the twenty dollars, in the leaning silence, the other thing was there, the thing from the dugout, and it was not pleasure and it was worse than fear, and she did not have a name for it and did not want one. It was the feeling of a wall she had spent forty-one years building being, for once, on the right side of. It was the feeling of the county's whole weight of fear turned, for the length of a held breath, into her tool instead of her sentence. And she understood, in the way you understand a thing before you have words for it, that a person could get to needing that, the way a man gets to needing the bottle he swore was only for show — and that the needing would be the most dangerous thing that had ever happened to her, more dangerous than the strikes, because the strikes had only ever tried to kill her body.

She let none of it reach her face. She let the silence run one beat more.

Then she lowered the rods, and she spoke, and she made it slow and weighted and left no room in it for the stammer, and it came out of her like scripture because she had trained it to.

"There's water here," she said. "Off the bend. Under the gravel, on the shelf. Not deep." She turned her head and looked at the Bar EM men, and only at them, because the water was Cade's and it did well to remind whose it was. "Dig at the foot of the cutbank, south side, in under the shelf. You'll be wet by dark."

Nobody moved.

Then everybody moved, and it was the Dobie men who moved first, the free-grass men, who owned no shovel between them that Cade would honor but who went and stood at the foot of the cutbank and looked up at the ten feet of undercut bank and the one green cottonwood and looked at her, and one of them — the older one, hard as a fence post, a man the drought had already taken most of — took his hat off too.

"Ma'am," he said. That was all. But she saw in it the thing beginning that she had told herself, in the dugout, she would not let herself see.

---

The Bar EM men dug because Cade's foreman rode up at noon with the shovels and Cade's word, and Cade's word was to dig where the Pike woman said and no place else. They dug in under the shelf on the south side of the bend, in the shade of the one green cottonwood, and the digging was hard because the caliche was hard, and the Slash W men stood back and made remarks that got thinner as the hole got deeper, and the two Dobie men stood the whole afternoon without a word, watching the hole with the fixed attention of men watching a birth or a hanging.

Odessa sat apart in the shade of the mule and drank from her flask so the men would see her drink, cold tea that they would take for whiskey, and she did not watch the hole. Watching the hole would have said she was not sure. She watched the men instead, which was the only thing worth watching, and she watched the Slash W man run out of remarks entirely by mid-afternoon and go quiet, and she counted the buzzards that came and stood at a distance in hope of the dead cattle and were disappointed and left, and she thought about nothing but the twenty dollars in silver already in the bottom of the flour sack, because Cade had paid all before, as she had made him, and the silver was hers whether the hole came up wet or dry.

That was the truth she held to. The silver was hers either way. Cade had bought the *looking.* If the hole came up dry, she had bought him his season of peace all the same, a field of armed men standing hatless and hoping instead of shooting, and no man could be angry at the Lord with a gun. She had built the whole thing so it could not fail her. That was the genius of it and she knew it was the genius of it and she took cold comfort in the knowing.

But the hole was not going to come up dry. She had read the bend and the bend was true, and she knew it with the same certainty she knew her own dead hand, and the certainty was the one part of the whole business she could not build and could not sell and could not, in her worst honesty, quite explain to herself past the plain fact that the ground told her things and always had.

She did not call it a skill. To call it a skill was to admit she had it.

At a little before dark, when the sun had gone red and low and laid the shadow of the cutbank across the whole draw, the Bar EM man in the bottom of the hole stopped digging.

He stopped and he stood very still with the shovel and he put his bare hand flat against the caliche at the foot of the hole, the way she had put her hand flat against the mud of her own dead seep three days before, and he held it there, and then he brought the hand up and looked at it, and the whole draw was so quiet she could hear the mule shift its feet behind her.

"Damp," he said. His voice cracked on it. "It's *damp.*"

He dug faster then, and the others crowded the rim, and within the hour the water came into the bottom of the hole the way water comes, from nowhere and everywhere at once, seeping up gray and then clear off the gravel shelf, cool, sweet, no gyp in it, exactly where she had said and no deeper than she had said, and the men made the sound again, the congregation sound, but louder now and broken, and one of the Bar EM riders went down on his knees at the rim and put his face nearly in it.

She got up and folded the flask away and took the mule's lead. She did not go to the hole. She had no need to see the water; she had known the water. She went instead to collect nothing, because Cade had paid all before, and there was nothing left to do here but leave before the leaving got harder.

But the older Dobie man came to her at the mule before she could go. He stood in front of her with his hat in both hands and the last red light on his burnt face and he did not know how to say what he had come to say, and she watched him not know it, and she felt the wall she had spent her life building shift under her a second time, and this time it did not steady.

"My boy died at a dry tank in April," the Dobie man said. "Fever off the mud. He'd have been eight." He turned the hat in his hands. "There's water on the Aguja and there's been water on the Aguja the whole time, forty paces from where my boy — " He stopped. He looked past her at the men crowded around the wet hole in the red dark, and then he looked back at her, and there was no anger in his face at all, which was the thing that frightened her, because anger she could have used. "You didn't find it," he said. "You *knew* it. You walked up on ground nine men give up on and you *knew.*" His voice dropped, and he said the next part almost gently, almost tenderly, the way a man tells you a thing he has decided is true and will not now be argued out of. "God's had His hand on you three times, ma'am. Everybody knows it. I only ever heard it as a curse."

He put his hat back on.

"I don't hear it that way anymore," the Dobie man said.

He walked back down toward the water, and Odessa stood holding the mule in the last of the light, and she did not say anything, because there was nothing she could have said that the man would have heard as anything but scripture — and out past the wet hole, past the crowd of hatless men, past the dead pan where forty head had died forty paces from a live thing nobody had dug, the first buzzard of the evening came down out of the red sky and stood on the rim of the dead tank and waited, the way everything in that county had learned to wait, for the thing it was sure was coming.


---

# CHAPTER 3 · Movement I — The Hiring

### "First in Time"

*Salt Fork, the county seat — early June, 1893.*
*Eleven weeks now without rain. The Rueda gone from the ford to a trickle a man could walk without wetting his boots to the ankle. Hauled water a nickel still, but the depot tank stood guarded day and night now, a man with a Winchester on a crate in the shade of it, because water you could see was water somebody had to keep.*

---

She came into Salt Fork on foot, leading the older nanny goat behind her on a piece of rope, because a woman who owned a horse was a woman with something to take, and she had learned the value of arriving as less than you were.

Cade's silver was sewn into the hem of her skirt, where it hung heavy and tapped against her shin at every step, and she had walked the eleven miles from the dugout with it tapping, and she had told no one she was coming. Twenty dollars in silver. She had counted it twice in the dark before she sewed it up, not because she distrusted her count but because the counting was a thing she liked to do — the flat cold pleasure of a sum that was hers and would stay hers if she was careful. She was always careful.

The town lay along the one wide street the way towns out here lay, strung out and false-fronted, the frame buildings raised up two stories at the front and one behind so they looked bigger to the railroad than they were, which was the whole architecture of the country in a single lie. There was the bank, brick, the one brick building, squatting at the corner like a thing that had eaten. There was the depot, and the tank on its tower beside it, and the man with the rifle. There was Halve's store with its porch and its barrels. And down at the end, past the courthouse, in a narrow frame building with a false front lettered in a paint gone the color of dried blood, there was the United States Land Office, and that was where she was bound, though not yet, and not for the reason a body might think.

She meant to buy nails. She meant to buy coal oil and a sack of meal, and to price the wire she could not yet afford. She had come to town to spend a little of Cade's money the way any careful person spends found money — grudgingly, and on things that lasted — and she had come to be seen doing ordinary business, because a woman who was only ever seen doing strange things became a strange woman and stayed one.

But the town saw her before she had got as far as the store.

She felt it the way she always felt it, the small cold prickle at the back of the neck, the turning of heads that pretended not to turn. Two men on the porch of the store stopped talking. A woman across the street took her child's hand. It was not new — she had been the thing the county looked at her whole life — but it was different today, and it took her half the length of the street to name the difference, and when she named it her stomach did a slow cold thing it did not often do.

They were not looking at her the old way. The old way was the flinch, the quick eyes-away, the sign a hand made low against the hip where they thought she couldn't see. This was a leaning-in. This was the look men gave a horse they were about to be asked to buy.

The Bar EM walk was four days gone. Four days. And the county already knew.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Town read, not ground read. Same method.
> Porch: two Bar EM hands, one Slash W by his hatband. Stand together. Four days back would have stood apart, hands near belts.
> Storekeep at the door, apron on, watching the goat, not her. Weighing what a woman with a goat can pay.
> Depot: guard new. Crate not weathered. Guard posted since the die-off, or since the walk.
> The child pulled back but the mother watched. Fear that wants a closer look is not the old fear. New animal in the pen.
> Old water lay under this town once. Cottonwood stumps on the street corners, cut for lumber, roots still drawing. The green in the churchyard grass is a lie the town tells the railroad.

---

Halve weighed out the meal and did not cheat her, which surprised her, and while he tied the sack he said, without looking up, "Heard you found Cade his water."

"I walked his ground," she said. "What comes up out of it is his business."

"They dug it Wednesday. Struck it at nine foot." He set the sack on the counter and squared it, a careful man squaring a thing. "Sweet water, they say. First sweet water on that stretch in living memory." Now he looked up, and there was in his face the thing she was going to see in a great many faces before this was over, and she was seeing it early, and she did not like how early. It was not belief, not yet. It was the calculation that precedes belief. It was a man with a ledger for a heart doing a sum he did not have the figures for and being frightened by the shape of the answer.

"Nine foot," she said. She had known it would be near there. She said nothing of that.

"There's men," Halve said carefully, "who'd want to know where else you'd walk."

"There's men who can afford to ask," she said, and put a coin on the counter, and took her meal, and went out.

That was the whole of the science of it, she thought, going down the steps with the sack on her shoulder and the goat swinging its head at the end of the rope. Say the least that would do the job. Let them fill the silence with their own wanting. She had walked one draw and delivered one pronouncement and let one crew dig, and the town had done the rest by itself while she slept in her dugout eleven miles off. The legend did not need her in the room. That was a thing worth knowing and she filed it and did not yet turn it over to see what was underneath it.

---

She was crossing to price the wire when the newspaperman fell into step beside her.

She knew him for what he was before he opened his mouth — the ink on the cuff, the thinness of a man the drought had not caused but the country had not helped, the restless hungry way he moved, a man forever a half-step behind a story he could smell but not yet see. He introduced himself as Vester Kyle and said he ran the *Clarion*, said it the way a man says a thing he is proud of and ashamed of at once, because running the county's one weekly in a dying county was to be captain of a boat with a hole in it.

"They tell me," Kyle said, keeping pace, "you told Cade's men to dig four rods east of where they'd been digging all spring. And there it was."

"They tell you a great deal."

"Is it so?"

She stopped. The goat stopped. She looked at Vester Kyle for a moment the way she looked at a stretch of ground before she read it — flatly, taking the whole of it in, the wanting in him as plain as a green cottonwood in a dead flat.

"Mr. Kyle," she said, and she made her voice go slow and level, the performing voice, the one with no room in it for the block, because she had understood in the space of three seconds that this man was a thing she must handle exactly right or not at all. "You want to write me up."

"I want to write the truth up. There's precious little of it going around and the county's dry of that too."

"The truth is I walked a man's ground for a fee and he dug and he struck water. That's the truth and it's a dull one and it won't sell you a single paper." She hitched the sack. "The thing you want to write is the other thing, and I'll tell you now, if you write the other thing you'll not be writing about a woman. You'll be building a — a—" and here, cruelly, the block caught her, the *b* stopping hard in her throat, and she saw him hear it, saw him file it the way Cade had filed it, and she hated it and rode over it. "A thing bigger than her. And you won't be able to un-build it. I've seen it done. I've had it done to me."

He looked at her with real attention then, and for a moment she thought she had reached him, and then she saw that she had only given him a better line, and that was the last lesson of the day and it came free: you could not talk a hungry man out of the meal by describing the taste of it.

"That," said Vester Kyle softly, half to himself, already composing, "is a hell of a thing to say."

---

The Land Office she came to last, and she came to it not for herself but because Cade had told her to, which was the first order he had given her beyond the walk, and she had turned it over the whole eleven miles.

*Go by the land office,* Cade had said, at the end, almost carelessly, a man dropping a coin he did not think you'd notice. *Delp the register. Tell him I sent you to look at the Waybourne quarter on the north line. Tell him I want to know is there water on it. Then come tell me what you told him.* And she had understood, or thought she had, that this was not about water at all, that Cade was moving a piece on a board she could not see the whole of, and that he was using her the way you use a tool whose reach exceeds your arm — and she had come, because twenty dollars the walk was twenty dollars the walk, and because a woman who wanted to be free of men learned first exactly how the men who owned things did their owning.

Horace Delp did not stand when she came in. He was a neat gray man with ink-stained cuffs and spectacles pushed up on a dry forehead, and a cough he let out in small measured amounts as though it too were a thing recorded in a book, and the office around him was all book — the great plat maps on the wall with the county drawn in townships and ranges and sections, the numbered squares marching off in their grid over a land that did not go in squares, and the ledgers, and the files, and the smell of paper and dust and the particular cold of a room where the drought was only ever a column of figures.

"Miz Pike," he said, and she understood he had known she would come, which meant Cade had told him, which meant she was later to the game than the players. "You'll be here about the Waybourne north quarter."

"Cade wants to know is there water on it."

"Does he." Delp took off the spectacles and cleaned them on his cuff, which did nothing but move the ink around, and studied her without them, and she saw that his eyes were the eyes of a man who had never once hurried and never once forgiven. "That's a curious thing for Mr. Cade to want to know. That quarter's proved up under the Desert Land Act. Filed three years back. It has water on it as a matter of record, Miz Pike. It has water on it because a man swore before me that he had brought water to it, and I entered the swearing, and the swearing is the water, as far as this office is concerned. Paper's the thing. Not the wet."

"And is there?" she said. "Wet."

He did not answer that. He put the spectacles back on. He turned, instead, to the plat map on the wall, and she followed him to it because he wanted her to, and he laid a dry finger on the grid.

"Here's the county line," he said. The line ran down through the middle of the map, a hard printed thing splitting the squares. "West of it, the tracts lie in the Territory. East, Texas." The cough. "Now. In the Territory a man may claim water by using it. First man to put it to beneficial use, first man to dig it and turn it onto his crop or his stock — he owns it. First in time, first in right. Doesn't matter whose ground it rises under. He that diverts it first, keeps it." The finger slid east, over the printed line, into the Texas squares. "Here, it's a muddier business. The law's not settled. A man owns the ground and near enough what's under it, but a river's a river and the courts have got themselves tangled and there's acts of '89 and there'll be more, and a lawyer can argue it whichever way his client pays him to."

He turned from the map and looked at her, and she understood at last, all the way down, faster than she let her face show, the thing Cade had sent her to feel the shape of.

"So here is your position, Miz Pike," Delp said, and his voice was mild and procedural and it was the worst thing she had heard in that dry town all that dry day. "You walk a man's ground and you say *here.* And whatever's under *here* — it belongs to nobody, until somebody digs it. It's not water yet. It's not property yet. It's a rumor in the dirt. And the man who gets a shovel to it first, and turns it to use first, owns it — over on the Territory side clean as a deed, and over on this side clean enough that only a court could say otherwise, and the courts are two hundred miles off and slow." The cough. "You don't find water, Miz Pike. You can't find water. There's no such thing as found water. There is only water that a man has got to first. What you make, when you say *here,* is a race. And you say *here* to a field with two men standing in it who both want it, and you've not found anybody anything. You've fired a gun to start them running."

---

> **ASSAY.**
> The map is drawn wrong. Sections square, land is not. The grid does not know where the water is; the water does not know the grid.
> The Waybourne north quarter: Desert Land Act. Requires water brought to it. Three years, no water hauled that anyone saw. The swearing is the water. A dummy entry.
> Delp's cuffs: ink fresh on the right, old on the left. Been writing this morning. Been writing before she came.
> He named the county line four times. A man names a thing that many times because it is the thing he is selling.
> Cade did not send her to learn if there was water on the Waybourne quarter. Cade sent her so Delp would say all this to her, so she would carry it back knowing exactly what she is worth and exactly how she can be hanged with it.
> Ground she has read her whole life tells her true or false, water or salt. This ground — the paper ground — tells her only who is fast and who is armed.

---

She came out of the Land Office into the flat white afternoon and stood a moment on the step with the goat blinking up at her, and she found that her hand — the good one — was not quite steady, and she made it be steady.

She had thought, riding the whole thing over on the walk in, that Cade was buying peace. She had thought the con was clean: raise a rod, make them wait, let the Lord withhold, take the silver, buy the deed, be free. She had thought the worst of it was that it was a lie, and she had made her peace with the lie long ago, because a lie you knew was a lie was a wall you could stand behind.

But Delp had turned the map to the wall and shown her the back of it, and the back of it was this: she was not selling a lie about water. She was pointing at ground and telling frightened, armed, ruined men to *run* — and the ones who ran fastest and dug first and stood over the hole with a rifle would own the last live water in a dying county, and the ones who came second would have dug their own graves running. Every time she said *here,* she was not finding water. She was choosing who got rich and who died, in a county already an inch from killing, and she did not even get to know which was which, because the water under *here* was a rumor until a man's shovel made it true or made it a lie, and by then the running had already started and could not be called back.

She had wanted to be feared because fear was a wall. She saw now that she had built the wall in a field, and put a gun in her own hand, and told the county to line up in front of it and wait for her to say *go.*

The man on the crate by the depot tank shifted his rifle across his knees and watched her the length of the street, not because he feared her, but because he feared everyone now, because water you could see was water somebody had to keep — and she understood, standing there with Cade's silver tapping against her shin and the goat pulling toward the trough it would not be let near, that in four days she had made herself, without lifting a finger past the one that pointed, into the most dangerous single thing in Cheyne County: not a woman who could find water, but a woman whose word could tell every desperate man in the county exactly where to go and get himself killed, and whom to kill to get there first.

She had come to town to buy nails.

She started back for the dugout on foot, leading the goat, with the silver knocking against her leg at every step like a small hard door being knocked upon, over and over, by something that had found out where she lived.


---

# CHAPTER 4 · Movement I — The Hiring

### "The Nesters"

*The dry-land belt, east Cheyne County — mid-June, 1893.*
*Third dry year. No rain in twelve weeks. The Rueda a trickle at the ford, the depot tank under guard. Hauled water still a nickel the barrel to a man who could reach the river; to the belt, where the river was a day off, it came to a dime by the time the freighter's mules were paid. The quarter-sections blowing east to west, a little more of each man's ground leaving him every afternoon on the wind.*

---

They came for her on foot, which told her most of what she needed before a word was said.

A man who rides to hire you owns a horse he can spare from the plow, and grain to keep it, and time in his day that is not owed to keeping a family alive. These came walking, four of them and a woman, up the wagon road that ran past her draw toward the flats, in the flat white glare of an hour past noon when no one with sense moved at all. They walked the way the ruined walk — not slow, but even, spending nothing, a gait that could go all day because it had to. Odessa watched them come from the mouth of the dugout with her flask in her hand and did not get up, and let them cover the last quarter mile under her looking, because a person you have made stand under your looking has already agreed to something before he opens his mouth.

The dust they raised did not fall. That was the belt's dust, finer than the county's, the plowed-open topsoil of a hundred and sixty acres that had never been broke before the boomer railroads sold the land to men who believed the rain followed the plow. It hung behind them at the height of their knees like something that had decided not to go back down.

The men stopped at the edge of her ground. The woman came on three steps farther, then stopped too, but only, Odessa saw, because it would have been unmannerly to come all the way to a stranger's door. Not because she was afraid. Odessa marked that. Every soul who had ever come to her ground came with the fear in them somewhere — under the hat, in the hands, in the little flick of the eyes toward the sleeve that hid the scar. This woman had it too. But she had folded it up and put it under something else, the way you fold a thing you mean to use later, and the something else on top of it was want, plain want, the nakedest Odessa had seen.

"Miz Pike," the woman said. "I'm Persy Gault. These are men off the belt. We've come to ask you a thing and pay you for it if you'll hear us."

Odessa tipped the flask. Cold tea, though they'd think it whiskey, and thinking it whiskey they'd think her ordinary, and thinking her ordinary they'd be that much easier to read. She swallowed and looked past Persy Gault at the men. She knew two of them by sight — the belt came into Salt Fork on Saturdays and she had watched them from the shade of the wagon yard for ten years, men going gray in their thirties, wives who bought thread by the yard and not the spool. The other two she did not know. One was big and slow-faced and had his hat off already, turning it in his hands like it was the only thing keeping him upright. The other was younger, hard-eyed, and had not taken his hat off and would not, and she liked him best of the four because he alone had come to hire her without deciding first that he had to worship her to do it.

"You've come about water," Odessa said.

"Cade's woman," the hard-eyed one said. Not to her. To the big one, low, like it settled a bet.

"I'm no man's woman." She said it flat, no heat in it, and let it sit. "Mr. Cade paid me to walk his range. He got what he paid for. That's the whole of what I am to Cade."

---

She let them into the shade of the cutbank because the woman would drop where she stood otherwise, and a body dropped on your ground was a story you did not want told. She did not offer water. She had none to offer that was fit; the seep behind her had gone to gyp on the day she'd said it would, and she was hauling from the Rueda now like the rest, and a barrel of hauled water was not a thing you poured out for strangers in the third dry year to be neighborly. They knew that. Not one of them looked at her water kegs. That was belt manners — you did not see a thing a person could not give you.

Persy Gault did the talking, and she did it the way the belt did everything, without waste.

"The wells are down to mud," she said. "We dug them shallow because the land man said we'd not need deep, said the rain'd keep the top of the ground wet, and it did, the first two years, and we believed it because we'd paid to believe it. Now they're mud. My own's mud. We drink the mud and the babies get the flux from it. Ronsard there" — she did not look at the big slow-faced man, but the tilt of her head found him — "lost a girl to the flux in May and buried her and the next week the well he buried her downhill of went to mud too, so now he hauls, and the hauling is a dollar a week to a man with no dollars, and when he can't haul he'll drink the mud again, and there's a boy of his still. That's the belt, Miz Pike. That's every quarter on it. We heard what you did on the Bar EM."

"You heard wrong, whatever you heard."

"We heard you walked a draw the cowmen had give up on and you found water in it and they dug and it was there." Persy Gault looked at her steady. She had gray eyes gone paler with dust, and a plain strong face, and hands, Odessa saw now, that were split at every knuckle and mended and split again. "Was that wrong?"

Odessa did not answer that, which was its own answer, and she watched the woman take it as one.

"We can pay," Persy said. "Not what Cade pays. We put it together. It's four dollars and some silver and a shoat that Ronsard'll bring you when it's weaned." She said the last without shame, which cost her, Odessa could tell, a great deal, and she paid it anyway, laying the shoat down on the ground between them like a coin. "It's what there is. We're asking you to walk the belt. Find us water we can reach with the tools we've got and the arms we've got left. That's all. Find us one place, and we'll dig it, and we'll share it, all of us, we've swore to it."

The hard-eyed young one made a sound in his throat at the *swore to it,* and Odessa understood that the swearing had been hard-fought and was not as sure as Persy made it sound, and she filed that too, because a thing men have sworn over water is a thing men will unswear over water, and she had learned in ten days of Cade's employ exactly what water did to swearing.

Four dollars and a shoat. Cade paid twenty in silver the walk and would not miss twenty walks. She turned the arithmetic over and it came out where it always came out. There was no money in the belt. There was less than no money; there was debt, the store's books and the land man's paper and the freighter's dime a barrel, a whole shelf of ruin waiting on these people that four dollars would not shift by a hair. To walk the belt for a shoat was to work for nothing, and she did not work for nothing, and she had told herself that with the flat clear cruelty she trusted more than any warmth all the way up the road while she watched them walk it.

And yet.

She looked at the woman's hands. She looked at the big man, Ronsard, who had buried a girl in May and would drink the mud again when the hauling money ran out, standing there turning his hat, believing already, before Odessa had said a word, that a lightning-struck woman with a copper stick was going to save the boy he had left. It was the believing that decided her, and not the way it would have decided a better woman. It decided her because she saw, standing in her own cool cutbank shade, that the belt had already made up its mind about her whether she walked or not — that Ronsard's face was the face of a man who had *already* handed her the boy, in his heart, days ago, on the strength of a story told in a wagon yard — and that a story like that, loose in a belt full of the ruined, was a thing that would grow whether she fed it or starved it, and a thing that grows is safer to hold than to run from.

"I'll walk it once," Odessa said. "For the four dollars. Keep your shoat till it's weaned; a dead shoat's no pay. I walk it once, and what I find I find, and what I don't you don't blame on me nor on the Lord nor on the day of the week. That's my price. You take it or you walk back."

"We'll take it," Persy Gault said, before any man could speak, and the men did not gainsay her, and Odessa saw who it was she was truly hiring to, and it was not the men.

---

They walked the belt in the cool of the next morning, which in that country in June was a thing that lasted maybe two hours past first light and then was gone.

She would not let them make a crowd of it. That was the first thing she said, and she said it hard, because she had learned on the Bar EM what a ring of watching men did to the reading — how the wanting pressed in on her from all sides and she had to hold it off with the theater or it would push her into calling water where she was not sure, which on Cade's range cost only Cade's disappointment but here would cost a man his last dollar and his last labor. So she took only Persy Gault and Ronsard and the hard-eyed young one, whose name was Neel, and she left the other two at the road to keep off the ones who would come — and they would come, she knew; word had gone up the belt overnight like fire in cured grass, and by the time she'd hauled her water and roped her goats there had been three more families' worth of eyes on the horizon, keeping their distance the way you keep your distance from a thing you have decided is holy.

She wired the rod to her wrist where they could see her do it and could not see what the wire was for. She raised the rods slow. And she walked the belt.

The belt was worse than she had let herself know. From the wagon road it looked only poor. Walking it, she read it the way she read everything, without wanting to, the reading going into her the way it always did, and what it told her was ruin laid down in the ground itself. The plow had cut the sod that had held the country together since before there were countries, and the sod once cut did not knit back, and now the fine dead soil went west on every afternoon wind and would not stop until there was caliche showing, hardpan, the bone of the land. The corn Ronsard had put in stood knee-high and yellow and would make nothing. The beans were gone already. A quarter-section — a hundred and sixty acres, a square half-mile — was a lordly thing to a man back in Missouri and out here was a joke God told at a farmer's expense, not water enough on it in a wet year to carry stock, and no wet years coming.

She did not say any of this. She walked, and let the wanting press on her back, and read the ground.

> **ASSAY.**
> Belt soil: fine, blown, gray on the crust, red-brown two inches down. Sod broke; no knit. Buffalo grass gone, only the plow's weeds — Russian thistle already curing to roll. Corn knee-high, yellow at the leaf-edge, tasseling short — no water in the root zone.
> Ground near dead level. But a swale runs SE off the Gault quarter, low by two, three feet across a half mile — old drainage, pre-plow. Grass in the swale-bottom greener, taller, a band maybe forty feet wide. Silver bluestem in it, and one clump of alkali sacaton — deep-root, water-hunting.
> At the swale's low end, a shallow closed basin. A playa. Cracked clay floor, plate-cracks curled up at the edge. Question: sealed, or open? Clay floor gray, not the dead white of a caliche-cemented pan. Ant mounds ring it — harvester, big, active, trenching NE, chaff-rings wide. Ants build up out of damp; they had not moved off it.
> Killdeer working the basin edge at dawn, two of them, running the crack-lines. Doves at first light off the west, low, dropping to the swale-band, not to the wells.
> Ground falls a hand's width in thirty paces toward the playa's low corner. Water lay here in the wet years and lies under here still, shallow, if the clay is open and not sealed. Not much. Not sure. Shallow enough to reach, if it is there.

She came off the swale toward the playa's low corner and she stopped, and she let the rods go still in her hands, and she did the thing she had built her whole trade on, which was nothing. She said nothing. She stood over the cracked gray floor of the playa with the copper hanging and she made them wait.

Persy Gault had come up behind her without being told and stood a little off, and Ronsard behind Persy with his hat already in his two hands, and Neel farther back with his hat still on and his jaw set, and past them, out at the road, the shapes of the ones who had come to watch a thing that had decided it was holy, and Odessa felt all of it lean in on her, the whole weight of the belt's wanting, pressing on her back like a wind that would not fall. She let it press. She counted, the way she counted everything — thirty of them out at the road now where there had been three at dawn — and she let the count of them be the measure of the silence, and she held the silence past the point where a lesser show would have broke it, held it until Ronsard behind her made a small broken sound like a man in church, and only then did she raise the right rod, slow, the way you lift a dying man's head to help him drink, and bring the tip of it down over the low gray corner of the playa.

"Here," she said. Her voice came out of her flat and slow and whole, no block in it, no stone in the mouth, the way it always came when she performed, so that the woman who could not always say *don't* was gone and in her place stood the one whose single plain word landed on thirty ruined people like the first drop of a rain three years overdue. "Not deep. You'll reach it with the tools you've got. But you dig here and no other place, and you dig it wide, not narrow, and you dig it slow. It's shallow water. Shallow water shames easy. You crowd it, it goes."

She had no notion whether that last was true. It sounded true, which in her trade was the same thing and better. And she watched it go into them — watched Persy Gault close her split-knuckled hands and press them to her mouth, watched Ronsard drop to his knees on the cracked clay not to pray, exactly, but because his legs had quit, and begin already to claw at the plate-cracked floor with his bare hands as if the water were an inch down and he could not wait for the shovel — and she understood, standing over the little gray basin in the risen heat with the wire cutting her wrist, that she had not sold these people water at all.

She had sold them a reason. And they were going to plant the whole rest of their lives in the hole she had just pointed at, and there was no unpointing it now.

---

Ronsard dug the playa corner all that day and half the night, and Neel and two others with him by turns, and Persy Gault carried the dug clay off in a feed sack because there was no barrow, and Odessa did not stay to watch. She took her four dollars — Persy counted it into her hand in silver bits and worn shinplasters and a single quarter-eagle in gold that Odessa knew to the bottom of her had been meant for something else, a coffin maybe, a fare east, the last hard thing between the woman and the road — and she rode her mule back toward her draw in the dying light with the money in her boot and the belt's wanting still on her like a smell she could not wash off.

She was a mile gone when the sound reached her. She thought at first it was more of the same digging, the chink of a spade in dry clay carried on the still evening air. Then it steadied and rose and she understood it was singing. The belt had come to the playa in the dark, all of it, families off every ruined quarter, and they had built fires around the hole Ronsard was digging on her word, and they were singing over it — an old camp-meeting tune she half-knew, the words gone thin with distance but the shape of them plain, the shape that every hymn in that country finally came to, which was *deliver us.* They were singing it at a hole in the ground. They were singing it at her word for the hole in the ground.

She sat the mule and listened, and did not feel what a better woman would have felt, and knew that she did not, and was, for once, not sure whether the flat cold place where the feeling should have been was her strength or the thing that was finally going to damn her.

She was still sitting so when the rider came up on her from the Bar EM direction — one of Cade's, on a grain-fed horse that came slow and unhurried the way its master's did, a young hand she'd seen at the range walk. He touched his hat. He did not get down.

"Mr. Cade sends word," he said, "he'd not have took it kindly, was he to hear you'd been walking ground for the sodbusters. Says the belt's got nothing to hire you with and men'll wonder is he still paying you or has some other party got you now." The hand's eyes went, in spite of him, out to the east where the fires burned in a ring and the singing rose. He looked at the fires a long moment, and something moved in his face that Odessa had seen move in a good many faces that spring, and would see move in a good many more, and had no name for yet that she would say out loud. "Says you're Association property," the young hand finished, quieter, "and property don't wander."

Then he wheeled the good horse and rode back the way he'd come, unhurried, a man who owned the time — and behind him, and behind Odessa, and out to the flat black edge of the burnt world, the belt went on singing *deliver us* over a hole one ruined man was digging in the dark on the strength of a single word she had made land like weather, and did not believe, and could not, now, take back.


---

# CHAPTER 5 · Movement I — The Hiring

### "The Second Water"

*Cheyne County, the Slash W's dry quarter west of the home place — mid-June, 1893.*
*Thirteen weeks now without rain. The Rueda gone from a river to a trickle you could dam with a hat. A Bar EM windmill on the flat between the ranches turning all morning and sucking air by noon, pumping nothing but the sound of pumping. Hauled water still at a nickel, but the nickel bought a fouler barrel every week.*

---

Royal Waybourne came for her himself, which told her the price of her had gone up again.

Cade had sent a rider each time before — a boy on a common horse with a note that said *walk the north tank Thursday* and a bag of silver counted out short by a dollar to see if she'd say anything, which she never did, because a dollar was cheaper to lose than the knowledge of exactly how closely she watched. That was Cade. Cade paid a boy to fetch a hired thing.

Waybourne came in his own saddle on a blood-bay that cost more than his ranch could carry, dressed in a town coat gone shiny at the elbows, and he took his hat off to her before he'd even stopped the horse, holding it against his chest like a man at a graveside.

"Miz Pike," he said. "Odessa. May I call you Odessa."

"You can call me what you're paying for," she said.

He laughed at that, longer than it was worth, the laugh he used the way another man used a handshake. He had good teeth and he showed them. She marked it. A man who laughs before he's afraid is a man saving the laugh up to spend later, when the room needs turning, and she did not trust anything a man saved.

"I'm not paying," Waybourne said. "Not yet. I'm asking." He swung down and stood holding the reins, closer than Cade had ever stood, close enough that she could smell the bay-rum on him and under it the sourer smell of a man who had ridden hard and told himself he hadn't. "Cade's had you three walks now on his own ground. His tanks, his flat, his say-so. You've found him water twice." He let that hang, watching her, the way they all watched her now — not the old flick to the sleeve to hunt the scar, but a new thing, a leaning-in, the thing she had felt coming for weeks and hated. "The county's talking, Odessa. They're saying the Lord's took a hand at last."

"The Lord's took nothing," she said. "Cade took my time and paid for it."

"You believe that."

"I d-don't have to believe it. It's what happened."

The block caught the *don't* and she felt her jaw set against it, and she saw him not file it the way Cade had filed it — saw him instead soften his face over it, sympathetic, which was worse, which was a man practicing how to seem kind to her in front of others later. She would rather be studied cold than pitied warm. Pity was a rope with a loop already tied.

"I've got a piece of ground," Waybourne said, "west of my home place. A quarter-section. Caliche country. My daddy fenced it in seventy-nine and never watered a head on it a day in his life, because there's nothing under it. Everybody knows there's nothing under it. It's the driest hardpan in the county and it's been dry since God laid it down."

"Then you don't want a water-finder. You want a fool."

"I want you to walk it." He said it plain, the laugh gone, and for a moment she saw the whole of it under the coat and the teeth — a man mortgaged past the roots of his hair, a man who watched Emory Cade sit in a chair a Waybourne had a right to, a man who had reckoned that if the cursed woman could find Cade water on Cade's ground, then the cursed woman finding water on *Waybourne's* ground would be a thing the county could not unsee. "I'll pay Cade's price. Twenty the walk, find or no. Silver before you raise the sticks." He put the hat back on. "And if you find it, I'll pay you again what Cade never will."

"What's that."

"The truth of what you are," Waybourne said, "in front of men who'll carry it."

She looked west, past his good horse, to where the heat already stood up off the flat in sheets though the morning was not old. Somewhere out there Cade's windmill was turning, turning, drawing nothing, and the sound of it did not carry this far but she knew the sound. She knew it the way she knew the ache would start in her dead hand by afternoon.

Twenty dollars. And a second man now, bidding against the first, over the use of her — which meant the price would climb, and a climbing price was the only prayer she had ever known answered.

"All before," she said. "And I walk it my own way, at my own pace, and no man opens his mouth while I'm on the ground."

"I'll gag them myself," Waybourne said, and showed the teeth again, and this time she believed the teeth, which was its own kind of warning.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Caliche cap, hard, gray-white, cracked in plates the size of a wagon-box. Rings a bootheel like fired clay. No grass but tobosa, cured to wire. Tarbush on the cap — stunted, leaves oiled dark, not thirsty. Nothing that drinks.
> But the cap tilts. Falls a hand's width in twenty paces to the northwest. Old channel under the hardpan, filled and sealed. Follow the fall.
> At the low corner: the cap breaks at a shelf. Below the break — a swale masked by the fence line. Tobosa here greener at the root, a finger's width of green above the wire-brown. Snakeweed dead on the cap; alive along the swale.
> Badger dug the shelf-base in the night. Fresh mound, damp at the throw — soil three shades darker than the cap, holds the shape of a fist when squeezed. Gnats over the dug hole at first light. A single killdeer working the swale-edge, running and stopping.
> Break-of-slope. The cap does not block here. It perches. Water held on the caliche and hunting the one seam where the seam lets down.
> Not deep. Not much. But water lay here, and lies here still, under the driest ground in the county — which is why no one has ever dug it.

---

They came out to the caliche quarter three days later in the worst of the heat, and there were more of them than Waybourne had promised, because there were always more than promised now, that being the whole engine of the thing and the part no man could gag.

Waybourne's own two hands. Three of the free-grass men down from the Dobie country, hats low, rifles in the scabbards, watching every rider the way men watch a thing that has cut their fence. And Cade.

She had not thought Cade would come. Cade did not attend a Waybourne's walk any more than the courthouse attended a hanging it had not ordered. But he came, alone on the grain-fed horse, and he did not get down, and he did not take off his hat, and he set the animal at the top of the caliche rise with the sun behind him the way he liked it, and he watched Royal Waybourne make a show of a thing Cade believed he owned. The two men did not speak. The not-speaking was louder than the free-grass men's rifles.

Odessa let them all sit in the heat a while. That was free, and it was worth money.

She got down off the borrowed mule and stood a moment with her back to the whole crowd of them, facing the low northwest corner where the fence ran along the swale, and she did the thing she always did first, which the crowd took for prayer and which was only her reading the wind. The wind came off the flat, hot, and it carried dust and the far sweet-rot of dead stock and, under it, faint, from the low corner, the least changed thing — a coolness, a mineral damp, the smell of ground that had something in it that the ground around it did not. It was so small a smell that a man standing where she stood would have sworn there was nothing. She had learned young that what a man swears and what his nose reports are two countries, and the swearing wins, and that was the whole of her trade.

She took the rods out of the sack.

The copper caught the sun and threw it, and behind her she heard the crowd go still the way a congregation goes still, and she wound the baling wire around her right wrist, tight, tight enough to bite, so the dead hand could bear the rod the grip could not hold. She kept her back to them for the winding. The wire was the truth and the truth was not for sale.

Then she turned and raised the rods slow, and walked out onto the driest hardpan in Cheyne County to find water where every man watching knew to a certainty there was none.

---

The walk itself was only the telling of a thing she had already read three days back — quartered high and dry first, so that when the copper drifted low the drifting would mean something. She gave them the whole of it, drawn out, because the drawing-out was the price they were paying for. But her mind was not on the rods. It was on the two men at either edge of the crowd, and on the current running between them that no theater of hers had made.

She turned down the fall of the cap toward the northwest corner and let the tips begin to drift.

"They're moving," one of Waybourne's hands said, and Waybourne hushed him, and Cade, up on the rise, said nothing at all, which was the sound she was working for.

She was over the shelf where the badger had dug in the night, and the ache had come up in her dead hand as she'd known it would, and the wire bit, and she let the pain and the theater carry the rods until they crossed. Then she stopped and stood with her head down over the driest-looking corner of the driest ground in the county and said nothing for as long as she had learned to say it — the held place between too short, which was a woman with sticks, and too long, which curdled the fear to anger and anger to stones. She made them all stand in it with her: Waybourne with his teeth put away, the free-grass men leaning on their pommels, Cade as still as his own good boots.

She let the silence get so heavy that when she lifted her head a man's horse shied.

"Dig here," she said.

Her voice came out whole and slow and weighted, the fluent prophet standing where the stammering woman had stood, and she felt the crowd take the words the way dry ground takes the first fat drops — not soaking them but beading them up on the surface, too fast, more than the words could hold.

"How deep," Waybourne said, hoarse.

She looked at the ground and named a depth low enough to be safe and high enough to be a marvel. "Not deep," she said. "It's holding shallow. On the rock." She turned and looked at him and let her face be the face they wanted, the one that had been three times to the ground under God's own hand and come back to tell them where the water was. "But it's salt-free and it's cold, Mr. Waybourne, and it's been under your daddy's fence since seventy-nine, and no man ever dug it because every man knew it wasn't there."

That last was the line she had built the whole walk to spend, and she spent it, and watched it do its work. Because it was the county's own certainty she was overturning — *everyone knew there was nothing under it* — and there is no faith so quick to catch as the faith that overturns what everyone knew.

---

They dug through the heat of the day with the free-grass men and Waybourne's hands trading the shovel and Waybourne himself in his town coat throwing dirt like a common laborer, because a man building a legend digs, and Cade watched from the rise and did not offer a hand or a man.

She stood off in the thin shade of the mule and drank cold tea from the flask and let them see her drink, an ordinary woman with an ordinary flask, and waited on the ground the way she had made them wait on her.
