They broke the caliche shelf a little after noon, and under it the soil came up dark, and dark, and darker, and then a man's shovel made a sound different from all the sounds before it — a wet sound, a suck — and a free-grass man went down on his knees in the hole and came up with both hands cupped and dripping, and he tasted it, and he looked up out of the hole with his face come open like a boy's, and he said, "It's sweet. God almighty, it's sweet water," and the men around the hole took off their hats.

They took off their hats to the water and then, one by one, they turned and took them off to her.

She stood in the mule's shade and felt it come at her, the wave of it, the wanting, all those faces turning, and her body did the thing it did at thunder — a flinch, deep, involuntary, an animal reading a weather it feared, because this was a weather, this leaning-in of frightened men, and she had felt it three times before with her hair smoking and the church-shaped county ringed above her waiting to see would she rise up cursed or chosen. She had built this on purpose. She had filed the copper for exactly this. And still her body flinched from it, because some part of her older than the arithmetic knew that a thing this many people wanted from you was a thing that would, in the end, be taken.

She did not let the flinch reach her face. She tipped the flask again, slow, ordinary.

Twice now, she told herself, doing the arithmetic that was the only prayer she trusted. Twice where the geology said none, and the price climbing, and two men bidding, and the deed a season closer. That was all this was. A skill she would not name and a lie that kept paying and the near-certainty that in this cursed country, if you truly knew how to look, there was more water than there was any right to be. That was all. She held onto it. She held onto it the way she'd held onto the flat cruel truth in the mouth of the dugout, because the other thing was rising under it again, and she did not want the other thing.

The other thing was that at the low corner she had known before she could have said what she'd read.

She knew the tells. She could have named every one of them if she'd let herself — the cap's tilt, the badger's damp throw, the finger of green at the root of the tobosa, the coolness on the wind. But at the corner, in the moment before the rods crossed, the certainty had come first and the tell she'd read it from had come after, and slower, the way a name comes after a face — and she could not swear, if a man had stopped her there and asked, which of the tells it had been. She did not call that a skill, because a skill was a thing you could account for after. And she did not call it the other word either, the word the men were already saying with their hats, because that word was a door, and doors had killed her three times.

She filed it, hard, under *luck* — a good nose reading faster than the mouth could follow — and would not look at it again.

---

Cade came down off the rise while they were still cupping the water up to drink, and he rode his good horse through the middle of Waybourne's triumph without hurrying and without a word to any man in it, and he stopped the horse in front of Odessa where she stood in the mule's shade, and he looked down at her a long moment.

"That's the driest ground in the county," Cade said.

"It was," she said.

He did not smile. He looked at the hole, and the men around it, and Royal Waybourne standing in it to his knees in dark mud with his town coat ruined and his face lit up like a man who has seen the chair he was born to at last come open, and Cade looked at all of it with the flat still gaze of a man reading a ledger that has begun, for the first time in his life, to come out wrong.

"You'll walk where I tell you," Cade said, low, so only she could hear it. "You'll recall our arrangement, Miz Pike. You're the Association's. You'll not be walking any more Waybourne ground."

"I'll walk whose silver's on the ground before me," she said, just as low. "That was never the arrangement. You bought walks. You didn't buy me."

Something moved behind Cade's eyes, and it was not anger, which she could have handled. It was calculation, cold and quick, a man watching a tool he had picked up turn in his hand into a thing that could be picked up by others — a thing with a price, and a following, and a will. She saw him understand, all at once and too late, exactly what he had made when he paid a nickel-cheap boy to fetch the cursed woman to buy him peace: that peace had a name now, and a face, and a growing crowd, and that the crowd did not belong to the Association at all.

"Twice," Cade said, very quietly, almost to himself, looking not at her now but at the men taking off their hats to a hole in the driest ground in the county. "Twice, where there's nothing." And then he looked back down at her, and for the first time since he had ridden into her draw with the sun behind him, Emory Cade — who owned the head-springs and the depot tank and every drop of counted water in Cheyne County — looked at Odessa Pike the way the rest of them did.

He looked at her like a man who had begun, against everything he knew, to be afraid she was real.

She had spent forty-one years teaching this county to fear her as a freak. She stood in the mule's thin shade with the wire biting her dead wrist and the water coming up sweet behind her, and she watched the most powerful man in the county begin, instead, to fear her as a sign — and she understood, in the flat clear place where she did her true reckoning, that she had just traded a fear she owned for a fear that owned her, and that there would be no trading it back.

Out on the rise the killdeer she had marked three days before ran a few steps along the broken cap, stopped, and cried its one thin broken-wing cry — the bird that feigns a hurt it does not have to lead you off from the thing it is hiding — and then it flew.

None of the men taking off their hats heard it. She heard it. She always heard it. That was the whole of the trade, and it was climbing now past any price she had set, out of her hands, up into the mouths of frightened men, where nothing she had ever wanted could follow it and nothing she feared could be kept out.


---

# CHAPTER 6 · Movement I — The Hiring

### "The Preacher"

*Salt Fork, the courthouse square — mid-June, 1893.*
*Fifteen weeks now without rain. The Rueda gone from the ford to a thing you waded, ankle-deep, over a bottom of green slime. Hauled water up to seven cents the barrel. Steers that fetched twenty dollars in the spring going for eight at the pens, and the pens full, and the buyers in no hurry, because a man selling in a dry year sells to men who own the time.*

---

They had built the brush arbor in the middle of the square, in front of the courthouse, out of mesquite branches laid over a frame of green cottonwood poles, and the poles were the last green cottonwood in the county, cut from the two trees down by the depot that Cade had let them cut because a man gives away what is dying to look generous.

Odessa came to town for her fee and stayed for the meeting because a woman who stays away from a prayer-meeting in a drought year is a woman the county talks about, and she had enough of that already. She stood at the back, in the shade of Halve's store porch, with the flask in her skirt pocket and her sleeve down over the wire and the scar, and she watched them fill the arbor the way water fills a low place — the nesters first, come in off the belt in their wagons with the children scrubbed and the women in the black they saved for church and burying, then the town people, then, last and along the edges, the men who did not pray and had come to watch the men who did.

She counted them without meaning to. She always counted. Near two hundred under the arbor and around it, and that was more people than she had seen in one place in Salt Fork in ten years, more than the Fourth, more than a hanging. A drought was better than a hanging for drawing a crowd, she thought, because a hanging you could only have the once.

The Methodist man opened it, old Pruitt, who rode from the county south of them and had a voice gone thin with the road. He prayed a long time and it did not carry past the third row. The people at the edges shifted and murmured and a baby cried and was carried off, and Odessa felt the meeting sag, felt the wanting in it go slack, and she thought: *they have come to be told, and he has nothing to tell them, and they will go home emptier than they came.*

Then the stranger stood up from the front bench, where nobody had marked him, and walked to the pole they were using for a pulpit, and put his hand on it, and did not say anything at all.

---

He let the silence run.

He let it run the way she let it run, and she knew him for what he was before he opened his mouth, the way you know a rifle from a shovel across a field by how a man carries it. He was tall and spare and road-burned, in a black coat gone rusty at the seams, and he stood with one long hand flat on the cottonwood pole as if he were feeling for a pulse in it, and he waited, and the waiting pulled the whole crowd forward off their spines the way a rope pulls a bucket, and by the time he spoke every face under the arbor was already leaning toward the sound he had not yet made.

"There's no rain," he said.

He said it plain. He said it the way you tell a man his horse is dead — not to inform him, because he can see the horse, but to make him say it back to himself.

"There's no rain," he said again, "and you have prayed for rain, every one of you, on your knees in the dark where nobody could see you do it, and there is still no rain. And you have begun to wonder — you have begun to wonder in the place where a man does his true reckoning — whether the sky has quit hearing you. Whether it ever heard you. Whether there is anything up there at all but the same hot nothing that has been up there since April."

Nobody moved. Old Pruitt, on the bench, had gone very still, the still of a man who has just understood he brought a shovel to a gunfight.

"I'm not going to tell you the sky hears you," the stranger said. "I've ridden a thousand miles of country drier than this, burying children in it, and I will not stand in front of you and lie about the sky." He took his hand off the pole. "I'm going to tell you something worse. I'm going to tell you the drought is *for* you. That it was sent. That every dead steer and every blown field and every dry teat on every milk cow in this county is a letter, addressed to you, in a hand you have spent your whole life pretending you can't read."

And Odessa, at the back, in the shade, felt the thing she had felt three times lying on her back in the road with her hair smoking — felt the crowd's want turn and find its shape and lock onto a man who was willing to be its answer. It went through them like the wind goes through wheat, all one way at once. Two hundred people deciding, in the space of a sentence, to be told.

*This one,* she thought, *is dangerous.* And under that, quieter, in the flat cold place she trusted: *this one is worse than dangerous. This one is good at it.*

---

He preached the drought as a verdict for the better part of an hour, and he did not raise his voice past a certain point, because he had learned, as she had learned, that a voice that goes up gives the crowd somewhere to push back against, and a voice that comes *down*, into a whisper that still carries to the last row, leaves them nothing to lean on but their own dread. He named their sins the way a store-keep names a bill — the fence-cutting, the coveting, the whiskey, the pride of a boom-time gone to dust — and every name landed, because in a bad year every man is guilty of something and grateful to be told which thing it was, guilt being easier to carry than luck.

And then he began to talk about water.

"The Lord dried up this country," he said, "and the Lord can wet it again, and He has been known — He has been *known*, brothers and sisters — to send His mercy ahead of His forgiveness. To send a *sign.* A drop before the rain. A finding." He let that sit. "There are those He has *marked.* There are those He has struck down and stood back up, three times struck and three times stood up, when a lesser vessel would have been carried off to the burying-ground the first time — and do you not think, do you not for one moment think, that a thing the lightning could not kill was *left here for a reason?*"

He did not look at her when he said it. That was the art of it. He did not need to. She watched the crowd do the looking for him, watched the heads begin to turn, one and then a knot and then a wave of them, turning at the back toward the store porch where she stood in the shade, and she stood very still and let them look, because she had learned young that to flinch from a crowd's eyes is to invite the crowd's hands, and she pushed her sleeve down another half-inch over the wire without looking as if she was doing anything at all.

He knew who she was. He had known before he stood up. He had built the whole hour to end pointed at her, and had done it so that it looked like the Lord's own finger and not a road-preacher's, and she understood, standing in Halve's shade with two hundred faces turned to her, that she had just watched a man far better at her trade than she was take her legend up off the ground where she had been carefully keeping it small, and hold it up over his head for the county to see.

She did not stay for the closing hymn. She went and got her twenty dollars from Cade's man at the bank and she was untying her mule at the rail when the preacher came across the square to her, not hurrying, a man who owned the time, and she thought that every dangerous man in this county walked at the same unhurried pace, and that it was the surest sign of them, surer than a good horse.

---

"Miz Pike," he said.

"You've the advantage of me."

"Absalom Rourke." He did not put out his hand, which she marked, because a man who wants something usually offers the hand first. He kept both of his loose at his sides and looked at her with a frankness that was itself a kind of pressure. "That was a fine piece of work you did on the Bar EM. And the caliche ground out on the Slash claim. Two waters in a country the Lord had written off. I heard about it in Pecos before I ever crossed the line."

So it had gone that far already. Pecos. She kept her face still.

"I d-didn't do any work," she said, and hated the block, and slowed herself, spacing it flat. "I walked over some ground with a stick. Men dug. There happened to be water. You'd know better than most, Preacher, that walking with a stick doesn't put water in the ground."

"No," Rourke said. "It doesn't." He smiled, and it was not Cade's cold thin thing and it was not Waybourne's too-white grin, which she had not yet met but would know when she did; it was something worse, something that had grief in it, or the memory of grief, or a good imitation of the memory of grief. "That's just it, Miz Pike. That's exactly it. Whatever it is you do or don't do with that stick — and I'd wager my coat you believe in it about as much as I believe I can turn this square to a lake by asking nice — it has stopped mattering. That's what I came across two hundred miles of dead country to tell you."

She waited. He had earned the waiting; she gave it to him.

"You could stand up under that arbor tonight," Rourke said, "and tell every soul in it that the copper's a peddler's tube and the wire on your wrist is baling wire and you never found a drop you didn't stumble on. You could swear it on your mother's grave. And do you know what would happen?" He leaned in, not far, just enough. "They'd love you the more for it. They'd call it humility. They'd say the vessel don't credit itself, that's how you know the Lord's in it. The truth, Miz Pike — you can't give it to them. They won't take it. You've made a thing bigger than what's true, and the thing has left your hands, and it doesn't need you to be real anymore. It just needs you to *point.*"

The mule stamped and blew. Somewhere behind them the hymn started up under the arbor, thin and off-key and rising, two hundred voices reaching for a note none of them could hold, and it lifted the small hairs on her arms in a way she despised.

"You want to use me," she said.

"I want to *build* on you," Rourke said, and for the first time something naked moved in his face, quick, gone. "There's a church in this. Not one church — churches. A tent that follows the drought north into the Territory and east into the blackland, and you at the front of it, and me beside you doing the talking so you don't have to do the talking, and money, Miz Pike, more money than twenty dollars a walk from a man who thinks he's renting you. You want to be free of Cade? I can make you a thing Cade can't afford."

She had wanted, for one flat treacherous instant, to say yes — not to the church, she had no use for a church, but to the freeing. To being a thing Cade could not afford. She put the instant down where she put things, under the deed and the money and the arithmetic, and she finished untying the mule.

"I've got a man renting me already," she said. "I know what he wants. He wants me to stand in a field and keep his cowboys from shooting each other. You want me to stand in a tent and keep two hundred people believing the sky's got a letter for them with their name on it." She swung up. "The one thing you've both got right is that neither of you wants the water. You just want the waiting for it."

Something went through Rourke's face then that she had not expected, and it was not anger. It was something close to relief, and close to hunger, the look of a starving man who has found, at last, one other person at the table who knows the meal is poison and is eating it anyway.

"No," he said softly, looking up at her. "You've got that wrong. I want the water. God help me, Miz Pike, that's the trouble with me. I have been asking for rain for thirty years and I have never once been sure whether anybody was listening, and if you found real water — if it was ever real, even the once — I would need it more than any nester under that arbor. That's why I'm not safe to you. A man who don't believe you can walk away." He stepped back from the mule. "I can't."

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Square dry, packed hard, oiled with spit and dung. No swallows working the eaves at the store — gone
> weeks. Well behind the bank cased in stone; windlass rope shows the wet-line four foot down from the
> old mark, a fresh pale band of dried stone above it. Water hauled in barrels stacked at the depot,
> staved, sweating a little — river water, green smell, not spring. Salt cedar planted years back along
> the depot siding for shade: alive, feathered, drinking something the cottonwoods can't reach — but
> the ground under it crusts white by the rail. Bad water there, near the surface, and men calling it
> shade. Wind out of the west since noon, steady, carrying gyp off the flats. Doves at dusk: none over
> the square. Two flights south, low, toward the river slime. Nothing living here chooses this ground.

---

She was almost clear of the square when the second one found her, and if the preacher had come across it at a walk that owned the time, this one came at a bustle that pretended to, a small round man in a checked suit and a watch chain, damp about the collar, carrying a valise as if it had something alive in it.

"Miz Pike! Miz *Pike.*" He got in front of the mule and the mule did not care for him, and neither did she. "Gaines Melbern. Meteorological practitioner. Atmospheric affinities. You'll have heard of the work up in the Panhandle — Melbern rain, they called it, three inches on Amarillo in a July drought, ask anybody." He said it all in a rolling patter that left no gap to answer into, which she recognized, because it was the same thing she did with the pronouncement, only run backward — he filled the silence instead of hoarding it. "Now. I'll be frank with a frank woman. There's a bounty being talked of. Rain bounty, payable by the Association and the merchants, and I mean to earn it, and I hear you've got the ear of half this county. I sell the *sky*, ma'am. You sell the *ground*. There's no quarrel in it. There's room for both of us under this drought. Why, between us —"

"There's no room under a drought," she said. "That's what a drought is."

He blinked, and for a second the patter stopped, and she saw the thing under it, quick and cold, the way she'd seen the grief-shape under Rourke's coat — except under Melbern there was nothing at all, no grief, no belief, no want past the next town, a man who was all surface because the surface was where he'd decided it was safe to live. He knew she was a fraud. She could see that he knew, the way one card-player sees another's hands and reckons them false, and she could see the thing that puzzled him, that he could not leave alone, that had made him waylay her at a mule's head in a dead square: *why did hers work and his not.*

"You called that rain up from below," he said, and now the patter had a whine in it. "That's what they'll say when I make it rain. They'll say you called it up from below to meet my sky. You watch. They'll give *you* the credit for my water." He gathered the valise up against his chest. "You be careful, Miz Pike. A man who sells the sky, when he fails, they call him a humbug and run him to the next county. A woman who sells the ground —" and here, for the first time, he smiled, and it was the meanest thing she had seen all day, meaner than Cade, meaner than the preacher's grief, because it had truth in it and no feeling — "when *she* fails, they'll want to see the failing. They'll want to dig it up. You can't run a dry hole out of the county on the noon train. It stays. And it's got a family in it."

He tipped his checked hat and bustled off toward the hotel, and she sat the mule in the emptying square and did not, for a moment, move, because the small round humbug had said the one thing in the whole long day that had gotten under the wall — not the preacher with his church, not the crowd with its turning heads, but the sky-seller, telling her flat what she already knew and had been keeping under the arithmetic: that his lie flew off and hers stayed in the ground where you had to dig it up, and that when it came up wrong it would come up with somebody in it.

She did not tell herself yet what she was going to do about that, because there was nothing to do about it, and you did not tell yourself the thing you could not fix. You made yourself wait.

---

Cade found her at the rail on the north end of town, where she'd stopped to water the mule at the trough the town kept filled at four cents a head, and he did not get down off his horse.

"You had a busy afternoon," he said. "A preacher and a rain-man both, and half the county watching a preacher point at you like the finger of God." He was not angry. Cade did not spend anger any more than he spent smiles. But there was a new flatness in him, a man who has watched a tool he owns get picked up and admired by other hands. "I want to be plain with you, Miz Pike, because plainness is cheaper than the alternative. You walk where I send you. Only where I send you. Not for the preacher's tent. Not for the rain-man's bounty. Not for the nesters when they come begging, and they'll come. You found two waters and now every soul in this county thinks he's got a claim on the next one, and there isn't going to be a next one unless I say the ground and the day. You understand me."

It was not a question. She looked at him a long moment, and she felt the thing close that she had felt starting to close since the day he'd sat his good horse at the lip of her draw — felt it settle over her the way the salt drew up out of the ground last, when everything sweeter was spent.

"I understand you," she said.

"Good." He turned the horse. Then, the way he'd done the first day, he stopped it, and looked back at her, and there was something in his face she had not seen in him before, and it took her a moment to name it, and when she named it, it was fear.

"They say a body can smell scorch on you," he said, not quite a question, the same words he'd used at her draw, and she understood that he was not asking her. He was telling her that he could smell it now, today, in the dead square, where two hundred people had turned their heads to her at another man's word — that the thing he had hired for twenty dollars a walk to keep his cowboys from shooting had grown, in fifteen rainless weeks, into a thing that stood up in the middle of his county and made the whole of it lean, and that he, who owned the head-springs and the depot tank and every drop of live water a man could count on, had just discovered there was one thing in Cheyne County he did not own, and had made himself.

He did not wait for her to answer. He rode north out of town at the unhurried walk of a man who owned the time, and the setting sun threw his shadow long and thin across the packed dead ground ahead of him, and Odessa watched it go, and thought that a man who owns the time is only a man who has not yet learned what she had learned three times on her back in the road: that the time owns you, and it is only ever lending.

Out past the depot, along the siding, the salt cedar stood green in the last light, drinking its poison, throwing its false shade over the white crust it made, and under the arbor in the square behind her the hymn came to its ragged end, and in the silence after it two hundred people waited, in the dusk, for a word, from anyone at all who would give them one.


---

# CHAPTER 7 · Movement II — The Following

### "The Name in the Paper"

*The Ojo Frio tract, north of Salt Fork — late June into early July, 1893.*
*Seventeen weeks now without rain. The Rueda gone from a wade to standing pools you could step between dry-shod, the current dead, the pools going the color of weak coffee and drawing flies. Hauled water still seven cents but the water-table down another foot on the depot windlass, a fresh pale band of dried stone above the old wet-mark. County dead-stock, by Kyle's own count in the last Clarion, past three thousand head and no man believing the count was full.*

---

She knew there was a crowd before she topped the rise, because the doves had gone wrong.

At this hour, past sunup, on any ground that had ever held water, the doves came in low off the flats to work the seep-lines before the heat drove them to shade. She had counted on it a hundred times; it was a thing the ground told you if you shut up long enough to let it. But the flat below the rise was empty of them, and the reason lifted to her a moment later on the still air — voices, the small massed murmur of people standing where people did not belong, and the doves had read it before she had and gone off south to the river slime rather than come down among the feet.

She stopped the mule at the crest and looked at what the Clarion had made.

There were forty wagons if there was one, drawn up in a rough horseshoe around the head of the draw, and teams standing in the traces because there was no shade to unhitch them into, and children up on the wagon-boxes, and women under sunbonnets, and men afoot in a loose ragged wall along the lip of the draw with their hats already in their hands though she had not yet come down off the rise. She counted them the way she counted everything. A hundred and thirty, a hundred and forty. More than the prayer-meeting, and this was no meeting; this was a Tuesday, on a dry tract two hours' ride north of town, on ground nobody had reason to be on but that they had read her name in print and come.

*This is the thing Kyle did,* she thought. She had told him, in the land office doorway three weeks back, not to build a thing bigger than her. He had taken it, she saw now, for a better line, and printed it. THE WOMAN THE LIGHTNING WOULD NOT KILL, the header had run, and under it a column and a half of the two Bar EM waters and the Slash W caliche and the word *diviner* set in type as though setting it in type made it a trade a body could hang a shingle on. She had read it by the coal-oil lamp in the dugout and folded it small and not slept, because a thing in your hand you can burn, and a thing in a hundred and forty hands you cannot.

She came down the rise slow, because there was nothing to be gained by hurrying toward it and something to be lost, and the murmur went out of them as she came, the way it goes out of stock when the rider they fear comes into the lot. By the time the mule's feet were on the flat the whole horseshoe of them had gone silent, a hundred and forty people gone silent at the sight of her, and she felt it hit the wall she had spent forty-one years laying up — that silence, that leaning-in, that terrible readiness — and she felt the wall hold, and was glad of the wall, because behind it her heart was going like a run rabbit's and she did not want them to see.

---

A man came out of the crowd to meet her, hat crushed to his chest, and she knew him for the one who'd sent for her — Sim Orley, the note had said, a name she did not know, a nester off a played-out quarter she did not know, and the four dollars had come folded in the note in two silver halves and a fist of dimes, which was every kind of money a poor man keeps, and she had come for it because four dollars was four dollars and because Cade had said walk only where I send you and she had not decided yet whether she was a woman who took orders.

"Miz Pike," Orley said. His voice shook and he hated that it shook; she saw him hate it. "We didn't— I sent for you private. I don't know how they— " He looked back at the horseshoe of wagons as though it had grown up behind him in the night like a mushroom ring, which in a manner of speaking it had. "The paper," he said. "It said you'd found the caliche water. It said you were about the county. Folks just— they heard you was coming out this way and they—"

"They came to watch," she said.

"They came to see it," he said, and there was a correction in it he did not know he was making, and it went through her cold. Not to watch. To *see.* A man watches a thing he is not sure of. A man comes to *see* a thing he has already decided is there.

She got down off the mule. Her right wrist was bare yet — she never wired the rod on until she meant to walk — and she felt the crowd's eyes go to the arm the way they always went, hunting the sleeve, and she let her sleeve be a sleeve and looked past Orley at his ground.

It was poor ground. It was the poorest she had been asked to walk. A quarter-section of blow-sand and shinnery scrub on a bench above the draw, the fence sagged on rotted cedar stays, a half-dugout house with the roof-sod slid off one corner, and, out front of it, a garden patch that had been a garden once and was now a square of powder with the dead corn standing in it gray and paper-dry, rattling a little in a wind she could not feel, which meant there was a wind moving up high that had no rain in it and never would. Nothing on this bench had root to reach live water. She had known it from the rise. She could have told him from the rise, and taken his four dollars, and ridden home, and let him keep his hope one more day.

But there were a hundred and forty people watching her not-decide.

"Where do you want it dug," she said.

Orley blinked. "I don't— wherever you say, Miz Pike. Wherever it's at."

Of course. Wherever she said. That was the whole of it now. Three weeks ago Cade had wanted the *waiting* for water; now this man wanted the water itself, and believed she had it in her gift to lay it under whichever square yard of his dying bench she chose to point at, the way a man lays a coin on a counter.

She looked at the ground a while longer, and the crowd let her look, and she made them let her, because the looking was the only honest thing she would do all day and she wanted it to last.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Blow-sand bench, shinnery oak, sand sage. No phreatophyte anywhere on
> it — no cottonwood, no willow, no green mesquite; mesquite here dwarfed,
> yellow, quitting. Corn dead standing, no ear. Fence stays cedar, bark
> gone, checked to the heart — dry post country, always was. Draw below
> the bench: sand-choked, dry to the touch a foot down, no cutbank seep,
> no gravel lens showing, no cool air pooled at dawn (came before them —
> none). Ant mounds up on the bench: harvester, ringed with chaff,
> moving south off the high sand toward the draw-head, not settling —
> going somewhere, not staying. One green thing on the whole quarter: a
> single wolfberry by the fallen sod, roots in the house drip-line where
> a family threw wash-water ten years. That is the only damp here and a
> man made it. Ground says no. Ground has said no a long time.

---

She raised the rods.

She had wired the right one on with her back half to the crowd, the way she always did, the wire wound tight enough to bite the wrist that could bear it because the hand could not grip, and she pulled the sleeve down over the wire and the scar the way she always did, and none of them saw the truth of it, and she turned and lifted the copper slow, the way you'd lift a dying man's head to help him drink, and the filed ends caught the high hard sun and threw it, and a hundred and forty people took a breath at once and did not let it go.

There was no water. She walked as if there were.

She walked the bench the way the books had it and the way she had made her own, quartering the ground with the rods held loose out ahead, her face gone smooth and empty the way she could make it go, the stammer nowhere in her because when she performed the stammer was simply not there, had never been there, would come back to her tonight in the dugout when she was alone and there was no one to lose it in front of. She walked, and the crowd walked with her — not close, they left her a ring, they had learned that much from the newspaper, that a body left the vessel her room — but they moved as she moved, a hundred and forty people drifting after her over the dead bench like iron filings after a magnet dragged under a paper, and she felt the drag of them in her spine, felt the wall behind her strain with all their leaning, and understood, in the flat cold place, that this was the new thing the paper had made and she had not reckoned it. It was one matter to have men wait on her in a field. It was another to walk and feel a whole crowd's belief moving where she moved, before she had said a word, waiting for her only to *confirm* what they had already come to see.

She could feel how easy it would be. That was the terror of it, though she did not call it terror; she called it nothing, she pushed it under the arithmetic. She could stop over any square of this powder and let the rods dip and hold the silence till it screamed and then say the water is here, dig, and they would dig. Sim Orley and his neighbors would dig this dead bench with their bare hands, believing, and they would give it a week, a month, they would go down forty feet through blow-sand into caliche and out the bottom of caliche into more nothing, and the whole time they dug they would be happy, they would be *saved*, right up until the day the hole told them what the ground had told her from the rise. And then the paper would say she'd been sent to the wrong ground by an enemy, or the men had dug on an unlucky day, or God had withheld to try them. It would not touch her. Rourke had told her so and she had believed him. The lie stayed in the ground where you had to dig it up, and when it came up wrong it came up with a family in it, and the family thanked her on the way down.

She came to the far fence and turned and started back and had said nothing and the silence was pulling at them past what was bearable, and a woman near the front began, very softly, to pray.

---

She stopped by the wolfberry.

It was the one green thing on the quarter and it was green because for ten years a family had thrown its wash-water in the drip-line off the fallen sod, and there was no water under it that any well would ever reach, only the ghost of ten years of dishpans soaked shallow into the sand where the wolfberry had learned to sip it. She knew that. She had put it in the assay of her own head from the rise. And she stopped by it because the crowd would read the green as she had refused to let herself pretend to, and she let them read it, and she held the rods over the drip-line, and she let the copper tremble in her wired hand, and she did not say the water is here, because there was a line she had not yet crossed and this, on this poor bench, with this poor man's silver in her pocket, was where she found she would not cross it — not out of mercy, she told herself, she had no mercy to spend and could not afford to buy any, but because a called water that came up dry on ground the whole county could see was dry was a called water that would, one day, get her hung, and she meant to die of the lightning or of nothing, not of a rope over a nester's bare bench.

She lowered the rods.

"There's no water here," she said.

She said it plain. She said it the way you tell a man his horse is dead. And she watched the thing she had not braced for go across the hundred and forty faces, because she had braced for anger, for the crowd's hands, for the turn — and what came instead was worse. It was relief on some of them. It was awe on more of them. Sim Orley's face, in the front, did not fall; it *opened*, the way a man's face opens at a wonder, and she understood before he spoke what he was going to say and could not stop him saying it.

"She won't lie to us," a woman behind him said, low, to the woman beside her, and the beside-her woman took it up, and it moved back through the wagons the way the wind moves through wheat, all one way at once: *she won't lie. She'd of said it was here. She'd of taken the money and said it was here, and she didn't, she told us the truth, she wouldn't lie to us*—

She stood by the wolfberry with the copper cold in her wired hand and watched her own refusal to lie about the water become, in the space of a sentence, the surest proof yet that the water she *did* call must be true. She had thought the failures were the danger. She had thought the honest no was the wall between her and the rope. And here, on Sim Orley's dead bench, a hundred and forty people took the one honest thing she had done all day and built it straight into the lie, made her truthfulness the mortar of it, so that now they would believe her callings the more for the times she declined to call — and there was no move left on the board, she saw, no honest no and no honest yes, because they had found a way to make even her refusals feed the thing.

"I'm sorry," Orley said, and he was not sorry; he was *shining*. "I'm sorry to've brought you out for nothing, Miz Pike."

"It wasn't for nothing," said the woman who'd started it. "She come. She *come.*"

---

Vester Kyle was at the edge of the crowd on a livery horse, hatless, sweating through his collar, and he had a pencil going on a pad braced against his saddle-horn, and he had been writing since before she raised the rods.

She walked the mule up to him because there was no sense pretending she had not seen him, and the crowd made a lane for her, and some of them touched the mule as she passed, quick, the flat of a hand on its flank, the way you touch a thing you want a little of to come off on you.

"You did this," she said to Kyle.

"I reported it," Kyle said. He did not look up from the pad. "There's a difference, Miz Pike, and it's my whole living." He wrote another line and then he did look up, and his face was hungry and a half-step behind her, the way it always was. "You told them there's no water. On ground you were paid to find it on. That's a better story than a find. You know that? A find's a rumor. A woman who won't sell a false find—that's a *creed.*" He shook his head, admiring, the way a man admires weather he has to ride home through. "I couldn't have wrote you worse if I'd tried to help you. I put your name in print and it made you real, and now you can't make yourself small again by telling the truth. The truth's the best line you've got."

"Don't print me again," she said.

"I'll print you Thursday," Kyle said, not unkind, which was the worst of it, "and Thursday after, till it stops selling, and it isn't going to stop selling till it rains, and Miz Pike—" he looked up at the high hard sky, empty, the color of the inside of a bell "—it isn't going to rain."

---

She rode out ahead of them and they did not follow her far, having no more reason to; they had come to see and they had seen, and what they had seen was a woman who would not lie, and they turned their wagons for home carrying it like water in their two hands.

Cade's rider was waiting where the tract road met the county road, sitting a Bar EM horse in the only shade for a mile, a stunt of dead mesquite, and he did not touch his hat.

"You walked ground Mr. Cade didn't send you to," he said.

"I walked ground and found no water," she said. "You can tell him that's what he's paying twenty a walk to keep off other men's tracts, and I did it for free."

The rider chewed on that and did not like it and could not find the wrong in it. "He read the paper," he said. "The whole county read the paper. He wants you to know he read it." He turned his horse. "He says the thing about a paper is, it don't ask his leave to print, and he don't care for a thing in his county that don't ask his leave. He says you'd do well to remember who found you before the paper did." And he rode north toward the Bar EM at a walk that owned the time, or believed it did.

She sat the mule in the road a while after he was gone. The dust of the forty wagons still hung over the flat behind her, a low brown smudge going south toward the belt and the town, and in it, small with distance, she could still make out the last of them, the slow beasts and the loaded boxes and the children, a hundred and forty people carrying home the news that the Salt Preacher would not lie to them—and she understood, sitting there in the road with four dollars of a ruined man's silver in her pocket and the sky the color of the inside of a bell, that the thing had gone past Cade now, and past the paper, and past her own two hands; that it did not need her to find water anymore, nor even to fail to; that it had learned to eat her honesty and her fraud alike and grow fat on either; and that there was, in all the burnt breadth of Cheyne County, no ground dry enough to bury it in.

She rode home. Behind her, on the poor bench, under the one green wolfberry a family had watered with its dishpans for ten years, Sim Orley had already put a spade to the drip-line, and was digging.


---

# CHAPTER 8 · Movement II — The Following

### "On the Books"

*Salt Fork, and the Sikes place out on the belt — mid-August, 1893.*
*Third dry year. Rainless week nineteen. The Rueda broke into standing pools below the ford, green and shrinking, a man's stride between them. Hauled water eight cents the barrel, nine to the belt. The bank had begun to call its paper, quiet, one letter at a time, so that no man knew for certain how many of his neighbors had gotten one.*

---

She smelled the store before she reached the step, and what she smelled was money going bad.

Not the coal oil, though that was in it, and the side-meat swinging in its cheesecloth, and the leather and the airtights and the sweetish rot of a cracker barrel gone to weevil in the heat. Under all of that was the other thing, the thing that had no proper smell and that she had learned to read anyway, the way you learn to read a horse that has decided to bolt before it moves a foot. The store smelled the way a room smells when the men in it are counting and have quit talking about anything but the count.

There were four of them on Halve's porch and they went quiet when they saw her, which they had not used to do. A month ago they would have gone quiet with the old quiet, the flinch, the drawing-in of a man who does not want the cursed woman's shadow to fall across his boots. This was a different quiet. This one leaned toward her. She had come to know the two by feel, and she trusted the old one more.

"Miz Pike," Halve said from the doorway. He had his apron on and his spectacles pushed up and a pencil behind his ear, and he was a careful man who did not cheat her, and she liked him about as well as she liked anyone, which was to say she watched him close.

"Mr. Halve."

"You'll have seen the *Clarion.*"

"I don't read it." That was a lie and he knew it was a lie and let it stand, which was one of the reasons she could stand him. Vester Kyle had put her name in his paper eleven days back, over two columns, with a woodcut that was not her but that everyone in the county now agreed was, and he had called her a thing in the headline that she would not say aloud even to herself, and since then the leaning quiet had followed her into every doorway in Salt Fork.

"There's men been asking after you," Halve said. "Waiting, some of them. Out front, off and on, since Tuesday."

"For water."

"For you." He said it flat, not unkind, a fact off his ledger. "I told them this is a store, not a church. Didn't make a dent."

She came up the step. The four men on the porch parted for her, and one of them, a lean young nester she half-knew, took off his hat, and she wanted to knock it back onto his head. A man with his hat off is a man who has decided something about you, and what he had decided she had not agreed to and would not be paid for.

Inside, the store was cool and dim and the shelves were emptier than she'd ever seen them, gaps where the flour should be, the coffee down to the dregs of the last sack. Halve went behind his counter and stood in front of the big ledger the way a man stands in front of a grave.

"I'll want coal oil," she said. "A can. And side-meat, what you've got that isn't turned. And I'll pay in silver."

That last was the thing worth saying and she watched it land. Silver. Not the book. Not credit. She laid the coins on the counter one at a time so he'd hear them, and so the men drifting in behind her would hear them, because a woman who pays silver in a county where the paper is being called is a woman who cannot be broken the ordinary way, and being unbreakable the ordinary way was the only wall she had left that the legend had not already knocked a hole in.

Halve took the coins and did not put them in the drawer. He held them.

"Ammon Sikes is going to lose his place," he said.

She let the silence go a beat, the way she would on the ground, though she was not performing and did not want him to think she was. "That's the bank's business and Sikes's. Not mine."

"He's told half the belt you're going to find him water Thursday and save the farm." Halve set the coins down on the counter between them, an even little stack, and looked at her over his spectacles. "He's told them he'll pay the note out of the crop the water'll raise. He's told them the Lord sent you to him particular." He paused. "He's told me he'll settle his book here Thursday night. Out of nothing. Out of a hole in the ground you haven't dug."

She looked at the stack of her own silver sitting there like a small argument, and she felt the thing she always felt when a man handed her a debt that was not hers, which was the cold flat rising of a woman who has spent her whole life being handed things that were not hers to carry.

"I never told Ammon Sikes anything," she said.

"No," Halve said. "I don't expect you did." He pushed the coins back toward her, and rang up her oil and her meat off a corner of the counter without opening the ledger at all, on nothing, on the memory of the price, and she understood that he was doing her the small courtesy of not writing her name into the book on a day when the book was killing people. "But you're the one that'll be standing on his ground Thursday. And when the hole comes up dry, you're the one that'll be standing on it."

---

She rode out to the Sikes place the next morning because she had decided the danger was worth the ride, and because the danger, once she named it, would not let her be.

She told herself it was the danger. It was true it was the danger. A man who bets his farm on your word and loses it is a man who owns a piece of you afterward, whether you want him to or no; he owns the piece that will need a reason for what happened to him, and the reason a ruined man reaches for is never his own foolishness and never the drought and never the bank. She had learned that under lightning three times over — that when the sky comes down on a man he does not blame the sky, he blames the woman it did not kill. So she rode out to see the ground for herself before she was made to stand on it in front of a crowd, and if she could read it dry from the fence line she would find a way to be somewhere else on Thursday, and Ammon Sikes could lose his farm without her name stapled to it.

That was the whole of her reasoning and she was satisfied with it and it was almost true.

The belt in week nineteen was a country the color of a bone left out. What grass there had been was gone past straw to a fine gray nothing that lifted off the ground in the wind and traveled. The quarter-sections stood on it in a broken line, the ones still lived-in and the ones abandoned, and you could not always tell which was which until you got close and saw whether the door was tied or swinging. She counted four swinging between the ford and the Sikes turnoff. There had been two, in June.

She came onto the Sikes ground and got down at the fence and did not go to the house.

She walked the field first. She always walked the field first; she told herself she walked it to know how to lie about it, and she walked it the way she walked everything, slow, reading, before she let herself think about the man in the house who was staking his children on what the ground would say.

> **ASSAY.**
> East forty, plowed once and gone back. Furrows blown half-full, crests
> bare, hardpan showing bald in the low spots. Soil: pale brown crust,
> then gray, then—at the badger's old cut by the corner post—a thin band
> of pinkish gray under the gray. Broke a clod off the cut with the heel.
> Dry to the palm. No cool in it. Gyp on the tongue, faint, like a coin
> left in the mouth.
> Mesquite along the south line: leaves small, curled, half of them shed
> already—reaching, not drinking. Roots gone down after water that has
> gone down ahead of them. Tarbush thick on the flat, oiled dark, alive:
> the plant that wants none.
> No cottonwood. No willow anywhere on the quarter. One dead hackberry by
> the house, dead three years by the bark.
> Ground falls west, a hand's width in fifty paces, toward the section
> line and off the man's land. Whatever old water lay here lay yonder,
> under his neighbor's fence. Doves at the tank this morning? Tank's dry.
> Doves at dawn went over, west, and did not stop.

She stood at the fence with the gyp still faint on her tongue and she knew the ground the way she knew the palm of her ruined hand. There was no water on Ammon Sikes's quarter that a man could reach with a season's digging, and what little the country had held here had gone west with the falling table, off his land and under the next man's wire, which was the cruelest arithmetic the drought did and the one it did most. The water did not leave. It only sank, and sinking, it slid downhill under the earth the same as it would on top of it, and it fetched up wherever the ground was lowest, and the ground was lowest under somebody else's deed.

She could dig here forty feet and find nothing but the bill for the digging.

She was still standing at the fence with that settled in her when the door of the house opened.

---

The man who came out was younger than she'd figured and worse-off. Ammon Sikes was maybe thirty and looked fifty in the way the belt made them look, and he came across the dead field to her fast, hat in his hand already, and behind him in the doorway stood a woman with a baby on her hip and two more children pressed to her skirt, all of them watching Odessa the way you'd watch weather come up out of the southwest, hoping and afraid at once.

"Miz Pike." He was out of breath and he had not run far. "Miz Pike, I didn't look for you till Thursday, I'd have—the house is poor but Ada'd have—"

"I don't want feeding."

"No. No." He stopped a few feet off and turned his hat in his hands and looked at her with his whole face open, which she hated, which she had always hated, the open face, the face that had already decided you were the answer and was only waiting to hear you say it. "I told the fellows Thursday. Kyle said—it was in the paper, what you done at the Slash W, the caliche where there wasn't none, and Ada read it out, and I thought, if she can pull it up out of the Slash W hardpan she can pull it up out of anything, she can pull it up out of mine—"

"Mr. Sikes."

"—because I'll tell you plain, I've had a letter." His voice dropped on *letter* and he glanced back at the house as if the word might carry. "From the bank. They want the note or they want the place, and I've got no crop to make the note, but if there's water—if there's water I can put in a fall garden and a winter wheat and I can show them a growing thing, Miz Pike, that's all a man needs to show them, a growing thing, and they'll carry me another year—"

The whole plan was in it, laid out plain, the whole doomed clockwork of it. He was not going to raise a crop that would pay a note. Nobody was going to raise a crop off a Thursday hole in week nineteen of a rainless year. He was going to dig, and show the bank a wet hole and some green sprouts and a face full of faith, and buy himself the one thing the belt had left to buy, which was a little more time before the end that was coming anyway. And to buy it he was going to spend the last thing he had, which was the labor of his own back and the last of Ada's silver, on a hole she already knew was dry.

She stood there and she did the arithmetic she always did, the cold true arithmetic, *what keeps me and mine alive and off other men's mercy,* and the arithmetic came out clear and it came out ugly.

If she told him the ground was dry, he would not believe her. That was the first stone. He had read her in the paper; he had built the whole of his hope on a woodcut that wasn't her face; a man who has decided you are the mercy of God does not take *no water here* from your mouth as a fact — he takes it as a test, a riddle, a thing you are saying to see if he is worthy. He would dig anyway, on Thursday, before a crowd, and when it came up dry with her having stood on the fence and said *no*, then she was the false prophet who had cursed his hole, and that was worse for her than the other thing, because a prophet who fails is tested but a prophet who forbids and is defied and is proven right is a witch.

And if she said nothing — if she came Thursday and walked and raised the copper and read the ground she already knew was bone and gave him some careful thing, *dig wide, dig slow, shallow water shames easy,* the words she had used on the Gault playa that she did not know were true — then he would dig his dry hole believing she had blessed it, and lose his farm, and it would be her name on the losing.

There was no move on the board that did not end with her name stapled to the man's ruin. Cade had bought her to keep armed men from shooting; the legend had gone and made her the thing frightened men would blame when the world did what the world was going to do regardless.

She was not going to save Ammon Sikes. She had known that at the fence. She was here to save herself, and she could not do even that.

"How deep can you dig," she said.

It was not a kindness. She told herself that hard and it was true. It was the only card left that was hers to play — not for him, for her. If he was going to dig a dry hole with her name on it either way, then the shape of the hole was the only thing left she could touch, and there was one shape of it that did not have a dead man in it.

He blinked at her. "Deep as it takes."

"No." She let the flat come into her voice, the slow weighted flat she used on the rods, though there were no rods here and no crowd, only a man and his open ruined face. "Not deep as it takes. This ground is hardpan under two foot and gyp under that. You dig a narrow well in gyp ground and you dig it deep and the walls sweat and let go, and they let go in the night with a man at the bottom of them, and the belt buries a father instead of a note." She held his eyes. "You want a hole here, you dig it wide. Wide as a room and no deeper than a man is tall, and you brace it. And you do it in the day where men can pull you out."

"But the water—"

"I haven't said a word about the water."

And there it was, the trick of it, laid down without her willing it, the old trick she despised: she had told him the truth — *there is no water, dig no deep hole* — and because she had wrapped it in the withholding, because she had not told him yet, he heard in it not a warning but a promise. His face changed. It filled up. He thought she was telling him how to dig his well so it would not kill him before it made him rich. He thought the wide shallow hole was where the mercy was.

"Thursday," he said, and his voice had the singing in it now, the belt's singing, *deliver us.* "You'll come Thursday."

She had come out here to find a way not to come Thursday. She stood in his dead field and heard her own withholding hand him back the exact hope she had ridden nine miles to take away, and she understood, cold and complete, that the legend had gotten so far out ahead of her that she could no longer even tell a man the truth without his hearing it as a blessing.

"I'll come," she said, because there was no version where she didn't. If she stayed away, he'd dig anyway, deep and narrow, alone, in the dark. At least if she stood on it Thursday she could make him dig the hole that would not fall on him.

That was the whole of what she could do for Ammon Sikes. Make his ruin a shape that would not kill him. She did not tell herself it was tenderness. It was not tenderness. It was the least a woman could do to keep her name off a grave, and she left it exactly there, unexamined, the way you leave a coal you do not mean to pick up.

---

She rode back the long way, past the section line, to see for herself where the water had gone.

The neighbor's quarter to the west lay a hand lower than Sikes's, and along its fence, thirty feet inside the wire, ran a green thing she had known she'd find and had not wanted to. A finger of saltgrass, low and blue-green and alive, following the swale — the plant that grows where shallow water lies and lies salt. And past the saltgrass, at the very corner where two fences crossed, stood a stake she did not recognize and a small painted board, and on the board in a surveyor's careful hand a claim number and the words *DESERT LAND ACT* and below that a name that was not the neighbor's name and not any nester's name she knew.

She sat her horse and looked at the stake a long time.

Somebody had filed on the water that had run off Ammon Sikes's dying farm. Filed on it under the Act, which required a man to swear he'd bring water to the land and raise a crop — and there was no man living on this corner, no house, no plow, no crop, only a stake and a board and a name that lived in nobody's memory. A dummy entry. The swearing was the water. Delp's business, and Cade's kind of business, and the kind of business that put stakes on the ground weeks before a nester across the fence had even had his letter from the bank.

Which meant somebody had known the water was sliding west under the Sikes place before Ammon Sikes did. Somebody with the maps and the money and the register in his pocket had watched the table fall and had bought the low ground quiet, under a name that wasn't real, so that when the last water in the belt fetched up where the ground was lowest it would fetch up on paper they already held.

And here she came Thursday to stand on the high ground and raise a copper rod over the dry, and point, in front of the whole belt, at a hole that could not hold — while thirty feet the other side of the wire, under a false man's stake, lay the only water for two miles, salt though it was, waiting for whoever meant to dig it first.

The doves came over again while she sat there, the dusk flight, low, and they did not go west now. They dropped, out of the reddening air, one flight and then another, down to the saltgrass along the false man's fence, and folded their wings, and drank.

She watched them drink on the other side of the wire, and she did not say the thing she knew out loud, because there was no one to say it to, and because she had learned before she could read that the ground keeps its secrets better than any man and asks nothing for the keeping. She turned her horse for home. Behind her the stake stood white in the last light with its false name on it, first in time, first in right, and the water it fenced had already been drunk by birds that could not read.


---

# CHAPTER 9 · Movement II — The Following

### "The Dry Hole"

*The nester belt, east Cheyne County.*
*Third dry year. Rainless week twenty-one. The Rueda not flowing now at all but standing in pools, each pool a shrinking coin of green water fenced by cracked mud, the fish in them going belly-up and the cranes come to eat the dying. The Bar EM's north windmill pumping air since Michaelmas, the fan turning all day over a dry pipe like a thing that had forgotten what it was for. Hauled water: eight cents the barrel, and the freighter charging ten to the belt because his mules drank on the way.*

---

They came for her on foot, which told her more than anything they said.

Ronsard had owned a wagon and a span of mules in the spring. Now he came up her draw on his own two legs with the boy behind him and his hat in his two hands, having walked the nine miles from the belt in the dark to reach her by first light, and Odessa knew before he opened his mouth that the mules were sold and the wagon with them, and that whatever he was going to ask her for was the last thing he had breath to ask anybody.

"Miz Pike," he said.

"Ronsard."

He had a big slow face gone slack in the months since she'd walked his playa, the flesh of it hanging off the bone the way flesh does on a man who has been giving his own supper to a child. The boy stood at his hip. Nine, ten years old, in a shirt cut down from a man's and cinched with rope, and he looked at Odessa's covered arm the way children looked, openly, hunting the scar, and she let him hunt. Children she did not charge for the looking.

"The playa's dry," Ronsard said.

She had known it would go. She had told him so, in the way she told them things — sideways, wrapped in *dig wide, dig slow, shallow water shames easy* — advice she had made up on the spot in May and did not know the truth of, and he had dug it wide and slow and it had made him a summer of muddy water and then, as she'd half-expected and wholly failed to warn him of, it had quit. A playa is a rain-catch. In a year with no rain it catches nothing and gives back what little it held and then it is only a floor of gray clay with the sun on it.

"I know it," she said.

"I got no water on the place." He turned his hat a half-turn in his hands. "The Ostranders pulled out. The Neels are pulling out. Halve's called his book on me and I owe him for seed and I owe him for the digging tools and I owe him for the—" He stopped. His throat worked. "I owe him. He'll take the quarter for it if I got no crop and no water to make one, and I can't make one on hauled water at ten cents. Nobody can. There ain't a man in the belt can."

"Then leave," Odessa said. She said it flat, because it was the true thing and the kind thing at once, and she had learned young that the true thing and the kind thing wore the same face more often than people liked. "Load what you can carry and walk out before the store owns the walking too. There's cotton coming in down on the Concho. Men are hiring."

"I got my girl buried on that quarter," Ronsard said.

There it was. She let it sit between them in the cold morning air. West of the belt she'd buried a daughter in May, to the flux, in the wet-that-wasn't, and now the man would not leave the ground because the ground had his child in it, and that was not a thing Odessa could argue with, because it was not an argument. It was a grave. A grave is the one deed no man can call your book on.

"I want you to find me water," Ronsard said. "On the quarter. Deep. Not the playa — I know the playa's a rain-catch now, I ain't a fool, I learned that much off you. Real water. Well water. The kind that don't quit when the sky quits." He was not looking at her now. He was looking at his hat. "Halve'll give me to Christmas if I'm digging a well. A man digging a well's a man improving the place, and Halve's a Christian, he won't foreclose on improvement, he told me so. You find me the water and I dig the well and I keep the quarter and I keep my girl." He looked up. "Folks say you found sweet water under the Slash W caliche where there weren't supposed to be any. Folks say you knew it before you raised the rod."

*Folks say.* The two words that had built the thing she now could not get out from under. She had watched them build it, brick by brick, all summer — the older Dobie man who'd said *you didn't find it, you knew it*; the belt singing deliver-us over a hole; Vester Kyle's name for her in the *Clarion* in type as big as a hanging. The words came back at her now out of a starving man's mouth, and she saw that they had traveled all the way around the county and come home carrying a family's whole life on their back.

"What does it pay," she said. It was the only honest question she had.

"I got two dollars," Ronsard said. "And the shoat, if the shoat lives."

Two dollars. She had turned a rider of Cade's off her ground last month for saying she was Association property, and here was a man offering her the last two dollars between his family and the road, to walk his dying quarter and lie to him for silver.

She looked at the boy. The boy looked back at her without flinching, which almost nobody did.

"Keep the shoat," she said. "I'll take the two dollars. Before I walk."

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Ronsard quarter, NE corner, high ground off the playa. Caliche at the surface, white, ringing under the heel — not soil over caliche; caliche *is* the surface here. Broke a piece: solid, no seam, no damp under. Mesquite scrub on the rise, low, roots gone lateral not down — a bad sign, roots run flat when they hit hardpan and quit. No cottonwood. No willow anywhere on the quarter. Snakeweed and broom, dryland trash, thick. Grama gone to dust. Ant mounds on the flat, harvester, but the chaff-rings blown out and scattered — old, dead mounds, no traffic, no digging. Doves at dusk crossing the quarter but not dropping — flying *over*, west, toward the Rueda pools four miles off. Nothing dropping here. Ground does not fall to any low place that isn't the playa, and the playa is a sealed rain-catch, gray clay floor cemented, cracked to plates. Break-of-slope on the north fence — checked it — caliche runs unbroken under the whole slope, no tilt, no perch, no seep. No smell of water anywhere on the wind. Caliche all the way, far as a read can carry. Water lay here in the rain years, on top, in the playa, and ran off or dried. Below: rock. If there is deep water under this it is under the caliche cap and there is no way to read down through solid rock from the top of it. The read stops at the cap. The read says: nothing findable here.

---

She walked it twice.

The second walk was for herself, though she let them think it was for them — Ronsard and the boy standing at the fence with the sun coming up hard behind them, and a half-dozen others who had drifted over from the wagons still loading on the neighboring quarters, drawn by the sight of her the way they were always drawn now, the way flies come to the one wet thing in a dry field. She walked slow with the copper up and the wire biting her wrist and she did not perform, because there was no water to perform toward, and a performance without a find under it was a thing she had sworn she would never do, back in her own dead draw in May, practicing the waiting.

There was nothing to find. She knew it the way she knew her own dead hand. The caliche ran solid under the whole quarter and the read stopped at the cap and stopped meant *stopped*, meant she could not see through rock, meant the honest answer was the one she had already given him at first light: *leave*.

She lowered the rods on the second pass and turned to tell him.

And she saw his face.

It was not the face of a man asking a question. He had stopped asking somewhere on the nine-mile walk in the dark. It was the face of a man who had already spent the answer — had spent it on the store, on the boy, on the grave, on staying — and had come to her not to be told but to be *confirmed*, the way you don't ask the doctor whether the child will live, you ask him to say so. Behind that face stood the boy, and the boy was watching her hands, waiting for the copper to move; and behind the boy stood the drifted-over others, six or seven of them now, and one of them, a woman, had her lips moving, and Odessa did not have to hear the words to know they were *deliver us*.

She had told him *leave* once already, at her draw, at first light, and it had bounced off him like hail off a tin roof. She could say it again now and watch it bounce again. And she saw — standing on the ringing caliche with the boy waiting on her hands — that whether she said the true thing or pointed at a place, the end of it was the same end. Ronsard would dig. He would dig on the strength of her having walked at all, dig into solid rock with his boy hauling the spoil out in a bucket, and find caliche, and find caliche, and lose the quarter and the girl in it. There was no version of this morning in which he kept his water. She saw that with the flat clear cruelty she trusted above any mercy. The only thing left to decide was whether he dug it hoping or dug it knowing, and whether she was paid.

She was quiet a long time. Longer than the theater. Long enough that Ronsard's throat worked again and the woman's lips stopped moving and even the boy went still.

Then she raised the right rod slow, the way you'd lift a dying man's head to help him drink, and she walked back to the northeast corner where the caliche rang loudest under the heel, the deadest ground on the whole dead quarter, and she stopped, and she let the copper dip.

"Here," she said. Her voice did not stammer. It never did, for this. It came out plain and level and it landed the way she had spent twenty years learning to make it land, and the six or seven people at the fence took off their hats, and Ronsard made a sound like a man set down after a long carry, and the boy ran up onto the caliche and put both his hands flat on the ground where she pointed as if he could feel it coming up already through the rock.

"Deep," she said. "You'll dig a long way for it. Dig straight down and don't quit and don't let anybody tell you it isn't there." She was looking at the caliche and not at the man. "It's there."

It was not there. She knew it was not there the way she knew the sun was up. She had picked the deadest ground on purpose, the one corner where the caliche was thickest and hardest, because — she let herself think it once, coldly, and then never again — because if he was going to dig his family into a hole either way, then let it be the hole that took the longest, that gave the store the most improvement to be Christian about, that bought the man the most weeks before the rock told him what she already knew and would not say. She was not saving him. There was no saving him. She was only spending his despair down slow instead of fast, and charging two dollars to do it, and telling herself the slow way was the kinder one because she needed, this once, to have chosen the kinder one.

Ronsard came and took her hand — the good one — in both of his and held it, and his hands were shaking, and he said, "God sent you here. God kept you alive three times to send you here to me," and Odessa let him say it and did not take her hand back, because taking it back would have cost him something he could not afford, and giving it to him cost her nothing but the truth, and she had stopped, some time in the last hour, being able to afford the truth herself.

The boy was already looking for the shovel.

---

She did not stay to watch them start. She never stayed. But the county had grown small around her legend, and the belt smaller still, and word of a Ronsard well ran ahead of her the way word always did now, so that she heard about the digging without ever going back to see it — heard it at Halve's, where the store owner told her flat and ledger-hearted that Ronsard had come in for a fresh handle when the first shovel snapped on the rock; heard it from a freighter who said the man was down eleven feet and it was caliche the whole way and hard as a courthouse step; heard it, at last, on a cold bright afternoon three weeks on, from Vester Kyle, who came out to her draw with his ink-stained cuffs and his hungry look and told her Ronsard had struck a seam of gyp at nineteen feet.

Not water. Gyp. A vein of the white poison rock running through the caliche, and the boy had hauled up a bucket of the crumbled stuff and it had smelled, Kyle said, of rotten eggs when they broke it, and Ronsard had wept over it there at the mouth of the hole because he thought the smell meant water was close, that where there was that mineral stink there was water below it — which was a thing the county half-believed and which Odessa knew to be, as often as not, exactly backward, the gyp being the thing that fouled the water where there was any at all.

"He's going on down," Kyle said. He had his pencil out. "Says you told him not to quit. Says you told him it's there and a body can't call the Salt Preacher a liar by quitting nine feet short of her word." He looked at her with the pencil ready. "That's a good line, ain't it. *Nine feet short of her word.* You reckon it's there, Miz Pike? Deep water under the gyp?"

She looked past him, east, toward the belt she could not see, where a man was standing his son at the top of a nineteen-foot hole in solid rock and lowering the bucket back down.

"I never told him not to quit," she said, which was a lie, and Kyle wrote it down without pausing the pencil, and she watched him write it, and watched him believe it more than he had believed her when she told the truth.

Kyle rode off with his line. She stood in the mouth of her dugout in the cold and watched him go, and she did the arithmetic she always did, and for the first time in twenty years the arithmetic gave her back a number she could not spend: a man and a boy, nineteen feet down and going deeper, in a grave they were digging on her word, with a dead girl above them in the ground and a live poison rising to meet them from below, and both of them praising her name into the dark of the hole every time they bent to the shovel. She stood with that number and could not put it down. And would not, she found, if she could, have given back the two dollars.

Out past Kyle's dust, a crane lifted off one of the last green pools of the dying Rueda with a fish in its beak, and beat west, low, over the flats, toward the salt.



---

# CHAPTER 10 · Movement II — The Following

### "The Boy"

*The burned-out claim on the county's east edge — mid-October, 1893.*
*Twenty-three weeks without rain. The Rueda no longer runs; it stands, a chain of green pools between bars of cracked mud, and a man can walk the length of it dry-shod and count the dead minnows. Hauled water: ten cents the barrel. The second Bar EM windmill has gone to pumping air. And this morning the sun came up the color of a healing bruise, because a wind out of the west has lifted the west into the sky — the first duster of the drought, a wall of grit standing a mile high, so that at noon a man could look straight at the sun and see it whole, a dull copper coin behind the dirt.*

---

The wagon that came for her this time carried no water and no money in front, and she almost sent it back for that alone.

It was a spring wagon with the box near rotted off it, drawn by a single mule too poor to sell, and the man driving it was Neel — the young hard-eyed nester from the belt who had stood at the Gault playa in July and would not take his hat off. He took it off now. That told her more than his words did, and she disliked it more.

"There's a thing needs looking at," Neel said. "On the Sablon place."

"The Sablon place is burned," Odessa said. She knew the ground. Ten years of walking worthless country had put every abandoned quarter-section in the east belt into her the way it put everything into her, against her will, and staying. The Sablon claim had burned in the second dry year — the sod house and the little half-dugout barn gone in a grass fire that came up out of the south one August night — and the family had loaded what the fire left and gone to the Territory or to the grave, and no one had proved up on the ground since because the ground was cursed and the well was bad and a burned claim wears a look that keeps men off.

"It's burned," Neel agreed. "That ain't the thing." He turned the hat in his hands. He was a man who had come a long way toward believing something he had spent the summer refusing, and it sat on him like a sickness. "There's a boy been living out there. In the old dugout barn, what's left of the roof of it. Nobody knew. A stray — nobody's — living on jacks and prickly pear and whatever he can steal off the belt at night, wild as a dog. Neel's boy caught sight of him a week back and we've been leaving out corn dodgers for him like you'd leave for a cur, to gentle him." He stopped. "Two days ago he dug something up."

Odessa said nothing. She had learned that if you left the silence open, men filled it, and what they filled it with was the truth they'd meant to save.

"He won't leave off the hole," Neel said. "He's dug it wider. He sits by it. He won't let a man near it and he won't say a word — he don't talk, the boy, near as we can tell he can't or won't — but he'll stand off from it and point down into it, and point, and he won't stop pointing till a man comes and looks." His jaw worked. "We looked, Miz Pike."

"And?"

"And we want you to come tell us what's in it," Neel said. "Because we know what we think's in it, and no man on the belt wants to be the one that says so out loud on his own word, and it come to us that you're the one whose word it don't cost anything to be wrong on." He heard himself say it and colored. "That ain't — I didn't mean—"

"You meant it exactly," Odessa said. "It's true. Get down off the mule and rest it. I'll get my things."

---

She did not take money. She marked that, walking back to the dugout, marked it the way she marked everything — with suspicion of herself.
