Four dollars from the Gault belt in July she had taken and told herself the taking was the whole of it. This she did not name a fee at all, and a thing you don't name is a thing that can get loose, and she knew it, and she went anyway, because a hole a wild boy would not leave off pointing into was a thing the whole belt would talk of by Sunday whether she walked it or not, and if they were going to talk, she had rather be the one who told them what to say.

She wound the baling wire onto her wrist in the dugout, out of habit, and then unwound it and left the rods hanging on their nail. There would be nothing to find here that a rod was any part of finding. She took instead the little folding trowel a surveyor had left in a boarding-house room she'd cleaned in another life, and a length of clean sacking, and she pushed her sleeve down over the fern-scar out of the same habit that had made her reach for the wire, and she got up on the rotted seat beside Neel, and the mule leaned into the traces and the burned claim came toward them under the copper coin of the buried sun.

The duster thickened as they rode. It did not blow so much as *stand*, the whole west a brown weather with no rain in it, and the grit came into the teeth and the eyes and the ears, fine as flour, and lay in the creases of a man's hands and in the corners of his mouth, and the mule walked with its head down and its ears back as if into a driving snow that was not cold and would not melt. The stock they passed stood tail-to-the-wind with their eyes crusted shut, gaunted to hide and hinge, and one steer down in a fence corner not yet dead lifted its head at the wagon and put it down again. Odessa counted. She could not help the counting. Eleven head down or dying between the crossroads and the Sablon line, on ground that in a wet year would have carried them fat.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Burned quarter, SE corner. Grass fire two yrs old — soil crust black-flecked, mesquite stumps
> coaled, new mesquite up from the roots, greener than it has right to be. Old sod-house footings.
> Half-dugout barn dug into the low bank, N side; roof pole and part sod stand. Well NW of the barn,
> stoned round, dry; throw-dirt beside it gray, salt-bloomed — bad water, why they left it.
> The hole is NOT the well. Hole SE of the barn, in the lee bank where the fire didn't take. Fresh
> dug over old dug: two throws of dirt, the deep throw weathered gray, the fresh throw dark, boy's
> throw, hand-clawed. Under the fresh throw: caliche broke and set back. Not natural set. Broke by a
> hand once, packed down, healed over two yrs, broke again by the boy.
> Smell off the hole: not gyp. Not sweet water. Sweetish-foul, close, the smell of a thing that was
> covered and is not covered now.
> No dove flight here. No ants working the throw. Nothing that lives comes to this hole.

---

The boy was standing off from the hole when they came, twenty feet off, at the corner of the fallen barn, and Odessa saw him before Neel pointed him out, saw him the way she saw a killdeer at a playa edge before the men did — a small watchful stillness that did not belong to the rest of the standing dead country.

He was maybe twelve and looked eight for want of feeding, wire-thin, burned brown to the color of the ground so that he came near to disappearing into it, barefoot, in a man's shirt gone to rags and belted with a strip of hide. His hair was matted stiff with dust. He had a jack rabbit's stillness, the stillness of a thing that has learned that moving is what gets it killed, and his eyes were on her from the moment the wagon stopped and did not leave her, and there was in them no child's fear and no child's hope, only a terrible flat watching, the watching of a thing that has decided nothing about you yet.

Neel got down. "That's him. Won't come to a man." He raised his voice, gentling it the way you gentle a colt. "Boy. We brung the woman. The one that finds things. She's come to look."

The boy did not move. His eyes came off Odessa long enough to go to the hole, and back to her, and he lifted one thin arm and pointed down into the hole, the way Neel had said, and held it there.

Odessa got down off the wagon. She did not gentle her voice. She had found, three times struck and forty-one years pointed-at, that frightened things could smell a gentled voice for the lie it was, and that the only thing they trusted was a body that treated them as no different from the ground.

"Stop pointing," she said to him, flat. "I see the hole. I've got eyes."

The arm came down.

Something crossed his face that was not a smile — he had no smile in him that she could see, it had been burned or starved or beaten out of him — but a loosening, a small settling, the settling she had seen once in a colt that had been whipped and then, for the first time, spoken to as a colt and not as a fault. She marked it and set it aside. It was nothing to her. She had come to read a hole.

She walked past him to the hole and he did not flinch from her passing, and she noted that too, and set that aside as well.

---

The hole went down maybe four feet, wider than a well, dug once long ago and dug open again this week. The old digging had been squared, the work of a grown man with a spade; the fresh digging over it was clawed, ragged, the work of the boy's two hands. Odessa knelt at the lip with her ruined hand flat on the throw-dirt, the way she knelt at every seep and tank in the county, and she read what she had already read on the wind coming across the flat.

The smell came up out of it, close and sweetish-foul, and under it the mineral of broke caliche, and she knew before she took the trowel to it what was down there, and she took her time about the knowing, because that was the one thing that never changed, that you took your time, and she was aware of the boy standing off at her left shoulder now, come three steps nearer, watching her hands.

She cleared the loose throw with the trowel. The old man's-work digging had gone down to caliche and stopped at it, and the caliche had been broke and lifted and set back down two years gone — she could see the weathered gray of the old break, and how a hand had packed the fill over it — and the boy had cleared the fill again and left the broke caliche lying to the side, and under the broke caliche was a hollow scraped in the softer marl below the shelf, and in the hollow was what the boy had been pointing at.

It was a man. Or it had been. Two years of dry ground and the sealed caliche over it had used him gently, as such things go — the flat is a great preserver, the salt and the dryness keep what the wet would take — so that he was not gone to bone but gone to leather, a brown dried shape drawn down onto its frame, the mouth pulled back, a scrap of shirt on the ribs of him and a boot on one foot and the other foot bare. Around the socket of him, in the marl, the salt had drawn up white the way it always drew up last, ringing the shape of him in a rime like frost, like the flat itself had reached up under the ground to claim him.

Odessa looked at him a long moment. She had seen dead men. The county was not gentle and she was not young. But she had not seen one buried under a broke caliche shelf with the throw-dirt of a well beside him, and she read that, read it the way she read the greener grass and the badger's throw, cold, complete, the reasons arriving one behind another.

The old digging was a well. A man had come onto the burned Sablon claim after the fire, when it stood empty, and had begun to dig a well in the lee of the barn — good ground for it, low, out of the wind. And someone had come on him digging, or had brought him here to dig, and the man had gone down into his own well-hole and the caliche shelf had been broke open below him and he had been put under it and the shelf set back over him and the fill packed down, and the throw-dirt weathered two years gray, and the well was never finished and never claimed, and the claim wore its burned-and-cursed look and kept men off, and the flat drew its salt up around him in the dark and preserved him against the day a wild starving boy would clear the fill with his two hands, looking, God knew, for water.

---

She did not tell them yet. It was the same in this as in the other thing — you did not tell them yet — but the reason was different now, and she felt the difference in her the way she felt the wire cut her wrist, a small hard warning. This was not a pronouncement to be delivered so it landed like weather. This was knowledge that could hang a man, and she had learned in the land office in June exactly what her word was worth and exactly how it could be used to hang her instead.

Because she knew whose man this likely was.

The boot on the dead foot was a good boot, or had been — a stitched-welt boot, a cattleman's boot, not a nester's brogan; a nester in this country was buried in a nester's shoes if he was buried in shoes at all. And there was a length of new-galvanized wire fetched up in the fill beside him, a coil of it, bright still where the dry ground had kept it, the kind of wire that in the second dry year came only off the big spools the Association freighted in — the wire the Bar EM and the Slash W strung to fence the country's last live water away from the free-grass men. A nester's ghost did not wear a cattleman's boot and lie down on a coil of the Association's own wire. And the Sablon claim sat, she knew, right on the line of a thing that had been argued in the county the year of the fire and gone quiet after — a water right, a seep in the draw a half mile south that the Bar EM had ended up holding and the Sablons had not, and the arguing had gone quiet the same season the fire came and the family left.

She knelt there with the salt-ringed dead man looking up at her out of his leather face and she did the arithmetic she always did, the only arithmetic she trusted. What she said here bought her something or cost her something, and she had better know which before she opened her mouth.

If she said *a man was murdered here and buried under his own well and he wore a cattleman's boot and lay on the Association's wire* — then she was a woman who had, in one summer, learned to find the county's water and learned to find the county's murder, and the men who ran the county would have two reasons to fear her instead of one, and a woman they feared for one reason they hired, and a woman they feared for two reasons they buried, under a broke caliche shelf, in the lee of a barn.

And if she said *nothing, it's an old grave, leave it* — then the belt, who had brought her here because her word cost nothing, would carry away that her word had failed them, and the legend she was chained to would take the crack of a failure and — she had watched it now with the dry hole, watched it with her own eyes — heal over it stronger, and rewrite her silence into something, and she did not know into what, and not knowing what a crowd would make of her was the thing she had come to fear worse than the crowd itself.

She chose the third thing. She had always been good at the third thing.

---

"There's a man down here," she said, standing, wiping the trowel on the sacking. Her voice came out flat and slow and unstammering, the performing voice, and she let it, because the men needed to hear it that way and because the boy was listening and she did not care to sound afraid in front of the boy. "Been down here two years or better. It's not water this claim was hiding."

Neel came to the lip and looked and went the color of the sky and turned away and was sick, quietly, into the wind, and the other two belt men who had ridden up looked and took their hats off, this time all of them, and one of them said, low, "Sablon," and the other said, "Sablon left. Sablon went to the Territory," and the first man said nothing, because they both knew that a family that leaves does not leave a man in a hole.

"Whose is it?" Neel said, wiping his mouth. "Miz Pike. Whose man is it?"

And here she held the silence, and it was the true withholding now, not the theater — she held it because the answer was a loaded gun and she had not yet decided where to point it. She looked down into the salt-ringed hole and up at the standing brown weather where the sun hung dull as a coin, and she felt the four men waiting on her, leaning in, the terrible readiness of them to be told, and she felt the boy waiting differently, waiting not to be told but to be *believed* about the thing he had found and could not say.

"He wore good boots," she said at last. "That's all his own mother could tell you now, and I'm not his mother. Cover him back over and mark it and let the sheriff dig him if the sheriff's got the belly, which he hasn't. And don't put my name on the telling of it." She said the last hard, and looked at each of them in turn until each one nodded. "You found him. The boy found him. I only came and looked. You hear me. I only came and looked."

They heard her. But she watched it happen in their faces even as they nodded — watched the crack open and heal stronger, exactly as it had at the dry hole. They had brought her to a hole to tell them what was in it, and she had knelt at the lip and told them a dead man was in it, and they would go home and say it, and it would not be *she came and looked* by the time it reached the belt. It would be *she smelt him.* It would be *she stood at the lip and said there's a man down here before ever the trowel touched him.* It would be *she finds the water and now, God save us, she finds the dead* — the woman who reads the ground, reads it all the way down, past the water to the bone.

She saw the myth take the new ground the way a fire takes a fence line. She had been a water-witch on Sunday. She would be, by the next Sunday, a woman who could hear the dead calling up out of the dirt, and there was not one thing she could do to stop it, because the thing she had done to stop it — the plain flat *I only came and looked* — was already, in the leaning-in faces of four frightened men, being remade into scripture.

---

They mounted up to ride and fetch the sheriff who would not come. Odessa did not ride with them. She said she'd walk the half mile to the crossroads and catch the belt wagon back, and that was a lie, and Neel knew it was a lie and was too sick and too frightened to argue it, and they went, and the duster swallowed them within a hundred yards, so that she stood in the brown standing weather alone.

Except she was not alone.

The boy had not gone to the hole. The boy had come and stood, while the men mounted, some six feet off from her, downwind, so that the grit blew off her and onto him and he did not mind it, and he was watching her still with that flat terrible watching, only it was not so flat now, there was a thing working in it, and she understood, with the same cold completeness she had read the grave, exactly what the thing was, because it was the thing she had lain in the road three times and felt the whole county aim at her.

He had found a dead man and been unable to say so, and had pointed and pointed and no one had come, and then she had come and had not flinched, had knelt at the lip of the horror with her hand flat in the throw-dirt as if it were only ground, and had told the men the true thing he could not tell them, and had made them believe him. She was the first grown body in whatever ruin of a life he had that had looked at the worst thing he knew and not turned from it and not made it his fault.

He was going to follow her. She read it in him plain as she read the salt ringing the dead. He was going to follow her the way the belt followed her and the county was coming to follow her, only worse, because he had watched her do the trick — had watched her kneel and read and withhold and pronounce — and it would not matter to him at all that he had watched it, it would only bind him harder, the way a thing seen close binds a child harder than a thing merely heard.

"No," she said to him. Flat. "Go back to your hole. There's nothing here for you. I don't feed strays."

He did not move. He did not weep, or plead, or point. He only stood downwind of her in the standing dust with the sun a dull coin overhead, and looked at her with the whole of his ruined face, and waited on her — waited the way she made men wait, the way the burnt county waited on the sky — as if he had already understood, faster than she had, that her *no* was the surest word she had ever spoken and the one she was least going to be able to keep.

Odessa turned her back on him and started for the crossroads through the blowing dirt. She did not look around. The grit came into her teeth and her eyes and she walked into it with her sleeve pushed down over the wire and the fern-scar and her ruined hand aching in the cold that was not cold, and she counted her steps to keep from counting anything else, and at forty paces, without turning, she knew he was behind her, six feet back and downwind, matching her step for step, silent as the salt, following the one body in the world that had not flinched — and she walked on, and did not send him off again, and told herself, hard, that letting a thing follow you was not the same as wanting it, and did not once believe herself, and let it follow her all the same into the brown standing weather where the buried sun gave no shadow to either of them, so that they crossed the dead ground as one dark shape, and left no track the wind did not take.


---

# CHAPTER 11 · Movement II — The Following

### "Melbern Sells the Sky"

*Salt Fork, Cheyne County.*
*Rainless week twenty-five. The Rueda a chain of standing pools between banks of cracked plate. Hauled water ten cents the barrel and climbing. Dead stock county-wide past counting — the men had stopped saying a number and started saying "the thousands," which is what a man says when he has quit counting and does not want you to know he quit.*

---

They put the platform up in front of the courthouse in a single afternoon, and Odessa stood across the square in the shade of Halve's porch and watched them build it, and she read it the way she read ground.

Green lumber. Sap still bleeding at the cut ends where the depot's shipment had been robbed for it, boards meant for somebody's floor gone instead into a stage for a man from Kansas. Six feet high. Higher than the brush arbor Rourke had preached from in June, and she marked that, because a man who builds his platform higher than the preacher's is a man telling the county something about the size of the thing he intends to be. They faced it east, into the wind, which was the only direction the wind had come from in twenty-five weeks and would come from tomorrow, and she marked that too, because it meant Gaines Melbern had asked somebody which way the wind blew and had listened to the answer, which was more than most men who came to the county to save it had troubled to do.

She did not go over. She let them see her not go over. That was work also.

Vester Kyle found her on the porch with his cuffs already gray by ten in the morning and his pencil behind his ear like a splinter he'd forgotten was in him.

"You'll want to hear this," he said.

"I doubt it."

"The Association voted a bounty. Last night. Cade in the chair." He watched her face and got nothing off it, which never stopped him trying. "Five hundred dollars, payable to any man who puts a half inch of rain on the courthouse gauge inside of thirty days. The merchants put up two hundred more. Halve's in it. The bank's in it." He wet the pencil against his lip, a habit that would kill him before the consumption did. "Seven hundred dollars, Miz Pike. In a county where a steer won't bring eight."

"Then a steer's the honest price and the rain's the swindle," she said.

He wrote that down. She had learned by now that anything she said flat enough, he wrote down, and it came back the next Thursday in the *Clarion* dressed up in his own longer words with her name over it. She had told him in June to quit building a thing bigger than she was. He had taken it, like everything, for a better line.

"He's got apparatus," Kyle said, still writing. "Melbern. Came off the noon train with four crates and a valise and a Chinaman to carry the crates, and he's took the whole loft over the feed store and nailed a quilt across the window so nobody sees the gas he cooks. Says it's a secret worth more than the bounty." He looked up. "You going to watch him do it?"

She looked across the square at the platform, at the men leaning green boards into place, at the crowd that was already a crowd though the man had promised nothing yet for two days out. She counted it out of habit. Sixty, seventy in the square by noon, and the tent-wagons of the ones come in from the belt still rolling past the depot, and it was not even market day.

"I'll watch," she said. "A body ought to know his own trade by watching a man work the opposite one."

Kyle put the pencil back of his ear and stood a moment looking across at the platform with her, and for once he did not write anything. Across the square a man was nailing bunting to the raw front of the stage — red cloth, brought in a crate, the only color in the whole burnt town — and the sight of it seemed to move Kyle in some private way she did not care to follow.

"You know what he charged them?" Kyle said. "Melbern. To even come. On top of the bounty." He shook his head. "A hundred dollars and his fare. To *try.* And they voted it him grateful, Miz Pike. Grateful. A county that can't buy shoes for its young ones voted a hundred dollars to a man to smoke up a roof." He said it half sour and half in wonder, and she understood that he was, under the ink and the hunger for a line, a man watching the place he was from go strange in front of him and setting it down because setting it down was the only thing he knew to do with it.

"You'll print it kindly," she said.

"I'll print what happens."

"That's the same thing," she said, "in a dry year," and Kyle laughed, short, and wrote *that* down after all.

---

The opposite one. She turned that over on the walk back to where she'd left the mule, and it stayed true, and the truth of it sat in her like a cold stone she was not sure she wanted to keep.

Melbern sold the sky. She sold the ground. And the sky could not be dug.

That was the whole of it, and it had taken her a rival to see it plain. A man paid Melbern five hundred dollars and Melbern cooked his gas and worked his crates and then everyone waited, and if it rained — and in this country it would rain, someday, on somebody, if you gave it thirty days and a wide enough sky — then Melbern had done it. And if it did not rain in the thirty days it would rain in the forty, and by then Melbern would be three counties on with the bounty of the next drought in his pocket, and the failure would have cost the men who paid him five hundred dollars they could not spare and not one thing more. Nobody dug a hole into the sky. Nobody's child was down a well of air.

Her fraud you could dig. That was the difference, and she had not let herself hold it until now. When she said *there*, a man took a shovel to the exact yard of ground her copper pointed, and either the water was in it or it was not, and if it was not there was a hole in the earth with a season's labor at the bottom and sometimes a family's whole holding, and the hole did not go away when Melbern's train pulled out because she did not have a train and the hole was in her county and it had a man standing in it.

Melbern was safe because Melbern was shallow. She had spent her life reading depth. She could not have said, before this, that a thing being shallow was what made it safe.

She thought of the playa on the Gault quarter, the hole Ronsard and the belt men had dug on her word back in June, that they had sung *deliver us* over by firelight. She did not know, this day, whether that hole still held. She had not gone back to look and would not, because there was nothing in going back but the answer, and the answer could only be bad two ways out of three. That was the thing about the ground. It kept a ledger, and it did not forgive an entry, and there was no train that carried you out from under what you'd wrote in it. Melbern could be wrong five hundred dollars' worth every thirty days from here to the sea and never once have to stand in a hole he'd been wrong about. She had been wrong exactly nowhere yet, so far as the county knew, and it terrified her more than being wrong would have, because a woman who had not yet failed was a woman the whole county was waiting to watch fail, and they would wait the way she made them wait, leaning in.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Sky white to the rim, hazed with dust, not cloud. No thunderheads west three days running. Wind SE, steady, hot, no shift at dawn — no moisture in it, no smell of wet caliche off any quarter. Swallows high, not low; no low-hunting, no insects driven down. Grasshoppers thick on the dead grama, wings dry-rattling — a dry-country brood, not a wet one coming. Ant lions' pits open in the dust under the porch, un-drowned, weeks old. Horned toads out at noon, not holed up. Nothing in the ground says water rising; nothing in the air says water falling. Bounty runs thirty days. Sky is wide. Somewhere in thirty days it rains on somebody. Not here. Not by the gas.

---

The day Melbern worked it, the square was full by the time the sun was two hours up.

They had come from the belt and from the free-grass country and from ranches that had let their hands off for want of anything to hand them, and they stood in the July heat with no shade but their hats, and the dust off their own feet hung over them so the far side of the square went soft and brown. Odessa took the corner of Halve's porch again where the overhang cut the worst of it, and she was not alone there long. The boy came and stood by the post below her without a word, the way he did now, having attached himself to her on a burnt claim two weeks gone and never explained it and never been asked to.

Tobe did not look at the platform. He looked at the crowd, which was the thing worth looking at, and she saw that he had learned that from her without her teaching it, and she filed it, and it cost her something small and buried that she did not examine.

Melbern came up onto his green platform in a checked suit that had never seen a day's real dust and a watch chain that caught the sun the way she made her copper catch it, and she gave him that — he knew a bright object made men look. He had a table up there with brass instruments on it that did nothing but turn and gleam, and a length of India-rubber hose that ran off the back of the platform to the feed-store loft where the quilt hung in the window, and he stood a moment and let the crowd get quiet, and she gave him that too. He let the silence run. He counted it the way she counted it. Then he ruined it, because he was the inverse of her, and where she hoarded the silence he could not bear it, and he filled it.

"Friends," he said, and his voice rolled out over them, trained and warm and never once sincere, "I am not a preacher, and I am not a prophet, and I will not stand up here and tell you the drought is God's judgment on your sins, because I have been in a good many dry counties and I never yet met a county sinful enough to earn *this*."

That got a low sound out of them, half a laugh, and she marked how fast he'd bought them — one line, and he'd set himself against Rourke without naming him, made himself the friendly science against the hard church. She would not have thought to do it that way. She did it with silence; he did it with talk; and the crowd, God help them, took either one.

"What I am," Melbern said, "is a practitioner. A meteorological practitioner. I study the affinities of the air — the great engines of moisture that ride the upper sky and pass over you, dry counties, without stopping, because nothing down here has ever *invited* them down." He laid a soft hand on his brass. "My gases raise an affinity. They reach up and take the moisture by the sleeve, as it were, and ask it, politely, to alight."

She watched him say *invite* and *ask it politely* and she understood the second half of his trade then, and it was cannier than the gas. He never once promised. He asked the sky. And a man who asks cannot be blamed when the asked-of says no. He had built himself, out of nothing but grammar, the exact thing she had never been able to build for herself — a fraud that could fail without a corpse.

---

He cooked his gas all that day and into the dark.

There was a stink off the feed-store loft by afternoon, a bad chemical sourness that drifted down over the square when the wind slacked, and a thin brown smoke came from a pipe he'd run out through the eave, and men stood in the heat and watched a roof smoke and believed they were watching the weather get made. He touched off charges too, out past the depot, three of them through the day — a hollow *whump* that rolled off the flats and came back, and each time the charge went the crowd flinched and looked up, and each time the sky was white and empty as a bone, and each time they went on believing, because a man does not walk home from a thing he has stood all day to see and tell his wife he stood all day to see nothing.

Odessa flinched too, at the charges. She could not help it. The sudden hollow crack of them went into her the way thunder did, the old animal jump under the skin, the shoulders drawing up toward the ears before the mind could send word down that there was nothing in the sky to fear — the body remembering three times what the sky had done to it and needing no permission to remember. She despised it in herself. And she saw the boy at the post did not flinch at all, not once, held still through all three the way a held-breath thing holds still, and it went into the growing account of Tobe she kept and could not read.

By dusk the sky was the same sky.

By the second dawn the sky was the same sky.

And on the afternoon of the second day, with the bounty's thirty days barely three days spent and the crowd thinning back toward the belt disappointed but not yet angry, the wind shifted.

She felt it before she saw anything. She was at her mule by the depot and the wind came round out of the southwest into the west and there was, in it, faint, at the far edge of what a nose could hold, the smell of wet caliche off some quarter a long way off — that changed smell, dust laid down by rain a hundred miles out where no man in Cheyne County would ever see the storm that made it. She stood with her ruined hand on the mule's neck and read the sky and knew, the way she knew the ground, that a little weather had wandered loose off the mountains west and was coming apart as it came, the way they nearly always came apart before they reached the county, and that some ragged tail of it might, or might not, drag across the flats before it died.

That night it rained.

Not a rain. A quarter of an hour of fat scattered drops out of a sky that never fully clouded, drops that hit the dust and balled it and rolled and were gone, that wet the top skin of the caliche and no more, that laid the dust for an hour and did not reach a root of anything, that the courthouse gauge caught to a bare wet film — not the half inch, not near it, not a tenth of it. It rained the way a dying man wets his lips.

And the whole county came apart over it like it was the flood.

---

She stood in the mouth of the livery where she'd put up for the night and watched grown men run out into it with their hats off and their faces up and their mouths open, watched them stand in the fifteen minutes of nothing-rain with their arms out like they'd been shot, and she heard, over the noise of them, a woman crying somewhere in the dark the way you cry at a graveside, and she understood she was watching the county drink a thing that would not fill a thimble and call it the sea.

By morning it was over and the ground was dry again by the time the sun cleared the depot roof, dry as if the night had never happened, the way a lick of water in this country was always a rumor by noon. And by the time she came out to her mule the argument had already started in the square, and it was not the argument she'd have guessed.

She'd thought they'd fight over whether it was enough for the bounty. That fight was there — the merchants saying a wet film was not a half inch and a half inch was the word wrote on the paper, and Melbern's man saying rain was rain and the practitioner had *brought* it and the gauge was a lawyer's quibble. That fight would run a week and Melbern would lose the money and leave on the noon train, and she had read that far and been right.

What she had not read was the second thing, and the second thing was worse.

She heard her own name in it.

She came up on the edge of the crowd by the platform with the boy trailing her and she stood at the back where they'd not see her, and she listened, and a belt man she half knew — Neel, the young hard-eyed one from Persy Gault's stretch, the one who would not take his hat off in June — was saying it loud to the men around him, saying it the way a man says a thing he has just now decided and is thrilled to have decided.

"He didn't bring it," Neel said. "The Kansas man didn't bring nothing. He cooked his stink two days and the sky laughed at him." He turned, taking in the ring of them. "It come the second night. You know what come the second night." He let it sit, and she watched him let it sit, watched a belt farmer teach himself her own trade in front of her eyes. "*She* was in town."

Somebody said something she didn't catch.

"I'm saying she finds it below," Neel said. "She finds it in the ground. And a thing she can find below she can call up. She called it up to meet his sky. That's what the second night was. She never even had to walk. She was just *here*, and it come."

---

She stood at the back of the crowd and felt it turn onto her the way she felt weather turn — that shift in the pressure of the air, the leaning-in, all those faces coming around toward the back of the square where she stood. And her body did the thing it did. The old animal jump, the same one the charges had pulled out of her the day before, only this time there was no hollow crack to blame it on, no gas, no whump off the flats — only faces, only the turning of frightened people toward her, and her shoulders went up toward her ears exactly as they had when she'd thought the sky might strike, because to her body the two were the same weather. She got the shoulders down. It took work. She had not had to work to get them down since she was a girl.

She had not walked. She had not raised a rod. She had not so much as looked at the sky in front of a witness. She had leaned on Halve's porch and read the wind to herself and kept her mouth shut — and there it was, the flat cold ledger doing its sums against her will: she had done *nothing*, the one act she still owned free and clear, and the county had taken the nothing and dug a miracle out of it.

The huckster had cooked his gas for two days in front of five hundred witnesses and gotten nothing. She had done nothing, in front of nobody, and gotten the rain.

Something went out of her, standing there, and she felt it go the way you feel water leave a seep — a small warm draining, low in her, that left the place colder than it had been before there was ever water in it. All her life the going-still had been hers. When the church-shaped county rang her round after each strike and leaned in to see would she rise up cursed or chosen, the one thing they could not make her do was perform for them; she could lie there and give them nothing and let the nothing be a wall between her and their wanting. She had carried that wall out of the road with her and lived behind it forty years. And they had just walked through it without touching it. They had turned her stillness into a yes. She stood in the square and understood, in her stomach and her drawn-up shoulders before she understood it anywhere she could put words to, that she no longer had the choice to not do the thing — that the county would go on finding her finding water whether she lifted a hand or lay down in the dust and shut her eyes, and that the last unperformed corner of her was gone, taken, spent, without her having so much as opened her mouth.

Melbern was on his platform above the argument, and she looked up at him through the leaning heads, and he was looking down through them straight at her, and his actor's face was not doing patter now. He had heard Neel too. She watched the Kansas man understand that he had cooked his gas for two days and stood up on his six-foot platform and paid a Chinaman to carry his crates, and that the rain, such as it was, the thing he could not get for five hundred dollars, had been handed for free to a woman leaning on a store porch who had not so much as raised her head. And she watched something go out of his face that had been friendly and something come into it that was not, watched him mark her the way she marked ground, and knew she had made an enemy standing still — the one kind she could not have seen coming, because she had not moved.

The wind came round to the west again as she stood there, hot and dry and empty of everything, laying the smell of far-off wet-caliche across the square one more time — the smell of rain that had already fallen on somebody a hundred miles gone, and would not fall here — and the whole crowd lifted its face to it, breathing it in, believing.


---

# CHAPTER 12 · Movement II — The Following

### "The Franchise"

*Salt Fork, Cheyne County — the middle of August, 1893.*
*Third dry year. No rain in twenty-seven weeks. The Rueda down to the step-across mark at the ford — a man could cross it and not wet his boots, and men did, to say they had. Hauled water: twelve cents the barrel. On the Blanco road the nester wagons went east, loaded and slow, and past them, coming the other way, came the pilgrims — afoot, on borrowed mules, in a spring wagon with a text painted on the side — coming to be near the woman the paper had named.*

---

She counted the wagons before she counted anything else, because the wagons were the truth and the crowd was the lie the truth had grown.

Nine going out. She sat the mule at the top of the Blanco grade where the road bent past Halve's and she watched them go — the nesters, the belt families, the ones who had sung *deliver us* over a hole in the dark four months ago and had waited on the water and the rain both, and had now loaded what a wagon would hold and pointed it east toward country that was, everyone said, no wetter, but was at least not this. She knew two of the drivers by name. Neither looked up at her. One of them, the young hard-eyed man named Neel who had never once taken off his hat to her, looked up, and did not take off his hat, and turned his face to the road, and that was a mercy she noted and did not name.

Coming the other way, up the grade toward town, she counted four. A mule cart. A man walking with a bedroll and a Bible tied to it with twine. A whole family on foot, the woman carrying a child that did not move the way a well child moves. And the spring wagon with the text on the side board, painted fresh in red, the letters running downhill toward the end where the painter had misjudged his space: THE LORD HATH SET A SIGN AMONG YOU.

She sat the mule and watched the two currents pass each other on the narrow grade, the leaving and the coming, and neither one hailed the other, because they did not believe they were traveling the same road. The ones going out had believed and been emptied. The ones coming in were full of it and had not spent any yet.

The mule shifted under her. Her right wrist ached where the wire had cut, days healed and aching still. She turned the mule and rode into a town that was not the town she had ridden out of in the spring.

---

Salt Fork had grown a skin of tents.

Along the wagon-yard behind the depot, where in May there had been nothing but the water-tank under its guard and a mesquite thicket, there were now tents — a dozen, more, canvas gone the color of the dust, a cook-fire, a man selling airtights of tomatoes off the tail of a wagon at a price that would have shamed a Comanche. A boy she did not know led two strangers past her, pointing at things, and she heard him say her name, or the name they gave her now, the one Vester Kyle had set in type — *the Salt Preacher* — the way a boy points out the courthouse or the jail, a fixed thing, a feature of the country. She rode past a woman kneeling in the dust of the road with her apron over her face, praying, or resting; it was hard, now, to tell the difference in this town.

The Rueda where it ran under the depot siding was a thread. Salt cedar had come up thick along the dry margin, feathery and green, throwing its false shade over the white crust the ground made where the salt drew up, and under the salt cedar a family was camped, and she saw the mother send a child to the thread of water with a bucket, and she did not stop, and she did not say what she knew, which was that the water that thin, this late, running that slow over that ground, was already halfway to poison and would be all the way there by September. She had a whole town full of things she knew and did not say. It was getting to be a weight she carried the way she carried the boy.

The boy was behind her. Tobe rode the sacked-out old horse Halve let him keep in the lean-to, and he rode it the way he did everything, silent, close, always half a length back and never once further, and she had stopped telling him to go home because he had no home to go to and because telling him to go was a door and she did not open doors. He watched the tents the way she did — reading them, counting them, and she did not know what a boy his age counted and did not ask.

She had come to town to be paid. That was all. Cade owed her for two walks and she wanted it in silver before the month turned and the silver got scarcer. She had come to be paid and to buy coal oil and side-meat and to ride home, and she understood, riding down into the swollen town with its skin of tents and its painted wagon and its praying woman in the road, that being paid was going to be the smallest thing that happened to her that day.

Rourke was waiting on the steps of the courthouse. So was Cade. They were not standing together.

---

Rourke came down the steps to her before she had the mule stopped, and he did the thing with his hand, the laying of the long fingers on the mule's neck as if he owned the mule, and looked up at her out of his road-burned face with the black coat gone rusty at the seams, and he did not smile so much as arrange his face into the shape of welcome.

"Miz Pike," he said. "You've seen the road."

"I've seen it."

"Nine out and four in, by my count." He had counted them too. That was the first thing she disliked about Rourke and the truest — that he counted the way she counted, that under the scripture-cadenced voice was a man doing arithmetic. "The out-going will be forgotten by their own children. The coming-in — " he turned his head, unhurried, and looked at the painted wagon working up the last of the grade — "the coming-in are the start of a thing. You could ride out to that grade every morning of your life and watch it grow."

"I'd rather be paid and go home."

"Get down," he said. "Cade wants you inside. So does Waybourne, though he's not shown himself yet — he will. There's a room and there's a table and there are three men who mean to divide you up on it like a shot deer." He said it plainly, without malice, the way a man tells you the weather. "I'd sooner you walked in knowing than not."

She looked down at him a moment. She had learned young the difference between a man who tells you the truth to warn you and a man who tells you the truth to be the one who told you, and Rourke was the second kind, and knew she knew it, and did not mind.

She got down. She left Tobe with the mule. The boy took the reins without a word and stood in the shade of the courthouse wall and became, as he could, a part of the wall.

---

The room was the county clerk's, off the court chamber, and it was hot as the inside of a stove, and there was a deal table and four chairs and a pitcher of water on the table that no one touched, because to touch it was to admit you were as thirsty as everyone else. Cade sat at the head of the table because Cade sat at the head of every table. Waybourne came in behind her, so that she was between the two of them before she had chosen a chair, and she understood that too, and took the chair with her back to the wall and her face to the door, the way she took every chair, so that Waybourne had to move around and sit with the window at his back and the light in his eyes.

Rourke stood. Rourke did not need a chair; standing was his instrument, the way silence was hers.

"We'll not waste the afternoon," Cade said. He had a paper in front of him, folded once, and he did not open it yet, and she clocked that — the paper he made wait, his own small theater, learned or born, she couldn't tell. "You've done what I paid you to do, Miz Pike. There's been no killing over the head-springs since May. The Dobie crowd and my riders stood in the same field and watched you and went home. That's peace, and I bought it, and it's held." He turned the folded paper a quarter-turn on the table with one finger. "The trouble is that it's grown past the fence I built it in."

"You built no fence," she said. "You lit a fire and told it where to burn."

Cade looked at her a long moment. It was not anger. Cade did not spend anger any more than he spent smiles. It was recalculation, the sum he had run on her at the ford in May run again and coming out larger.

"Be that as it may," he said. "The fire's loose. There's a boomtown growing on my depot yard I never voted for. There's a preacher — " he did not look at Rourke — "wants to put a tent up and take a tithe. There's men riding in from three counties to have you walk their dry ground, and every one of those walks, if it hits, is a claim, and a claim is a thing men shoot over, as you well know by now. I hired a quiet thing and I've got a loud one. So." He opened the paper. "We'll make it orderly."

---

"It's an agreement," Cade said. "Between you and the Association. You walk where the Association sends you and no place else. Every find is filed by the Association, in the Association's hand, at the land office, before a shovel goes in — so there's no race, so there's no killing. You're paid a wage — a good wage, a hundred the month, more than a top hand makes in three — and paid whether you find or dry. And you don't walk for the preacher, and you don't walk for a Waybourne, and you don't walk for a nester with four dollars, and you don't walk for a pilgrim with a painted wagon. You walk for the county." He turned the paper so it faced her across the deal table. "Which is to say, you walk for me."

She did not reach for it. She read it upside down as far as she could, which was far, because she read men's hands and men's papers the way she read ground, and what the paper said was what his mouth had said, only longer, and with a line at the bottom for her mark.

"A hundred the month," she said.

"A hundred."

"And I go where you send."

"You go where the county needs water."

"And if the county's need and the Bar EM's need aren't the same water." She let it sit. "If the Dobie men are dying for a drink and the water I'd find for them is water you'd sooner they died without — where do I walk then, Mr. Cade?"

Cade did not answer that, which was the answer.

Waybourne laughed. It was a good laugh, warm, and he had good teeth and used them, and he leaned forward into the light he'd been given the worst of and made even that look like a choice.

"There it is," Waybourne said. "Odessa — may I — Miz Pike. You see what he's offering you. He's offering to make you a Bar EM windmill. A hundred a month to pump where he says and go dry where he says." He spread his handsome ruined hands. "I'll better it. I'll not put you on a wage like a hired man — I'll cut you in. You find water on Slash W ground, you own a piece of the water. A share. A woman with a share of live water in this county in this year is a woman who never wants for anything again, ever, world without end." He'd been saving *world without end;* she heard him spend it. "That's a deed, near enough. That's the thing you're after, isn't it. Everybody knows what you're after."

That last landed harder than he meant it to, or exactly as hard; with Waybourne you could never tell where the calculation stopped and the appetite began. Everybody knew what she was after. She had let it be known, once, to one man, in the spring, at a ford, and it had gone from Cade's mouth outward the way everything did, and now a mortgaged man across a hot table dangled her own want at her like meat on a wire and watched her face to see the dog move.

She kept the dog still. She had spent forty-one years keeping the dog still.

"A share of nothing is nothing," she said. "You're offering me a cut of water you don't have. When you've got water, Mr. Waybourne, come talk to me about shares of it."

The teeth went away for a half-second and came back.

---

Then Rourke spoke, and the room changed the way a field changes when the wind lays down before hail.

He had not moved from where he stood by the shut window, and he did not raise his voice; he lowered it, and the lowering pulled every head in the room toward him, and Odessa felt her own head want to turn and did not let it, and understood in that not-letting exactly what she did to a crowd, felt it done to her, and did not like the feeling and filed it.

"You're both talking about a woman," Rourke said, "and a well, and a wage, and a share. And there's no woman and no well in this at all anymore. That's what neither of you has grasped, and it's costing you." He turned his head to Cade. "You think you own a peacekeeper. You own a peacekeeper the way a man owns a match after he's dropped it in the grass." He turned to Waybourne. "And you think you're buying a partner. You're buying a plank off a raft that's already left the shore." He came off the wall, at last, one step, and laid his long hand flat on Cade's paper, on the county clerk's deal table, and did not press it, just laid it there, covering the line where her mark was meant to go.

"There are four wagons a day on that grade now," Rourke said, "and next month it's eight, and by the spring — if there's water, if there's not, it doesn't signify — by the spring it's a road, a regular road, with a name, and the name is hers. People don't come three counties to see a woman find water. Water's a thing you dig for. They come to see the one the lightning wouldn't kill. They come to stand where a marked woman stands and hope some of the mark rubs off before they go home to bury their stock." He looked at Odessa now, only at her, and his voice went to the whisper that carried, the trained whisper that reached the back of a tent. "You could die tonight, Miz Pike, and it wouldn't slow that road down by a single wagon. It might speed it. That's the thing I'm here to tell you, and neither of these men will, because neither of them can afford to know it." He took his hand off the paper. "The faith doesn't need you real. It just needs somewhere to point. I'd build it a house. That's all a church is. A roof over a place people already can't stop coming to."

Nobody said anything. Cade's paper lay on the table with the print of Rourke's hand still fading out of it in the heat.

Odessa reached out and turned the pitcher on the table a quarter-turn with one finger, the way Cade had turned his paper, and did not pour any, and let the silence run the length she had learned to run it, and when she spoke the stammer was not there, because when it mattered most the stammer was never there, and that was the cruelest joke God had left her and the one she used.

"Then you've all got nothing to buy," she said. "You've told me so yourselves. He can't own a fire. He can't cut me a share of dry ground. And you — " she looked at Rourke — "you say the thing walks whether I walk or not." She stood. "So none of you needs my mark. Keep your paper, Mr. Cade. I came to be paid for two walks, in silver, and I'll be paid, and I'll go home."

---

She got as far as the door.

"Miz Pike." Cade had not risen. He never rose. He had folded the paper again, once, along the crease, and put it away inside his coat, unsigned, and he did not seem troubled by it, which troubled her more than argument would have. "Go on and go home. But hear the one thing before you do, because it's the true thing and the others were noise." He set both ruined hands flat on the deal table. "I don't need your mark on a paper. Rourke's right about the road and he's right about you and he's a fool about the rest, because he thinks the road makes you safe. It doesn't. It makes you dear." He said the word plainly, as a price. "A quiet thing, a man can let alone. A dear thing, in a dry year, a man has got to own or destroy, because a dear thing another man might get to first. You made yourself dear, Miz Pike. You did it walking. I couldn't stop you now if I wanted to, and I don't want to, because dear is worth money." He nodded, once, toward the window, toward the tents, the grade, the painted wagon, the whole grown thing. "You didn't take my paper. Fine. That was a courtesy. The paper was so you'd get to choose which of us owns you. Now you don't choose."

She stood in the doorway with the heat behind her and the hotter yard in front of her and she did the arithmetic, the only arithmetic she trusted, the sum she had run at a ford in May — *what keeps me and mine off other men's mercy, one more year* — and for the first time since she had filed the copper bright she could not make it come out. There was no line at the bottom of it for her mark. There never had been. She had taken twenty dollars to keep men from shooting and she had, without once meaning to, made herself the thing worth shooting over, and there was no walking home from that, because home was a dugout on worthless ground and the worthless ground was worthless no longer, being ground the Salt Preacher slept on, and men would come there too, now, with wagons and want.

"I'll take my silver," she said, "at Halve's. In an hour." Her voice was flat and slow and left no gap. "Send it down."

She went out into the light.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Depot siding, Rueda margin. River a thread — call it three feet wide, a finger deep,
> no current a body can see. Salt cedar in flood along both banks, feathery, green,
> thickened since spring — takes shallow water, takes salt water, thrives on the second.
> White crust drawing up on the bar where the cedar shades it: gyp bloom, thin now,
> spreading. Break the crust — bitter, sulfur on the far edge of it, faint. Doves
> not coming to this water at dusk; two flights seen, both bearing off west, toward
> the flats, toward worse. Bees the same. The stock that drinks here standing off it
> longer each morning. Water lay sweet here once. Not now. Poison by the change of
> the month. Nothing on this reach worth a shovel. The pilgrims camp on it anyway.

---

In the yard Tobe held the mule where she had left him, in the courthouse shade, and the four pilgrims from the grade had come up into the square while she was inside, and they had found the boy.

Not the boy. The mule she rode. They had found the mule the Salt Preacher rode, and the family on foot — the mother with the child that did not move right, the father hollow as a gourd — had come and stood by the mule, not touching it, and the mother had put her hand out toward the beast's flank and drawn it back, and put it out again, and Odessa watched from the courthouse steps as the woman finally laid one flat brown hand on the neck of a borrowed sway-backed mule and shut her eyes and stood there in the dust getting whatever it was a person got from that, some warmth off a thing that a marked woman had sat, and the child in her arm did not move and the father watched his wife's face and hoped.

Tobe stood at the mule's head holding the reins and he did not tell them to stop. He watched the woman's hand on the mule's neck, and then he looked across the square at Odessa on the courthouse steps, and for once his silent face was not blank; it had something in it she recognized because she had worn it, three times, on her back in the road with her hair smoking and the county standing over her in a ring — the look of a thing being touched by people who need it to be a sign.

He was not afraid of the woman's hand on the mule. That was the part that stopped Odessa's breath in the heat. The boy had watched the whole of it, all summer — the wire, the waiting, the copper filed to catch the sun, the hole dug on her word that came up mud, the road filling with wagons east and wagons west — the boy had seen every seam of the trick, closer than any man living. And he stood at the head of the mule and let the pilgrim woman lay her hand on it, and he was not afraid, and he was not ashamed, and his face said the thing plainest of all, plainer than Cade or Waybourne or Rourke had said it across the hot table with all their words.

He believed it too. He had seen everything, and he believed it too, and his belief was a small hard hand closing on the one thing she had left that she thought was hers.

She came down the steps into the square. The pilgrim woman took her hand off the mule and looked at Odessa coming and her face began to open like ground before rain, and Odessa did not stop and did not speak and did not look at her, because there was nothing she could say that the woman would not carry off and plant, and she took the reins out of the boy's hand and her ruined fingers would not close on them and the boy, without being told, without a word, wrapped the leather twice around her wrist and cinched it, the way she cinched the wire, so the hand that could not grip could hold — and she saw that he had learned that too, that he had learned all of it, and that there would be no unteaching him, or the town, or the road, or the county, or the year.

She got up on the mule the pilgrims had blessed and rode it down toward Halve's to collect her silver, and behind her the woman with the still child began, softly, to sing.


---

# CHAPTER 13 · Movement III — The Midpoint Turn

### "The Fenced Spring"

*The Ojo del Muerto tract, on the New Mexico Territory side of the line.*
*Third dry year. Rainless week twenty-nine. The Rueda at the step-across mark clear to the county line now, a bed of cracked plate between standing pools gone the color of weak coffee. Hauled water: fourteen cents the barrel. The water-table down so hard the depot windlass came up on a hundred and ten feet of rope where in May it had made eighty. On the Territory tracts the prior-appropriation law waited like a loaded gun a man had left on a table where children played.*

---

She had told Cade she would not walk for him and she was walking for him, and the arithmetic of how that had happened was the arithmetic she no longer trusted, so she did not run it. She rode.

The Ojo del Muerto lay a half-day northwest of Salt Fork, over the line, on ground nobody had wanted until nobody could afford not to. It was a long blister of country between two low mesas, tarbush and creosote and the gray bones of dead grama, and it belonged, on paper, to a man named Ferris Quinn, who had filed on it under the Desert Land Act three years gone and had never once put a plow to it or a house on it, which meant it belonged to Ferris Quinn the way a hat belongs to the peg it hangs on. Quinn rode beside her now on a horse that had cost more than his claim, and he talked, and she let him.

Behind them came the others. Cade had not come — Cade never came; that was his art — but Cade's man Rakes came, and two Bar EM riders with rifles in scabbards, and Vester Kyle on his livery mare with his notebook, and a scatter of others who had attached themselves to the walk the way iron filings attach to a magnet, drawn by the name. Twenty-odd, all told. She had learned to count them without turning her head. Twenty-odd, and behind them, strung out on the flat where the mesa threw its shade, a further clot she did not like the look of, afoot and mounted both, keeping their distance and their pace, matching hers.

The free-grass men. She knew them by the way they rode — the way a poor man's horse comes to water, greedy, because the man has nowhere else to be greedy.

"They've been on us since the river," Rakes said, low, drawing his horse up level with the mule. He was a spare, sun-cured man, all gristle, and Cade paid him to be exactly as unpleasant as he was. "The Dobie crowd. Quinn's got a claim they say ought to be free grass, and they've come to see what you dig up on it so they can want it too." He spat. "Say the word and I'll ride back and turn them."

"Turn them how."

Rakes didn't answer that, which was the answer, and she thought of the Sablon well and the good stitched boot on the leathered dead man in it, and she did not tell Rakes to turn anyone.

"Leave them be," she said. "A man watching is a man not shooting." She had said it so many times now it had worn smooth as a river stone in her mouth, and she no longer believed it, and she said it anyway, because it was still true of a walk that had not yet found anything, and this one had not yet found anything.

Tobe rode half a length back, silent, the sacked-out horse picking its feet. He was watching the free-grass men. He had learned to watch the ones nobody was watching.

---

She got down where the country told her to get down, which was where the two mesas leaned closest and the flat between them pinched to a swale, and she walked it once with the rods up and the men behind her holding their breath, and she read the ground the way she read the ground, and the ground told her what the ground told her.

> **ASSAY.**
> Swale between the mesas, pinch of the flat. Creosote thins here, then a band of
> fourwing saltbush, gray-green, leafed out fuller than the flat — deeper root,
> water down but reachable. Then, low against the west mesa's foot: three seepwillow,
> alive, and behind them a stand of sacaton grown waist-high where the whole flat
> is ankle-straw. Break-of-slope. The mesa's caprock sheds its rain in-country and
> the water runs the rock face down and pools against the slope where the caliche
> catches it. Ground at the sacaton: dark two spades down, cool, holds the boot-heel.
> No gyp on the break. Sweet, faint, mineral-clean. Ants gone off the low ground —
> up out of the damp, mounds ringed on the high side, moving toward it not away.
> A phoebe working the seepwillow at midmorning for what only lives near water.
> Old water-cut in the caliche, a hand deep, choked with wash. Water lay here.
> Lies here still. Shallow. Sweet. Not much — but live.

She stood at the sacaton with the sun on her back and the rods loose in her ruined grip and the wire biting her wrist, and she knew there was water under her boots the way she knew the free-grass men were still behind her on the flat, without turning to look, and she did not tell them yet.

She made them wait.

She made them wait until Quinn's expensive horse had shifted its weight three times and Kyle's pencil had gone still and even Rakes had stopped chewing, and she felt the whole strung-out crowd of them lean in against her silence, the near ones and the far ones both, the twenty who wanted her to find it and the ten who wanted her to find it so they could take it, all of them pulled toward her word like stock toward a windmill they could hear turning but not yet see, and when the wanting was as heavy as she could make it she let the rods dip and she said it the way you say weather.

"There's water here," she said. Flat. Slow. No gap for the block to sit in. "Shallow. Sweet. Dig at the tall grass."

The sound the crowd made was not a cheer. It was lower than that, and worse — a kind of groan, a letting-out, the sound men make when a thing they have been afraid to hope is finally allowed. Quinn was off his horse and on the ground with his hat off and his hand in the dark soil before she had finished lowering the rods.

And she thought, standing there, that finding it was the smallest part of the day, and she was right, and she did not yet know how right.

---

They dug fast, because Quinn had brought men to dig, and because the ground at the sacaton was soft as promised, and because Quinn had a reason to dig faster than any man there and he was the only one who knew it yet.

By the time the sun was straight overhead they were down six feet and the bottom of the hole was black mud, and a Bar EM rider hauled up a bucket of it and there was water standing in the mud, gray and cool, real water, live water, the first live water most of the men there had seen come out of new ground in half a year. A man put his whole face in the bucket. Another one crossed himself. A third — the youngest of Quinn's diggers, a boy not much older than Tobe — laughed, once, high and cracked, and then looked ashamed of it and went quiet. Kyle wrote and wrote.

She stood off from the hole and let them have it. She had learned that too: you did not join the crowd at the water. You stood apart, so that when they turned to thank the sky they had to turn past you, and their thanking swept over you like a wave that has already broken. A man she did not know took his hat off to her and held it against his chest and could not find a word, and she looked at him until he put the hat back on.

And Quinn, before the hole had made a foot of standing water, before a soul had drunk more than a swallow, sent his two hired men not deeper but sideways — out onto the flat with a wagon and a roll of new galvanized wire and a bundle of split cedar posts he had brought all this way in the wagon-bed under a tarp, brought before he had any water to fence, brought on the strength of nothing but her name and his own reading of the law.

She watched him do it and she understood it whole, the way she had understood Cade at the ford in May, faster than she let her face show.

Quinn was not fencing cattle. There were no cattle. Quinn was fencing the *water*, and he was fencing it fast, in the same hour it was found, because on this side of the line the law was prior appropriation — first in time, first in right — and the man who first diverted the water to a use owned the water, owned it against every thirsty man and beast in the Territory, owned it the way Cade owned the head-springs, and a fence around a spring was the visible sign of a diversion, and Quinn meant his sign to go up before the water had finished rising, before the free-grass men on the flat could send a rider to the land office to contest a claim that had, an hour ago, been worthless desert and was now the most valuable eighty acres in the county.

She had found him a spring. And in finding it she had, with one flat sentence delivered like weather, turned a worthless Desert Land Act fraud into a water right worth killing for, and she had done it in front of the very men it would cut off, and they were watching the posts go into the ground.

---

"Quinn." She said it once and he did not stop, so she walked out to where the first post went in and stood over him. "You'd best look at your flat before you nail your wire to it."

He looked. She watched him see them — the free-grass men, no longer strung out and idle, drawn in tight now against the mesa's shade, and more of them than there had been, because the ones who had ridden had ridden back to fetch the ones who had not, and there were dry-land men among them she knew, belt men, ruined men, a wagon or two, and out at the front of them, hatless, sitting a bony horse, the young hard-eyed one. Neel. The one who would not take off his hat to her. He was not looking at the posts. He was looking at her.

"That's my line," Quinn said, and his voice had gone up. "That's my filing. That's the law and they know it's the law."

"They know it's the law," she agreed. "That's what they're looking at."

Rakes had come up on her other side with his rifle out of the scabbard and laid across his saddle, not pointed, just out, the way you lay a thing on a table where children play, and the two Bar EM riders had drifted their horses to the flanks, and the whole shape of the afternoon had changed from a well-digging to something else, and it had changed in the span of one fence-line, and she had built the fence-line by finding the water, and she had found the water for twenty dollars she had not even wanted to earn from a man she had told she would not earn it from.

Neel walked his horse forward, slow, one hand loose, the other flat on his thigh where a man keeps it when he wants you to see it is empty and wants you to know it will not stay that way. Behind him the free-grass men did not move, and their not-moving was its own kind of moving, the way a held breath is louder than a shout. She counted them without turning her head. Sixteen, seventeen. There had been ten at dawn.

He stopped his horse a length off, close enough that she could see the salt-rime dried white in the seams of his hatband, the burst vessels in the whites of his eyes that a man gets from a summer of wind and no water to wash it out of him. He looked at the posts going in. He looked at the black-mud hole with the men crowded to it. He looked at her.

"Miz Pike," he said. He had said her name to her once before, hat in his hands over the Sablon hole, asking her to come look at a thing that was not water. He said it different now — no hat, and he said it like laying down a card. "You find that water?"

"I did."

"On free grass." He said it without heat, which was worse than heat. "Ground that's been open to any man's cattle in this Territory ten years. Quinn there never run a hoof on it nor turned a furrow nor dug a post-hole till today, and now he's got live water on it inside the hour and a fence going up around it while the mud's still wet, and by tomorrow noon he's got a paper at Delp's office in Quinn's own hand says it's his, first in time, and my neighbors that watered free on this flat since before Quinn ever heard of it get to buy their own water back off him a barrel at a time or drive their stock forty mile to the depot tank and pay Cade for the privilege." Neel's voice stayed level all the way to the bottom of it, which took work, and she could see the work. "So I'll ask it plain, because you deal in plain. That water you found for the county, like the paper's going to say Sunday? Or is it water you found for him?"

She said nothing.

"Because I'll tell you what it looks like from where I sit a starving horse," Neel said. "It looks like Cade sent his lightning-woman out ahead of his wire to point at the ground he wanted, so his man could fence it lawful before anybody could say it ought to be free, and dress the whole robbery up in a miracle so nobody'd think to call it robbery. It looks like you're not a diviner at all. It looks like you're a surveyor Cade don't have to pay a surveyor's wages, and the copper's just to make us take our hats off while he steals the water out from under us." He let that sit. "Tell me I've got it wrong."

She had a hundred things she could have said, and every one of them was true, and not one of them was hearable. She had not known Quinn meant to fence it. She had not been sent to point at anything; she had read the ground where the ground told her to read it, and the ground would have said water there to any man who knew how to shut up and look, Cade's or Neel's, it did not care whose. She had told a rider not two hours past to leave the free-grass men be. Every word of it was true and she opened her mouth on none of it, because she had learned at Ojo Frio that there was no honest word left on the board — her yes was a starting gun and her no was proof she wouldn't lie and her silence, God help her, her silence was heard now as a *yes* — and whatever she said Neel would carry off and turn against her or for her, and either one killed somebody, and so she gave him the only thing she had, which was the silence, and she stood in it and let it run.

She saw the exact moment he took it for the answer. Something behind his ruined eyes closed like a gate.

"That's what I thought," he said, and turned his horse, and rode back to the mesa shade, and the free-grass men closed around him the way water closes over a dropped stone.

---

She rode back toward the river with the walk breaking up behind her and Quinn's hammers still going and the free-grass men not gone, only fallen back to the mesa shade to wait, which is what men do who have decided and are only choosing the hour. Rakes rode close to her a while.

"Cade'll be pleased," Rakes said. "Quinn's Association. Quinn owes Cade for the wire in that wagon and the posts and the horse he's riding. You found water for the Association today whether you meant to or not, and you cut the Dobie crowd off it, and that's Cade's fight won without Cade lifting a hand." He looked almost cheerful about it. "You're better at his work than he is."

She did not answer him. She was doing the arithmetic. Twenty-odd men had ridden out to watch her find water and a release valve had opened, and then in the space of one hour the valve had become a fuse, because the water she found had not gone into thirsty mouths — it had gone into the law, into a fence, into paper, into a man's exclusive right against every other man, and a fenced spring in a dry year was not peace bought, it was a war lit, and she had lit it with the same flat sentence that in May had put men's hats in their hands.

That was the thing she had not understood at the ford and understood now, riding away from the Ojo del Muerto with the hammers small behind her. She did not own the fence. She had never owned the fence. She only pointed, and other men decided what her pointing was for, and they had stopped, this summer, deciding it the way that fed anybody but themselves.

Kyle brought his mare up on her right. His pencil was away. "That's a story," he said, and there was no relish in it, which frightened her more than relish would have. "That's a bad story. Quinn's going to file tonight. The Dobie men are going to cut that fence tonight or tomorrow night, and fence-cutting's a felony in this Territory now, they hang for it in some counties, and when Quinn's men catch them at it — " He stopped. "You gave that fence a spring to stand on, Miz Pike. Before today it was posts around dirt. Now it's posts around water. There's men who'll die for the difference."

"I found water," she said. "That's what I was paid to do."

"I know it." Kyle looked at her sidelong, ink on his cuffs, a half-step behind the story and, for once, wishing he were further behind it. "Only it isn't water anymore, once you find it. That's the thing. The minute you find it, it stops being water and starts being a filing, and a filing's got Quinn's name on it, not the county's." He shook his head. "I'll write it up as a miracle. That's what they'll pay to read. But it's not a miracle. It's a survey stake with your name on it."

---

They came onto the river road at dusk where the Rueda lay in its bed of cracked plate, the standing pools strung out like coins somebody had dropped and not stooped to gather, and she stopped the mule to let it drink at a pool that was already too far gone for a horse to want and made itself want it anyway. The pool had shrunk a stride since she'd crossed it that morning. She noted it the way she noted everything — the water going down while men fought over where more of it lay, the fighting speeding as the going-down sped, the two things winding each other tighter.

Behind her, back the way they'd come, low on the darkening flat, she saw the small orange stars of fires — Quinn's men, camped at the fence to guard it through the first night, because a fenced spring on this side of the line unguarded the first night was a fenced spring with a cut in it by dawn. And further off, hard to be sure in the failing light, a second cluster of fires, apart from the first, keeping their distance and their pace: the free-grass men, camped where they could watch the water they could not drink.

Two fires facing each other across a spring she had found that morning, and neither of them was thirsty enough yet to cross the dark to the other, and both of them soon would be.

Tobe drew up beside her at the pool. He was looking back at the two fires the way she was. And then he did a thing he had not done — he lifted his hand, the boy who did not speak, and he pointed, first at the one cluster of fires and then at the other, and then he turned his silent face to her and held up two fingers, and closed them slowly to a fist, the way you close a hand on a thing to break it, and she understood him whole, because he had learned to count the way she counted, and he had counted the fires, and he had done the arithmetic she had done, and come out where she came out.

Two fires. One spring. Her word between them, and no way to take it back.

She turned the mule up out of the dead riverbed toward home, and behind her the pool she had let it drink from gave up another finger of its coffee-colored water to the dry air of the twenty-ninth week, and out on the flat the two sets of fires burned steady and apart, waiting on the dark to close the distance no one had yet been thirsty enough to cross.


---

# CHAPTER 14 · Movement III — The Midpoint Turn

### "The Land Office Business"

*Salt Fork, the courthouse square and the U.S. Land Office — the thirty-first rainless week.*
*The Rueda cross-able dry-shod at the ford, standing pools going the color of weak coffee below it. Hauled water fifteen cents the barrel. A duster in the night had walked a wall of grit down the Blanco grade and laid a fence line under two feet of drift, so that a man's wire ran into the ground and came out nowhere, and the last of the Dobie stock stood in the drying mud of a tank west of town with their tongues out, and did not stand there long.*

---

The summons came on paper, which was how she knew to be afraid of it.

A boy she did not know rode out to the Ronsard place — she had been at the Ronsard place, though she did not go there to be there; she went to see whether the son had quit hauling spoil out of the gyp hole yet, and he had not, and she had left without a word, which was all she had to give him — and the boy handed her a folded sheet with a red-wax daub on the fold and Delp's dry copperplate hand across the front: *Mrs. Pike.* Not *Miz.* The land office did not deal in the county's soft mouth. It dealt in *Mrs.*, which made her a widow she had never been, and in ink, which outlasted men.

She read it twice. It requested her attendance at the office of the Register, U.S. Land Office, Salt Fork, at her earliest convenience, upon a matter of the proving-up of a certain entry, her testimony being desired as to the improvements thereon. It was courteous. It was the courtesy of a man laying a snare and dusting the leaves over it.
