Tobe watched her read it from the back of his sacked-out horse, half a length off, the way he always sat, near enough to follow and far enough to be flinched from without insult. He could not read. He knew paper anyway. He knew it the way a dog knows a raised hand.

"Salt Fork," she said, to no one, and folded it small, and put it inside her coat against the wire on her wrist, where it would sit all the ride into town and remind her.

---

The office was the brick building's poor cousin — a plank front with a false top, wedged between the bank and the surveyor's shingle, a stovepipe, a window gone amber with old varnish and older dust. Inside it smelled of ink and of the particular mildew of paper kept dry too long in a country with no damp to give it, a smell like a Bible left in a drawer through three funerals. There was a rail. Behind the rail there were pigeonholes to the ceiling, every one of them full, the plats rolled and tied, the tract-books spined in leather gone the color of a saddle. A man's whole life in this country could be reduced to a line in one of those books and a fee, and Horace Delp kept the books.

He rose when she came in, which was worse than if he had not.

"Mrs. Pike." He had a way of setting his spectacles more firmly on his nose before he looked at anyone, as if bringing them into focus were a favor he was extending. Ink-stained cuffs, kept back with black elastics. A dry cough into a folded cloth, twice, a punctuation he had used so long it had stopped meaning anything but *I am not finished.* "I am obliged you could come. Won't you sit."

There was one chair on her side of the rail. She did not sit. Standing, she was taller than most men in the county, and she used it the way she used silence.

"You have a matter," she said.

"A small one." He drew a tract-book to him and opened it to a place marked with a black ribbon and turned it so it faced her across the rail, though he did not push it near enough for her to read it, which was also on purpose. "A Desert Land Act entry, out in the northwest. NM Territory ground — or arguably. The seam runs right through the quarter, which is neither here nor there for our present business." He touched the page. "The entryman has three years to prove that he has conducted water upon the land. Reclaimed it. Made it fruitful, in the words of the statute, though the statute has never once seen this country or it would not use the word." The cough. "His three years are near out. He requires witnesses who can swear to the water on the land."

"And you thought of me."

"The whole county thinks of you, Mrs. Pike, where water is the question." He said it without weight, which was how she knew he had rehearsed it. "You are become a kind of authority. A man who has your word that there is water on his ground has, in the eyes of a good many people, a thing worth more than my seal. It occurred to the receiver and to myself that in a proving-up where the water is — let us say — *not obvious to the eye* — your testimony might carry where a hired man's would not."

She let that sit in the room. She was good at that. In the sitting she did the arithmetic she always did, and the arithmetic came out ugly.

"You want me to swear there's water on ground I've never walked," she said.

"I want you to *look.*" He smiled, and it was Cade's smile, the same thin thing that cost nothing, because they were the same animal wearing different coats — one owned the grass and one owned the paper, and between them they owned the county down to the caliche. "Ride out, walk it, do — whatever it is you do. And then swear to what you find. If you find water, you swear to water. That is all the office asks."

"And if I don't find water."

Delp adjusted his spectacles. "Then I imagine the entryman is in difficulty," he said, "and looks to his witnesses to be — accommodating."

---

He gave her the plat and the section numbers and the entryman's name — a name she did not know, *T. R. Beal* — and the fee schedule for filing an affidavit of witness, as though she might want to know the price of her own testimony, and he coughed her out the door with the courtesy of a man who has set the hook and gone back to his mending.

She stood on the plank walk with the plat in her hand and read the country off it.

> **ASSAY.**
> Plat, not ground — read it as a map of water anyhow.
> Section corner ties to the White Draw benchmark, two mi. SE. Ground falls that way.
> Entry quarter: NW, high, on the caliche bench. Contour lines crowd close = steep = shed, not catch.
> One "reservoir" penned in on the plat, dotted — the surveyor's dotted line = *reported, not seen.*
> Improvement column: "irrigation ditch, 40 rods; reservoir; 3 ac. in sorghum." Ink fresher than the rest of the page.
> A ditch runs downhill. The dotted reservoir sits *above* the ditch it feeds. Water does not climb.
> The whole quarter is on the wrong side of the fall-line for a catch. High ground gathers nothing.
> No section note of a spring, a seep, a well depth. Blank where water would be written if water were.

The paper lied the way a badly told story lies — not in what it said but in what it could not have said and still be true. The ditch fed a reservoir uphill of it. The three acres of sorghum sat on caliche that would grow sorghum the year the Rueda ran gold. There was no water on that quarter. There had never been. Somewhere a witness had already sworn there was, or would, and Delp wanted her name on the same page to make the lie a legend instead of a fraud, because her name had gotten to be worth that, and she had let it.

She understood, standing there, the shape of what she was being asked. Beal was a name. There would be no Beal, or a Beal who lived in Kansas and had never seen the quarter, a dummy, a straw man, and behind him a man with maps and money who was patenting the water rights to a whole reach of the Territory a false quarter at a time, buying up the low ground and the fall-lines before the water table dropped past reach, so that when the county came to its knees the only live water for forty miles would already be somebody's on paper, and the paper would be Delp's, and the man behind Delp would sell thirst back to the dying at whatever the market bore. It was Cade's game with the head-springs, only quieter, and legal, and forever.

And they wanted her word to seal it. Because her word could make a dry quarter wet in the minds of men, and a thing that is wet in enough minds is, before the law and before God, a well.

She had thought, up to that morning, that the danger of the legend was that it could get her killed. She saw now it could do worse. It could make her useful.

---

She did not go home. She rode out to the quarter, because she wanted to be sure of the ground before she was sure of the men, and because a walk was the one thing in the world she trusted her own hands to do.

Tobe came behind. She did not send him back. There was a duster's leaving in the air — the sky the flat yellow of scorched cloth, the sun a smear you could look at, the horizon gone to a stain — and the light lay on everything like a judgment nobody could read. West of the road the last of the Dobie steers had lain down in the mud of the drying tank and not gotten up, and the mud had drawn its salt up around them white while they still breathed, so that they died half-preserved, the way the flat kept its dead. She counted them against her will. Nine down, two standing, and the two would be down by night. She stopped counting. The county had stopped counting; she had heard the men say *the thousands* the way you say a thing you have quit measuring, and counting was the last honest work she did and she was losing even that.

The quarter was what the plat had told her before she reached it.

> **ASSAY.**
> Caliche bench, hard white cap breaking through the crust in shelves. Tarbush, creosote — desert scrub, no water-hunters. No mesquite green enough to mean depth.
> No cottonwood. No willow. No salt cedar — even the salt trap wants water, and there is none to be bad.
> The "ditch": a scraped line 40 rods long, banks not slumped, no water-cut, no algae line, never run. Dug to be photographed, not to carry.
> The "reservoir": a pan scraped in the caliche at the high corner, cracked plate all across the bottom, a duster's grit settled in it, dry so long the crack-edges have curled. Uphill of the ditch by six feet. Water would run *out* of it, downhill, into nothing.
> Three acres "in sorghum": a few rows of dead stalks set in furrows never wet, planted for the swearing. Chaff blown clean off. Roots when pulled: dry as excelsior, no white feeder-hairs, dead in the seed-bed.
> Ants: none working. Gone. Ground reads no; has read no a long time; will read no when the sorghum-liars are bones.

She knelt at the reservoir pan and broke a curl of cracked plate off with her heel and smelled it. Nothing. Dust and old dust. Not even the gyp-bitterness of the flats, which would at least have meant water had once stood and gone. This pan had never held water. It had held a photographer's afternoon and a witness's afternoon and would hold her afternoon if she let it.

She stood a long time in the yellow light with the plat in her hand. Tobe sat his horse at the fence corner where a strand of the new galvanized wire ran taut and bright as a lie against the dead ground, and watched her the way he watched everything, and did not flinch when a gust took the top off the dead sorghum and carried it away.

She could swear it. That was the plain fact she made herself hold, the way she held every plain ugly fact. She could ride back to Delp and say she had walked it and found sign — say she had felt the rod pull at the high corner, say the ground *told her* there was water perched under the caliche the way water sometimes perched, and no man could gainsay her, because gainsaying her was gainsaying the Salt Preacher, and the whole county had agreed she was not to be gainsaid. And Delp would file it, and the paper would seal it, and the man behind Beal would own another reach of the dying, and she would be paid the affidavit fee and a good deal more under the table, and Cade would learn she had done it and know a new thing about how far she could be bought, and the land office would count her a friend, and a friend of the ledger did not get filed *gone.*

It was, she saw with the flat clear cruelty she trusted more than any warmth, the safe thing. The prophet's business had a body count and the land office's business did not; nobody dug a grave over a false patent; the fraud that Delp was running was Melbern's kind of fraud, the kind that costs money and not families, unfalsifiable, clean. Beal was not real. Nobody would die because she swore water onto a quarter where a man who did not exist grew sorghum that had never grown. It was the shallowest lie she had ever been offered and it paid the best and it hurt no one she could point to.

She wanted to take it. She stood there and wanted it, and did not pretend to herself she didn't.

---

Here is why she did not, and she would not have called it a reason, because it was not one.

If her word could patent a dry quarter, then her word was no longer hers to spend. That was the arithmetic. Not the fraud — the fraud was Delp's and welcome to it. But the day she swore water onto Beal's caliche, her name stopped being a thing she performed on ground she had read and became a thing men could file. It became paper. And a thing on paper Delp could copy, and enter, and re-enter, and swear to in her absence, and attach her name to a hundred quarters she had never seen until the Salt Preacher's word was a fee in a schedule, a line in a tract-book, an improvement in a column, and she was as owned by the ledger as the Dobie steers were owned by the mud. Cade wanted to own her walking. Delp wanted to own her writing, which was worse, because writing outlived the walk, and outlived her, and she had spent forty-one years making sure the only thing that outlived her was the fear.

She had built the legend to keep herself off men's mercy. She would not now hand it to the one power in the county that never forgot and never forgave and never once needed her to be in the room.

So she did not swear it. But she was too far into the country and too well read in men to think that not-swearing was the end of it, because a snare that fails to catch does not go away; it tells the man who set it what you are.

---

She rode back into Salt Fork in the failing yellow of the duster's tail and did not go home. She went to the *Clarion.*

Vester Kyle was setting type by lamplight, his cuffs black to the elbow, thin as a lath and coughing his own dry cough that was a worse thing than Delp's and meant a worse ending. He looked up and saw who it was and something moved across his face that she had learned to read on every face in the county now — the leaning-in, the readiness, the story a man saw walk through his door.

"Miz Pike," he said. "You've never once come to me."

"Something's changed the price of it." She laid the plat on his stone, over the set type, and put her finger on the dotted reservoir uphill of the ditch it fed. "Read that like a man who prints the truth when it sells. There's a Desert Land Act entry proving up out on the northwest seam. A quarter named to a man called Beal. There's a ditch that runs uphill to a pond that's never held water, and three acres of sorghum planted dead in a dry bed for a photograph, and the register wants sworn witnesses to the water on it, and there is no water on it, and there was never going to be." She let the silence run, the way she ran it over a dead field, and watched him lean into it. "Beal isn't a man. He's a name over a claim, and behind the name there's somebody buying up the fall-lines and the low water a false quarter at a time, so that when this county finishes dying the water it's dying for'll all be spoken for on paper before a soul can dig."

Kyle looked at the plat a long time. He was not a fool. He was a half-step behind a story his whole life, and here one had walked in and laid itself on his stone, and she watched him understand that the water fraud was the biggest thing that had ever come near his little weekly, and watched him understand, right behind it, what it would cost him to print it, because Delp filed the town's paper too, and Delp's man had money, and a consumptive editor in a dying county held his subscriber list and his lungs on borrowed time.

"They'll ruin me," he said, not as a refusal. As a price he was reading.

"They'll ruin everybody," she said. "You'll just get it in print."

"Why come to me?" He was watching her the way Delp had watched her, the way they all did now, hunting the thing behind the thing. "You don't do a kindness, Miz Pike. The whole county knows that's the one honest thing about you. Why hand me this?"

She could have told him the true reason, which was not a kindness and not a creed and not the truth-telling the county had already decided she stood for. She had come to Kyle because a thing that is printed cannot be filed quietly, and a fraud that everyone in the county is reading over breakfast cannot be sworn to in a back office by a woman who is not there, and by putting Beal's dry quarter on the front of the *Clarion* she was nailing her own name up out of Delp's reach forever — making it worth too much watched to be worth anything owned. She was not saving the county. She was making her word a thing that could only be spent in the light, because the dark was where the ledger lived.

She did not tell him that. She let him keep the version he wanted, which was cheaper for her and heavier for him.

"Print it or don't," she said, and left the plat on his stone, and walked out.

---

She rode home in the dark with Tobe half a length behind. The duster had blown itself out and left the sky scoured and enormous and full of stars, the cold clean sky of a country with nothing left in the air to hold the day's heat, and out east the nesters' fires burned low, fewer than there had been, more than there would be. She counted them from habit. She did not tell herself the number.

She had made an enemy that afternoon that would not shoot her, would not court her, would not preach her, would not need her in a room. She had made an enemy of the paper, and the paper was patient, and it never once forgot a name it wanted gone, and now it wanted hers.

She did not tell herself yet what that would cost. You never told yourself yet. You made yourself wait, so that when the knowing came it landed like weather.

At the fence corner where the road turned down to the draw, in the starlight, someone had driven a new stake into her ground — into the worthless caliche she squatted on, that no man had ever wanted, that she had lived on ten years because nobody would trouble to put her off it. A survey stake. Bright new pine, squared, a strip of red flannel tacked to the top so it would show, and a number burned into the side in the land office's own iron.

She sat her mule and looked at it a long time.

Then she got down, and put her ruined hand flat against it the way she put it flat against the mud of a dying seep, feeling for what the ground under it would tell her, and the ground told her nothing, because there was nothing under it, because the stake was not about water at all.


---

# CHAPTER 15 · Movement III — The Midpoint Turn

### "The Salt Fork Boom"

*Salt Fork, Cheyne County — mid-September, 1893.*
*Rainless thirty-three weeks. The Rueda a chain of standing pools between bars of cracked plate, walkable dry-shod the whole length of the county. Hauled water: sixteen cents the barrel, and the depot tank now the most valuable object between here and the Pecos, watched day and night by two men Cade paid to watch it. Steers won't bring six dollars. Nobody in town is counting the dead stock anymore; they are counting arrivals.*

---

She came into Salt Fork from the north at first light and did not know the town.

It was the same town. The bank was still the one brick building, squat and ugly, and the courthouse still stood behind its dead square of grass, and Halve's porch was where it had always been. But between them and around them and out past them into the wagon-yard, the town had grown a skin overnight, the way a dead thing grows a skin, and the skin was canvas. Tents. She counted them the way she counted everything, without meaning to, and stopped at forty because forty was where the counting stopped meaning a number and started meaning *too many* — the same place, she thought, the county had gotten to with the dead cattle, and she did not like the rhyme of it.

Tobe rode a half-length back on the sacked-out horse, the reins doubled twice around his fist the way she doubled the baling wire, and he was reading the tents the way she was reading them, his head turning slow. He did not flinch at any of it. He had learned that from her, and she wished he had learned something else.

A man on a soapbox at the north end of the wagon-yard was selling water rights to land nobody in his hearing owned. He had a map tacked to a board and a pointer made of a willow switch, and he was saying *quarter-sections, gentlemen, quarter-sections on the very ground the Salt Preacher walked in June,* and there were eleven men standing in the dust in front of him with their money already half out of their pockets, and Odessa rode past close enough to hear the willow switch tap the board and she thought: *June. I never walked north of the river in June.* But it did not matter what she had done. The map had her walk on it in a dotted line, a route she had never taken, going to water that was not there, and men were paying silver for the dotted line.

She rode on. She did not stop him. Stopping him would have been the same mistake she had made with Kyle at the start, when she had told him not to build a thing bigger than her, and he had taken her fear for a better story and printed it. There was no telling any of them anything now. The thing was built.

---

She had come for her money. That was all. There was money owed her — Cade's, for the fenced spring in the north tract, the one the free-grass men had cut the wire off of two weeks gone and bled for — and she meant to take it out of Halve's box and turn around and ride back to her dugout and her goats and the deed she did not have, and be gone before the town was fully awake. She had told herself that on the ride in. She told herself a great many things on the ride in.

But there was no riding through Salt Fork quiet anymore. She was ten rods from Halve's porch when the first one saw her.

It was a woman, a pilgrim, one of the ones who had come in on the painted wagons off the Blanco grade. She was standing at the water-seller's dray with her barrel and her sixteen cents, and she turned with the sixteen cents still in her hand and she saw Odessa on the mule, and Odessa watched the recognition go through her the way a change goes through weather, a stillness first and then a leaning.

"It's her," the woman said. Not loud. She said it the way you'd say *it's raining,* to no one, to test whether the thing was true.

Then it moved.

It moved through the wagon-yard faster than a horse could have carried it, which was the part Odessa never got used to — that the belief traveled without legs, that it was already ahead of her at Halve's porch before she reached it, that men she had never seen were taking off their hats to the mule. A boy ran alongside for a while, not Tobe, a town boy, running with his hand out not quite touching her stirrup, and an older man cuffed him off and then himself reached to lay two fingers on the mule's flank, quick, the way you'd touch a stove to learn if it was hot. To get some of the mark. That was the phrase now; she had heard it in the belt. *Some of the mark.* As if it came off on the hands.

She kept her sleeve down over the wire and the fern-scar and let her face be nothing.

"Miz Pike." A man had his hat in both hands at her stirrup, walking backward to keep his face to her, and she did not know him. "Miz Pike, I got a quarter out past the Ojo Frio, my wife's people's ground, and I'd pay — I'd pay whatever you — if you'd only come and — "

"I don't walk on my own account," she said.

"They said — the paper said — "

"The paper says a great many things." She rode past him. He kept the hat in both hands and watched her go, and she felt the wanting come off him like heat off caliche at noon, the leaning-in she knew to the ounce, and she thought of Cade at the lip of the draw four months gone, saying *a man who is hoping is a man who is not yet shooting,* and she thought: *he did not know the half of what he was buying. Neither did I.*

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Town read like ground. Rot shows the same.
> Tents on the wagon-yard: canvas new, guys taut — money came fast, from off, not
> from here. Local ground don't buy new canvas in the third dry year.
> Water-dray charging sixteen; barrel half salt cedar taint at the bung — hauled
> from the low pools, not the depot tank. Depot tank guarded; two men, rifles,
> shade of the water-house. The one live thing in the county behind a gun.
> Bank: new paper in the window, land notices, four of them, dummy names — no
> improvement described, "irrigable," swearing where the water is not.
> Pilgrims thin at the ankle, swollen at the belly — the belt's sickness, bad
> water, the flux. They drink the pools. They came for a sign and drink the
> poison to reach it.
> Grass in the square dead a year. Fresh dug at the northwest corner — a lot
> staked. New graves outside the fence, six, small, none marked. Children's
> length.
> Ground falls, under all of it, toward the river, toward the salt, the way it
> always did. Town built on the fall.

---

Halve was on the porch with his ledger on his knee and his spectacles down his nose, and he did not get up when she tied the mule, and she was glad of it. He was the one man in the county who treated her arrival like weather he'd expected, neither dread nor hunger in him, only the tired ledger-look of a man watching numbers do a thing he'd warned them they'd do.

"You'll want your box," he said.

"I'll want it."

"It's grown." He turned a page without looking at it. "Money keeps coming for you, Odessa. Not from Cade. From — off. Men send it. A dollar in an envelope from Pecos, from Roswell, from a woman in El Paso wanting you to walk her dead husband's claim. There's near sixty dollars in there now you never earned and never asked for." He looked up over the spectacles. "I've kept it separate from Cade's. I don't want it said I mixed the Association's silver with the — other."

"The other."

"The offerings." He said the word flat, and it was the flattest, ugliest word she had heard in a month, uglier than *some of the mark,* because Halve did not deal in the myth and would not use a myth-word unless the myth had gotten so thick that even the ledger-hearted had no other name for the thing. Offerings. Money laid down not for a walk, not for a find, but for *her,* against no service, the way you'd lay it on a plate at the front of a church.

"Send it back," she said.

"There's no return on most of it. No name to send it to. A dollar in an envelope, Odessa. From strangers." He set the ledger aside, which for Halve was a large thing to do, and he took his spectacles off, which was larger. "You want my counsel, you'll take your box and Cade's silver both and you'll be gone from this town before the noon train, and you'll not come back to it, because this town has stopped being a town. Look at it." He did not gesture. He did not need to. "Six weeks ago I sold flour and wire and coal oil to men I'd known twenty years. Now I sell airtights and canvas and cheap boots to men I never saw, at a price I'm ashamed of, and I take it, because if I don't the man next to me will, and there's a man next to me now where there wasn't one." He put the spectacles back on. "You built me a boom, Odessa. I never asked you to. I'd trade it back for the twenty men who've left."

She said nothing. She was good at nothing.

"You didn't build it," she said finally. Which was the truth and was also the lie she most wanted to be true.

"No," Halve agreed, and did not believe her, and got up to fetch the box.

---

While he was inside, Kyle found her.

He came up the middle of the street with his coat off and his sleeves rolled and the ink to the elbow now, not just the cuffs, a man who had been setting type all night and had the color of it in his face, and he was grinning before he reached her, which he had not done in June. In June Kyle had been a half-step behind a story, hungry, a little ashamed of the hunger. He was not behind it now. He had caught up to it and the catching had fed him. He had put on flesh in three months the way the town had.

"I printed four hundred this week," he said, by way of good morning. "Four hundred, Odessa, and sold every one, and turned away a man from Roswell wanted to buy the whole run to carry back east. East. They read you in *Kansas* now." He said Kansas the way another man would say heaven. "There's a fellow off the Fort Worth paper come in on Tuesday's train wants to interview you. A real paper. Columns wide as your hand."

"I don't talk to papers."

"You don't have to talk." He was still grinning. "That's the beauty of it. You never say a thing and I never had a better week's copy. You know what sells? Not the finds. The *silence.* I ran a piece last week — 'The Woman Who Will Not Say' — three columns on you saying nothing at Cade's spring, and it — " He stopped, because she had turned and looked at him, full, the flat look, and even Kyle fattened on her legend could not hold that look long. The grin went down a little at the corners. "It's not a thing to be sorry for," he said. "You'd have starved on your seep. I made you worth money. The whole county's worth money now that wasn't."

"There's six new graves outside the church-fence," she said. "Children's length. I counted them on the ride in."

Kyle's grin went the rest of the way down.

"That's the flux," he said. "That's the water. That's not — you can't lay the water on me, or on you neither. They drink the pools because they're poor. They were poor before you." He wiped his forehead with the back of his ink-black wrist and left a gray smear there like a thumbprint of the road. "You want to know a thing? I'll tell you a thing, and I'll not print it, because it doesn't sell." He came a step closer and dropped his voice under the noise of the water-seller and the soapbox man and the hammering at the northwest corner. "The finds have stopped. You've not found water in a month. The last three walks were dry or salt or somebody else's fence. And it hasn't slowed a soul." He looked at the street, at the tents, at the money moving. "That's the story I can't write, because I don't understand it and my readers wouldn't believe it. You could stop finding water forever and they'd come faster. Why is that, Odessa? You're the only one I can ask. Why is that?"

She could have told him. *Because it was never the water,* she could have said, and stopped there, and let him carry the rest himself the long way she had carried it. It would have been the truest thing said in Salt Fork that morning, and he would have taken it, the way he took everything, and turned it into three columns and sold four hundred.

So she said the least that would do.

"Ask the church," she said. "They built it faster than you build a paper."

She went down the porch steps past him and into the street.

---

She walked the boomtown once, on foot, the mule tied and Tobe left with it, because she had learned to read ground with her feet and the town was ground now and she wanted to know what was under it before she let Cade or Rourke or the new man at the land office pretend to sell it to her.

She read it the way she read a draw. The tents nearest the depot were the newest and the richest, guys taut, canvas white, and the men in front of them were not thirsty — they wore town coats and watched the wagon-yard the way a coyote watches a chicken run, and they were selling paper, quarter-sections and water rights and shares in a townsite company that had a name painted on a board and no ground under it. Behind them, back from the depot toward the flats, the tents got poorer and older and browner, and the people in front of *those* were the pilgrims, the ones who had sold a wagon and a team to get here on a sign, and they had the belt's sickness in them, the thin ankle and the swollen middle, and they were drinking the sixteen-cent water and the free bad water from the pools both, and a woman in one tent-door was singing low to a child that did not answer, the same *deliver us* they had sung over Ronsard's hole in June, and Odessa stopped in the street a moment and listened to it and then went on, because there was nothing under the song but a dry playa and a dead well, and she had known that in June and knew it harder now.

At the very edge, where the town's skin gave out and the true ground began again, a man was digging.

He was digging a well. Alone, at the back of the last poor tent, in the caliche, and he had gone down maybe four feet and struck the cap and was hammering at it with a bar in the noon heat with his shirt off, and his ribs stood out, and he was digging where no fool would dig, on the high ground, on the fall, above the salt, on the exact worst ground in the whole townsite, and Odessa stood at the lip of his hole and looked down at him and knew before she opened her mouth that there was no telling him anything.

"There's no water there," she said anyway.

He looked up. His face went through the change, the stillness and then the leaning, and she watched it happen at the bottom of a four-foot hole, and it was worse down there in the hole, the wanting, closer, like a thing at the bottom of a dry well looking up.

"You're her," he said.

"There's no water there. You're on the fall. It's all going away under you toward the flats. You'll dig to China and hit gyp before you hit sweet water, and the gyp will smell like water and it won't be."

"You're *her.*" He had heard none of it. He was smiling now, up out of the hole, the bar loose in his hand. "You come to say where. They said you'd come to say where. I picked this ground because — " He stopped. He looked around him, at the high dry corner he'd chosen, and she saw him reach for the reason and find it. "Because it felt right," he said. "I stood here and it felt right and I thought, she'll come, and she'll say this is the one, and — you come." He dropped the bar and put both his hands on the lip of his hole, the way a man holds the edge of a pew. "Bless it," he said. "You don't got to find it. Just bless the ground and I'll dig it. I'll dig however deep. Just say."

She looked at him a long time. Longer than the theater. This was not the theater; the crowd could not see her; there was no fee in it; there was only a starved man in a hole he had dug on the strength of a June walk that never happened, waiting for her to say a word that would kill him slower or faster depending on the word.

"I don't bless ground," she said.

"Then just — stand on it. Stand on it a minute. Some of the mark."

And because there was no telling him anything, and because standing off it or on it made no difference to a hole already dug on faith, and because — she told herself — it cost her nothing, she stood on it. A minute. On the high dead corner above the salt, over a starved man's grave that he was calling a well, and he watched her boots on his ground with tears standing in his eyes, and when she stepped off he took up the bar again and hit the caliche like a man who had been given water instead of the word for it.

She walked back into the town not looking at the six small graves by the church-fence, and failing not to count them again, and getting seven.

---

She meant to be gone before noon. She was not gone before noon.

Because Cade found her, or Cade's men did — two riders at the porch rail before she had the box counted, not asking her anything, only *there,* the way Cade's men had a way of being there, and behind them, further off, at the courthouse, she saw the other watchers she had learned to read since June. Waybourne's man loafing at the corner of the bank with nothing in his hands and nowhere to be. Rourke's people, three of them, unloading green lumber from a wagon at the northwest corner of the square where the fresh lot was staked — building already, on the ground she would have bet a deed they had bought in her name.

And a fourth kind of watcher she had not seen before, and did not like. A man in a town coat, clean, with ink on his cuffs like Kyle but not Kyle, standing in the door of the land office across the square with a paper in his hand, watching her the way Delp watched things — patient, unforgiven, arithmetical. The land office had a new man. The land office had gotten bigger too. Everything that fed on her had gotten bigger.

She stood on Halve's porch with sixty dollars of strangers' money in a box and did the arithmetic she always did, and for the first time since Cade rode into her draw the arithmetic would not come out. There was no figure for it. She could take the money and ride and the town would keep her walk on its dotted-line map and keep selling quarter-sections on ground she'd never seen and keep laying offerings in a box against her absence, and the thing would go on eating whether she was in it or out of it — *the belief traveled without legs* — and she understood, standing there in the smell of new canvas and old salt, that she had passed some line she had not seen in the road. She was no longer a woman a county had hired. She was the ground the town was built on, and the town was going to keep falling toward the salt whether she stood on it or not.

Tobe had come up on the porch beside her without a sound. He was looking at the northwest corner, at Rourke's men setting the green sills of the church-house on the staked lot, and he was not flinching, and she wished to God he would flinch, once, at anything, so she'd know there was still something in him the town had not yet taken.

"Come away from the rail," she said, low.

He came, but he came still watching, and she saw him do a thing then that stopped the breath in her worse than any find had. A pilgrim woman had drifted up to the foot of the porch steps with a child on her hip, one of the thin-ankled, swollen-bellied ones, and she was not looking at Odessa at all. She was looking at Tobe. At the boy who rode a half-length back and cinched his reins the way the Salt Preacher cinched her wire, the boy who had been *there,* at the graves and the dry holes, the boy the belt said had *found the dead* by digging. The woman was looking at Tobe the way the whole town looked at Odessa. And Tobe, twelve years old and mute as a fence post, did not shrink from it.

He straightened his back to it.

He had learned that too. Not from being told. From watching her do it a hundred times — square the back, keep the sleeve down, let the wanting come and give it nothing and let the nothing be the thing they took away. He had learned every seam of the trick, and he had learned to *stand into* the wanting instead of away from it, and the pilgrim woman leaned toward him the way she'd have leaned toward Odessa, the same stillness and then the same tilt, and did not so much as glance at the lightning-struck woman on the porch six feet off. The crowd had furnished a June walk Odessa never made and a map she never drew and sixty dollars in a box from strangers who would never see her face. It could furnish this too. It was already furnishing it, in the noon light, off a boy's straightened spine.

She did not tell him to stop. There was no telling any of them anything now. She only put her ruined right hand — the claw, the dead one, the one that could hold nothing — flat between his shoulder blades and pressed him back a step from the rail, out of the woman's sightline, and it was the one gesture in the whole burnt county she could not have named or charged for or explained, and she took her hand away fast, before it could mean anything, and pushed her sleeve down over the wire and the fern-scar, and picked up the box.

Below the porch, in the dead grass of the square, the pilgrim woman set her child down to rest her arm. The child stood a moment on its own. Then it sat, in the dust, where the town had fallen toward the salt, and it did not get up, and its mother did not at first notice, being busy still looking at the boy — and the child sat there in the noon light with its swollen belly and its thin gray ankles, patient, the way the ground was patient, waiting to be told what it was.


---

# CHAPTER 16 · Movement III — The Midpoint Turn

### "The White Draw"

*The Rueda Salt Flats, west of Salt Fork — the White Draw. Rainless week thirty-five.*
*The worst dust yet: the west came up whole one afternoon and stood in the sky three days, so that noon was the color of weak tea and a man lit a lamp to eat by. The Rueda a chain of green pools between plates of cracked mud, no current, salt cedar in flood on the dead margin. Hauled water sixteen cents the barrel. The town's last sweet well drawing sand by the third bucket. And in the belt, where the pools were gone entire, men had begun to say a thing they had not said before: that they would drink whatever there was to drink.*

---

They did not send for her this time. They came and got her.

There was a difference in it and she felt the difference the way she felt weather, before it had a name. A man who sends for you leaves you the door — you can be out, you can be sick, you can be a day late on the road. A man who comes and gets you has decided the door is his. So when the wagons came up the Blanco grade in the brown noon, four of them and a dozen riders strung behind, and Neel got down off the lead seat with his hat already in his hand and his eyes not meeting hers, she knew before he opened his mouth that the walk was not going to be a thing she agreed to. It was going to be a thing that had already happened to her, and she was only now catching up to it.

"They found water," Neel said.

"No," Odessa said. "They didn't."

He looked at her then. He had been the one skeptic in the belt, the young hard-eyed one who would not take off his hat over the Gault playa, and the drought had taken the skepticism out of him the way it took the green out of everything, slow, and against the will, and complete. Now he wanted her to make it true. She could see the want on him like sweat.

"There's standing water in the White Draw," he said. "Persy Gault's boy found it — the deep cut past the salt, where the flat drops. A whole reach of it. Clear." He stopped. "We need you to say it's good."

She said nothing. Behind him the wagons stood with their people in them, and the people were looking at her the way the county had always looked at her, that old ring-around-the-fallen-woman look, waiting to see whether she would rise up cursed or rise up chosen — except now they had decided which, and the deciding was the thing pressing on her, sixteen faces of it, and more coming up the grade.

"There is no good water in the White Draw," she said. "There has never been good water in the White Draw. That is why it's white."

"Then come and say so," Neel said. "To their face. Because they've already started hauling it."

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Rueda Salt Flats, W. Evaporite basin, old lake bed. Ground white — gyp, thenardite, epsom under the crust; boots break through to a gray paste. Salt cedar solid on the margin, twenty feet high, deep-rooted, greener than anything living for ten miles — a lie the county learned to read wrong. Saltgrass, pickleweed, iodine bush. No cottonwood. No willow. No rush. No dove-line at dusk — birds bear off north, none drop. The reach of standing water past the drop: clear, dead-still, no skin of insects, no frog, no minnow, a rim of white two hands wide drying at the edge where it falls. Wind off it: sulfur, faint rotten egg, then the flat bitter of gyp on the back of the tongue. Break the crust at the water's lip: gray, then a seam of clear crystal that is not ice. Salt lay here. Salt lies here. The water is the salt, dissolved and standing. Bad. Not deep, not shallow, not a question of depth. Bad the way the sea is bad. It will kill what drinks it slow and salt the ground it touches for a generation.

---

They rode her out to it under the tea-colored sun. The dust had thinned to a haze by then but it hung in the low places, so the flats came at them the way a drowned country comes up through water, white and wrong, and the salt cedar stood along the margin in a green wall that any fool who had never lived here would have taken for the edge of a river. Odessa had lived here. She took it for what it was, which was the drought's best liar, a tree that put its roots down into poison and drank the poison and threw up green leaves to say *come.*

There were more than sixteen at the draw when they got there. There were seventy, eighty, and wagons still coming, and a line of them down at the water's lip with buckets and barrels and a woman filling a nursing bottle by dipping it under with her thumb over the neck. Someone had set a plank across two kegs and laid out tin dippers on it like a communion table. And out past the drinkers, in the dead-still clear reach, Persy Gault's boy stood knee-deep with his trousers rolled, grinning, holding up cupped handfuls of it to the light so it ran bright through his fingers, and every time it ran the people made a sound.

Odessa did not get down.

"Miz Pike," said a man she half-knew, a belt man, reaching up for her mule's bridle the way pilgrims had started to. "Miz Pike, you'll bless it. You'll say the word over it."

"Take your hand off my animal."

He took it off. But he did not step back, and behind him none of them stepped back, and she understood then, sitting the mule above eighty upturned faces at the edge of a lake of poison, exactly the shape of the thing that had come up the grade for her. They had not brought her to ask. They had brought her to *ratify.* She was the seal. They had already decided the water was mercy — how could a whole reach of standing water be anything but mercy, in the thirty-fifth week — and they wanted the disgrace-become-saint to press her mark into the wax so the mercy would hold.

And the terrible arithmetic she always ran ran itself now, fast, the way it had run for Emory Cade in the mouth of her draw a whole dying year ago. *What keeps me and mine alive one more year.* Eighty people who had come up the grade to be told yes. One word to send them home in peace and off her back and still calling her holy. Or one word to make eighty desperate mouths hear that the disgrace had come out to their one water in the drought to take it from them — to stand at their communion table and say *no*, and be the woman who said *no* to water in the thirty-fifth week.

She thought about Melbern, who could stand on a green-lumber stage and fail and take the noon train and leave nothing behind him but an empty gauge. Her failures stayed. Her failures had families in them.

"It's salt," she said.

She said it into the sound they were still making, and it went into the sound and drowned, and no more than the three or four nearest her even turned their heads.

---

She got down. She had not meant to. Her body got her down, out of the fixed high place where she could be shot at, and into the crowd where she could at least be heard, and she pushed through them toward the plank table with her ruined hand pressed flat to her own thigh, and the salt smell came up off the reach so plain now, so plainly wrong, sulfur and gyp and the bitter under it, that she could not fathom how they did not smell it too. But they did not. A frightened man's nose reports what the man needs, and always, always the need wins; she had known that a year ago about ozone and she knew it now about salt.

She got to the table. Persy Gault was there.

Persy had a full dipper in her two hands, held out, and her plain strong face was doing a thing Odessa had not seen it do — it was lit, the dust-paled eyes gone wet and bright, and she held the dipper up to Odessa like an offering, like the widow's mite, like the thing she had waited her whole ruined year to be able to give.

"I dug where you said," Persy said. "The playa. It never came. You know it never came. I sold the last shoat and I dug on your word and it never came, and I never once — Miz Pike, I never once said a hard word about you, ask any soul on the belt." Her voice was not accusing. That was the horror of it. Her voice was *grateful.* "And now it's come anyway. In its own time. The Lord kept it back to teach us and then He set it out in the open where a child could find it, and my boy found it, my own boy — " She pushed the dipper the last inch. "Drink first. You found the two waters. You ought to have the first drink of the free one."

Odessa looked at the dipper. She looked at the water standing clear in it, and the rim of white already drying on the tin above the line where the wet stopped.

She could smell the gyp coming off the surface of one dipperful.

"Persy," she said. Low. The stammer moved in her throat, the block sitting down hard on the *don't* she meant to say, so that what came out first was only the shape of a stopped consonant, a small stone, and she saw Persy see it, and file it, and read it — not as fear, not as a woman blocked on a word, but as *the prophet gathering the word up*, the way they read her silences now, the way Neel had read the rain. There was no move left on the board. She had known that since Ojo Frio. Her truth and her stammer and her silence were all mortar now, and the wall they built was not hers.

"Don't drink it," she said. She got it out clean, at last, flat and weighted, the performing voice, because the performing voice was the only voice that left no gap for the block. And because it was the only voice that left no gap, it landed the way she had trained it to land. It landed like weather.

And every soul in earshot took it as the word over the water.

She heard the sound move outward from her through the crowd, that leaning-in gone loose into relief, *she spoke, she said it, the Salt Preacher blessed it* — she watched the belt man who had grabbed her bridle turn and cup his hands to his mouth and holler it down the line, *she says drink*, though she had said the opposite word, though she had said the plainest opposite word she owned, and it did not matter what word she had said. She had *spoken over the water.* That was the yes. The word was decoration on the yes. The yes had come up the grade three hours before she did.

---

They drank.

She stood at the plank table in the middle of it and watched them drink and could not stop them drinking, and this — she would think later, when she let herself think, which she would try not to do — this was the thing the whole burnt year had been walking her toward, plainer than any rod, plainer than the copper or the wire or the fern under her sleeve: that she had made herself into a mouth the county listened to, and the county would hear out of that mouth only the thing it had already decided to hear. She could scream *poison* and they would carry it home as *proof.* She had built a wall of fear to keep men off her, and the wall had grown a gate she could not shut, and the whole desperate county was walking in through it to the water and dipping and drinking and holding it up to their children.

Persy drank. She drank the dipper she had offered, tipped it back with her eyes closed and her throat working, and made a small sound at the end of it that Odessa understood was the sound of a woman tasting deliverance, and did not once flinch at the bitter, because a woman who has decided a thing is deliverance does not taste salt in it. The boy in the water drank from his hands. A man held his baby's head and dribbled it between the baby's lips off his finger.

Odessa did not stop them.

She would go over that, after. She would run it the way she ran everything, cold, in the flat clear place she trusted more than any warmth, and she would find that she could have kept screaming it. She could have knocked the dipper from Persy's hand. She could have waded into the reach and hauled the boy out by his collar and stood in the water shouting until her voice cracked, and the only thing she would have bought with it was to be the disgrace who fought the county over its one water in the thirty-fifth week — to be, at last and for certain, the thing they blamed. And a woman alone did well to not be that. She had a deed to buy. She had a self to keep off other men's mercy. She had said the true word once, plainly, and they had eaten it and made it false, and there was no second true word that would come out any different, and so she stood at the plank table and let the arithmetic close over the top of her like the dust had closed over the sun, and she said nothing more.

That was the walk. She had walked worse ground. She had never walked worse than standing still.

---

By evening the first of the cattle were down.

Not hers. She had none out here. But a free-grass man had driven a gaunt little bunch of forty head up to the draw on the news, the way men had driven stock to every rumor of water for six weeks, and the stock had gone into the reach up to their hocks and drunk, drunk the way only a thirsty steer drinks, and by the long brown dusk they were staggering. Salt does not kill a beast the way a bullet does. It kills it the slow honest way the flat had promised her it would in the assay — it pulls the water out of the animal to answer the salt it has swallowed, so the thing dies of thirst with its belly full of water, dies drinking, and by full dark three of the forty were down in the crust with their sides going like bellows and their tongues out, and the free-grass man was walking among them in the last light with his hat off, not understanding, saying to no one, *they had water, they had all the water they could hold.*

Odessa watched from the mule. She had known it would be exactly this. She had smelled it three hours before the first steer lowered its head.

And she watched the crowd watch the cattle die, and she watched the thing she had learned to watch for, the thing that had frightened her worse than the strikes ever had — she watched them *make it hold.* A woman near her, seeing the steers thresh, said it first, quiet, to steady herself: "It's the beasts. It's given to man and not to the beast." And a man took it up: the water was set out for God's people and not for the free-grass men's godless stock, and hadn't the free-grass men cut the Dobie fence, hadn't they always taken what wasn't theirs, and here was the water refusing them the way the Red Sea refused Pharaoh. By the time the third steer stopped moving they had built the whole of it — a water that killed the unworthy beast and blessed the worthy man, a proof, a sorting, a judgment standing right there in the flat with its white edge drying. The salt had not shaken their faith. The salt had *fed* it. She had watched a dry hole feed it and a false rain feed it and a murdered man feed it, and now she watched poison feed it, and she understood, finally and all the way down, that there was nothing she could hand this county that it would not eat and call bread.

The free-grass man's stock died in the crust behind them as the wagons started home singing.

---

Persy Gault came to the mule before she left. She had a face on her that Odessa would carry a long time, though she would not have called it carrying and would have gone more clipped than usual if anyone had named it — a face gone easy for the first time in the year Odessa had known it, the deep-graved lines of it slackened, a woman who had set down a weight she'd carried since her husband went into the ground.

"I'm glad you were here for it," Persy said. "I prayed you would be. It didn't seem right, the free water coming, without you here to see the Lord answer you." She laid her chapped hand on the mule's neck, the pilgrim's touch, and did not know she did it. "You'll not haul from town anymore. None of us will. We've got our own now." She smiled, and the smile had salt drying at the corners of her mouth. "I filled six barrels."

Odessa looked at the salt drying at the corner of Persy Gault's mouth.

She had one true word left and she knew now exactly what it would buy — nothing, and worse than nothing, one more brick in the wall that was closing over her. She had the arithmetic. She had run it and it had come out the way it always came out. And still, for the length of one breath, in the flat clear place, she felt the true word rise up her throat past the block, whole and plain, *don't cook with it don't give it the baby haul six days from town before you touch a drop of it Persy for the love of your own life* — she felt it stand there ready.

She let it stand. Then she pushed her sleeve down over the wire and the fern, out of pure habit, though there were no rods to hide, and she turned the mule for the grade.

"Six barrels," she said. "That's a good many."

Behind her the White Draw lay flat and clear and bright in the last of the dust-colored light, and the salt cedar stood green along the margin telling its green lie, and the wagons went down the Blanco grade full of poison and full of song, and out on the crust the last of the free-grass steers heaved once and was still, dead of thirst with its belly full of water, its tongue gone white to the root with the same white that was drying, even now, at the corner of Persy Gault's mouth, and on the mouth of every child on every wagon, and on the rim of six barrels rolling home in the dark to a widow's dugout to be drunk.


---

# CHAPTER 17 · Movement III — The Midpoint Turn

### "Owned"

*Cheyne County — the Bar EM home place. Rainless week thirty-seven. The Rueda a string of dead pools going the color of weak coffee, the ford long since walked across dry-shod. Hauled water sixteen cents the barrel, and the depot tank guarded now day and night by two men with rifles. The Bar EM's own head-springs down to a seep a boy could dam with his hand — a thing the county did not yet know, and the thing that had brought Odessa here.*

---

The rider had come for her before light, which told her most of it before he opened his mouth.

Cade did not send a man out in the dark to fetch a thing he owned lightly. A summons at that hour was the summons of a man who did not want to be seen doing the fetching, and a man who did not want to be seen was a man who had grown afraid of the seeing, and there was only one thing in Cheyne County left that could frighten Emory Cade. She rode in behind the rider with the sun coming up dirty over the flats, and she counted, the way she counted everything, the things that told her the Bar EM was dying.

She counted the windmills. Both stood dead-vaned in the still morning, the pump rods hanging in the casing, and under the nearer one the trough was a dry pan cracked into plates like the river. She counted the hides. Green hides pegged out to cure along the corral fence, forty of them, more, the Bar EM shipping its own dead the way the little men shipped theirs, and the stink of them came to her on the first hot breath of the day, that sweet meat-rot she had smelled off three ranches at once all summer. She counted the men. Fewer than there should have been. A big outfit in a wet year kept forty hands; she saw eight, and half of those were watching her come with the flat look of men who had not been paid in a while and had begun to wonder if they would be.

The Bar EM was a corpse standing up. It only had better boots than the other corpses.

A hand crossed the yard toward Cade as she came up — a lank man, hat in his hand, not looking at her at all. "Mr. Cade," he said, low, urgent, "you said Saturday. My wife's people are—" and Cade said, "Get on back to the tank, Willem," without heat and without a nod, and the man stood a moment with his hat still in his two hands and then put it on and went, and that was the whole of it, and it told her more than the strongbox would.

The house was low and long, adobe faced with plank, the one glass window in the county after the bank's. A boy took her mule. She did not give him the reins off her dead wrist; she worked them loose herself, slow, the way she did everything with that hand, and she felt him watch her do it, and she let him watch.

Cade met her on the gallery. He did not come down the steps.

"Miz Pike."

"Mr. Cade."

He looked older than he had in May. Not thinner — Cade would go to his grave heavy — but grayer, a gray that had got up under the skin, and there was a stillness in him now that was not the old still. The old still had been a man who owned the time. This was a man listening for something.

"You'll come in," he said. "There's men to see you."

---

There were three of them in the long room, and she read the room before she read the men, because the room was easier and told her nearly as much.

The table was set with the Association's papers, weighted at the corners against a wind that was not blowing, because Cade set a table the way another man cocked a gun. There was a decanter of good whiskey on the sideboard with the stopper out, poured for effect and not for drinking — Cade did not spend liquor before noon on business. And there was, along the far wall, a thing that did not belong: a strongbox, iron-strapped, the padlock hanging open on the hasp. A man does not leave a strongbox open unless he means you to look in it, and she was not fool enough to look, so she looked once, quickly, the way you look at a snake to know where it is, and away.

The men.

Royal Waybourne sat where the light was best, which was Waybourne all over — a man who would take the good chair at his rival's table and think it a victory. He had on a coat too fine for a dying county and his teeth were too white and he smiled at her like they shared a secret, which they did not.

The second man she did not know. Neat, gray, spectacles, ink on his cuffs, a leather case on his knees and both hands folded on it. He did not smile. He had the face of a man who had read everything about her that could be written down and had found it wanting because it was not yet on the right form. She did not need Cade to name him. The land office had a smell, and it was this: paper and lamp oil and patience.

"Delp," he said anyway, when he saw her looking. "Horace Delp. From the office at Salt Fork. You'll have heard of me." He had a mild dry voice with a cough folded into it. "I've heard a great deal of you."

"Then we're square," she said.

Cade did not sit. He stood at the head of his own table with his ruined hands flat on it and let the silence run, and she saw what he was doing — he was using her own instrument on her, the waiting, the thing she had built her whole trade on — and she saw that he had learned it from watching her — that the man had studied her the way you study a horse you mean to break.

"You made a mistake at the White Draw," Cade said.

---

She said nothing. She had known the salt flats were coming into this the moment the rider came in the dark. A field was ruined out on the low ground west of town — gyp water pumped up glad onto a nester's beans and the beans dead in a week, the soil gone white and sour and dead for years — and a man's mule dead of drinking it, and, worse, a child sick from a bucket carried home. She had smelled the gyp on that ground before she raised a rod. She had known it for poison the way she knew her own name. And she had been made to stand there and be *heard* to bless it, her withholding taken for a yes, because the crowd had already decided what her silence meant and there was no longer any silence she could offer that did not speak.

She did not say any of that. She said, "The ground was salt. I read it salt."

"That's not what the county heard," Delp said, gentle. "The county heard the Salt Preacher find water in the white flats and a man's field die of the finding. There's a woman in Salt Fork saying her sister's child took sick of your water. There's talk of an inquest." He touched the case on his knees, once, like a man reassuring a dog. "I don't say there's cause. I say there's talk, and talk is a thing that goes into the record if a man lets it, and comes out of the record if a man will not."

There it was. She turned it over. Not a threat about the child — Delp did not care a straw about the child — a threat about the *paper*, which was the only thing Delp cared about, and the paper could hang her or clear her depending on which man's hand was on the pen. The three of them had spent a poisoned field and a sick child into a lever, and she was the thing the lever was set under.

"You've brought me here to be afraid," she said.

"I've brought you here to be *safe*," Cade said.

---

He came around the table then, slow, and stood closer than a man stood to her, close enough that she had to make herself not step back, because stepping back was a door and she kept her doors shut.

"You know what's under this county about as well as any man living now," he said, low, and it was near the very words he had used in the draw in May, and she heard him hear them too. "Better than any man living. I told you in the spring there was no more water to find. I was wrong. You find it where I'd have sworn on my mother there was none." A muscle went in his jaw. "And every drop you find, some man kills for. You found the Dobie spring and it got fenced and the fence got cut and there'll be a killing over it before the month's out, mark me. You found the widow's playa and half the belt planted on a hole. You found salt at the White Draw and a field died." He put one ruined finger down on the table, hard, on the top paper. "You are the most dangerous thing in this county, Miz Pike, and I am the only man in it big enough to hold you."

"Hold me," she said.

"Own you," he said, because Cade did not lie about the size of a thing. "Say it plain. The Association will put its water and its lawyers and its name between you and an inquest, and between you and a rope, and between you and the day the crowd that made you decides it wants a saint to blame — and that day is coming, you know it's coming, you can read a crowd the way you read a draw. In exchange you walk where we send you. You find water for the Bar EM and you find *salt* for everyone we point you at. You do not walk for Waybourne. You do not walk for a preacher. You do not walk for a nester who waves four dollars at you. Your word is the Association's word, filed at the office" — a nod at Delp — "before a shovel touches ground, so no fence gets cut and no man gets shot, because we will already own what you find before you finish saying you've found it."

It was, she thought, the cleanest thing anyone had said to her since May. It was the whole machine, named. He wanted her to be a gun the Association loaded and the land office registered. He wanted her word to stop being a starting-pistol that any desperate man could run at and start being a deed with the Bar EM's brand already burned in it.

And he wanted, though he would not say this part and did not need to, to make his own dying springs the county's secret a while longer — to have the woman who could smell water in dead ground bound so tight she could not tell a soul that the great Bar EM was down to a seep a boy could dam with his hand.

She had seen the trough. She had smelled the seep on the wind coming in. That was the true reason for the dark rider and the open strongbox and the whiskey no one would drink. Cade was drowning, and he had sent for the one person in the county who could see the water leaving, to buy her silence before she knew she had a thing to sell.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Bar EM home place. Trough cracked to plates, mud floor sun-curled, last wet ring a full hand below the old high mark — dry three weeks, four. Cottonwoods by the spring house yellow at the crown, shedding out of season, roots gone dry. Willows behind them dead to the tips. Salt cedar come up through the willow — the salt cedar always comes when the sweet water quits and the ground turns. Cattle trails all bear one way, north, off the home place — stock walking to water that is not here anymore. Green hide count forty-plus at the fence. Grass gone to bare caliche in the near pasture, cropped and blown. Head-springs: no run in the ditch, mud dark at the box, seep only, a cupful an hour by the wet. Ants moved off the spring-house shade to the wet under the trough crack — following the last damp down. Water leaving here, fast, and the man knows it, and does not want it counted.

---

"What's it pay," she said.

Waybourne laughed. It was the wrong moment to laugh and he knew it and did it anyway, because a laugh was a tool he could not stop reaching for. "There," he said, to Cade, to the room. "There's the woman. Not *is it right*, not *what of the child* — what's it pay. You'll never own that, Emory. You can't own a thing that's only ever asking the price."

Cade did not look at him. "A hundred the month," he said to her. "Silver. Find or dry, same as the walks. And" — he weighted it, the way she weighted a pronouncement, and she gave him that; he had learned the trade — "a quarter-section. Deeded. Yours. Fenced ground with the old Sablon seep on it that the office" — Delp inclined his head a quarter-inch — "will find is unclaimed after all. A deed, Miz Pike. Your name on paper the law can't lift. I know what you want. I've always known what you want. No man in this county but me can give it to you."

And there it was, the other thing, and it went through her worse than the rope had.

He had found the only price that could touch her. Not the money — the money was air, the money came and went. The *deed*. A fenced quarter-section with water on it and her name on the paper, the exact thing she had wanted since she was a girl the county pointed at in the road, the thing she had taken his silver in May to inch toward, the wall behind the wall, the ground no Cade and no Waybourne and no man who had ever flinched from her could put her off of. He was holding it out across the poisoned field and the sick child and the open strongbox the way a man holds a full bucket to a beef three days off water — slow, and watching the animal's legs to see if they would carry it in.

And it was salt. She knew it the moment she wanted it. It was gyp water in a clean glass. A deed from Cade was a deed she held at Cade's pleasure — the Sablon seep was the seep a man had been *killed* over, the man she had read out of the ground with the Association's own wire coiled beside his boots, and to take that ground was to take the murder with it, to stand on a grave the Bar EM had dug and call it home and know that the same men who deeded it to her could unmake the paper and hang the killing on the woman standing on it any morning they chose. He was not offering her a wall. He was offering her a longer rope and calling it a fence.

She wanted it anyway. That was the terrible part, the part she kept off her face. She stood in the long room and wanted the thing that would finish her, wanted it the way the nesters wanted rain, and she knew — the way you know a man is lying before he finishes the sentence — that this was exactly how she had them all. This was the leaning-in. She was, for one breath, the crowd, and Cade was raising the rod, and she felt in her own chest the pull she had been selling for silver all summer.

She made her voice go flat and slow, the trained voice, the voice with no gap in it for the block to catch, so it would land the way she needed it to land.

"No," she said.

---

The room did a small thing then, a settling and unsettling both, the way a herd shifts when it smells something on the wind and cannot name it.

"No," Cade said, not a question. He had not expected it. He had priced her to the penny and she had refused the price and it had never once occurred to him that she might, because a man who believes everything can be owned cannot conceive of the thing that will not be, and his flaw stood up in him plain for a moment, and she saw it.

"You'll want to think," Delp said, mild. "There's the inquest to—"

"I heard the inquest." She did not look at him. She looked at Cade. "You'll not raise an inquest on me, Mr. Cade. You brought me here in the dark. You'd not have come in the dark to threaten a woman you meant to hang; you'd have let the sheriff do it in the daylight and stood on your gallery and watched. You came in the dark because you can't afford the county to know what I'll know the second I've said it out loud, which is that the Bar EM's own water is down to a seep and the great man is hauling like a nester by Advent." She let that sit. She let the silence run the way she'd taught herself, the way he'd tried to learn off her and could not, because you cannot learn the waiting from a man who is afraid; the waiting is a thing you can only do from a wall. "You can't hang me. I know where your water isn't."

Nobody moved. The whiskey no one would drink caught the dirty light. Out past the glass a windmill stood dead against the white sky with its vane hung and not one blade turning, and she looked at it while they took her measure, took the measure of the thing they had thought they were buying and had found, instead, standing in the room with them holding the one card the whole game was played to keep off the table.

Waybourne recovered first, because Waybourne was always looking for the door.

"Well," he said, soft, and the smile came back but changed, gone conspirator, gone *we two against him*, and he half-rose and spread his hands to her the way a man spreads his hands to a horse he means to gentle and steal. "Miz Pike. You see how it is. He can't hold you and he can't hang you, and a woman in a spot like that wants a *friend*, wants a man who'll stand between her and all this and ask nothing but—"

"Sit down, Royal," Cade said, without heat, without turning, and Waybourne sat.

Cade came the last step to her. Close. Closer than the whiskey, closer than the paper. Low, so it was for her, though the room heard it, because he meant the room to hear it — he was raising his own rod now, slow, and making them wait.

"You made yourself dear walking," he said. "I told you that in the winter and you didn't hear me. A cheap thing a man can leave lie. A dear thing he has got to own." His ruined hand came up, not to touch her — he was not a fool — but flat between them, fingers spread, the same gesture Rourke used, the same gesture the whole county was learning, a hand held up over her like a benediction and a claim in one. "You'll not take my deed and you'll not take my rope. All right. Then hear the last of it, and go home to your dugout, and count your fires like you do." His eyes flicked once to her right sleeve, hunting the wire, the scar, the map of the thing he could not own, and away. "There is no home to go to that I do not reach. There is no ground you'll stand on I can't lift the paper off of. There is no crowd that made you I can't turn. I made you and I can unmake you, and the only day I won't is the day it costs me more than it pays — and you've just made it cost me more. So you go on and be free, Miz Pike. You be the freest woman in Cheyne County." He stepped back. "Right up to the morning I decide you're not."

She held his eyes and did not blink and did not stammer, because she had trained the stammer out of the places where it mattered, and the block was down in her chest with the want and the fear where none of them would ever see it.

"I'll want my mule," she said.

---

The boy brought the mule around. She worked the reins onto her dead wrist herself and did not let him help, and she rode out past the pegged hides and the dead windmills and the eight unpaid men, and none of them touched a hat to her the way Cade had in May, because the thing that had touched a hat to her then had been a man who thought he was buying a curse, and the thing that watched her go now was a man who had learned she was worse than a curse — she was a fact he could not file.

She did not look back until the home place was small.

When she did, she saw the dust of a second rider, well behind her, holding back, not closing. She knew the seat of him. It was Waybourne, gone out the other way and swung round to meet her clear of the house, coming to make in the open the pitch Cade had made him swallow in the room — *a woman in a spot like that wants a friend* — coming to sell her a rescue she would have to pay for the rest of her life. She would let him talk. She would take nothing. She had learned the price of every man in the county now, and the price of the county itself, which was that it would deed her a grave and swear it was a home, and turn her out to be free the way you turn a beef out on burnt grass and call it grazing.

The want was still in her, low, where she kept it. A quarter-section. A deed. Water she owned. She had ridden into the Bar EM this morning still able to believe she was working her way toward it, one twenty-dollar walk at a time, a hard careful woman keeping her own counsel and her own accounts. She rode out knowing better. There was no walk that led to a deed of her own. Every drop she found deeded itself to a man the second she named it, and the man it deeded itself to could not afford a well she owned, because a well she owned was a well she could keep from him.

Ahead of her, at the crossroads where the Bar EM lane met the Salt Fork road, someone had set a thing in the caliche while she was in the house. A cross of two mesquite sticks lashed with baling wire, driven upright in the dust, and hung on the crossbar a strip of white rag and a tin cup, an offering, the kind the pilgrims had begun leaving on the road she rode, left there by someone who had heard the Salt Preacher would pass this morning and had come to leave the ground a sign the way you leave a grave a flower. The rag lifted a little in the hot wind and fell. The cup was empty. It had been set out to catch a rain that was not coming, at a crossing where no water had run in living memory, by a hand that believed, against the whole white witness of the sky, that where she passed the ground might give.

She rode around it and did not stop, and behind her, patient, Waybourne's dust came on.


---

# CHAPTER 18 · Movement III — The Midpoint Turn

### "A Death That Follows Her Word"

*The fenced spring on the Rueda's north bench, near the Territory line — rainless week thirty-nine.*
*The Rueda a bed of cracked plate below the ford, wet only in the deep holes where the cranes stood all day and did not fly. Hauled water sixteen cents the barrel and short at that. Bar EM's head-springs failing so that Cade's own riders now watered stock by night, quiet, so the county would not count it. And on the north bench, where Odessa had found water in the third week of the fence war, a man lay under a wagon-sheet with his boots pointing at a sky that had given nothing in thirty-nine weeks and did not appear to be sorry.*

---

She had not meant to come. The boy had come for her, riding the sacked-out horse Halve let him keep, and he had come at a lope, which Tobe never did, because he spared the horse the way she had taught him to spare everything, and when a boy who spares everything comes at a lope it is a kind of speech, and she read it before he reached her fence line and had the mule saddled before he swung down.

He did not say it. He never said anything. He pointed north and east, toward the bench, and he made with his hand a small flat cutting motion low across his own body, once, and she knew.

The fenced spring. Somebody had died at the fenced spring.

"Whose," she said.

He looked at her. His face did the thing it did, which was nothing, a stillness she had come to read the way she read a playa edge — the watchfulness under it, the held wing. He did not know whose, or he knew and had no word for it, and either way the answer was going to be a man she had made rich or made a corpse, and there was no third kind of man at that spring.

They rode.

---

She had found the water in week twenty-nine, on ground everyone knew for dead, a break in the caliche bench where the cap tilted and perched a shallow sweet table off an old sealed channel, and she had raised the copper slow and made them wait and told them where. That was the walk. The walk had been clean. It was the tenth week of what came after the walk that she was riding into now, and nothing after the walk had ever been clean again.

Prior appropriation, Delp had said, in his mild voice, the register's voice that phrased a killing as a rule. First in time, first in right. On the Territory side of the line a man did not own the water in the ground; he owned it by taking it — by ditching it, boxing it, turning it onto a field or into a trough. First to divert, first to hold. So when Odessa's copper had swung down over the bench and she had said *there*, she had not found a spring.
