She had fired a starting gun, and the man who ran fastest with a shovel and a deed had won the county's last sweet water, and the men who ran second had won nothing but the sight of a fence going up around water they had watched her find with their own eyes.

The Bar EM had run fastest. Cade had had a filing on Delp's desk before the hole was proved, and by the time it made water fit to drink there was new galvanized wire strung on new cedar posts all around it, four strands, tight, and a lock on the gate, and a man of Cade's paid to sit the gate with a rifle across his knees. The free-grass men — the Dobie cluster, the little cowmen with cattle dying in the sight of water Cade would not sell them at any price because selling it would mean losing the hold on them — had cut that fence twice already. Twice it had gone back up. She had heard the second time they left no note.

She had found them their salvation and it had gone up behind wire the same week, and now she rode toward the third cutting, and this one, the boy's flat hand had told her, had wet the ground.

---

They came up onto the bench in the flat white light of the middle of the day, no shade anywhere on it, the tarbush low and oiled and dark and the grama gone entirely to straw, and she saw the fence first — a long stretch of it down, posts pulled clean and lying, four strands snarled and dragged, the way men pull wire when they mean it — and then she saw the wagon and the sheet, and the horses standing, and the men.

Eight, ten men. Free-grass men on one side of the down fence, hats off, and that was how she knew for certain before she reached them, because a free-grass man does not take his hat off for the sun. Two Bar EM riders on the other side, hats on, rifles not quite up and not quite down. And between them, on the trampled ground where the wire had been, the wagon-sheet, and under it a shape, and one boot standing up at the end of it, and a dark stain gone brown and stiff in the dust reaching out from under the sheet toward the spring, toward the water, the way a spilled thing runs downhill.

She got down. Her right wrist ached where no wire was; she had reached for it out of habit on the ride and found her sleeve empty and left it empty. She had not brought the rods. There was nothing to find here that a rod could pretend to.

The free-grass men looked at her. She knew most of their faces. She had walked their dying ground; some of them had taken their hats off to her at the Slash W in the second month, when she was still a woman who found water and not yet a woman men died beside.

Old Dobie stood at the head of the sheet. He was a hard proud landless-in-effect man of near sixty, and his face was doing something she did not often see on a grown man's face in the county, which was grief with nowhere to put itself, grief hunting for a shape.

"Miz Pike," he said.

"Who's under there," she said.

He crouched and took the corner of the sheet and turned it back, and she made herself look, because to flinch from it in front of them would be to give them a thing, and she never gave them a thing.

It was the younger Dobie. Cordy, they called him — not thirty, a good hand, married to a girl over on the Blanco who was carrying, the whole county had heard she was carrying because good news traveled that far in a dry year and got told over and over. He had a hole in him just below the collarbone and another lower, and the second was the one that had emptied him out onto the ground toward the water. His mouth was a little open. There was dust in it. There was dust on his open eye and no one had thought to close it or had not been able to stand to.

She looked a while. She was good at looking. Then she straightened.

> **ASSAY.**
> Two entries, front, low. First above the second, a hand apart. Powder-scorch — none: shot from off, not close. Blood run downhill NW toward the spring box, ten, twelve feet, soaked and set. Fence-wire snarl beside him, cutter still in the right hand, jaws open on a strand. Post-hole spades three, dropped, not stacked — men left in a hurry, not a stand. Boot-sign both sides the wire: free-grass brogans NE, worn; stitched-welt Bar EM boots SW, on the higher ground, on the rise. Two brass casings on the rise, .44-40, walked into the dust by a heel that meant to. Spring box wet, seeping still. He fell reaching it. He did not reach it.

She let none of that onto her face.

"He was cutting the wire," she said. Not a question. The cutter was still in his hand, the jaws still bit on the strand.

"They been cutting the mud out from under our stock a month," Old Dobie said. His voice came level and that was worse than if it had broke. "We got forty head down in sight of that box. In *sight* of it. My brother's boy took the nippers to it because I sent him to. I sent him." He said it again, giving it the shape it had been hunting. "I sent him, and Cade's man on the rise put two into him from forty foot and walked his brass into the ground after, and then they lit out for the sheriff to tell it first."

She knew that was true too, all of it, because the brass was walked into the dust by a heel that meant to and the spades were dropped and not stacked and the men on the rise had gone. The one who tells the sheriff first owns the story, and the story, in this county, was the only law there was.

---

One of the Bar EM riders on the far side of the down wire spat and shifted his rifle. He was young and he did not like where he was standing.

"He was cutting a lawful fence," the rider said, to no one, to the air, saying the words he had been given to say. "Man's got a right to defend—"

"Shut your mouth," Old Dobie said, without turning, and the rider shut it.

Then Old Dobie turned and looked at Odessa, full on, and she saw the grief in him find, at last, the shape it had been hunting, and the shape it found was her.

"You found it," he said.

She said nothing. She had learned long ago that the first thing to do with a thing coming at you is to hold still and let it declare itself.

"You come out here in the fifth month with your copper and you walked this bench and you raised it up and you said *there*." His voice was climbing now, not loud, but climbing, and the other free-grass men had gone very still around him, and she felt the ground under the moment tilt the way ground tilts toward water. "You said *there*, and we all took our hats off, God help us, we stood in a row and took our hats off like it was church, and you found the last sweet water in this county and set it down right *here* where Cade could throw a fence around it inside a week and shoot my brother's boy dead for wanting a drink of it."

"I don't put up fences," she said. Flat. Slow, so the block found no gap. "And I don't shoot men."

"No," he said. "You just tell folks where to dig, and other men fill in the rest." He was shaking now. Not with fear. "If you'd not found it there'd be nothing here to die over. Cordy'd be home with his wife and his coming child on a dry quarter, alive. You *made* this water, Miz Pike. You made it out of a stick and a lie, and now it's got a boy's blood in it, and God forgive me but I do not know anymore whether you are a woman the Lord kept alive for a mercy or a curse he forgot to finish."

There it was. The old question, the one the whole county had carried at her since the first strike — cursed or chosen, and never simply a woman — and here it was again on a dead man's ground with the water seeping ten feet from his open mouth.

She held very still and did the arithmetic she always did. What keeps me alive. What gets me off this bench with my hide. There were eight, ten armed and grieving men on one side and two frightened men with rifles on the other and a body between them, and the thing that decided which way the guns went was going to be whatever she said in the next breath, and every one of them was waiting on her to say it. She felt the waiting. She had spent her life learning exactly what it weighed. It was the same weight she manufactured on purpose with the copper, the lean-in, the readiness of frightened people to be told — only she had not built this one, and she could not end it with a slow rod and a withheld word, and for the first time since Cade had ridden into her draw with the sun behind him she did not know what to say.

---

Then a thing happened she had not reckoned on, and it was worse than the guns.

One of the younger free-grass men — a boy really, not much older than Cordy, hat crushed in his two hands — was not looking at her the way Old Dobie was looking at her. He was looking at her the other way. The old way. The way the Dobie man had looked at her at the Aguja in the first month, when his own boy was three weeks dead of fever off the mud and she had found water forty paces from a dry tank and he had said *you didn't find it, you knew it.*

The young man's eyes were wet and shining and there was nothing in them of blame at all. There was hope in them. There was the terrible thing.

"He died getting to it," the young man said, low, not to her, to the men around him, to himself. "He died reaching for the water she found. He put his hand on the wire she—" His voice broke and he did not stop for it. "There's a *meaning* in that. There's got to be a meaning in a thing like that. She found it and he died for it and that means the water's—" He could not finish. He did not need to finish. She watched the thing take him. She watched it move through him and change his face, watched grief turn its coat and become faith right there in front of her over the body, because the alternative to the water meaning something was that Cordy Dobie had bled out in the dust over nothing at all, and no man alive can stand a nothing that size, and so he reached for the meaning the way Cordy had reached for the water, and unlike Cordy he closed his hand on it.

*He died for the water she found.* She heard it land in the young man, heard it settle and take root, and she knew — the way she knew a seam of gyp before the shovel struck it — that he would carry it home to the Blanco, to the widow, to the belt, and that it would grow. A man had died reaching for her water. In a county that read weather as verdict and drought as judgment and a lightning-struck woman as a sign the Lord had refused three times to strike down, a man dying for her water was not a horror. It was a proof. It was the first drop of a thing she could already smell coming, the way she smelled rain that never fell — a martyr made on her word, and behind him, coming, the need for more.

Old Dobie saw it too, saw it in his own kinsman's wet shining face, and something in the old man broke that the guns had not broken.

"No," he said. "No, Wick. Don't you—" But he had no words for it either, no shape to put against the shape the young man had found, and the young man was already past hearing.

---

She should have let it run. That was the arithmetic. A county that made her water holy was a county that would not, that day, put the free-grass guns to work — the young man's faith was, in the cold ledger, the thing most likely to get her off the bench alive. She knew it and she felt the pull of it, the old pull, feed the fire and let it warm you.

Instead she said, "There's no meaning in it."

The words came out of her flat and hard and without a stammer, in the performing voice, the weighted slow voice that left no gap for the block, the voice that landed like weather — and that was the ruin of it, because she had spent thirty-nine weeks teaching this county that when she spoke in that voice she was speaking the truth of the ground, and now she used it to say the one true thing she had, and it landed on them exactly as everything else she had ever said in that voice had landed.

"There's no meaning in it," she said. "A man cut a fence and another man shot him. Water's water. It doesn't know his name and it doesn't want his blood and it won't taste different for having it. He didn't die *for* anything. He died because Cade put wire on ground I never should have—" She stopped herself. She did not finish it. She never finished the ones that could hang her.

But it did not matter which words she stopped. The young man, Wick, was looking at her now with his wet eyes gone wide and full. She had spoken the truth in the voice of prophecy, and he had heard the voice and not the words. He heard humility. He heard a holy woman waving off a death too great for her to claim, and it made the meaning larger, not smaller. It made her larger.

She felt it arrive in her, cold and complete, the way the third strike had arrived. The plain flat truth had been the one lever, and it was gone from her hand.

---

"Take him home," she said, to Old Dobie, because there was nothing else to say that was not fuel. "The sheriff's Cade's. He'll write it down the way Cade's man told it. Take the boy home to his wife before the paper does."

Old Dobie looked at her a long moment. The grief in him had nowhere to go now; the shape it had found — her — was already being taken from him by his own kinsman, remade into something he could not curse without cursing the water his own stock was dying in sight of. She watched him give it up. She watched an old hard man understand that he could not even keep his own grief clean, that the county would take Cordy from him the way it took everything, and make him a saint's first martyr, and that there was no ground low enough to bury that where it would not draw up like salt.

"They'll come to you," he said, at last, quiet. "You know that. Wick, and them like him. They'll come to you now with this in their mouths and they'll want you to make it mean the thing they need it to mean. And someday they'll want you to make it stop, and you can't, and when you can't—" He looked down at his brother's boy. "A county that'll make a saint out of a shot boy will want somebody's blood back for it, in the end. Salt draws salt." He pulled the sheet back over Cordy's face, over the open eye, gently, the way you would not think a man his size could do. "I'd get clear of this county, Miz Pike, if I was you and I could. But you found the water. Reckon you can't."

They loaded him in the wagon. The free-grass men put their hats back on, all but Wick, who kept his in his hands and kept looking at her, and the two Bar EM riders sat their horses on the rise and let them go, and the spring box seeped on into the trampled dust where the blood had set, sweet water and set blood a foot apart and neither one knowing the other was there.

She stood on the bench until the wagon was small on the flat. The boy Tobe stood beside her, holding both horses, watching the crowd go the way he always watched the crowd instead of the show. He had watched Wick's face. He had watched the thing turn its coat and become faith. And when she looked down at him she saw nothing move in the feral quiet of his own face, no doubt in it and no flinch, only a small settled recognition, the way a boy nods at a thing he has known a long time and is glad to hear said out loud at last.

The wagon went down off the bench and out of sight into the heat, carrying the first of them. She stood over the wet ground and the set blood a moment longer. She had thought she was driving the fear the way you drive a mule, hard and cold and toward your own gate. She said nothing of that to the boy, and she did not let it onto her face, and she turned to the mule.

She got on. Her right wrist ached where the wire wasn't. She turned it toward home and did not look back at the spring, and the cranes stood in the last deep holes of the dead river below and did not fly, and to the east the small orange stars of the belt's fires would be lit by dark, fewer than last month, and every one of them, she knew now, was waiting on her, and there was no longer any word she could say that would make them stop.


---

# CHAPTER 19 · Movement IV — The Siege

### "The Suspicion"

*Salt Fork, the boomtown souring — rainless week forty-one.*
*The Rueda a bed of cracked plate the whole length of the county now, wet only in three deep holes a man could jump. Hauled water eighteen cents the barrel, and salt-cedar taint in every drop of it, so that the town drank its own thirst back at itself with the bitterness of the flats folded in. Two hundred tents on the wagon-yard and the flat past it, and the counting had stopped meaning a number a long while ago. And the pilgrims who had come to be near the mercy were beginning, the way a barrel of water goes, to turn.*

---

She smelled it before she saw it, which was how she smelled most things.

Not the ozone. There was no ozone; there had never been any ozone; that lived in the noses of other people and always had. What she smelled, coming up the depot road on the mule with the boy half a length back, was the town gone sour — the plank-and-canvas reek of two hundred families camped where there was no water to wash in, the sweet-rot off the dead stock nobody could afford to drag far anymore, the sick-sweet of the flux going through the tents like a rumor. And under all of it, threading it, a thing she had no name for but read the way she read a gyp seam before the shovel struck it: the smell of a crowd that had begun to want something back.

She had walked into a great many crowds that wanted. She had built her living on the wanting. But there was a turn a want could take, a souring, the moment the wanting stopped being *give me* and started being *you owe me,* and she knew that turn the way she knew the caliche went white before it went hollow. She had felt it three times in her life, standing up out of the ground with her hair smoking and the ring of frightened faces closing an inch, deciding.

The town was deciding.

Nobody said anything to her. That was the first sign. A month ago the road had opened for her like a Sunday — hats coming off, women lifting their sick children to be near the mule, that hush that ran ahead of her that she had learned to hate and to spend. Today the men on the tent-lines watched her come and did not take their hats off, and did not look away either, which was worse, because a man who looks away is still afraid of you and a man who looks at you level has decided you are a thing that can be looked at.

She rode up the middle of it slow and did not hurry the mule and did not look at any one of them long enough to give him the thing his looking wanted.

---

Halve was on his porch with a broom he was not using, and when she came even with the store he came down the two steps into the dust to meet her, which he did not do, and put a hand on the mule's neck the way a man does when he means to say a thing quiet.

"Don't get down here," Halve said.

"I've silver coming."

"It'll keep." His ledger-eyes went once up the street and once down it, counting. "Miz Pike, there's talk."

"There's always talk."

"Not like this talk." He said it flat, and Halve did not waste words on weather. "Melbern's back."

She let that sit on her a moment. The rainmaker. The little round man in the checked suit who sold the sky, who had blown out of the county on the noon train after the bounty went unpaid — the fraud that failed without a corpse, she had thought, watching him go, the safe con leaving because it had stopped paying. She had made an enemy of him standing still, the day the county decided she had called his rain up from below to meet his sky.

"Selling what," she said. "There's no sky left to sell."

"He's not selling. He's telling." Halve dropped his voice under the porch shadow. "He's got a room over at the depot hotel and he holds it of an evening. Free. That's how you know it's poison — a man like that giving a thing away. He tells them how the sky-work is an old honest science and how a woman with two copper sticks is old dark humbug, and how the reason it hasn't rained is a fraud has got between the county and the Lord, drawing the mercy off. He don't say your name. He don't have to. He says *the woman.* And they're listening, because—" Halve stopped.

"Because it hasn't rained," she said.

"Because it hasn't rained since he left, and it hadn't rained while he was here, but a man remembers the order of a thing wrong when he's thirsty. And because—" Again the stop. Halve was a careful man and he was picking through a thing that had teeth. "Because the finds stopped, Miz Pike. You know they stopped. I know they stopped. There's not been sweet water found in this county in six weeks, and there's a salted field out east where the widow's boy waded, and there's a boy dead on the north bench cutting a fence around water you called, and there's a hole out on Ronsard's that went to gyp, and there's—" He shook his head. "A month back all of that was proof. God testing them. The sinner digging wrong. You know how it went. Now it's starting to add up the other way. Same figures. Different sum."

She sat the mule and looked down the sour street and did the arithmetic she always did, the only one she trusted. Eighteen cents a barrel. Two hundred tents. Six weeks dry of any find. A dead boy on the bench with a martyr's story growing on him like the fern grows on a burned tree. A rainmaker giving away his patter for free of an evening, which meant he was being paid in something other than money, and there was only one other coin in Cheyne County worth a man's evening, and that was her.

"They made me a saint out of a hole that held water," she said, half to herself, "and now they'll want a witch out of the hole that didn't."

Halve did not tell her she was wrong. That was the last sign, and the worst one.

---

She got down at the store's back, in the alley, out of the street's eye, and that was already a defeat and she took it because taking a small defeat cheap was how you stayed alive to refuse the large one dear. Tobe held the mule. He had gone very still on the ride in, the held-wing stillness, the watchful thing, and she knew he had read the street the same as she had, faster maybe, because he had been the flinched-from all his short life and knew the smell of a turning crowd in his skin.

Vester Kyle was waiting in the store's back room among the coal-oil and the airtights, and he had the look of a man who has printed a thing he is beginning to be afraid of. Ink to the second knuckle. A hunger in him gone a little sick.

"Miz Pike," he said. "I need to ask you a thing, and I need you to know I'm asking it as the man who put your name in type, not as the man who'll print your answer."

"Ask it."

"Did you salt the widow's field."

She looked at him.

"That's the talk," Kyle said, fast, getting it out. "That's Melbern's talk gone down among them and come back up changed, the way it does. That the White Draw water was salt, and you knew it — and there's them that'll swear you *said* it was salt, said *don't drink,* which was the truth, but you know how a truth travels in this county — and the sum they're making of it is: she knew it was poison and she let them drink it, and she let the Gault woman haul six barrels home to salt her own ground, because—" He wet his lips. "Because a prophet needs the county desperate. Because you're keeping the county thirsty on purpose, to keep them needing you. That's the shape of it. That you poison the water so they'll come to you for it."

The room was very quiet. Somewhere in the store a fly worked the airtights.

She had known it was coming and it still went into her cold, because it was the exact and perfect inversion of the thing that was true, the way the salt water was the exact and perfect inversion of mercy. She *had* known the White Draw was poison. She *had* said don't drink, plainly, in the performing voice, and watched them hear the yes inside the no. She had let the true warning to Persy die in her throat. Every fact they had, they had right. And they had arranged the right facts into a lie so clean it was nearly beautiful, a lie that fit her like a boot fits a foot it was cut for.

"I told them it was salt," she said. Flat. Slow, so the block found no gap. "I stood on the white rim and I said *don't drink it,* and they drank it, and Persy Gault filled six barrels while I stood there, and I could no more stop her than I could stop the sun coming up. That's the whole of it."

"I know," Kyle said miserably. "That's the hell of it. I *know.* But—" He spread his ink-black hands. "I put you in type. I made you spendable. And a coin that spends one way spends the other, and I don't know how to unprint a woman."

---

She stayed in the back room the rest of the afternoon because the street was not a thing to be walked twice in a day, and toward evening the men came.

Not a mob. That was the thing she made herself see clearly, sitting in the coal-oil dim with the boy at the door like a dog that would not be sent off — not a mob yet, because a mob is a made thing, it has to be built the way a fire is built, and this was only the kindling walking around in the shape of men, testing itself, seeing what it was. Four of them came up onto Halve's porch as the light went the color of weak tea, and Halve went out to them, and she listened through the plank wall.

She knew the voices. That was the worst of it, the thing that put the cold all the way down. She had walked their dying ground. She had taken their hats off with her copper. One was Neel — Neel who would not take his hat off at the Gault playa, Neel who had taken it off at last, Neel who had come and *got* her, driven half out of his mind by the drought, to the White Draw to beg her to make the salt water true. She had watched that man's belief go up and go down like the Rueda. And now here he was on Halve's porch in the weak-tea light with a thing in his voice she had put there.

"We just want to know where she is," Neel said. Reasonable. That was how it started, always, reasonable. "That's all. We got a right to ask where she is."

"You've a right to ask me the price of coal oil," Halve said. "Miz Pike's not on my books."

"She's in your back."

"There's a good deal in my back. It's a store."

A silence. Then a younger voice, one she did not know, one of the pilgrim two hundred, the flux-thinned reedy voice of a man who had walked a hundred miles to be near a mercy and found salt: "My girl drank that water. Off the white flat. She's laying in the tent right now and she can't keep water down that isn't salt and there isn't any water that isn't salt, and *she said don't drink it.*" His voice climbed. "She *said don't drink it,* which means she *knew,* which means she could've—" And there it was, the turn, the *you owe me,* the exact place where a want sours. "She could've kept us from it and she stood there and let it be a lesson. That's what he says. The rain man. He says she keeps a whip on this county and calls it a blessing."

"Boy," said Halve, "your girl is sick because there's no water, and there's no water because it hasn't rained in forty-one weeks, and it hasn't rained because—" He stopped. Even Halve, ledger-hearted Halve, had no end to that sentence a thirsty man would hear.

Because it hadn't rained. That was the whole of the theology of Cheyne County in five words, and it had no comfort in it and no author a man could reach, and so the county did what the county always did with a judgment it could not read: it looked for a face to hang it on. She had been the face they hung the mercy on. It was the same nail.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Four on the porch. One at the rail, hands loose, weight even — that one only came to see. Two behind, weight on the balls, one already looked twice to the alley mouth — those two are the fire if it's lit. The reedy one at the step: hands shaking, but not from anger — hunger, flux, grief with no floor under it. He is not dangerous tonight. He is the kindling; he burns first and lights the others; tomorrow he is dangerous.
> No lamps struck in the tents yet — coal oil short, so no wives watching from lit doors to shame them back. Wind out of the SW, hot, off the flats, carries the salt-cedar taint and the dead-stock sweet — a wind that thins a man's patience the way it thins the water. Dogs quiet. A crowd's dogs go quiet before the crowd finds its shape. Boot-sign on the porch: three nesters' brogans, worn through, and one stitched-welt — a rider's boot among the ruined men. Somebody stands them. Somebody always stands them.
> Count them again by the true measure: not four men. Four this dusk. Twelve by Sunday. The whole two hundred by the week the last hole comes up dry, which at eighteen cents a barrel and no rain is a week she can nearly name.

---

She could have gone out to them.

She thought about it with the flat cold clarity she trusted more than any warmth. She could go out onto the porch in the weak-tea light and raise a rod, slow, and make them wait, and say the least that would do the job, and it would very likely still work — the reedy boy would weep and the fire would go out of the two on the balls of their feet, because the tool still worked, the waiting still worked, the voice still held. She had spent forty-one weeks teaching this county to obey that voice. She could reach for it now.

But she had reached for it on the north bench, over Cordy Dobie's body, and used it to say the one true thing she had — *there's no meaning in it* — and it had landed as prophecy and made the meaning larger, and she had felt the last lever go out of her hand. The voice was not hers anymore, not clean; it belonged to the thing they had made, and the thing they had made would take whatever she poured into that voice and turn it to fuel, praise or truth or denial alike. To go out and perform peace tonight was to feed the fire. To go out and confess was to feed it. She had learned on the bench that she could no longer be disbelieved, and a woman who cannot be disbelieved cannot end a thing by speaking.

So she did the other thing, the hard cold thing, the thing that kept her and hers off men's mercy one more night: she waited them out in the dark.

She let Halve be Halve — patient, procedural, wearing them down the way a ledger wears down a debt, no water in his voice for the fire to catch — and she sat in the coal-oil dim with her ruined hand in her lap, working it slow the way she worked it when no one could see, the dead fingers that would not close, and she counted. Not sheep. She counted the way she counted everything. Barrels. Cents. The three deep holes left in the Rueda a man could jump. The weeks between now and the last dry hole, which was the week the porch would not be four men. She counted it down like a fuse, because counting was the last honest work she had left and she was not going to lose it tonight to fear.

The porch talk ran out the way porch talk does when nobody will light it. Neel said, at the end, not loud, "You tell her we come and asked her civil. You tell her that." And that was a threat wearing its Sunday coat, and she heard it plain, and she filed it. *We asked civil.* A man says that when he is telling himself, in advance, that the uncivil thing to come was her doing and not his.

They went. The boots went off the planks, three brogans and the one welt, and out into the tents where no lamps burned.

---

Tobe had not moved from the door. When the last boot faded he turned and looked at her in the last of the coal-oil dim, and his face did the thing it did, which was almost nothing — but under it she read the question, because she had learned to read him the way she read a playa edge.

*Are we going.*

"No," she said. "Not tonight. A woman who runs at dusk gets caught at dawn, and a woman who runs at all tells them they were right." She stood, and her right wrist ached where no wire was, the phantom of it, the map of the thing under her sleeve gone to aching without the copper to justify it. "We wait for light. You go up to the lean-to. Nobody minds a boy."

He did not go. He came the two steps across the room instead and stood in front of her, close, the wild-quiet nearness of a creature that had decided you were the one that would not flinch, and he did a thing he had never done. He reached out with one thin brown hand and touched, once, light, the cloth of her right sleeve — over the wire that was not there, over the fern-scar climbing the ruined arm like a dry creek system, the branching white channels of the three strikes drawn on her body like an aquifer, like a map of water. He touched it the way the pilgrims touched the mule. To get some of the mark. And in his still feral face there was no fear at all, only the terrible thing, the shining thing, the thing she had watched turn its coat over a dead boy on the bench and become faith.

He had seen every seam of the trick. He had watched her wire the dead hand to the copper a hundred times. He knew the wire was the truth and the copper the lie. And he touched the sleeve over the scar as if the lightning were still in it, as if she carried the storm the whole county swore it could smell on her — the ozone that was not there, that had never been there, that lived only in the space between what a frightened boy needed and what his own senses reported, and always, always, the need won.

She looked down at the small hand on her sleeve.

She did not take it off. Neither did she cover it. She let it lie there the length of a breath — costly, private, changing nothing — and then she stepped back out from under it, slow, the way she did everything with that side of her body, and pushed her own sleeve down flat over the wire and the scar with her good hand, out of pure habit, though there were no rods to hide and no crowd to hide them from, only the one witness who already knew and believed anyway.

"Go on," she said. "Up to the lean-to."

He went. At the door he stopped and made, low, with his hand, a small flat motion she had seen him make once before — not the cutting motion of a killing. The other one. He laid his palm flat and pushed it down slow, twice, the gesture he made when he watched her raise the copper over a crowd: *the waiting.* He was telling her to do the thing. He was telling her the tool still worked. He was telling her, in the only language he had, that she had only to go out and raise it and make them wait and it would hold — because he believed it would hold, because he believed *her,* because he was the purest and most dangerous believer she had ever made, the one who saw the wire and the fern-scar and the ruined dead hand and reached for the mark anyway.

Then he was gone up into the dark, and she stood alone in the coal-oil dim in the sour souring town her mercy had built, and she understood, with the cold complete clarity of the third strike arriving, what he had shown her without a word.

They would not stop believing when they came to burn her. That was the thing. She had thought, riding in, that the turning crowd was the end of the belief — that the salt and the dead boy and the dry holes had at last spent the faith down to fear, and that fear would come for her, and be done. She saw now that she was wrong the way she was always wrong about this, one turn behind. The boy's hand on her sleeve was the crowd's hand on the mule. It was the same reach. When they came for her they would come the way the pilgrims came — reaching, believing, wanting some of the mark — only the mark they wanted now was not the water. Old Dobie had told her plain, over his brother's boy, and she had heard it and filed it and not let it all the way in until this minute with a mute child's touch still warm on her ruined arm: *a county that'll make a saint out of a shot boy will want somebody's blood back for it, in the end. Salt draws salt.*

They did not want to stop believing in her. They wanted to believe in her the *other* way now — as the thing to be laid in the ground and called a mercy.

She sat back down in the dark and did not light the lamp, to save the coal oil, the way she had sat in the mouth of her dugout the first night with the twelfth fire going dark out east, forty-one weeks and a whole life ago. She could not run tonight. She could not perform tonight. She could not confess her way out of it, because they would take the confession for humility and love her worse. She had spent forty-one years laying up the wall of fear to keep men off her, stone by stone, and she had laid it too high and too well, and now she was inside it with them, and the wall was theirs.

She worked the dead hand in her lap, slow, in the dark, the fingers that would not close, and out the plank wall's one gap she could see a single lamp finally struck in a distant tent — some wife home at last, some child fevered, some ordinary desperate light. And around it, unlit, the two hundred, waiting; and somewhere among them the little round man in the checked suit, giving away for free of an evening the one thing in the county still worth a man's whole heart; and she smelled it on the hot salt wind coming off the flats, plain as gyp under a shovel, the thing that had no name but the county's oldest name for her, coming: not *chosen* now, and past *cursed.* The word after cursed. The word the county said when it had made a thing holy and needed the holy thing to bleed.

She did not tell herself yet what she would do in the light. You never told yourself yet. You made yourself wait, so that when the knowing came it landed like weather. But she knew the shape of it already, the way she knew a seam of gyp before the shovel struck it, and the shape of it was that the lie would keep her alive one more week, and that she would tell it, because a woman alone does well to be paid and does better to be breathing, and because the only god she had ever served was the arithmetic, and the arithmetic said: *live.*

Out in the dark a second lamp went dark, and did not come back.


---

# CHAPTER 20 · Movement IV — The Siege

### "Persy Gault's Field"

*The Gault quarter, the east belt of Cheyne County — rainless week forty-three.*
*The Rueda a chain of dead pools going the color of steeped tobacco, walkable dry-shod from the ford clear to the county line. Hauled water eighteen cents the barrel where a man could get it hauled at all; the drays running to the boomtown first, the belt last. The nester road east out of Salt Fork loaded axle-deep both mornings running — wagons, hand-carts, one family walking with a milk cow and a mattress and nothing else. And on the Gault stretch, where four months back eleven quarter-sections had made eleven thin fires at dusk, one fire, and one only, and it belonged to a widow who had stayed.*

---

She had not been sent for. That was the new thing, and it sat wrong in her the whole ride out.

For four months men had come to her fence line with their hats in their hands or with silver in their fists, and she had learned to read the coming of them the way she read the coming of weather — who paid, who begged, who meant her harm. Nobody had come for the Gault quarter. She had heard it in town, at Halve's, the way you hear that a well has gone dry three ranches over: Persy Gault had dug where the Salt Preacher pointed and dug again where the Salt Preacher pointed after that, and had planted her last seed corn on the strength of it, and the corn had come up a hand high and cured to straw standing, and the playa Odessa had called for her in the spring had never once held water past a morning, and now the woman was the last soul on a stretch that had emptied around her like a tank going down.

Odessa rode out because a thing that had gone that wrong on her word was a thing she wanted to see with her own eyes before somebody else told her what it meant. That was the reason she gave herself. She was good at giving herself reasons. It was the same skill as reading a man; you fed yourself the one you could stand.

The boy came behind her, half a length back, on the sacked-out horse. She had told him to stay at the dugout and he had saddled up anyway and she had not made an argument of it, because making an argument of a thing that followed you was a way of admitting it was yours.

The road told the story before the quarter did. Two days of loaded wagons had cut it to powder, and in the powder she read the leaving of the whole belt — the deep double ruts of a family that owned a wagon, the narrow single track of a hand-cart pulled by people who did not, and off in the bar-ditch a chair, a good ladder-back chair with a rush seat, set down upright and left, because a thing you cannot carry and cannot burn you set down gently, out of some last politeness to yourself, and walked away from. She counted the abandoned places as she passed them. The Ronsard quarter, dark; the Neel quarter, dark, the dugout door standing open on nothing; the Sikes place, dark, a foreclosure notice nailed to the doorframe going soft in the dust with the bank's ink run to a bruise. She stopped counting at eight because eight was where counting a thing stopped telling you how many and started telling you what it was.

The ninth fire she did not have to count. It stood in the middle of the emptiness like the last tooth in a jaw.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Playa dry, cracked to plates, mud curled up at the edges like old paint.
> No water past a morning here since March; the crack-pattern deep, no
> fresh silt in the bottom — nothing has stood in it. Salt bloom coming up
> white in the low corner where Odessa called it, drawn up by the sun,
> the ground telling on itself. Corn planted in rows on the swale: knee
> high, tasseled, dead standing — cured, not wilted; it made grain and
> the grain came up chaff. Grama gone to straw. Tarbush oiled and dark,
> living on water forty feet down that no shovel reaches. One green thing
> on the whole quarter: the salt cedar in the playa's throat, feathered,
> false, drinking the salt. No dove-line at dusk. The birds have gone.
> Ground reads dead, and read dead in the spring, and was walked and
> called anyhow.

---

Persy Gault was in the field when Odessa rode up, and she was hoeing.

That stopped Odessa at the fence more than a grave would have. There was nothing to hoe. The corn was dead, cured brown clear to the tassel, and there was no weed alive to fight it and no water to make the fighting matter, and the woman was going down the rows anyhow with a hoe worn to a nub, chopping the crust between the dead stalks, opening the ground the way you open ground to take a rain that is not coming. She wore her black dress gone the red-brown of the dust and mended so many times the mending had its own mending, and her sunbonnet was down her back, and her face when she turned it up was burned past a farm woman's burn, into something scoured, but her eyes were steady and they were not the eyes of a woman ruined. That was the part Odessa had ridden out to see and had half not believed until she saw it. The eyes were glad.

"Miz Pike," Persy said, and set the hoe against her shoulder, and came to the fence wiping her hands down her skirt though there was nothing on them but dust. "I told the boy you'd come. I said it Sunday. I said, she'll come before the corn's all down."

"You've no water," Odessa said. It came out flat and she had meant it to.

"No," Persy said, easy, as though Odessa had remarked on the weather. "Not yet."

Not yet. Odessa let it sit. Behind her she heard the boy's horse stop, heard the small creak of him staying up in the saddle, watching, the way he watched everything now, filing it against the day he would know what it was for.

"I called that playa in the spring," Odessa said. "Week twelve. I stood where you're standing and I called shallow water in the low corner and you dug it and it never held."

"You did."

"It never held, Persy."

"It never has held yet." The woman said it the way another woman might say a child was slow to walk — a fact, and no grief in it, a patience so deep it had gone past hope into something Odessa had no word for and did not want one. "The Lord's slow. You know that better than most, being as He was slow with you three times and let you up every time. I figure He's teaching me the waiting." She smiled, and the smile had a tooth gone at the side, and it was the worst thing Odessa had seen on the whole ruined stretch. "You taught me the waiting first. Everybody thinks the trick is the finding. It's the waiting. I watched you do it. You never tell 'em the minute you know. You make 'em wait so the word lands right."

Odessa's right hand tried to close and could not, and the wire was not on it, and she felt the ache come up in the dead wrist anyway, the ache that was not from anything.

---

She got down. She had not meant to get down. Getting down was staying, and staying was owning the thing, and she knew it as she swung her leg over and knew it worse when her boots hit the dust, and she did it anyway, and gave herself a reason on the way down: she would see the playa. A person ought to see the ground they had ruined. That was not tenderness. That was the same cold accounting she trusted more than warmth — you looked at the dead animal you had killed so you would not lie to yourself about the killing later.

The playa was worse in the ground than it had looked from the fence. Persy walked her down to it, talking, and the talk was the terrible part. She talked about the digging as though it had been a season of grace. She and the boy had gone down eleven feet in the low corner where Odessa pointed, hand-dug, and had walled it with brush against the cave, and had found the ground go damp at nine feet — "damp, Miz Pike, wet enough to darken your thumb, I called the boy to feel it" — and then no more, the damp going away as they dug, the way it did, and she said this last without any weight on it, without seeming to hear what it meant, that the damp going away as you dug meant the water was not below you but only the memory of water, moisture wicked up in an old crack from a wet year and no more coming. She had read that ground right in the spring. She had stood here and read it dead and called it anyhow, because the belt had been on the edge of leaving even then and a called well was a reason to stay one more season, and she had judged, coldly, that a reason to stay was worth more to these people than the truth that there was nothing to stay for.

She had judged that four months ago about a stretch of eleven families. Ten of them had left. This one had believed.

"You dug the second place too," Odessa said. The white salt bloom was coming up in the corner where the damp had been. She looked at it and not at the woman.

"The corn ground. Where you swung the rod the second time you came, out on the swale. We put the last of the seed corn in it. Isaiah's seed corn." A pause, small, the only crack in her the whole hour. "My husband. He kept it back three dry years running, wouldn't touch it, said seed corn's a man's last argument with the drought. I planted it where you pointed." She looked out over the dead field with the tasseled stalks standing brown and still in the heat, no wind to move them. "It come up beautiful. You never saw corn come up like it. It made grain and all."

"It made chaff, Persy."

"It made grain first." The steadiness in the eyes did not move. "The grain's in there yet. It's just dry. A rain'd — " and she stopped herself, not out of doubt, Odessa saw, but out of a kind of manners toward the rain, the way you would not speak too plainly of a thing you were sure was coming, for fear of seeming to hurry it.

Odessa stood in the dead field where a woman had buried her husband's last argument on the strength of a lie Odessa had told to keep a stretch of the belt from emptying, and the stretch had emptied anyhow, all but this, and she understood with the flat clear cruelty she trusted that she had ruined this one woman more completely than the drought had ruined the ten who left. The ten who left were poorer and alive and somewhere else. This one had turned every last thing she owned — the corn, the seed, the digging, the dead man's argument, the future itself — into an offering laid at Odessa's copper, and was standing in the ash of it glad, because the belief had given her the one thing the drought could not take, which was a reason, and a reason was worth more to a starving woman than water, and that was the whole horror and Odessa did not let one line of it reach her face.

---

She meant to tell her. That was the thing she would not, afterward, let herself call by any soft name. She stood in the corn with the salt coming up white behind her and she opened her mouth to say the truest thing she had ever tried to say to a living soul — *there is nothing under this ground, Persy, there never was, I read it dead and called it living, the rods are tube off a dead peddler and the wire is baling wire and I filed the ends so they'd catch the light, go while the road's open, get in a wagon and go, there is no water and there was never going to be and I knew it standing here in the spring* — and the stammer took her.

It took her on the first stopped sound, the way it always took her worst under strain and never under the performing voice, and *there* stuck in her throat behind the *th* and would not come, and she stood there with the block sitting in her mouth like a stone, and the widow watched her struggle with it, and did the worst thing anyone had done to her in the whole four months of it.

Persy Gault put out her chapped dust-red hand and laid it on Odessa's dead right forearm, on the sleeve, over the wire that was not there and the fern-scar under the cloth, and she said, gently, the way you gentle a horse that has spooked, "Take your time. You don't have to say it. I know it's hard for you, the words. It's alright. You don't ever have to say the thing straight to me. I always know your meaning."

*I always know your meaning.* And Odessa understood then that there was no true word she could say that this woman would not hear as its holy opposite — that if she stood in the dead corn and swore on her own scarred arm that it was all a fraud, Persy Gault would hear humility, would hear the prophet made low, would fold the confession into the faith the way the belt had folded the poisoned water and the shot boy, and it would come out the other side stronger, and the woman would go on hoeing dead ground into the dark. The truth had nowhere to land. The one thing Odessa Pike could no longer do, standing where her lie had done its cleanest work, was be disbelieved by the person she had ruined.

The block let go. She said, "You should get in a wagon."

That was all of it. That was the whole of what she let out — not the truth, which could not land, but the least thing that might do the job, dressed down so far it had no prophet in it, a plain hard woman telling a plain hard woman to save herself. She heard the failure of it as she said it. It came out sounding like a test.

"And leave it the season it comes?" Persy said, and smiled the gap-toothed smile, forgiving her the smallness of her faith. "No, Miz Pike. You found it. I'll be here to draw it."

---

The boy had got down off the horse.

Odessa turned and he was standing at the edge of the dead corn, small and wire-thin in the white light, and he was looking at the playa, at the low corner where the salt was coming up, and then he looked at Persy, and then he looked at Odessa, and his still face did a thing it had not done before. It softened. Not toward Odessa. Toward the woman. He looked at Persy Gault standing glad in her ruin the way you look at someone who has been given a great and enviable thing, and Odessa saw, in the boy's softened face, that he did not pity the widow. He wanted what she had. He had watched every seam of the trick, had cinched the reins to Odessa's dead wrist the way she cinched the wire, had stood at the White Draw and the fenced spring and seen the water fail and the man die, and he looked at a woman ruined into gladness and he wanted it, and Odessa understood that the belief did not skip the ones who saw the trick. It took them harder. Knowing the wire was there did not cut the copper's light; it only made you love the woman brave enough to file the ends.

She had made two of them now. The widow, who believed and had lost everything. The boy, who had seen everything and believed anyway. And between them, in the dead field, the woman who had made them both and believed nothing, and could not, by any word she owned, un-make what she had lit.

"Get on the horse," she told the boy, and her voice came out rougher than she meant, and he came, but he came slow, walking backward two steps to keep the widow in his sight, the way you keep a warm window in your eye walking out into the cold.

She did one thing before she rode out and she never afterward let herself count it as a kindness, because a kindness was a door and she did not open doors. She took the offering money from her coat — the strangers' dollars from the box at Halve's, the unearned tribute mailed from Pecos and Roswell by people who had never seen her, money she had told herself she would keep for the deed — and she counted out eleven silver dollars, one for each family that had made a fire on this stretch, and she put them under the good ladder-back chair at the edge of the road where the leavers had set it down, wedged in the rush seat where the widow would find them or would not, where they might buy a wagon-seat east or might buy six barrels of eighteen-cent water to pour down a dead playa, and she did not know which and told herself she did not care which, and rode away fast so she would not have to know.

She did not look back to see whether the woman had gone to the chair. She looked back once, at the top of the grade, at the whole emptied stretch — ten dark quarters and one fire not yet lit against the coming dark — and what she saw was Persy Gault, small and black in the white ruin, gone back down the rows with the worn hoe, opening the dead ground stroke by stroke to take a rain that was not coming, waiting the way Odessa had taught her to wait, so that when the water came at last it would land like weather; and the salt cedar in the throat of the dry playa stood over her, green, feathered, false, the one thriving thing on the quarter, drinking the poison forty feet down that no shovel could reach and no widow would believe was there.


---

# CHAPTER 21 · Movement IV — The Siege

### "Find the Water or the County Burns"

*Salt Fork, the Association hall over the bank — first week of November, 1893.*
*Rainless week forty-five. The Rueda dried to plates the length of the county, wet only in three deep holes the cranes stand in and will not leave. Wells county-wide sucking air; the depot tank guarded by three riflemen now, not two. Hauled water twenty cents the barrel and short at that. The fence war one spark from a range war — the Dobie men and the Bar EM sleeping in the brush with rifles, waiting on the other to move first.*

---

They did not send a rider this time. They sent the sheriff.

Ellet came out to her dugout at first light with his paunch over the horn and his badge dull with dust and would not get down, the way none of them ever got down, and told her she was wanted at the hall by nine and that he'd been asked to see her there. He did not say *arrest.* He did not have to. A weak man delivering a summons he is afraid of makes it plainer than a strong one ever could.

"Whose asking," she said, though she knew.

"Association's sitting," Ellet said. "All of it. Cade in the chair." He looked past her at the boy, at Tobe come up out of the lean-to with the sacked-out horse, and something moved in his face that she took for pity and filed as fear. "You'll want to come, Miz Pike. It's got past the point where not coming is a thing you can do."

So she went. She wired the copper to her dead wrist before she rode, out of a habit she no longer examined, though there would be no ground to read in a room full of men, and she pushed the sleeve down over the wire and the fern-scar the way she had pushed it down a thousand times, and she made the boy stay. He stood in the yard and did not argue, because he could not, and watched her go with the held-wing stillness he had learned somewhere before her, and she did not look back at him because looking back was a door.

---

The hall was the second floor over the bank, the one brick building in the county, and it was full.

She had never seen it full. She had seen the Association as men on horses, as a rider with a warning, as Cade alone at her draw with the sun behind him. She had not seen it as a body — thirty men, forty, ranged on benches and standing along the walls, hats in their laps, the room close with the smell of them, sweat and horse and the particular sourness of frightened men who have not washed because there is no water to wash in. Cade sat at a plank table at the head with the ledger-books stacked square in front of him like a wall he had laid up himself. Waybourne sat at his right, too well dressed, his teeth ready. Delp sat farther down with his spectacles and his ink-stained cuffs and his little dry cough, the land office come to watch title get made. And Rourke — Rourke stood at the back against the wall in his rusty black coat with his long hands folded, not of the Association, invited by none of them and there anyway, the way weather is there.

They watched her come up the stairs. The whole room turned its heads to her on the same beat, the way the crowd had learned to turn at the prayer-meeting when Rourke pointed, and she understood in that turning that the thing had come all the way around: she had been hired to make men wait on her, and now the county's whole hard machinery sat in a room waiting on her, and there was no fee in it this time, and no walking away down a draw when the show was done.

"Sit," Cade said.

There was a chair set for her in the open floor before the table, alone, the way a chair is set for a man being tried. She did not sit. She stood behind it with her ruined hand on the back of it and let the silence run, because the waiting was the one instrument still hers, and she out-waited the room until Cade's own men began to shift on the benches.

"You'll have heard," Cade said at last, "that Cordy Dobie is dead."

"I stood over him," she said.

"Then you'll know what it means." He did not raise his voice. Cade never had to. "The Dobie men cut my wire three times. My riders shot the fourth man to try it. There's a girl on the Blanco carrying a dead man's child and there's forty Dobie guns that mean to bury somebody for it, and there's the little cowmen behind them, and there's the free-grass men behind them, and every one of them is out of water. My own stock" — and here he stopped, and she watched him decide how much of the corpse to show — "my own stock is drinking what a boy could dam with his hand. The county has come to the bone, Miz Pike. It has come past the bone. What happens now is a range war, and a range war in a country this dry is not a thing that stops when the killing does. It stops when the last man alive rides out and leaves the buzzards the county."

"And you want me to look for water," she said, the way she had said it to him at the draw in May, and she saw the whole room hear the echo without knowing it was one.

"No," Cade said. And he did not smile this time. "In May I wanted you to look. That was cheap and it bought me a season. That season's spent." He laid his ruined hand flat on the ledger. "I want you to *find* it. Enough of it. Sweet water, and enough of it to water the free-grass stock and the Bar EM stock both, so there is nothing left to kill each other over. You will walk where the whole county can see you walk, and you will find the water that ends this, and you will find it inside the fortnight, before the first frost drives them to move on each other for the winter grass. Or —" He stopped.

"Or," she said.

"Or you will have failed the county in its need," Cade said, "and a county in its need does not forgive a false prophet. You made yourself dear, Miz Pike. I told you that. A dear thing a man owns or destroys. There's no owning you — you've seen to that." A dryness came into it that was nearly admiration and was worse than hatred. "So it comes to the other."

---

She stood very still and did the arithmetic, the only arithmetic she trusted, and for the first time in forty-one years it would not come out.

There was no line on either side of the sum. She could not find water enough in a fortnight to water a county because there was no such water under Cheyne County — she knew that the way she knew her own dead hand, knew it from ten years of walking the worthless bones of it, knew it better than any man in the room who was pretending, out of terror, to hope she could. What lay under the county now was a chain of dying holes and a fenced seep men had died reaching and, west, the white poison of the Draw. There was no more water to find. Cade himself had said it to her in May — *I know what's under this county about as well as any man living; there's no more water to find; not in time* — and she looked at him now across the ledger and saw that he still knew it, and that he was going to make her do it anyway, because a man who is drowning does not care whether the plank is real; he only cares that the crowd stops looking at him and looks at the plank.

"You can't make me find water that isn't there," she said, and the block caught the *there* and turned it to a stone in her mouth, and she saw Delp's head lift at it, cataloging, the way Cade had cataloged the stammer at the draw in May, and she hated it worse in this room than she had ever hated it anywhere.

"No," Cade agreed. "I can't. That's your trouble, not mine."

"P-put it plainer," she said. "You're saying — walk, and lie, and point at nothing, and let them dig a county's grave believing it a well. Or you'll hand me to the Dobie men."

"I'm saying," Cade said, "that I've stopped being able to protect you from a thing I never made. I hired a woman to keep men from shooting. I did not make you the ground the town is built on. You did that, walking. I'm telling you where the walking has brought us, and I'm telling you there is one road out of the room that doesn't end with you in the road with the county standing over you. Find them their water."

---

It was Waybourne who leaned in then, warm, conspiratorial, his teeth out, calling her by a name too soon.

"Odessa," he said — and she had never given him the name — "there's no call for it to sound so hard. Cade makes everything sound like a ledger. What we're asking is only what you've done a dozen times. You found the Slash W caliche when I'd have sworn on my father's grave there was no water in it. You found the Ojo del Muerto. You've a gift, whatever the plain fact of it —" and here he glanced, quick, at her right sleeve, the whole room's glance running with his, hunting the wire and the scar under the cloth, the map of the thing not one of them could own — "and all we're saying is, use it now, when the whole county has its hat off waiting on you."

"You want the water for the Slash W," she said. "Fence it before the Bar EM can, and be the man who saved Cheyne County, and sit in Cade's chair by spring."

Waybourne's smile did not move. "That," he said pleasantly, "is a very hard way to describe a rescue."

"There's no rescue in this room," she said. "There's men deciding whose name owns the water when the woman's used up. I've sat in it before. I know the furniture."

Somewhere on the benches a man laughed, short, and swallowed it. Cade did not so much as glance to see who.

---

Then Rourke spoke from the back, and the room went to him the way a room goes to a preacher, because his was the one voice among them that had learned Odessa's own trade.

"They're asking you the wrong thing," Rourke said. He came off the wall slow, his long hands loose, his voice pitched low so they leaned to catch it — a whisper that carried, the very instrument she used, and she felt the old cold recognition of a man who could do the thing she did and knew he could and knew she knew.

"You gentlemen keep asking her to *find* the water," Rourke said. "As though it were still in her hands. It isn't. It hasn't been since the spring." He turned, and his eyes came to her, and there was in them the thing that made him worse than any hypocrite — the half-belief, the man who could not tell whether he was speaking a con or a creed and had stopped trying. "Miz Pike. You have never once, that I've heard, told this county a thing it did not turn to its own use. You told the Orley crowd there was no water and they called it proof you'd never lie. You stood over the Draw and said *don't drink,* and they drank it as your blessing. You stood over that dead boy and said there was no meaning in it, and there's a hundred people on the Blanco tonight who'll tell you he died for the water you found." He spread the long hands. "You cannot find them water. And you cannot fail to find it either. There is no word left in your mouth the county won't hear as a *yes.* That's not a threat, and it's not the Association's. It's only the plain shape of the thing. You have run out of *no.*"

The room was quiet. Even Cade was quiet, watching the preacher say aloud the thing the ledger had been circling.

"So you needn't decide whether to lie," Rourke said, gently, and it was the gentleness that was terrible. "You've only to decide where you'll stand when they need to see you standing. Come stand where I'll build. Let it be water when there's water and mercy when there's none, and let the finding be the Lord's business and not yours. It's the only room in the county, Miz Pike, where a false word doesn't get somebody dug into the ground."

And she understood that this was the third jaw of the same trap — that Cade would own her finding, and Waybourne would fence it, and Rourke would make a church of her failing, and that all three of them, and the forty men on the benches, and the girl on the Blanco, and Persy Gault standing in her ruined salted field, wanted the identical thing, which was for her to be true, so that they would not have to be alone in front of a sky that had given nothing in forty-five weeks and did not appear to be sorry.

---

She looked at the chair set for her in the open floor. She had not sat in it.

"Give me the fortnight," she said, and made her voice go flat and slow and spaced so the block could not sit in it, the performing voice, the one that left no gap — and heard the room take it as the prophet gathering the word up, and could not stop them taking it so, because Rourke was right, there was no *no* left in her mouth. "I'll walk. You'll pay me in silver, before, whether I find or don't, twenty the walk, the way it was in May. And I'll say this once, in front of the land office and the sheriff and God's own preacher, so nobody can say afterward they weren't told." She let it hang. She made them wait. Forty men leaned in against their will, and it disgusted her, and she used it anyway, because using it was the only thing she had ever known how to do.

"There is no water in this county that ends a range war," she said. "I've known it since May. Mr. Cade has known it longer. Everything I find from here is a hole for somebody to dig his grief into. I'll walk because a walking woman is a woman men are watching and not shooting. But if any man in this room tells you afterward that I promised you a well, he's a liar, and you can nail this to his post."

She watched it land. She watched it land the way everything landed now. Cade's face did not move; the ledger balanced either way. Waybourne's smile held. Delp made a small note. And on the benches a graybeard she did not know took off the hat he had already had off, held it against his chest, and said, low, to the man beside him, in a voice thick with a thing she had spent her whole life trying not to light in people, *"You hear her. She won't even claim it. That's how you know it's God and not her."*

The room breathed it in like water.

---

She rode home in the last light with the copper riding useless against her dead wrist and the fern-scar aching under the sleeve where no wire was, and she counted the fires out east in the belt the way she counted everything, and there were fewer than the month before, and among the fewer, out on the Gault stretch where Persy stood her ruined ground alone, one burned steady and would not go dark, and she knew without being told that it was a lamp Persy had lit and set in the window facing the road, the way you set a light for someone you are certain is coming.

At the crossroads by the dead cottonwood the offering-cross still stood, mesquite lashed with Association wire, a white rag knotted to the arm gone stiff and gray. Fresh at its foot the ground had been broken — a spade's width of it, turned and tamped and turned again, where some believer that day had begun to dig, in the packed caliche under a cross, in the dead middle of a fortnight she had promised to walk, a hole he did not yet have a name for. He had left the spade standing in it, upright, the way a man leaves a tool he means to come back to.

She sat the mule and looked at the standing spade a long time, and thought that a man will start a hole on faith long before he knows whether it is meant to hold water or hold what water is buried instead of. Then she rode on, and did not tell herself yet what she already knew about the fortnight and the hole and the man who would finish it, because you never told yourself yet — you made yourself wait, so that when the knowing came there would be nothing under it but salt.


---

# CHAPTER 22 · Movement IV — The Siege

### "What a False Prophet Owes"

*Cheyne County, across the dying range — rainless week forty-seven.*
*The Rueda a bed of cracked plate the length of the county, wet only in three deep holes where the last cranes stood and would not fly. Hauled water: twenty cents the barrel, and no barrel to be had by noon. And a duster building in the southwest since the day before — worse than the three-day west that had stood over the White Draw in the fall, worse than any the county had raised, a brown wall that took the sun by the middle of the morning and pared it down to a coin you could look straight at, a dull copper coin held up behind smoke, giving no more light to the ground than a lamp gives to a shut room.*

---

She rode out alone before it was full light, which was the only hour left in the county that belonged to no one.

She had told no one she was going. She had cinched the reins to her dead wrist herself in the mule-shed at the edge of Salt Fork, working the leather over the ruined hand slow, the way she did everything with that hand, and she had not let the boy help, though he stood in the shed door watching, and she had ridden out the north road while the pilgrims were still asleep in their wagons on the wagon-yard with their sick children breathing hard in the dark. She left the copper behind. She left it hung on the nail. There was no water she was going to find today that copper could dress up, and no water she was going to find today that anybody would ever see her not find.

Cade had said find water or watch it burn, and the room had said it with him — Waybourne saying it, and Delp saying it in his neat gray paper way, and the crowd outside the hall saying it with their faces the way the crowd said everything now, which was worse than saying it. Find water enough to break the drought, enough to feed the boom and defuse the range war and float the whole county off the bar it had gone aground on. Find it or be the woman who would not. They had left her one door and stood in it.

So she was going to do the only honest thing she had left to do, which was ride the county and look, the way she had looked at ten thousand cutbanks in ten worthless years, and find out, once and for all and for nobody but herself, whether there was one drop of live sweet water anywhere in Cheyne County that a woman could point at and not be lying.

She did not expect there was. But she had never in her life let another man's word stand in for her own read of the ground, and she was not going to start now, on the day it might cost her her neck.

---

The wind was at her back going north and it pushed the mule along faster than she wanted. Behind her the whole southwest quarter of the sky was gone — not clouded, gone, a standing brown nothing with the town's roofline drowning in the bottom of it — and the light that reached the ground came sideways and thin and the color of weak tea, and by the time she made the first draw the sun was the coin, and she could look at it without her eyes watering, which no living person should ever be able to do to the sun.

She got down at the first draw and knelt and read it.

> **ASSAY.**
> First draw, N of town. Bed sand, dry to the arm's length — dug it. No dark
> at the bottom. Mesquite along the rim: bark gray, seed pods light, half the
> crown shed. Tobosa gone to straw, roots lifting from the crust. No sacaton.
> No saltbush leafed. Ant mounds: three, all trenched SW, moving off, chaff
> banked on the downwind rim — leaving, not building. No damp in the ant
> spoil. Ground falls a hand in forty toward the bend; broke the caliche at
> the bend with the heel — dust, white, no cool, gyp faint. Grasshoppers up
> in the tobosa, thick, in a drought that should have starved them — the
> plague-year sign. No bird. Ground said no a long time back and has not
> changed its mind.

She sat back on her heels. She did not need the rest of the morning to know what the first draw had told her, but she was going to give the county the rest of the morning anyway, because if a woman was going to lie to save her life she wanted to be able to say to herself, in the flat clear place where she did her reckoning, that she had first ridden out and made dead sure it was a lie.

She wiped the white dust off her heel on the grass and got back on the mule.

---

By the middle of the day she had read four more, and the duster had come up over her and the light had gone from tea to something with no name, a brown dusk at noon, and the grit came into everything — her teeth, the corners of her eyes, the fold of the reins where they crossed her dead wrist. She tied the flask-scarf over her mouth. She did not drink from the flask. She never drank from the flask. She had a swallow of her own hauled water left in the canteen and she made it last.

The second draw was dry. The third was a pan of cracked plate where a tank had been, and the mud at the deepest crack, when she dug it, was gray and then a red seam and then, a foot down, the gyp — she smelled it before her fingers reached it, the far bitter edge of rotten egg, the county's meanness drawing up last the way it always did, sweetness spent, salt left. She smelled the fourth before she got off the mule and did not get off the mule.

The fifth was the one she had ridden all this way to read, and she had known she was going to read it since before Cade had opened his mouth in the hall, and had not let herself say so.

The fifth was the Rueda's north bench, above the ford, where the old channel swung wide before the river cut down to the deep holes. It was the last ground in the county where a person who knew how to look might, in the third dry year, still hope. She had walked past it a hundred times in ten years and read it in passing the way you read a face across a road, and it had always had a little more green in it than it had any right to, a band of grass a shade darker along the old cut, mesquite along the bench rooted deep and lying — the greenness a lie the whole county had learned to read wrong. She got down and read it right.

> **ASSAY.**
> N bench, above the ford. Old channel wide, filled soft, wash over caliche.
> Mesquite deep-rooted, crown still holding some green — reading down, not up;
> chasing what isn't there. Grass band a shade darker along the cut: forty
> paces, then straw. Broke the bench at the low of the band: soil dark two
> spades, cool — then the caliche cap, hard, unbroken, dry under. Tapped it
> along thirty paces: no hollow, no give, no damp weep at the break of slope.
> Cap not perching now — cap holding a dry roof over a dry room. Seepwillow
> along the cut: dead, gray, brittle to the hand — dead this year, not last.
> No phoebe. No dove-line. One dead crane down in the deep hole below, on the
> plate, wings out, sanded over at the tips. The water that lay here lay old
> and lies lower now than an arm and a shovel will ever reach. Not deep. Gone.

She stood a long time on the bench with the grit walking across it in ropes and the brown roof of the sky coming down to the tops of the dead willows, and she looked at the dark two spades of cool soil over the dry cap, the last of the county's old sweetness held in the top of the ground like the last of the color in a dead man's face.

There was no water. Not here. Not enough. Not in time. Cade had said so on the first day, sitting his grain-fed horse over her seep, and he had been right, and he had hired her to pretend he was wrong, and now the county had gone and believed the pretending harder than it had ever believed the true dry ground under its own feet, and it was going to make her prove the pretending or die for stopping.

She had said, once, ten years and a lifetime ago in a dugout by a coal-oil lamp, that she would let no man's word stand in for her read of the ground. She had ridden all day into the worst duster the county had ever raised to keep that one promise to herself. And the ground had told her what the ground always told her, if you shut up long enough to listen.

It had told her the same thing Cade told her. It had told her she had nothing to sell but the lie.

---

She sat down on the bench with her back to the wind and did the arithmetic.

She did it the way she had always done it, the only arithmetic she trusted, which was: *what keeps me and mine alive, and off other men's mercy, one more year.* She had done it over Cade's silver on the first day and she did it now over her own life, cold, with the grit sifting into the crease of her knees, and she made herself do it honest, because a woman who lied to the county all day had no business lying to herself at night.

Here was the ledger. If she pointed — if she rode back into that brown town and raised the copper slow, and made them wait, and said, low and flat and sure, *there,* and named a corner of dead ground — they would dig. They would dig deep and long on her word, faster than they had ever dug, because the whole county was down to this one hole now. And there would be no water. And a man would put his family's last savings and his last season and maybe his last strength into that hole, and it would come up caliche and then gyp and then a red seam and then nothing, and the crowd would rewrite it, the way the crowd had rewritten every failure she had ever handed it, into a test or a punishment or a withholding, and the faith would come out of the dry hole stronger than it went in, and they would ask her to point again. That was the ledger on pointing. It cost some other man everything and cost her nothing but the price of her own knowing, which she had long ago decided was cheap.

And here was the other side.
