If she did not point — if she stood in the square and said, plain, *there is no water, I never found any, the rods are tube from a dead peddler and the copper is a lie and I have known it every day of the three years you have been on your knees to me* — they would not hear it. She knew that now for certain; she had said the true thing plainly four or five times since the fall and watched it turn, every time, into scripture in the mouth of the crowd, her honesty read as humility, her no read as yes, her silence read as the prophet gathering the word up. The one thing the county would no longer let her do was be disbelieved. And into that unhearing the men would move — Cade to say she had failed him, Melbern to say she was false, the free-grass men to say she had made the water they died for, the betrayed pilgrims to say she had eaten their offerings and given them salt — and somewhere in there a rope, or a rider on a rise with the sun behind him and no scorch on his brass. That was the ledger on not pointing. It cost her, maybe, everything, and it saved nobody, because the digging would go on without her the moment she stopped, on the word of anyone who would stand up and say *there.*

She turned the two sides over in the grit and the brown light and looked for the third column, the one where a woman came out ahead, and there was none, and there had been none since the day she took Cade's silver. The plain sum of the two, cold, was this: she owed the county nothing, and it did not matter that she owed them nothing, because the fire she had been paid to light did not care what she owed.

She did not feel it as grief. She would not let it be grief. She had spent forty-one years learning that grief was a door and doors got you killed, and she was not going to open one now on a dead bench with the sky falling. She felt it the way she felt the gyp coming up in a seep — as a fact about the ground, bitter, drawing up last, when everything sweeter was spent.

There was a thing she let herself think once, briefly, and then pushed down.

The thing was that she had, once, wanted only a deed. A fenced quarter-section with a well in it that no man who had ever pointed at her in the road could put her off of. That had been the whole of her god, and it had been a small clean god, hers, and now she was one, and she had never asked to be, and the difference between the two was the difference between a well and a grave, which the county had lately taught her was no difference at all, only a hole and what you believed was in it.

She got up off the bench. Her right wrist ached where no wire was.

---

The ride back was into the wind, and the wind was full of the county now, dust and the sweet-rot of dead stock and the mineral bitterness of the flats blown clear across from the west where nothing lived, and the mule fought it every step and she let it fight and did not hurry it. The sun was gone. There was no telling the hour. The brown had come all the way down and she rode through it as through the inside of a hide, and the fenceposts came up out of it one at a time and went back into it, and a dead cow stood up sanded to the ribs at the edge of the road and she rode past it.

A wagon came out of the brown ahead of her, going the other way, north, loaded to the bows and canted over on a cracked wheel, a family leaving. The man walked at the heads of his played-out team with a scarf over his face, leading them by the cheek-straps because they would not face into the grit on their own. She would have passed him without a word — she had nothing to spend on a man going the right direction — but the team balked at the mule in the road, and he had to stop them, and he stood a moment with his hand on the near horse's nose, close enough now to see her.

"There's no water north," she said. She did not know why she said it. It was true, and true was cheap, and she had said it. "The Rueda's plate clear to the line. You'll drive them to death on that wheel before you make Portales."

He pulled the scarf down. He was young under the dust, younger than the wagon and the two children she could make out humped under a quilt in the bed made him look, and his eyes went over her the way eyes went over her now, fast, and then went over her again, slower, and stopped, and she watched him arrive at who she was.

"You're her," he said. The scarf hung at his throat. Behind him, in the bed, the quilt moved; a woman's face came up out of it, and then went still, watching. "You're the Salt Preacher."

"I'm a woman on a mule," Odessa said.

"They said you rode out." He said it the way they all said it now, as though the fact of it were a coin he could turn over and read a fortune off the back of. "They said you rode out into this to find it. My wife wanted to stay for it. I told her we were three days past staying." He looked back at the quilt, and then at Odessa, and the thing came into his face that she had learned to dread worse than the fear, because the fear at least kept its distance. It was hope. "Did you find it," he said. "Is there water."

The honest answer sat in her mouth, dug that morning off five dry draws and a dead bench, cold and complete: *No. Not here, not enough, not in time, and I have known it three years.* She looked at the two shapes under the quilt and the cracked wheel and the horses that would not face the wind, and she understood — the way she understood the gyp coming up in a seep — that the true word would not send him north. It would send him back. Hope read her silence as yes the way the crowd read everything as yes, and if she let it, this man would turn his cracked wheel around in the road and haul his children back into the dying county to wait on a hole that was a grave, because she had not been quick enough to lie him out of it.

"No," she said. Flat. Fast. The word she could never make the county hear, she made this one man hear, in the dark, where it cost her nothing and might save the two under the quilt. "There's no water. Go north. Don't turn around for anything anyone tells you."

For a moment she thought he would take it. Then the hope did its work — she watched it happen, watched the *no* turn over in him the way it always turned, into a test, into a hard saying, into the prophet withholding to see who was worthy — and his jaw set, and he pulled the scarf back up over his mouth. "The Lord withholds from them that turn back," he said, from behind the cloth, not unkindly, the way a man corrects a child, and clucked to his team, and led them on past her into the brown, north for now, and she knew he would be southbound again by morning, and there was not one more word in the world that would stop it.

She sat the mule and listened to the cracked wheel knock away behind her until the wind took it.

She thought, riding on, about the man who would dig.

She did not know yet who he would be. She knew there was one — there was always one, the whole machine ran on there being one — a man deep enough on the books and long enough on his knees that when she said *there* he would go home and take up a shovel that night by lantern and not stop. She thought about him without warmth and without pity, because warmth and pity were doors, but she thought about him hard, the way she thought about the ground, because he was part of the ground now, he was a fact of the tract she was being made to walk, and she had never in her life walked a tract without reading everything that was on it.

She read him the way she read the bench. A man who had planted where she pointed and starved. A man who had dug where she pointed and hit gyp and been told by the crowd that the gyp was his own lack of faith. A man who had one hole left in him and would put it wherever she said, deeper and deeper, past the caliche, past the red seam, past the point where a wet-year man would have quit and gone home to his wife, because a dry-year man on his last hole does not have a home to go to that is not the hole. She read the whole of him standing in the dark, and she read the cave of dry caliche closing its dry roof over him, and she did not look away, because looking away was the one thing she had never once let herself do at a cutbank or a grave, and this was going to be both.

She could stop none of it by knowing it. That was the sum of the day's honest work. She had ridden into the worst weather the county had ever made to read the ground true one last time, and the ground had said no, and her true reading of the no was worth exactly nothing, because the county had bought the yes off her three years ago and was going to collect.

She came down off the north road into what had been Salt Fork.

The town was a smear of lamplight low in the brown. They had lit the lamps at the middle of the day and left them lit, a whole town lit against a noon gone dark, and around the church-house lot the pilgrims had built up a fire against the duster and were standing in a ring at it with their faces turned out toward the north road, waiting, the way they always waited now, leaning in, ready to be told. They had heard she rode out. Of course they had heard. There was nothing in the county left to know but her. And they had come and stood in the worst wind of the worst dry year and turned their faces to the dark to watch for her coming back, because if the Salt Preacher rode out into a duster like this one and came back, then it meant something, it had to mean something, everything meant something now, and the one thing they could not bear was that it might mean nothing at all.

She reined the mule at the edge of the lamplight where they could not yet see her, and she sat in the dark a moment with the grit going across her and the ring of upturned faces bright and hungry at the fire, and she made them wait. She did not know she was doing it. It was the oldest thing in her, older than the copper, older than the strikes; she had made them wait so long that the waiting made itself now, without her leave. She could feel it come off them across the dark — that pull, that leaning-in, that terrible readiness of frightened people to be told — and she understood, sitting there unseen, that she had nothing left to tell them that was true and nothing left to tell them that was safe, and that she was going to have to choose, and that whichever she chose, the digging would begin.

At the fire, a man she could not yet make out stood up out of the ring, a big slow shape against the flame with a shovel already in his hand, and turned his face to the dark to find her.

---


---

# CHAPTER 23 · Movement IV — The Siege

### "The Grave That Was a Well"

*The Sikes quarter, on the east belt of Cheyne County — rainless week forty-nine.*
*The Rueda a bed of cracked plate the whole length of the county, wet nowhere a man could reach, the cranes gone off it at last to somewhere no one knew. Hauled water twenty-two cents the barrel where a man could get it hauled, which on the belt he could not. Dead stock past all counting. And on Ammon Sikes's dry quarter, in the third week of a hole he was calling a well, the belt had gathered to watch a man go down into the ground after the water Odessa Pike had not said was there.*

---

They had not sent for her. That was the first thing she understood, coming up the belt track in the white noon with Tobe half a length back on the sacked-out horse: no rider had come to her fence, no note had been left weighted with a stone, no boy had come at a lope. She had come because Kyle had come to her, ink to the cuffs, a half-step behind it as always, and had said the one thing that could move her off her own ground in the forty-ninth week of a drought, which was a number.

"Thirty-one feet," Kyle had said. "Sikes is down thirty-one feet by the belt's count, and he went past caliche at nineteen, and he is still going, and half the belt is out there with him. They've been three weeks at it. He told them you'd find him water. He's telling them you did."

"I never walked his ground," she had said.

"I know it," Kyle had said. "That's why I come."

So she rode. Not for Sikes. She did the arithmetic on the ride, the only arithmetic she trusted, and it came out the way it always came out: a man dead in a hole dug on her word was a thing the county would lay at her feet whether she had said the word or not, and it was better to stand on the ground and be seen to have tried than to be the woman who stayed home while the belt buried a father. She rode to be seen. She told herself that all the way up the track and did not once believe the whole of it.

---

The quarter was as dead as she remembered it. She had read it dry in the nineteenth week, standing where the wire ran, and she had told Ammon Sikes then to dig wide and shallow and braced and in daylight, so that whatever hole he dug would not close on him — and he had heard the warning as a blessing, had taken his hat off to it, and she had ridden home knowing she had shaped a man's ruin and could shape it no further.

He had not dug wide. She saw that before she was off the mule.

The spoil-mound stood higher than a man, gray broken caliche gone white in the sun, and the mouth of the hole in the middle of it was narrow — a bare shoulder's width, the way a poor man digs when he has run out of everything but the digging, because a narrow hole is less to lift and a narrow hole goes down faster and a man three weeks into faith does not want wide, he wants deep. A windlass of green cottonwood poles stood over it, rigged from the last of somebody's fence, and a bucket-rope went down into the dark of it and did not come back up while she watched.

The belt stood around the mound. She counted them the way she counted everything, without turning her head — thirty, thirty-five, more than she had thought Kyle's number would draw, women among them now, and children, and a wagon or two, and a fire kept going off to the side though there was nothing on it to cook. They had come to wait. She knew the wait. She had built it a hundred times with a copper rod and a slow hand, and here it was built without her, standing around a hole in the ground with its hats in its hands.

Ada Sikes stood at the head of the mound with the baby on her hip and the two other children pressed to her skirt, and her face when she saw Odessa did the thing that was worst of all, worse than blame, worse than the guns at the fenced spring: it opened. It lit. It looked at Odessa the way a dry-land woman in the forty-ninth week looks at the one thing she has left to believe, and Odessa felt it land on her like a weight laid across the shoulders, and she did not let it reach her face.

"He said you'd come," Ada said. "He said if it come slow you'd come and stand over it and it'd come faster." She shifted the baby. The baby did not move right; it lay against her too still, thin at the ankle and swollen at the belly, the flux-look Odessa had learned to read on the pilgrims in town. "He's down there, Miz Pike. He won't come up. He says he can smell it."

"Smell what," Odessa said, though she knew, and the knowing was already turning cold in her.

"The water," Ada said, and smiled, and it was a terrible smile because it was a happy one. "He come up last night and his hands were shaking and he said he could smell it. He said it was close."

---

Odessa walked to the mouth of the hole. The belt drew back from her the way it always drew back now, a little ring opening, and she stood at the lip and looked down into the dark of a shaft dug narrow and deep by a man who could smell water, and she smelled it too. She had smelled it from the mule. She had been smelling it since she came off the track and had made herself not name it because naming it changed nothing and would only start the thing sooner.

> **ASSAY.**
> Shaft mouth eighteen inches short of two feet across. Down thirty-plus by the rope-marks knotted on the windlass, one knot the fathom. No cribbing below the caliche — bare dirt walls, dry, but the color wrong, going dark the last two fathoms, a wet-dark, a seep-dark, not a water-dark. Air off the mouth cool. Air off the mouth carries it: sulfur, faint, the rotten-egg far-edge, and under it the bitter. Gyp. White bloom starting on the spoil at the mound's foot where the deep dirt was thrown, drying white as the caliche was not white, a different white, a salt white. Ants gone off the mound entire. No fly on the wet dirt. He is not smelling water. He is smelling the seam.

She straightened from the lip. The smell of it was in her mouth now, the bitter behind the teeth, the same bitter that had come up out of her own seep the morning Cade rode into her draw a lifetime and forty weeks ago, the far edge of poison, the county drawing up its meanness last when everything sweeter was spent. Ronsard had struck it at nineteen feet through the caliche and had wept for joy thinking it was water. Sikes had gone past nineteen and down another dozen into it, narrow and unbraced, chasing the smell that was not water but the ground beginning to sweat its salt.

There was no water down there. There was gyp seeping into a hole too narrow to work in and too deep to climb out of fast, dug in dry caliche country by a man who had not braced it because faith does not brace, and the walls of it were going dark the way walls go dark just before they remember they are only dirt.

She knew all of that in the time it took to straighten up. She knew it the way she knew a man was lying before he finished the sentence. And she knew, straightening, that every soul on the mound was watching her know it, and waiting.

---

"Ammon," she called down. Flat. Slow, so the block found no gap. "Come up."

The rope went taut on the windlass. Somewhere below, in the dark, a man laughed — a cracked, joyful, drunk-on-nothing laugh, the laugh of a man three weeks from bread who has decided God is close.

"Miz Pike," his voice came up, small and hollowed by the shaft. "You come. I told 'em you'd come. It's close, Miz Pike. I can smell it. Come down and tell 'em how close."

"Come up," she said.

"Just a foot more," he called. "Two. It's *sweating*, Miz Pike. The wall's sweating. Tell 'em."

The wall was sweating. That was the whole of it, that was the whole horror of it: the wall was sweating gyp, salt water weeping out of the seam the way it wept out of the flats, and to a man who had prayed for water for forty-nine weeks a sweating wall was the answer to prayer, and there was no telling him it was the poison because the poison and the deliverance looked exactly the same coming out of the dirt, and always would, in this country, to the desperate. She had known that since the White Draw. She had watched eighty people drink the White Draw as mercy. She had watched Persy Gault fill six barrels of it and haul it home.

She stood at the lip and did the arithmetic.

If she said *there is no water*, they would not hear *there is no water*. She had learned that on the north bench over Cordy Dobie's body. She had said the truest thing she owned in the performing voice and they had heard humility, heard a holy woman waving off a miracle too great to claim, and it had made the death larger. Ada Sikes was looking at her now with her lit-up face and her too-still baby, and thirty-five belt people were looking at her, and if Odessa said *come up, there is no water, the wall is sweating salt and the hole will kill him*, they would hear the voice and not the words, they would hear the prophet gathering the word up, and Ammon Sikes down in the dark would hear it as *close*, and he would dig the last foot.

If she said nothing, they would hear the nothing as a yes.

She had learned that too. She had learned it at the White Draw and she had learned it at the fenced spring: her silence was a pronouncement now. The county had taken even her silence from her and made it speech, and it was a yes, always a yes, because a frightened man hears in a silence the thing he needs to hear, and what Ammon Sikes needed to hear was that the water was close.

There was no word she could say. There was no word she could not say. She had come up the belt track with a mouth full of things and found, at the lip of the hole, that the county had emptied her of all of them years ago and she had only just now reached the bottom of the sack.

---

"He's near it," said a man at her shoulder — a belt man, Neel it might have been in another season, but it was not Neel, it was a younger one, hat off, eyes wet and shining with the thing she had watched turn its coat over a dead boy on the bench. "Ain't he. You come because he's near it. You don't come for nothing." He said it to the crowd as much as to her. "She don't come for nothing."

"She come to bless the hole," a woman said.

"She smelled it from the track," said another. "Look at her. She's smelling it now."

She was smelling it now. She stood at the lip with her nostrils full of gyp and her face gone still as caliche, and she watched them read her stillness, watched them read her arithmetic, watched them take the exact shape of her horror and hang it on the wall of the church they had built without her and call it grace. She had made her face a wall her whole life because a wall could not be argued with, and here at last was the wall's wage: they read the wall as scripture.

She opened her mouth to say *come up*. She got as far as the sound.

"C—" The block caught it. It caught it hard, harder than it had caught anything in a year, because the strain was on her the way it had not been on her since before she learned to perform, and the block sat on the word and turned it to a stone in her mouth, and she stood at the lip of a dying man's grave and could not say the one word that might, said in the plain flat voice of a woman and not a prophet, have been heard as a woman's warning.

The belt watched the block. She saw them watch it. She saw the young man's wet eyes go wide and full, saw him take the stammer — the stammer she had spent a lifetime hating in front of men who filed things — and file it as the prophet struck dumb before the water, the tongue of the woman the lightning would not kill stopped up at the very lip of the well by the size of what was coming.

"She can't hardly speak of it," he breathed.

And down in the dark, forty weeks and thirty-odd feet from the sky, Ammon Sikes heard the crowd go quiet the way a crowd goes quiet when the word is coming, and he did the thing she had built into a county with a copper rod and a slow hand and could no longer stop with any word or any silence: he leaned into the wanting, and he dug.

---

She heard the pick go in below. She heard it strike, and strike, the wet-heavy sound of it in salt-soaked dirt, and she heard him laughing while he swung it, that cracked joyful laugh, and she heard — everyone heard, the whole mound heard, and the young man's face broke open with it — she heard, under the laughing and the pick, the wall begin to talk.

It is not a loud sound. She would think about that after, on the ride, in the years: how small a sound it is, the sound of dry-country dirt letting go, a sifting, a whisper, a thing you would take for wind if you did not know it, a thing that sounds like the ground clearing its throat. She had heard it before at cutbanks. She knew it the way she knew the smell of gyp.

"Ammon," she said. It came out whole this time, the block gone, the performing voice arriving too late and for nothing. "Up. *Now.* Up the rope. Now."

He heard the voice. He heard the voice and not the word, and what a man hears in that voice when he is standing at the bottom of his faith with a pick in his hands and the wall sweating salt is not *danger*, it is *arrival* — it is the prophet crying out because the water is here — and he did not go for the rope. He went for the wall. She heard him drop the pick and heard his hands go into the sweating dirt, heard him call up her name and God's in the same breath, joyful, *it's here, it's here, Miz Pike, I got it* — and then the wall cleared its throat and answered him, and the small sifting sound became a heavier one, a settling, a weight of dry caliche and salt-wet dirt remembering all at once that it was only dirt, and the narrow unbraced walls of a thirty-three-foot hole came in on Ammon Sikes the way water comes, from every side at once and downward, and filled the space where a man had been standing with the ground he had dug out of it, and the laughing stopped, and the pick-sound stopped, and there was no cry, there was not even time for a cry, there was only the sifting settling exhaling sound of a hole becoming solid again and then, after it, the enormous silence of the belt.

The bucket-rope on the windlass hung slack. It had been taut. Now it hung slack, going down into a mouth of settled dirt eight feet below the lip, and it swayed a little, and stopped.

---

For a long moment nobody moved. The white noon lay on all of them without a shadow. Somewhere the kept fire ticked. The baby on Ada's hip made a small sound and Ada did not seem to hear it.

Then the mound came apart into men — belt men throwing themselves at the settled dirt, digging with their hands, with the pick-that-was-left, with a bucket, hauling out the caliche they had watched a man haul out for three weeks, faster than any man could dig it, screaming his name down into the fill as if the fill could hear, and Ada Sikes standing at the head of it with the baby and the two children not screaming, not weeping, only standing, only watching the hole where her husband had been with the same lit-up look on her face, because the look had not had time yet to learn what the hole had done.

Odessa did not dig. She stood at the lip a moment longer and looked down at the slack rope and the settled dark, and she did the last of the arithmetic, and it came out the way she had known it would come out from the mule: he was thirty-three feet down under the weight of everything he had dug, and there were no water down there to have drowned him and no air to have kept him, and the fastest men in the county with the sharpest shovels would reach him tomorrow, or the day after, and would bring up a shape, and it would be his, and it would already be starting to go the way the flat took things.

She stepped back from the lip and let the men have it. She had learned young that where frightened people were tearing at the ground with their hands there was no place for a still woman, that stillness in the middle of a frenzy is a thing they will remember and hold against you, and she went and stood by the mule, and Tobe came and stood at her shoulder holding both animals, watching the crowd the way he always watched the crowd instead of the show, and she did not look at him because she did not want to see whether his face had learned the thing yet either.

---

She did not go until near dark, because to go before dark would be to be the woman who left the digging. She stood by the mule in the white noon going to copper going to red and she watched them not reach him. She watched Ada's face learn it at last, watched it happen the way she had watched the young man's face turn its coat on the bench — a slow arriving, a shape coming up out of the ground of the woman that was worse than the shape they were digging for — and she watched the lit-up look go out of Ada Sikes the way the twelfth fire had gone dark on the horizon her first night, all at once, and not come back.

And she waited for the other thing. She knew it was coming. She had smelled it coming the way she smelled the rain that never came, since the bench, since Cordy Dobie: the county needing the death to mean something, because the alternative to it meaning something was that a father had suffocated under his own faith in a dry hole over nothing at all, and no belt in the forty-ninth week could stand a nothing that size.

It came at dusk, from the young man with the wet eyes, standing at the settled mound with his hat still in his two hands.

"He was reaching for it when it took him," the young man said, low, to the men digging, to the dark, to himself. "She said *up* and he went for the wall because he had it. He *had* it. He put his hand on the water she found and the ground took him for it." His voice climbed and did not break. "She come. She never comes for nothing. She smelled it from the track and she come and she couldn't hardly speak for the size of it, and he died with his hand on it. There's a *meaning* in that. There's got to be a meaning in a thing like—"

"There's no meaning in it," Odessa said.

She said it flat and hard and without a stammer, in the performing voice, the weighted slow voice that left no gap for the block, the voice that landed like weather — and it was the ruin of it, again, still, the same ruin as the bench, because she had taught this county for a year that the voice was the truth of the ground, and now the young man heard the voice and not the words, heard a holy woman refusing the credit of a death too great to claim, and it made Ammon Sikes larger, and it made the hole holy, and it made her, standing by her mule in the red dusk with the smell of gyp still in her mouth, larger than she had ever been and less able than she had ever been to stop any of it.

She got on the mule. Her right wrist ached where the wire wasn't. Ada Sikes stood at the head of the settled mound in the last light with the too-still baby on her hip, not looking at the hole now, looking at Odessa, and her face was not lit anymore and it was not blaming her either; it was doing a third thing, a thing Odessa had not been ready for, which was hoping — hoping still, hoping at her, because if the woman the lightning would not kill had come and stood over the hole and been struck dumb at the lip of it, then Ammon had not died for nothing, and the baby on her hip might yet, and the water might yet, and God had left the sign among them for a reason. She was going to bury a husband tomorrow or the day after and haul what came up out of the ground to the churchyard, and she was going to go on believing, and the belief was the only thing the drought had not taken from her, and she was going to hold it the harder for the hole.

Odessa turned the mule east into the dark, and behind her the belt lit torches and went on digging toward a man they would not reach, and out past them the small orange stars of the belt's fires stood on the edge of the world, fewer than last month, fewer than the month before, and every one of them was waiting on her, and there was no longer any word she could say — not the truth in the plain voice, not the truth in the prophet's voice, not the silence, not the stammer that had stopped her at the lip — that would not, coming out of her, be heard as the water was close.

The wall had talked. She had heard it and named it and said *up*, and it had killed him anyway, on her word, and it would have killed him on her silence, and behind her, in the torchlight, the belt went on digging toward the thing it had made her mean — a woman the lightning would not kill, standing at the lip of a hole and struck dumb — and it had not once, in three weeks of digging, needed her to be there to do it.


---

# CHAPTER 24 · Movement IV — The Siege

### "The Turning"

*Salt Fork, the county seat — rainless week fifty-one.*
*The Rueda a bed of cracked plate from the ford clear to the county line, no wet in it anywhere now, not even the deep holes; the cranes gone, no man knew where, gone the way the water went, without a sound. Hauled water twenty-two cents the barrel and the dray owner shooting over it. The boom gone bust in a fortnight the way a boom does — the tents thinning, the airtight-seller's wagon gone in the night, and the pilgrims who had come to be near her still coming, only now they came the other way, come to be near a thing they had begun to want to hurt.*

---

She did not go to town for the burying. She went to town for her silver, which was a different errand, and she made a point of telling herself so as she rode in, because a woman who told herself the true reason for a thing kept the true reason where she could watch it.

The Ronsard boy was three days in the ground on his father's ruined quarter — the boy of nine or ten who had hauled the spoil, who had climbed down into the well his father dug on her word and been standing in the bottom of it passing up buckets when the caliche shelf let go above him, and there was no more true thing to say about it than that. His father had dug a well believing it a well and it had turned out to be his son's grave, and the county could not leave it at that, because the county could not leave anything at that anymore.

She rode in past the church-fence where the child-graves stood, more of them now than she had counted in the fall, small and unmarked and dug close, and she counted them out of the old habit and stopped at eleven the way she always stopped now, because eleven was where the counting quit meaning a number.

Halve was on his porch. He did not come down the steps. That was the first thing she read, and she read it clean before she was off the mule — Halve, who had kept her offering-box separate from Cade's silver with his own hands and had told her twice to leave the county for good, standing at the top of his own steps and not coming down, with his apron on and his arms folded over the ledger of his heart, looking at her the way a careful man looks at weather coming that he cannot get his stock in from.

"You oughtn't have come in," he said.

"You've got money of mine."

"I have got money of yours." He did not move to get it. "There was near forty men on this porch last night, Miz Pike, and it was not to buy coal oil."

---

Tobe took the mule. He did it without being told, the way he did everything now, and he took it around back to the lean-to where Halve let him keep the sacked-out horse, and she watched him go and read the town off his back while he went — read it the way she had learned to read it off him, because the boy read a crowd better than any grown soul in the county and did not have a word to lie to her with.

His back was not right. He carried his shoulders high and drawn, the way she had taught him to sit into a crowd's wanting, only there was no wanting in the town this morning that a boy would want to sit into. He went quick and low and close to the buildings. He had read it before she got down.

She went up onto the porch. Halve stood aside from his own door and did not follow her in, and that was the second thing, and she filed it with the first.

The store was dark and close and smelled of side-meat and coal oil and the ledger-dust it always smelled of, and it was empty of everyone but the two of them and, at the back by the barrels, sitting on a nail-keg with his good black coat rusty at the seams and his long hands folded on his knee like a man in a pew, Absalom Rourke.

"Miz Pike," Rourke said. He did not get up. He had a way of not getting up that was its own kind of pulpit. "I hoped you'd come for your money. I told Halve you would. A woman who wants a deed as bad as you want one does not leave her silver in a town because the town has turned. That's a want I can trust." He smiled, the road-burned generous smile that slid so easy between comfort and threat that you could not find the seam. "It's about the only thing in this county I can trust this morning."

---

"Say what you came to say," she said. She did not sit. She stood in the middle of the dark store with her back to no one, the way she stood in a field of armed men, and Halve stayed out on his porch and let the preacher have her, and she read the meaning of that plain — that Halve had gone outside so as not to be a witness to whatever this was.

"I came to save your life," Rourke said. "Or your name. I don't yet know which one's still worth the saving, and I'll be honest with you, because you're the one soul in Cheyne County I've never once been able to lie to and see it take. The name is worth more. You know that. You've always known that better than I did, and hated it worse."

He unfolded his hands and laid them flat on his knees.

"There's a man in the ground out on the Ronsard place," he said, "a boy, that dug his own grave and called it a well, on your word."

"He didn't have my word." Flat. Slow. "I never walked that ground. I never raised a rod on it. Ronsard dug where he dug because—"

"Because you found his neighbor water in the wet corner in the spring," Rourke said, gently, "and because the belt says the Salt Preacher blessed the whole low draw when she walked it, though she never walked it, and because a man whose girl is buried on that quarter and whose son is starving on it will hear a blessing in a thing you never said, the way a dry man hears water in the wind." He let that sit. "You know all this. You knew it before I said it. You've known since the fenced spring that you don't have to say a word anymore for the word to be said. Your silence has a voice now, Miz Pike, and it says whatever the man listening needs it to say. That boy's father heard *dig here* in a walk you never made, and he dug, and the caliche came down on his son, and now there is a father out there who has got to choose, this week, between two things, and the whole county is choosing along with him, because it is their boy too now, it is everybody's boy."

"Between what two things," she said, though she knew.

"Between *she killed my son*," Rourke said, "and *my son died for the water she'll yet find.*" He spread his long hands. "Martyr, or murderer. There is no third thing. There was never going to be a third thing. The county cannot bear that a child died in a hole over nothing, so it will make the hole mean something, and it can only mean one of two things, and both of them are about you."

---

She stood in the dark and did the arithmetic. She was always doing the arithmetic; it was the last honest work she did, and she did it now cold and fast the way she did it over a bench of guns. She had thought — she saw now she had thought, and been a fool for thinking — that when the finds stopped and the dry holes came and the salt water killed the free-grass stock and a boy went down in a well, the belief would break under the weight of it, the way a rope breaks, and set her free, and she would take her silver and her mule and the deed she did not have and get clear.

She had been wrong about that the way she was wrong about weather she wanted.

"You want the martyr," she said.

"I want the water," Rourke said, and for one flat second the smile was gone and she saw the thing under it, the thing that made him the most dangerous man in the county — not that he lied, but that he did not know if he was lying. "I have wanted the water since the day I heard your name in Pecos before I ever crossed the line. I am fifty years old and I have preached to empty country for thirty of them and passed a hat that came back with buttons in it, and I have never once, not one time, had the smallest reason to think the Lord was listening. And then there was you. A woman the lightning wouldn't kill, walking dead ground and pulling water up out of it. I do not know if you are real. I have never been able to make myself know it either way, and that not-knowing is the truest thing I own." His voice dropped to the whisper that carried. "But I will tell you what I do know, Miz Pike, because you're owed it. A prophet is a woman who has to keep finding water, and she can't, because the water's gone. A martyr never has to find another drop. A martyr is done. A martyr is *safe*. You cannot fail the county from inside the ground."

---

She had come for her silver. She kept that in front of her.

"You're telling me to run," she said.

"I'm telling you the town met on this porch last night," Rourke said, "forty men, and the Ronsard father was not among them, which is a mercy, because if the father had been among them there'd be no meeting needed. But Melbern was among them."

"Melbern's gone. Melbern left on the noon train in the fall."

"Melbern came back," Rourke said, "the way a fly comes back. He came back the day the boom started to bust, because a bust is the best market there is for a man selling the notion that somebody did it to you. He has been three weeks in the wagon-yard telling anybody who'll stand still that the Salt Preacher salted the White Draw on purpose, that she pointed a boy's father at killing ground on purpose, that she has been Cade's paid witch from the first walk, salting the little men's water and blessing the big men's, and that the drought will not break while a false prophet walks the county under God's own weather." He said it evenly, the whole slander, the way you would read a man his charges. "Half of it's a lie. The other half's near enough true that the lie rides it easy. And the men on this porch last night were not the ones who never believed you. Melbern's slander does not take with the ones who never believed. It takes with the ones who believed hardest and got nothing for it. It is the *faithful* who are going to want your blood, Miz Pike. It is always the faithful. A man does not burn a church he never prayed in."

---

The door stood open behind her onto the white glare of the square, and through it she could hear the town now that she was listening, which she had not let herself do coming in. Not a mob. Not yet. A mob has a sound and this was not it. This was the sound before it — a gathering quiet, the quiet of men standing in twos and threes on the courthouse side of the square who were not doing their errands, men who ought to have been anywhere in a working town and were instead standing, and watching a store, and waiting.

She knew that quiet. She had stood in the middle of it three times with her hair smoking and her hand gone dead, laid out on the ground in a ring of faces waiting to see which she would be. She had felt this exact quiet lean in over her while she lay there and could not rise, the terrible patient readiness of frightened people waiting to be told what a thing meant. She had spent her whole life since learning to make that quiet on purpose with a copper rod and a withheld word, learning to own it, learning to be the one who told them, so that never again would she be the thing on the ground they stood over and waited on.

And here it was again. She had made it into a wall and lived behind the wall for a year, and now she stood in the middle of it and it was pointed at her the way it had been pointed at her the first time, when she was a girl on the ground with the storm still in her teeth, and she understood, cold and complete the way she understood a seam of gyp before the shovel struck it, that the wall and the ground were the same quiet, had always been the same quiet, and that she had never gotten off the ground at all. She had only learned to stand on it and charge admission.

Her right wrist ached where no wire was. She had reached for it out of habit, in the dark of the store, and found her sleeve empty and left it empty, and pushed the sleeve down anyway over the wire that was not there and the fern-scar that was, the map of the thing she could not own and could not put down.

---

"There's a woman among them who'll come to you," Rourke said, quieter now, watching her face. "Persy Gault. She's on that porch too, in her way — not last night, but she'll come to you today, before the men do. She's been telling the belt the boy died for the water you'll yet find. She's been saying it the way she says the Lord and your name in the one breath. She believes it harder than Wick, harder than any of them, because she's got nothing else left to hold, she planted where you pointed and dug where you said and salted her own field with hope and buried it all, and if the boy died for nothing then everything she has done for a year died for nothing, and she cannot carry that, so she will make him a martyr with her own two hands, and she will make you a saint over his grave, and she will do it out of love, Miz Pike, and it will get you killed faster than Melbern's hate."

She said nothing. She had known Persy would come. She had known it since the White Draw, when Persy had filled her six barrels of poison and drunk the first dipper and called it late mercy, and Odessa had let the true warning die in her throat because there had been no telling her anything, because a woman who has decided a thing is her deliverance does not thank you for the salt in it.

Outside, the gathering quiet had grown a degree deeper, the way a thing gets quiet just before it moves. She marked it without turning her head. She marked, too, the empty ache in her right wrist, and the little sack of silver Halve had not yet brought her, and the door standing open on the white square, and she did the sum of them the way she did a bench of guns — distances, exits, the one door at her back, how many steps to the mule.

"The saint and the mob want the same thing from you, Miz Pike," Rourke said, watching her count it. "They want you gone. Persy wants your blood in the ground so the meaning will hold; Melbern's men want it there so the shame will stop. They will meet on the same night over the same rope, and half of them will be weeping when they do it. That is the only forgiveness a county has left when the water's gone."

---

He got up then, at last, unfolding to his full spare height in the dark store, and he took his hat from the barrel-top and turned it in his long hands, and he looked at her with the not-knowing plain on his face, the engine of him showing.

"I can carry it after you," he said. It was the softest he had said anything, and it was the coldest. "That's my offer. It's the only honest one you'll get in this county, so hear it plain. You cannot stay a prophet — the water's gone and the county's turned and there's a boy in the ground. But the name doesn't have to die with the woman. I'll build the church-house I've been building. I'll keep the Salt Preacher. I'll keep the walks, the waiting, the copper — I've watched you long enough to keep them, and the boy's watched you longer and better than I have. And I'll keep *you*, the true you, the way a thing is kept holiest — gone. Away from all this. A woman nobody can put a hole in or fail to make it rain. The name lives, purer than it ever was with you standing in it, and the woman"— he lifted the rusty hat toward the door, toward the white glare, toward the road out — "the woman gets clear, with her silver, before tonight, and lets me have the rest."

She looked at him a long moment.

"You'd rather have me dead," she said. Flat. "You just said it. A martyr's worth more than a prophet. You don't want me clear. You want me a story with no woman in it to trouble the story. You'd sooner they hanged me tonight than that I rode out and turned up alive in El Paso in a year to spoil the tale."

Rourke did not deny it. That was the worst of him, that he would not stoop to deny it, that his half-belief had no floor she could stand on.

"I'd rather the water came back," he said, "and it isn't going to." He put the hat on. "Everything after that is arithmetic, Miz Pike, and you're the best I ever met at the arithmetic. Do the sum. There is no number in it where you keep both the name and the woman. There never was. You made yourself dear walking, and Cade told you a year ago what happens to a dear thing." He touched the hat brim. "Now the county's going to own you or destroy you, same as he said, and the only choice left to you is which of them does it, and how far off you are when it's done."

---

He went out past her into the white square, and she heard the gathering quiet shift as he came out onto the porch, heard the twos and threes across the square turn to see the preacher come from the store, and heard, under the quiet, the thing begin to move that a man could not stop once it moved, the low first stir of a crowd finding its shape the way grief had found its shape on the bench over Cordy Dobie, hunting a thing to land on. She stood in the dark alone and listened to it choose.

Then she heard Rourke's trained voice go up over the square, filling it the way it filled a tent, warm and generous and terrible, and she heard him not damn her and not save her but do the thing that was worse than either, the thing she had watched him decide to do in the space of turning a hat in his hands: she heard him begin to *grieve* her. Out loud. In front of them. In the pulpit-voice that filled the square and left no man out of it.

"...a woman the Lord marked three times and would not take," Rourke was saying, out in the glare, and the quiet was leaning into it, taking off its hats, "and a boy in the ground who reached for the water she called up, and I say to you the water is not gone, brothers, the water is only *withheld*, and it is withheld for a reason, and the reason is standing among us still, and the Lord in his season will show us what is asked—"

She did not go out to hear the rest of it. She knew the rest of it. She had been hearing the rest of it since she was a girl on the ground with the storm in her teeth. She stood in the dark store where Halve's careful heart had left her, alone with the smell of side-meat and coal oil, and she reached up with her ruined right hand and pushed the sleeve down over the wire that was not there, and out in the white square a preacher was building her grave out of a good word and calling it a church, and the town was taking off its hats to help.

On the counter, where Halve had at last set it down and gone back out to stand on his porch and not be a witness, was a small sack of her silver, tied at the neck, and beside it, laid out plain and deliberate for her to find, one thing more — a length of new baling wire, coiled tight, the kind she wound the copper to her dead wrist with, the kind a man leaves for a woman he can no longer come down the steps to warn: *they know how you're made now. Go.*


---

# CHAPTER 25 · Movement V — The Mercy

### "The Last Withholding"

*Salt Fork, the county seat — rainless week fifty-three.*
*Every dug well in the county sucking air or gone to gyp; the last live water in Cheyne County the depot tank, guarded now by four riflemen who slept in shifts on the platform with the tank between them and the town. Hauled water twenty-five cents the barrel where a man could get it hauled at all, which by week fifty-three was mostly he could not. The Rueda a road of cracked plate you could drive a wagon down the middle of, ford to county line, and the cranes gone off it, and the buzzards gone too now, because a buzzard needs a thing to die and the county had run low even on the dying.*

---

She did not run.

She had the silver in the sack and the coil of new wire beside it and the mule saddled in Halve's lean-to, and the road out went south and west toward the Territory line and El Paso beyond it, open, empty, a man's whole life's-worth of getting-clear laid out plain in the dust. She had counted the hours it would buy her. Three days' hard riding to the line, and a week past that to a town that had never heard the name, and the arithmetic of it was clean as any she had ever run — the safest number on the board was the road.

She sat the mule at the mouth of the lean-to in the gray before light and did not take it.

Not because she had gone soft. She turned that over in the dark to be sure of it, the way she turned every want over to see what was under it, and there was no soft thing under this one. It was the other kind of clear. Rourke had told her the truth in the dark store and she had heard it clean: a live prophet gone to El Paso was a story with a hole in it, and a story with a hole in it grows *worse*, not better — she had seen it in her own life, she was the county's own long proof of it. Vanish, and they would say the Lord took her up. Turn up alive in a saloon in a year, and they would say the Devil had. There was no getting clear of a thing you had planted in other people. You could get your body clear. The name would ride after her the whole way and be waiting at the other end wearing her face.

So there was a thing she had not tried yet. She had tried the yes and the no and the silence and they had all been eaten. She had not, in one year, tried the truth said all the way to the bottom, in the daylight, to the whole of them at once.

She did not think it would work. She had stopped thinking things would work around about the White Draw. But it was the last number she had not spent, and a woman who would not spend her last number had already decided to die owing it.

She stepped down and unsaddled the mule.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Town well by the courthouse: windlass turning free, rope wet only to the last two fathom,
> bucket up with a cupful and sand. Third bucket, dry. Puddle at the trough gone to a rind of
> mud cracked in curled plates, salt bloom white at the edges. Dead sparrow in the trough,
> dry, no ants on it — ants gone off entire, hills abandoned, the crater-mouths drifted shut.
> No fly. No fly anywhere, and it week fifty-three and warm. Tank on the platform: full, high,
> dark under the guard-board, a man's rifle laid across his knees on top of it. The only wet
> in the county a thing four men are sitting on with guns. Ground: no seep-line, no fresh-drawn
> salt, no dove-track to any wet, no reading left in it to take. Nothing to read.

---

She waited for the town to gather because she had spent her life learning how to make a town gather, and it was the one skill left to her that still worked. She did not send word. She did not need to. She rode the mule out into the middle of the courthouse square at full light and got down and stood by the dead trough with her back to the tank, and she took the copper rods out of the saddle scabbard where she carried them, and she wired the right one to her dead wrist with the wire she carried, slow, in the open, where they could all watch her do it.

They came the way she knew they would. First the ones already on the square, the men standing in twos and threes who had been standing there since the Ronsard boy went in the ground, waiting on a thing to happen so they would not have to be the ones who made it. Then the store porches emptied. Then the tents. She stood and let them come and did not raise the rods and did not speak, and the not-speaking pulled them in tighter than any word, the way it always had, drawing the town in close around the quiet, and she stood in the center of it and felt the wall she had built pressed up all around her now on the inside, holding her in.

Halve came out onto his porch and stood at the top of his steps and did not come down. She saw him see the saddled-nothing where the packed mule should have been. She saw him understand she had not run. His careful face did a thing she did not have a name for and did not try to find one, and filed it, out of habit, though she did not know what shelf a thing like that went on.

Rourke came too. He came from the church-house lot with his rusty coat brushed and his hat in his hand, and he did not push forward and he did not hang back. He took a place at the front edge of the crowd where she could see him plain, and he folded his long hands, and he waited, because he had learned the waiting from watching her, and he was better at it than any of them save her, and he wanted to see what she would do with a town she had called up out of the ground.

Tobe was not in the crowd. She found him at the last, off to the side, standing in the shade-line of the store with his shoulders high and drawn and his weight up on the balls of his feet, the held-wing stillness, and he was not watching her. He was watching *them*. He had read it already, whatever it was, and he was ready to run and would not, and she took her eyes off him because looking at the boy was the one thing that could still put a shake in her hands.

Persy Gault stood near the front, hands folded, dust-pale eyes lifted, her mouth already moving on a thing she was saying under her breath, and Odessa knew without hearing it that it was the Lord and her own name in the one breath.

---

She did not raise the rods.

That was the first break in the ritual, and they felt it. A man who has taken his hat off for a show and the show does not start will stand a while and then begin to be afraid, and she let that fear come up in them, standing there with the copper down at her sides and the wire biting her wrist, until the square had gone dead quiet in the heat with two hundred people in it and the only sound the tank ticking as the sun took it.

Then she said it. Plain. Flat. In the voice she used for the performing, the one the stammer never came into, because she needed every word of this to land clean and the block would ruin it and she could not afford to be ruined today.

"There is no gift in me," she said.

The square held still.

"There is no gift in me, and there never was one. The rods are tube off a dead peddler behind the livery. I filed them bright so they'd catch the sun, because a bright thing draws the eye. The wire is baling wire. My hand died in the third strike and won't close, so I lash the rod to my wrist, and from where you stand it looks like I'm holding it, and that is the whole of the trick. I have told you a lie for a year. Cade paid me to tell it. I told it for the silver and for a deed I never got and to keep men who wanted to shoot each other standing in a field instead. There is no water I called up. There is water in this country the way there is water in any country if you know how to look at the ground, and I know how to look at the ground, and that is not a gift and it is not the Lord and it is not the lightning, it is a thing a body learns walking dry country forty years with its eyes open. That is all I am. I am a woman who can read dirt and could not close her hand, and I let you make me into something else because it paid, and I am telling you now, in the daylight, so you will quit: there is nothing here. There never was anything here. Go home."

She had never said so many true words at one time in her life. It came up out of her level and cold and complete, the way the water at the Aguja had come up under the caliche shelf, from a place she had kept sealed, and she stood there with it spent out of her at last and felt the strange lightness of a thing set down, and looked at their faces to see it break.

It did not break.

---

She watched it not break the way you watch a shelf of caliche take the whole weight of a hill and not crack.

The faces did not go slack with hurt or hard with anger. They went *soft*. That was the thing she had not counted on and could not have counted on, because there was no arithmetic for it — they went soft the way faces go at the graveside when the preacher says the dead one's finally at peace. A woman near the front put her apron up to her eyes. A man she did not know took his hat clean off and held it to his chest. Down the front rank a low sound started, not a murmur of doubt, but the other thing, the warm swelling thing she had heard three times over her own smoking body, and she understood, cold and complete and too late, exactly what she had done.

She had not confessed. She had testified.

She heard it start to come back at her, the meaning they were making of it, passed low from mouth to mouth the way water finds the low ground, and it was worse than anything Melbern had said, because Melbern's lies she could have fought and this she could not, because it was built out of her own truest words.

*Hear how she puts herself down.*

*A false prophet brags. A true one says she's nothing.*

*She's telling us it's the Lord and not her. She's giving Him the glory. She always did. She never once took the glory to herself, not once, and that's how you know.*

Persy Gault was weeping without a sound, her plain strong face lifted, shining, and her lips moved, and Odessa did not have to hear the words to know them: *I always know your meaning.*

---

She raised her voice. She had never had to raise it in a year; the whole art had been the quiet, the making them lean; and now she raised it because the quiet was drowning her, filling up with their meaning the way a low place fills.

"You aren't hearing me," she said. "I'm not being humble. There's no humble in it. I'm telling you I *cheated* you. Every one of you. I took Cade's silver to keep the peace and I took your dollars in the mail that I never earned a cent of, and I pointed a man at dead ground for two dollars once because I'd rather he died hoping and I got paid than he died knowing and I didn't. I read that boy's father's well the day he dug it and I knew the caliche wouldn't hold and I said not one word, because a word from me is a thing that kills people now and I have known it since the fenced spring, and I said nothing, and the boy's in the ground. There's no gift in that. There's no meaning in that. It's just a woman who wanted a deed and let your children die to get the money for it. *Do you hear me.* There is nothing holy in this square. There is only me, and I'm the worst thing that's happened to this county since the rain quit."

She had never in her life told a room how bad she was. She had spent forty years keeping it behind the wall. She threw it out over them now, the whole black inventory, because the truth had failed and she thought maybe the ugliness would do what the truth could not, maybe if she made herself vile enough they would have to stop loving her.

A voice came out of the crowd. It was an old man's voice, cracked, and it was not angry, and it carried in the dead-quiet square the way a voice carries over water.

"That's the storm talking," the old man said, gentle as anything. "The Lord marked her three times and she's carried it three times, and now she's carrying *this*." He said it to the people around him, not to her, explaining her, the way you explain a thing you have already decided the shape of. "A woman that takes the whole county's sin on herself in the middle of the square. I never seen the like. I never in my life seen the like."

And the warm sound swelled, and somewhere in it a woman began, unsteady, to sing, the deliver-us hymn they had sung over the playa hole a year gone, and it caught the way a grass fire catches, one throat and then a dozen and then the square, and Odessa Pike stood in the middle of the last live water in the county with the copper wired to her dead wrist and her whole life's worth of truth spent out of her onto the ground, and heard them turn it into a psalm.

---

She looked for Rourke.

He was still at the front edge, hat in his long hands, and he was not singing. He was watching her, and there was on his road-burned face a thing that was almost pity and was worse than any hate, because it was the pity of a man watching another try a door he already knows is a wall. He had known. He had told her in the store. *You've run out of no.* She had thought she had one thing left he did not know about, one number unspent, and he had known about that too, had known it before she did, and had let her come out here and spend it in front of the county so that when it failed she would have nothing left, so that the confession itself would become the last stone in the shrine.

He inclined his head to her. Just that. A small grave courteous bow, across the singing, the way one craftsman salutes another's work.

Then he turned to the crowd and lifted his hands, and his trained voice went up over the hymn and gathered it and shaped it, warm and terrible, and she heard him take the thing she had said to destroy herself and lay it into the wall of the church he was building.

"...and did you hear her, brothers," Rourke was saying, "did you hear the *Salt Preacher* stand up in her own square and call herself nothing, call herself the worst of us — I say to you, when did a false thing ever do that, when did a liar ever stand in the light and confess her lie, save that the Lord was working in her too strong to hold — she has emptied herself out before you, she has poured herself out like the water she found, and I tell you the emptying is the sign, the emptying is the very sign we prayed for—"

She did not hear the rest. She had heard the rest a year ago, and three times before that, lying on her back with the storm in her teeth. There was no bottom to it. That was the thing she understood, finally, standing there — there was no word in any language she could say that the wanting would not turn to bread. She could not be disbelieved. A woman who confesses a fraud and is believed anyway has learned that belief is not a judgment about the truth. It never had been. It was a hunger, and it would eat the truth and the lie down to the same paste and be no less hungry after.

She reached up with her good hand and began to unwind the wire from her dead wrist, there in the square, slow, letting the copper rod come loose and hang and then drop, and it hit the cracked mud of the trough-rind with a small flat sound that no one heard over the singing, and she stood there with her ruined hand open and empty and the fern-scar bare to the sun for the first time in a year, the branching white channels of it climbing her forearm like a dry creek, like an aquifer, like a map of every water she had ever pretended to find, and it did not matter. She had shown them the trick and dropped the copper and bared the scar and told them she was nothing, and the singing did not falter, and Persy Gault reached out across the front rank toward the bare scar with her split-and-mended hand, weeping, reaching, and behind Persy the rest of them began, softly, to reach.

Over their heads, on the platform, one of the four riflemen shifted his weight and settled the gun and went on watching the only water in Cheyne County, and did not look down at the square at all, because it was not the water they were reaching for.



---

# CHAPTER 26 · Movement V — The Mercy

### "The Sacrifice the God Demands"

*Salt Fork, the county seat — rainless week fifty-four.*
*The Rueda a road of cracked plate ford to county line, no wet in it anywhere, not even under the deep holes where the cranes had stood before they went off wherever the water went. Hauled water twenty-five cents the barrel where a man could get it, which by now was mostly he could not, the dray owner having sold the last of it and shut the tap and gone to sit on his own dry cistern with a rifle. The depot tank guarded by four riflemen still, the tank a black held thing over the town like a coffin lifted onto a shelf. The sky iron. Not a cloud in it. Not the memory of one.*

---

They came for her the morning after the confession, and she knew from the sound of them coming that the confession had not saved her, and had not damned her, and had done the one thing she had feared worst, which was nothing.

She had slept in Halve's lean-to among the barrels because she had nowhere left that was hers, and she woke before light with the taste of dust in her teeth and lay still and listened to the town assemble. She had spent her whole life listening to towns assemble around her. She knew the difference between a town gathering to watch and a town gathering to want, and this was the wanting, low and steady, feet on the packed square, and under the feet no shouting, which was the worst of it, because a mob that shouts is a mob still arguing with itself and a mob that comes quiet has already had its meeting.

She did not run. There was nowhere the name would not ride after her and she had proved that to herself on the mule at the mouth of this same lean-to a day ago and she did not need to prove it twice. She sat up in the dark and worked the dead right hand the way she did every morning, the fingers that would not close, curling them against the palm and feeling them refuse, and then she wound the wire onto the wrist, though she did not know yet whether there would be rods, because a body does the thing its body knows to do while its mind is elsewhere doing the arithmetic.

The arithmetic came out the way it had come out for a week. There was no third column. There had not been a third column since the day she took Cade's silver.

She heard Halve on the boards over her head, moving slow, an old careful man not coming down. She heard him not come down. She filed it, the way she filed everything, though she had run out of shelves to put such things on.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Square before light: two hundred, standing, no lamps, no talk. Men to the
> front, women behind, children held. Rope on a shoulder — not coiled for
> stock. New rope, store-bought, the store's own, Halve's, taken off Halve's
> nail. Wagon backed to the courthouse steps, tongue down, bed empty, waiting
> to be a platform or a scaffold, the same lumber does for both. Rourke's men
> at the church-lot fence, not in the crowd, watching the crowd, the way you
> stand near a fire you built. Cade not here. Cade's man Rakes here, horse
> held loose at the rail, one boot out of the stirrup — the Association come
> to watch, not to save, to see the county spend itself on the prophet so the
> ledger comes out clean. No wind. The dust hangs where a foot lifts it and
> does not travel. Ground says nothing. Ground has been dead so long it has
> forgotten it was ever asked.

---

Rourke came into the lean-to alone.

He came the way he did everything, without hurry, ducking the low lintel, and he took off his hat in the dark of the place though there was no woman present he owed the courtesy to unless it was her, and she understood he had come to owe it to her, which was its own kind of theater and better done than most.

"They want you to walk," he said.

"There's no water."

"I know it." He turned the hat in his long hands. "You know it. Cade's known it since May. There is not a soul in that square who does not know it in the place a man does his true knowing." He said it gently, and the gentleness was the terrible part, because it was not put on. "And they want you to walk anyway. Do you understand what you're being asked?"

"To find water where there is none."

"No." He said it soft. "They're past that. They gave that up somewhere around the boy. They want the *walking*. They want to stand in a field and watch a woman the lightning couldn't kill raise a rod over dead ground, and hold their breath, and be, for one hour, a people still waiting on a mercy instead of a people who buried two children in a well." He watched her. "And then they'll want a reason it didn't come. That's what a prophet is for, at the end."

She said nothing. She had known most of it. She had not known it said so cleanly, and she disliked, coldly, how much she preferred hearing it from a man who half-believed it than from the arithmetic, which believed nothing and was always right.

"You could refuse," Rourke said.

"And they'd hang me for a fraud instead of a failed saint. I've done that sum."

"You've done that sum." Something moved in his road-burned face that was near respect. "Then you know the only door left standing. Walk. Let them watch you fail against a dead sky. And when they turn — and they will turn, in the field, with the rope on a man's shoulder — you let me be there to catch it. I'll grieve you before they can hang you. A grieved prophet the county could not save is a treasure. A hanged fraud is a shame they'd have to bury and forget." He set the hat back on. "Give me the martyr, and I'll keep the name gentle. It's the last mercy on the board, and it's mine to give, and I'm offering it to you plain because you're the only one in this county I've never once had to lie to."

"Because I never bought it."

"Because you never bought it," he agreed. "Which is why you're the realest thing that ever happened to my faith, and I have hated you for it the way you hate a true word." He stooped for the lintel. "They'll be ready when the sun's up over the tank. Walk, Odessa. It's the last thing left that you know how to do better than anybody living."

---

They gave her the rods back.

That was the thing that told her Rourke had read it right to the bottom. A man came into the lean-to — Neel, drought-broke, hollow, the skeptic gone all the way over to the far side of himself — and he carried her copper rods in both hands the way you carry a thing you are afraid of and would not touch except that carrying it is a grace done to yourself. She had dropped them in the square a day ago. Someone had picked them up out of the trough-rind and cleaned the mud off and here they were come back to her, because the county could not let the prophet stand without her instrument any more than it could let her confess her way out of being one.

"We saved 'em for you," Neel said. His voice was not the accuser's voice of the fenced spring. It was worse. It was hoping. "We knowed you'd want 'em."

She took them. The dead hand could not, so she took them with the left and laid the right rod across the dead wrist and let Neel watch her wind the wire, because there was nothing to hide anymore; they all knew how she was made; Halve had left her a coil of it on the counter to tell her so. She wound it in the open and Neel watched the trick performed with the trick exposed, watched the wire cinched to the wrist that could not hold, and it did not shake his hope by the width of a hair. She had known it would not. She had stopped being surprised by it around the White Draw.

"Where," she said.

"Cade's man says the north bench," Neel said. "Above the ford. Where the last of the river was."

Of course it was the north bench. She had read it dry with her own heel four days ago in the worst duster of the year, no copper on her, a dry cap over a dry room, the last of the old sweetness held in the top of the ground like the last of the color in a dead man's face. Cade had chosen the ground she had already privately proved was hopeless. Cade did nothing that was not on purpose. He wanted the walk to fail on the exact ground where the county's own eyes could watch the river's grave refuse to give up the river.

"Fine," she said.

---

She looked for the boy before she went out.

She did not look openly. She found him the way she found water she was not going to admit to finding — off to the side, at the edge of her eye, without turning her head. He was crouched in the shade-cut behind the barrels with his back to the plank wall and his knees up under his chin and his hands flat on the ground on either side of him, the way a boy sits who has decided to be furniture, to be a thing the room forgets. He was not watching her. He was watching the door of the lean-to, and past it the square, and he had one hand already curled around a loose slat as if the wall might be a place a body could go into. He had read the whole morning off the men's backs, the way she had taught him without teaching him. He was ready to run and would not, because she had never sent him and he did not know how to send himself.

She had a thing she meant to do about the boy and she did it now, fast, and did not let herself feel it.

She crossed to him in the dark of the lean-to and she took the reins of his sacked-out old horse off the peg and she put them in his good live hand, the animal's rope, and she took him hard by the wrist — his wrist, the thin wild wrist of him — and she turned it over and she pressed into the palm the little sack of Cade's silver, the whole of it, the twenty-dollar walks and the hundred she never earned and the strangers' dollars in envelopes from Roswell and Pecos, the entire freight of the lie, the deed she had wanted more than breath melted down into coin and never spent on any ground because there had never been any ground to buy that a man could not put her off of. She folded his fingers on it with her dead hand pressing his live ones shut, because her dead hand could push even if it could not close, and she held his shut fist inside her broken one and she looked at him once, straight, the way no one had looked at either of them without flinching, and she said the least that would do the job.

"South," she said. "Now. Before the sun's up on the tank. You don't stop at the Territory line. You don't say my name to anybody, ever, your whole life. It isn't a name. It's a thing that eats. Go."

He did not move. His eyes did the thing she could not stand, the wanting, the belief in her going all the way down past the trick he had watched her work a hundred times, and she saw that he would not run, that he would stand in this shade-cut and watch her walk out onto the north bench and fail against the sky and be caught by whatever caught her, because he believed, and belief does not run from the thing it believes.

So she did the last of it. She lied to him.

"I'll come after," she said, in the performing voice, the fluent one, the one the stammer never entered, and she spent it on a child in a lean-to for the price of getting him gone. "Three days. El Paso. I'll come after and we'll dig a well nobody can put us off of. I found the ground already. It's there." *It's there* — the same two words she had spent on Ronsard over dead caliche for two dollars, the killing words, the ones she had sworn she would never say true and had said false so often they had worn smooth. She said them to the boy and watched them work on him the way water works into caliche, slow, against his will, and stay. His hands came off the ground. He believed her. He would carry the silver south and believe her the whole three days and past them, believe her at the line and past the line, and go on believing there was a woman coming and a well waiting, and she thought, coldly, with the flat clear cruelty she trusted above any warmth, that it was the truest kindness she had in her, to leave a child a lie big enough to walk on. She did not call it a kindness. She had spent her life not opening doors and she was not going to open one now, in the dark, at the end, for a boy who had never once flinched from her.

He went. She heard the old horse's feet go soft and then softer on the packed ground, south, away from the tank, away from the square, and she did not watch him go because watching would have been the door, and she stood in the lean-to with the copper wired to her dead wrist and her hands empty of everything she had ever wanted and heard the last of the boy's going swallowed up in the sound of the town wanting her, and she turned and walked out into it.

---

The square took its hats off when she came out.

They did it the way they always had, the ripple of it, the men uncovering, and the terrible readiness of them leaning in, and she walked through it to the wagon and did not get up on it and no one made her, because a prophet walks to the ground; a scaffold you have to be helped onto but a walk you go to on your own feet, and they wanted the walk. Rourke stood at the church-lot fence with his hands folded. Rakes sat his horse at the rail with one boot out of the stirrup, the Association's cold eye, come to see the county spend itself. Persy Gault stood at the front with her dust-pale eyes lifted and her split-and-mended hands folded and her mouth already moving on the Lord and Odessa's name in the one breath. And the rope was there, on a man's shoulder, new, off Halve's own nail, and no one looked at it, and everyone knew where it was, the way you know where the one loaded gun in a room is without once turning your head to it.
