They walked her out to the north bench in a long slow column through the dead town, past the busted boom, the thinned tents, the child-graves at the church-fence she had stopped counting at eleven, out to the lip of the Rueda where the river had been, where the cracked plate ran off to the county line under an iron sky. And they ringed the bench the way they had ringed her three times over her own smoking body, in a circle, waiting to see whether she would rise cursed or chosen, wanting her to be one or the other because a woman lightning cannot kill is unbearable as merely a woman.

She raised the rods slow, the way you'd lift a dying man's head to help him drink.

And she made them wait.

She was good at the waiting. It was the last skill left to her that still worked, the one thing the strikes had left her more of and not less, and she stood on the dead bench over the river's grave with the copper catching the iron light and she held the pronouncement she was not going to make and felt the whole ring of them lean in against the silence, two hundred souls straining toward her word the way the burnt county strained toward the sky, and she understood, standing there, cold and complete, that she could hold this silence forever and it would change nothing, because they had not come for what she would say. They had come for the saying. She was not the water. She had never been the water. She was the woman they made wait so they could go on being a people who were still waiting, and the waiting was the whole of it, and the waiting did not need her to end, because the moment she ended it — with a yes, with a no, with the truth, with her death — the waiting would simply take up the next thing to wait on, and the next, and the ground would be as patient as it had ever been.

She held the copper up against the iron sky and did not lower it and did not speak, and behind her, unheard by anyone but her, the rope came off the man's shoulder with the small dry rustle of new hemp, and the ring drew in a hand's width, quiet, and settled, and waited. They would give her the whole silence she asked for. They had waited on the sky fifty-four weeks and it had taught them how. She stood over the river's grave with her arms up and the county drawn close around her in a ring, patient as the ground, and knew they would not move until she did, and that she did not have to, and that this — the held breath, the closing circle, the two hundred faces turned up to a woman who had nothing left to give them and would give it slow — was the only thing she had ever truly found down there in the dead ground, and it had been under every tract she walked, and it would still be here after the rope, after her, after the rain, waiting to be raised.


---

# CHAPTER 27 · Movement V — The Mercy

### "Rain That Saves No One Cleanly"

*The Rueda's north bench, above the ford — rainless week fifty-four, the fifty-fourth week ending.*
*The river a road of cracked plate ford to county line, no wet in it anywhere; the last live water in Cheyne County the depot tank behind them in the town, four riflemen sitting on it. Hauled water twenty-five cents the barrel where a man could get it hauled, which by now was mostly he could not. Dead stock past all counting a season ago. The sky iron since before the boy went in the ground.*

---

She held the copper up against the iron sky and did not lower it, and behind her the rope came off the man's shoulder with the small dry rustle of new hemp, and the ring drew in a hand's width, quiet, and Persy Gault began to sing.

Odessa did not turn to the rope. She had learned young that turning to the thing that would kill you was a door, and she was not going to open one now, on the dead bench, over the river's grave, with two hundred souls leaning in against her silence. So she held the rods up and held the pronouncement she was not going to make and watched, instead, the only thing left worth the watching, which was the sky, because a body caught out will look at the weather the way a drowning man looks at the far bank.

The sky was iron. It had been iron so long the word had worn out of meaning anything, the way *drought* had worn out, the way her own name had worn out into a thing that ate.

Then it was not iron.

She did not name what she saw at first, because she had spent a lifetime not naming things until they had proved themselves, and she did not believe the sky any more than she believed the rods. But there was a thing in the southwest, low, where the flats were, where nothing lived and the salt lay on the ground like a lie about snow, and it was the color of a bruise coming up under skin, and it had an edge to it, a hard clean line at the top, and above the line the iron had gone a strange bad yellow, the yellow of a healing wound gone wrong.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> SW, low, above the salt: a wall. Blue-black at the base, hard-edged
> at the crown, boiling up over itself, greenish-white where the crown
> catches the light — hail-green. Top sheared flat, drawn off to the NE
> in a long anvil. Not dust. Dust hangs and travels level. This stands
> and climbs. Air off it before it: cold, coming in low against the hot,
> the two of them meeting at the ankles first, a smell in it — wet
> caliche, wet dust, petrichor. Doves up off the plate, three, four, low
> and fast, going NOT to the water — going before the wall, ahead of it,
> the way birds go ahead of a thing. Ants out on the mound, out and
> trenching, out in the heat of the day when no ant works, mounds ringed
> fresh. The ground goes quiet the way it goes before. Not dry-quiet.
> Held-quiet. Wind drops to nothing and the nothing has a weight in it.
> This is weather. This is weather coming.

---

The cold came in against her ankles.

She felt it before any of them, because she was the only one on the bench who was reading the ground and not reading her, and it went up her legs under the skirt, a live cold, a moving cold, and the hair on her arms lifted the way it had lifted three times in her life a half-second before the light took her, and her body, which knew things her mind did not trust, flinched all the way down to the dead hand.

Somebody in the ring said, "Wind."

They said it low, disbelieving, the way you'd say a dead man's name if he sat up. There had been wind for fourteen months — the hot southwest wind that carried dust and the sweet-rot of stock and the mineral bitterness of the flats and never once carried rain. This was not that wind. This wind came out of the wrong quarter and it came cold and it came smelling of wet, and every soul on the bench felt it at once, felt it lift the hats they had taken off, and the singing stopped in Persy Gault's throat with a small catch, like a rope going tight.

Odessa stood with the copper up and did not lower it, because now — now, at the very last, when it did not matter and could not be made to matter — she understood exactly what was about to happen, and she understood it faster and colder and more completely than any of them, and there was not a thing in the world she could do to stop the meaning they were going to make of it.

The rope was still off the man's shoulder. She heard it stop moving.

---

The first drop hit the back of her dead right hand where it held the wired rod aloft, and she felt it because the skin there was dead only to fine things and not to weight, and it was a fat cold drop the size of a two-bit piece, and it struck and sat and did not soak in, because the hand had forgotten how, and then another struck the copper with a small bright note like a fingernail on a cup, and then the plate of the dead riverbed below the bench went *tick*, and *tick*, all across itself, a sound she had not heard in fourteen months, the sound of water hitting dry ground and not being taken.

The ring made a sound.

It was not a word. It went up out of two hundred throats at once, low, torn, the sound a body makes when the thing it has stopped letting itself want walks in the door, and it was the ugliest and the most human sound she had ever heard, and it was aimed at her.

She lowered the rods.

She had held them up so long her arm had gone to fire, the dead wrist screaming under the wire, and she lowered them now not as a pronouncement, not as the *word* they were straining for, but because her arm had simply given out at the exact wrong second — and she watched, cold, unable to stop it, as the whole ring took the lowering of the copper for the answer. As if she had reached up into the iron sky with a peddler's tube and hooked the weather down. As if the rain had been up there the whole time behind a door and she had been the one, at last, with the key.

"She called it," someone said, wondering, wrecked. "She called it *down*."

And the drops came harder, and the plate went from *tick* to a long hiss, and the dead riverbed began, for the first time since the second dry year, to shine.

---

They fell down.

Not all of them, and not all at once, but it moved through the ring the way the singing had moved — the men going first to their knees in the wet dust with their hats crushed to their chests and their faces turned up into it, and the women after, and the children turned loose to run out onto the shining plate of the riverbed and stamp in the rain that stood in the cracks, screaming, because a child does not know it is a miracle, it only knows it is wet. Persy Gault knelt with her split-and-mended hands open at her sides and her dust-pale eyes wide open into the falling rain, not flinching from it, letting it fill them, and her mouth moved, and this time Odessa could hear it, because the rain had made the crowd go quiet with its own weeping.

"Salt Preacher," Persy said. Just that. Over and over. Her whole ruined life laid down in the two words. *Salt Preacher.*

The rope was on the ground. Odessa saw it when she looked, finally, because it no longer mattered what she looked at — the new hemp of it lying in the wet, and the man who had carried it down on his knees in the mud with the rest of them, both his hands over his face, his shoulders going. He had come to hang her and the sky had opened and now he was on his knees in the rain weeping for the wanting to hang her, weeping harder for it, she thought, than any of them, because a man who nearly does a black thing and is spared the doing loves the thing that spared him worst of all.

She stood in the middle of it on her feet.

She was the only one on her feet. She stood in the rain she had not called with the copper hanging from her dead wrist and the water running off her white-streaked hair and down the fern-scar bared to the weather, filling the branching white channels of it, running down her arm the way rain runs a dry creek, and she felt the cold of it and the weight of it and the animal old relief of it in her body that could not be argued with, her own thirst answering the wet whether she willed it or not — and over the top of the relief, cold and clear and complete, she did the only thing she had ever truly known how to do.

She counted.

---

She counted the way she counted everything, because it was the last honest work she had and she was not going to lose it to a miracle.

She counted that the rain was falling on the dead nester belt to the east where ten of eleven quarters stood dark and empty, falling on ground with no one left on it to plant, fourteen months too late for every family that had loaded a wagon or failed to afford the loading. She counted that it was falling on Persy Gault's playa where the salt had come up white in the low corner and the salt cedar stood green over the poison, and that the rain would run off the salt and leave the salt, because a rain does not unsalt a field; the salt was forty feet down where no shovel and no weather reached, and the field was dead and would stay dead and Persy would kneel in the wet of it and thank her for it.

She counted that it was falling on the Sikes quarter where Ammon Sikes lay thirty-three feet down in the hole he had dug for water on the strength of her broken silence, and that no rain that ever fell would raise him, that the shaft would fill from the top now and stand a well at last, sweet water over a drowned man, the deliverance arriving to fill the grave it had dug. She counted the Ronsard boy under his caliche shelf on the next quarter, three days and now four in the ground, the rain coming down on the mound of him. She counted Cordy Dobie in the churchyard, shot reaching for the water she found. She counted the child-graves at the church-fence she had stopped counting at eleven, the small close mounds going dark with wet, and she thought, with the flat clear cruelty she trusted above any warmth, that the rain would sink into those eleven small graves faster than into any ground in the county, because a small grave is loose-turned and takes water easy, and that this was the truest thing the rain would do all day, and no one kneeling on the bench would think of it, because they were all looking at her.

She counted that not one drop of it undid one thing.

The dry holes stayed dry-dug. The salted field stayed salt. The drowned man stayed drowned. The bust boom stayed bust. The rain did not roll time backward off the county like a stone off a well-mouth; it only fell, indifferent, on the wreckage, the way it would have fallen on a county that never heard her name, on ground that had been on its own road with the weather since the Gulf, since before the walk, since before the boy — and it fell so hard now that the flats to the southwest were a gray wall of it and the runoff had begun to move in the dead cracks of the Rueda, a thin bright thread of it finding down the plate toward the ford, the river starting, an inch of it, to remember it was a river.

And every soul on the bench believed she had done it.

---

Cade's man Rakes had come out from the town.

She saw him at the edge of the bench, still horsed, one boot out of the stirrup the way the Association sat to watch and not to save, and the rain was running off his hat brim in a sheet and he was not kneeling and he was not weeping. He was looking at her across the two hundred wet backs, and she read his face the way she read ground, and she saw the arithmetic run behind it clean and cold — that the Association had sent him to see the county spend itself on the prophet against a dead sky, and the sky had not stayed dead, and now the ledger came out the other way, and Cade would want to know before nightfall what a woman was worth who could stand on the north bench and pull the river up out of its own grave in front of the whole county.

She had told Cade in May there was no water to find, not enough, not in time. She had been right. The rain proved her right — it was not her water; it was no man's water; it would go, and the wells would come up a little and fall again, and the third dry year was not broken by an afternoon, only interrupted. She knew that the way she knew a man was lying before he finished the sentence. She could have stood up on the wagon and said it, said the truth all the way to the bottom in the daylight one more time — *this is weather, it does not know my name, it will go, count your barrels* — and she had already learned, standing in the square four days back with her whole life's worth of truth spent out onto the ground, that there was no word she could say the wanting would not turn to bread.

So she did not say it. She had run out of no. She had run out of yes. She had run out of the truth and the lie both, and there was only the rain, falling on everything the same, saving no one, and being called her mercy by a county on its knees.

Rakes touched his hat to her across the crowd — the way Cade had touched his to her a year and fourteen months ago at the mouth of a dying draw, the first man in ten years to do it, the courtesy that was a price — and he turned the horse and rode back toward the town through the rain to tell Emory Cade that the woman the Association had come to watch die had just become the most valuable thing in Cheyne County a second time, and that the depot tank was, by afternoon, no longer the only water, and that the reckoning would have to start over.

---

Rourke came to her last.

He came the way he did everything, without hurry, walking through the kneeling and the weeping with his rusty coat gone black with the wet and his hat in his hand though the rain fell on his bare head, and he did not kneel and he did not sing, and when he reached her he stood close, so that only she would hear it, and he looked out over the two hundred faces turned up into the weather and not at her at all.

"You didn't do this," he said. Quietly. Almost tender.

"I know it."

"I know you know it. It's why I could say it to you." He watched the rain run off the plate toward the ford. "There's not a soul in this county but you and me and Cade's man knows it, and Cade's man is already gone to sell it. By the time the rain quits, you'll have done it. By Sunday you'll always have done it." He turned the wet hat in his long hands. "You had your chance to die on this bench and be a treasure. The sky took it from you. It gave you the worst thing it had." He said it without any triumph, a man reading a hard verdict off a true page. "A miracle you have to go on living inside of. Now you can't die a fraud and you can't die a martyr. Now you're just a woman a whole county saw pull the river up, and there is no El Paso far enough for that, and no truth loud enough. You are the realest thing that ever happened to this county, and none of it is real, and you'll carry that the rest of your life, and they will follow you every step of it, reaching."

The rain came down between them.

"Where's the boy," Rourke said.

She did not answer. She looked at the flats where the gray wall stood, and she kept her face the flat closed thing it had been in the road for forty-one years, even now, even in the rain, even in front of the one man in the county who had asked.

South. Before the sun was up on the tank. Not stopping at the Territory line. A child riding out of a dry country under an iron sky with a sack of a false prophet's silver in his fist and a lie big enough to walk on — *I'll come after, three days, El Paso, I found the ground already, it's there* — riding, right now, into a rain he could not have known was coming, the first rain of his remembered life, falling on him somewhere south of the county on a road she had sent him down to save him from exactly this, from the reaching, from the wanting, from a mercy that saves no one cleanly and asks the saved to kneel.

She thought, coldly, with the cruelty she trusted above any warmth, that at least the boy would have water now. That she had sent him into a country that was about to be, for one afternoon, less deadly than the one behind him. That it changed nothing and cost her everything and was the last arithmetic she would ever run for him, and that it came out, for once, in his favor, and she would never know it, because he would go on believing there was a woman coming and a well waiting, and the rain would only make the lie easier to walk on.

"He's gone where the name can't ride after him," she said.

Rourke looked at her a long moment. Then he set the wet hat back on his bare head, and he did the small grave courteous thing he had done in the square, the craftsman's bow, and he turned and walked back down into the crowd, and he raised his trained voice up over the sound of the falling rain, warm and terrible, and began to gather it and shape it into the thing he was building, and she heard him take the weather off the Gulf and lay it into the wall of his church.

Odessa Pike stood on the north bench in the first rain in fourteen months with the copper hanging useless from her dead wrist, and did not kneel, and did not sing, and did not correct them, and watched the thin bright thread of runoff find down the dead plate of the Rueda toward the ford — the river coming back to a bed that had forgotten it, an inch of live water moving over the drowned man and the dry holes and the eleven small graves alike, saving none of them, called a miracle by everyone who saw it, owned by no one, on its own road, going.


---

# CHAPTER 28 · Movement V — The Mercy

### "Destroyed as Odessa"

*Cheyne County — the third day after the rain, in what the county would afterward call the year the water came back, though it did not come back.*
*One rain in fourteen months: a hard afternoon and a soft night, then the iron sky again, dry as before. The Rueda ran a thumb's depth over its cracked plate for two days and quit; by the third morning it was pools once more, weak coffee, going down. The depot windlass came up wet a foot higher than it had in a month and held there and began, already, to fall. Hauled water dropped to fifteen cents the barrel the day after the rain and was climbing back before the mud had crusted. Dead stock past all counting still dead. The count that mattered now was the one no man kept: how long a single afternoon of weather would be made to mean.*

---

They did not hang her, in the end, because the sky had made it impossible.

She had understood that on the bench with the rope going slack on the man's shoulder and the rain filling the branching white channels of the scar — that a mob cannot hang a woman it has just watched pull the river up out of its grave, that the rope does not know it, but the hands that hold the rope do, and their hands had gone from the rope to their own faces and then to the wet ground, and a rope on the ground is only rope. She had lived because the weather had come on its own road and put itself between her and the killing, indifferent, the way it had put itself between the county and the summer, saving no one, saving her.

She wished, in the flat clear place where she did her true reckoning, that it had let them do it.

A hanged fraud is a shame a county buries and forgets. She had heard Rourke say it in the lean-to and she had known it for a true word the way she knew all his true words, by the way they cost her. If they had hanged her on the bench she would have been a lie the county had killed and lived past, the way you live past a bad debt paid, and inside of a year the men who pulled the rope would have been the men who always said she was a humbug, and the name would have gone down into the ground with the woman, and that would have been an ending. That was the last clean ending there had been on the board and the sky had taken it, and given her instead the thing Rourke promised: a miracle she had to go on living inside of.

She was not going to go on living inside of it.

---

She left the town on the third morning before the tank was lit.

She left on foot, because the mule had been taken. She did not know by whom and did not much care; a prophet's mule is a relic the moment the prophet is a saint, and someone had led it away in the night the way someone had cleaned the mud off her dropped rods and carried them back to her, out of a reverence that was only appetite wearing its Sunday coat. She had woken in Halve's lean-to with the barrels sweating faintly in the changed air and the mule-peg empty and the reins gone off it, and she had lain still and listened, the way she had listened her whole life to the thing coming for her, and she had heard the town not-sleeping. Fires banked. A murmur under the eaves of the church-lot that was men keeping a watch, or keeping a vigil, which she had learned were the same posture from behind. They were not guarding her. They were guarding the *place she was*. She was a spring now, and springs get fenced.

So she took what she could carry, which was little, because she had spent her want down to nothing — the deed melted into silver and pressed into a boy's fist and sent south, the whole freight of the lie riding out of the county in a child's hand three days gone. She had a canteen she filled at the trough while the guard-man dozed with his chin on his rifle. She had the flask she carried to seem ordinary, which had cold tea in it going to vinegar, which she left. She had a strip of side-meat off Halve's shelf that she took and left a silver dollar for, the last of the strangers' money, because she was not a thief and had never once been a thief, whatever else they would say. She had the copper rods, because they were hers, because she had filed the ends of them herself by lamplight ten years and a lifetime ago, and because a woman does not leave the one thing that is truly hers for the pious to touch and weep over.

She wound the wire onto the dead wrist out of a habit she no longer examined, and then she looked at it, the ruined hand curled to its claw and the copper lashed to it, and she unwound the wire and put the rods across her shoulder in the canteen strap and walked out of Salt Fork west, into the flats, toward the White Draw and the salt and the dead country past it where the maps went white and stopped meaning anything, because that was the one direction the name could not follow her, being the direction with nothing in it to tell.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> West road out of town before light. Ground still holding the rain in the
> low places, a skin of damp on the caliche gone slick, tracked over already
> by every foot that walked to the bench and back — two hundred sets, the whole
> county's print pressed once into the one soft morning it will get, then baked.
> Grass not up. Grass will not come up; one rain does not raise grass on ground
> grazed to the dirt three years running; the seed is eaten or blown or dead in
> the crust. Mesquite beans set thin on the branch. Salt cedar on the dry margin
> feathered green, drinking. Toad tracks in the mud below the trough — spadefoot
> up out of the ground it slept in fourteen months, one night's frogs, gone to
> ground again by noon. No dove-line. The birds not back. The cranes not back.
> Ground falls away west toward the flats. Gyp smell rising off the Draw on the
> changed wind. Poison drawing up to meet the rain, last, when everything
> sweeter is spent. Nothing out this way. Nothing to find.

---

Persy Gault was on the west road.

She was sitting in a spring wagon at the fork where the town's ruts gave onto the flats road, a wagon with a played-out horse in the shafts and a load in the bed under a tarp, and she was waiting, and she had been waiting a long time, from the set of the horse and the cold ash of a little fire she'd built and let die. She had known which way Odessa would go. Of course she had known. She had learned the waiting from Odessa herself, and a woman who had learned that would know the teacher would go the one way that had nothing waiting on the end of it.

Odessa thought about walking past. The flats road was wide enough. But Persy stood up in the wagon-bed with her dust-pale eyes and her split-and-mended hands and there was no walking past a thing that had already forgiven you for everything you were about to do.

"I brought you the horse," Persy said.

Odessa stopped in the road.

"It's Isaiah's horse," Persy said. "It's about done, but it'll carry you further than your feet will, and where you're going a body wants further than her feet." She said it plainly, no word wasted. "There's meal in the bed, and a side, and the last of the seed corn I was keeping. I'm not going to plant it. I dug that ground eleven foot and it took a rain that same as spat on it. I'm done arguing with the Lord about that field." She climbed down over the wheel, careful, favoring a hip. "You take the horse and the load."

"I don't want your horse."

"I know it." Persy came around and stood close, the way she had stood at her ruined field with her hand on the dead forearm. "You never once wanted a thing anybody offered you. That's how I always knew you was true. A false one takes what's give."

The block came up in Odessa's throat, hard, on the *don't* she meant to say again, and sat there, a stone, and she let the hate of it settle and did not fight it, because there was no one left to perform for and the fluent voice was a tool she had put down for good on the north bench.

"P-Persy." The name came out broken and she let it. "I never found you any water."

"No," Persy agreed. "You never did." And she smiled, a small ruined true smile, and Odessa understood, cold, that she had said the whole confession at last, said it flat and plain and stammering to the one person in the county her ruin sat heaviest on — *I never found you any water* — and that Persy had heard every word of it and taken it in and set it down inside herself and it had changed nothing, because Persy had never needed the water. "You give me the *waiting*," Persy said. "I'd have died of the going-empty years back if I hadn't had the waiting to do. My man's in the ground and my field's salt and I got a wagon and a horse I'm giving you and a road out of here, and I'd not trade the last three years for a wet season, because they had a *reason* in them, and a reason is worth more than a rain." She put the lines in Odessa's live hand and folded the dead fingers over them the way Odessa had folded the boy's over the silver. "You go on. You done your part. I'll do the waiting. I'm good at it now. You learnt me good."

---

She took the horse.

She told herself it was arithmetic and it was arithmetic — a horse carried further than feet, and further was the whole of the plan, and to refuse the horse to spare a ruined woman the giving of it was a warmth, and a warmth was a door, and she did not open doors. She took the horse the way she took everything, hard, and did not thank her, because thanks was a door too, and she climbed up into Isaiah Gault's wagon among the meal and the side-meat and the seed corn that would never be planted and she looked down once at Persy Gault standing in the road in her black dress gone brown-red with dust, and Persy raised her split hand, not waving, only lifting it, the way you lift a hand to a thing going, and her mouth moved, and Odessa did not have to hear it to know the shape of the two words, *Salt Preacher*, laid down over her going the way they had been laid down over her staying.

She did the thing she never did. She let it cost her. For the length of one breath, up on the wagon-seat with the lines in her broken hand, she let the boy be gone and the field be salt and the man be drowned and this upright ruined woman be glad of all of it, and she did not push it down, and it went through her like the cold coming in against the ankles on the bench, a live moving cold, all the way to the dead hand. Then she pushed the sleeve down over the wire that was not there and the fern-scar that was, the map of the water she never found drawn up her own arm where no shovel and no weather reached it, and she clucked to Isaiah Gault's horse, and she drove west, and she did not look back, because looking back was the door of all doors, and she had spent forty-one years laying up a wall against exactly this, and she was not going to breach it in the last mile for the one soul who had never once flinched.

---

What happened to her after the west road, no one in Cheyne County ever truly knew, which was the making of it.

The wagon was found. That much was fact, and the fact went into the *Clarion* under Vester Kyle's hand and out from there to Pecos and Roswell and El Paso and up the line as far as Kansas, and it grew in the telling the way water grows in a dry man's memory. The wagon was found nine days on, out past the White Draw where the salt lay on the ground like a lie about snow, the horse dead in the shafts of thirst with the mud of the one rain still crusted on its hocks, the load untouched under the tarp — the meal, the side, the seed corn — and the canteen empty, and the flask that was not there because she had left it, and the copper rods laid across the wagon-seat side by side, filed ends bright, catching the sun the way she had filed them to catch it, pointing west into the country that had no water in it and no name and nothing to find.

No woman.

They searched the flats a week. The Association sent Rakes and four riders because Cade wanted a body, wanted the thing accounted for on the ledger, a corpse being a closed entry and an absence being a debt that draws interest forever. They found her boot-track going out into the white and losing itself where the salt crust took no print. They found nothing after. The country out there does not give a body back; the salt takes the water out of a thing and holds it, dry, forever, the way it had held the murdered man under the Sablon well — she had read that ground herself and knew what it kept and what it did not — but the flats are wide and white and blind, and a woman who walks out into them on purpose, having spent her whole life learning exactly where the eye of a searcher fails, is not a woman who means to be found.

Rakes came back and told Cade there was no body, and Cade sat very still, the way he did, and understood that the worst thing had happened, which was nothing he could put in the book.

---

They made it what they needed it to be, which was everything.

The faithful said the Lord had taken her up, the way He took up Elijah, in the whirlwind — and never mind there had been no whirlwind, only a hot dry morning and a dead horse; the whirlwind grew into the story inside a month, and by the fall the men who had walked her to the bench with a rope on a shoulder would swear they had seen the west go dark that day and heard a wind that was not there. The betrayed said good riddance to the humbug, she had run for El Paso ahead of the rope and died of her own salt in the dark she'd sold the county, and they were nearer the truth of the walking and further from the truth of the woman than any of them, and it did not matter, because the county did not want the truth of the woman. It never had.

And a third thing was said, quieter, at the fires and the pump and the church-lot, by the ones who could not choose between the whirlwind and the humbug and did not have to — that she had gone west into the flats and *found the water there.* That the Salt Preacher had walked out into the one country nobody could follow her into because that was where the deep water was, the real water, the water under the salt that no shovel had reached, and she had gone to it and would send for them, and that a body ought to keep a barrel ready and a hole started, because when the Salt Preacher's word came it would come from the west, off the flats, where she was, tending the water for them against the day.

That was the one Rourke took.

She had known he would. He had stood in the rain on the bench and told her he would grieve her before they could hang her, that a grieved prophet the county could not save was a treasure, and he had grieved her true — had preached her going from a plank pulpit in a half-built church with his trained voice dropped to the whisper that carried, and the county had wept, and the collection plate had come back heavy, heavier than a live prophet had ever filled it, because a live prophet can turn up in El Paso and spoil the tale and a vanished one cannot, a vanished one only deepens, the way a dry hole deepens the longer you dig it, filling with everything but water. He had built her grave, as she'd said he would, and called it a church, and the county had taken off its hats and helped him raise the ridgepole.

---

The last of Odessa Pike that anyone could hold in a hand was the copper.

Rourke had it. He had ridden out with Rakes's party the second day, not to find her — he did not want her found, a found woman being worth nothing and a lost one being worth a Territory — but to be there when the wagon turned up, and he had lifted the two rods off the seat with his long hands the way he laid a hand on a shoulder, like ownership, and he had wrapped them in a cloth and carried them back to the half-built church, and he did not tell the county he had them, not yet. She would have understood the withholding. It was her own trade. You never told them yet. You made them wait, so that when the thing came it landed like weather.

He set the rods behind the pulpit-plank in the dark of the unfinished church, wrapped, waiting, and he stood over them a while in the last of the light with the smell of the new-cut green lumber around him and the changed dry air coming through the gaps in the walls, and he was not sure — he was never sure, that was his whole engine, the not-being-sure — whether he had a fraud's tin tube back there or a relic of a woman the lightning could not kill and the salt could not keep, and he found, as he always found, that he did not want to know, and that the not-wanting-to-know was the truest thing he owned, truer than the coat and the voice, and that it would carry a church further than any answer.

Out west, on the flats, the salt lay white and blind under the iron sky, and gave nothing back, and drew its poison up to meet the memory of the one rain, and held whatever it held, forever, dry, and did not say, and would never say — and the wagon stood where it had stopped with the seed corn under the tarp and the horse gone to bone in the traces, pointed the one way there was nothing, and the copper was gone off the seat, carried east into a church, and there was no woman anywhere in the county at all, and the whole of Cheyne County, on its knees in the mud of a rain that had saved no one, had never wanted her less or believed in her more.


---

# CHAPTER 29 · Movement V — The Mercy

### "The Name Without the Woman"

*Salt Fork — the fall after Odessa Pike walked out into the salt, in the fourth dry year, which the county had by then decided was the last of the drought, though it was not.*
*One rain in nineteen months. The Rueda ran two days off it and quit; by fall it was a bed of cracked plate ford to county line again, wet nowhere a man could reach. Hauled water back to eighteen cents the barrel and short at that. Dead stock past all counting still dead, the counting long abandoned. The count that mattered now was the one Vester Kyle kept in his ledger of subscribers: two hundred the year the woman was here, four hundred the season the boom ran, and — this was the thing that could not be explained by any weather — eleven hundred the fall after she was gone.*

---

There was a boy at the new well who was not Tobe, and that was the first thing Rourke had to see to.

He came up on the digging in the middle of the afternoon with the sun a white nail overhead and the crowd standing off the spoil-pile the way a crowd will stand off a thing it has decided is holy, in a loose ring, hats in hand though the day did not warrant it, and in the middle of the ring a green-lumber windlass and a rope going down into the dark and a boy at the crank. Fourteen, maybe. Wire-thin and barefoot and burnt the color of the ground, watchful about the eyes. Somebody's stray. There was no end of strays that fall; the belt had emptied and the boomtown had busted and the roads ran with people who belonged to no place, and a boy who belonged to no place will find his way to a crowd the way water finds the low ground, because a crowd is the one thing that will feed a stray without asking whose he is.

Rourke stood at the edge of the ring in his good black coat gone rusty at the seams and watched the boy crank, and he understood inside of a breath what the crowd had made of him.

They had made him hers.

Not by anything the boy had said. The boy had said nothing — that was half of it; a silent boy near a hole was a shape the county had learned to read a particular way, and it read him now without being told, the way you read weather off a sky you have watched too long. But it was more than the silence. It was the *stance.* The boy stood the way she had stood, or the way the story now said she had stood, which had already come loose from the woman and gone to live in the general memory the way a hymn comes loose from the man who wrote it: he stood over the hole and let the crowd wait on him, and he did not hurry, and when a woman near the front asked him low was there water, is there water down there, boy, he did not answer, he only cranked, and his not-answering ran through the ring like a current, and the hats came further off.

He made them wait, Rourke thought, and here it was, the whole of her trade, running by itself in a boy who had likely never laid eyes on the woman, who had picked the posture up off the county's talk the way a child picks up a limp off a limping father — and it was working. Rourke could feel it working in his own chest, the pull, the leaning-in, and he was the one man in the county who knew exactly how it was built, and it worked on him anyway, and that was the thing he had never once been able to resolve and did not want to.

He did not stop it. He came into the ring instead, slow, and laid his long hand on the boy's shoulder the way he laid a hand on any shoulder he meant to own, and the boy flinched under it and then went still, the stillness of a thing deciding not to run, and Rourke turned to the crowd and did not raise his voice, because he had learned long since from a woman he had watched work that the man who does not raise his voice is the man the room leans toward.

"Whose word sent you here?" he asked the boy, low, so the ring had to strain for it.

The boy said nothing. Of course he said nothing. He looked down the hole.

"He won't say," a man offered from the ring, eager. "He come out of the west three days ago and pointed here and he won't say. Same as she wouldn't. Same as *she* wouldn't."

Out of the west. Rourke let that stand a moment in the hot air, and felt the ring take it, and did not correct it, though he knew what west the boy had come out of, which was the west of a boy with no home walking toward the smoke of a town, and not the west of the flats where a wagon stood with the seed corn rotting under a tarp and a woman's boot-track lost in the salt. But the two wests were the same word, and the word did the work, and Rourke had learned from her that the work was the thing and the truth was only the tool you shaped it with and threw away.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> New hole on the Furlong quarter, NE belt, off the old wagon-road. Ground:
> gray crust over red seam, gyp smell faint on the throw when broke. Caliche
> shelf shows in the spoil at fifteen feet by the color-change on the piled
> dirt — hard cap, no water sits on it here, the cap tilts wrong, sheds
> in-country away from the hole. Spoil going grayer with depth. No damp on
> the deep throw. No fly on the wet dirt because there is no wet dirt. Salt
> cedar on the low margin forty paces off, green, drinking something the hole
> is not near. No dove-line. No phoebe. Ants gone off the fresh mound entire,
> trenching away NW, off the ground, the way ants go off ground before they
> go off ground that will not keep them. Grama straw. Nothing here. The boy
> did not read it. The boy pointed at the crowd, not the ground. The crowd
> read the boy. This hole will go to caliche and then to gyp and then to
> nothing, the way the last four went. Water does not lay here and did not
> ever lay here. She would have known it in one turn of her heel. There is no
> one here now to know it.

---

The finds had stopped a year ago and the digging had not.

That was the arithmetic Rourke turned over as he rode back to Salt Fork with the boy walking at his stirrup, because the boy would not ride and Rourke did not press it; a prophet's foundling who walked was worth more in the eye than one who rode, and Rourke had a keen eye for what a thing was worth in the eye. The finds had stopped while she was still living, back in the boom, and Kyle had said so to her face, that the water was a year dry and the belief had only got fatter — and Rourke had marked it then and had not understood the whole size of it until now, until she was gone, when he saw the thing come clear the way a range comes clear when the duster lifts off it: *the belief had never needed the water at all.* It had needed a place to point and a body to point it and a silence to pour itself into, and the water had only ever been the first pretext, the way a church needs a first collection to raise the walls and then does not need the collection to be true. The water was the scaffold. They had taken the scaffold down and the thing stood.

He knew he ought to have been afraid of that. He was not. He was — and this was the part of himself he had stopped examining, the way she had stopped examining the wire she wound to her dead wrist — he was *fed* by it. A man who has preached to empty country for thirty years and suspected the whole while that God had never once been listening does not turn away from a faith that runs without fuel. He warms his hands at it. He does not ask what is burning.

The county had grieved her true, that was the thing. He had told her on the north bench in the rain that a grieved prophet was a treasure, and he had been righter than he knew. Every man who had walked her to the bench with a rope on his shoulder had gone home and lain awake and, in the dark where a man does his true reckoning, had needed her not to have been a fraud, because if she was a fraud then they had loved a lie and buried children on a lie and killed a boy over a lie, and no man can bear the weight of that; so they had made her holy instead, which was lighter to carry, and Rourke had helped them carry it, and the collection plate came back heavier every Sunday than it had ever come back for a living woman.

---

The church had a roof on it now.

He had the ridgepole up before the first frost and the shakes on before the rain that came in the fall and did not stay, and the county that could not haul water for less than eighteen cents the barrel had somehow found the lumber and the labor and the nails, because a people will build the house of a god sooner than it will dig its own well, the house being a thing you can raise together in an afternoon and the well being a thing that either has water in it or does not and shames you either way. The church stood at the NW corner of the square where the boom-tents had been, plank walls squared and true, one glass window Kyle's brother-in-law had freighted from Pecos, and a plank pulpit, and behind the pulpit, in a box Rourke had made himself of the same green lumber, wrapped in a cloth, the copper.

He had not shown it yet.

He had carried it a year now, unshown, the two rods with the ends she had filed bright by lamplight ten years and a lifetime gone, and he had known from the first day he lifted them off the wagon-seat that he had the whole of the thing in his two hands, that the copper was the one relic the county could hold, the true cross of it, and that the showing of it was the biggest thing he would ever do and could be done only once, and so must be done right. He had learned the withholding off her, and did not smile at the theft of it, because it was not theft; she had taught the whole county the trade and he had only been the best pupil. You made them wait.

He waited until the boy from the Furlong well had been in the town three weeks and the county had settled it among themselves that the boy was hers, come back out of the west with her gift in his silent hands. He waited until a Sunday the church was full and standing at the back and out the door, eleven hundred souls by Kyle's count and Kyle counting from the front pew with his hungry consumptive face, waited until the hour was built and the crowd was leaning, the whole packed hot room leaning the way a field of grass leans before the wind you cannot yet feel. Then he went behind the pulpit and lifted the box and set it on the plank and opened it and took out the copper and raised it.

He raised it slow.

He raised it the way she had raised it, the way the story said she had raised it, the way you would lift a dying man's head to help him drink, and the light from the one Pecos window caught the filed ends and threw itself back off them, because she had filed them to catch the light, because a thing that catches the light is a thing men will watch — and eleven hundred people watched it, and the room made a sound like the sound wind makes coming into dry brush, and a woman near the front went down on her knees, and then the row behind her, and Rourke stood with the dead woman's copper raised in his two live hands and felt the thing pour up into him out of the crowd, the leaning, the wanting, the terrible readiness of frightened people to be told, and he had built his whole life toward this hour and it was every inch as large as he had known it would be, and he understood, standing in it, that he would never be sure whether he held a fraud's tin tube or a relic that the lightning could not kill and the salt could not keep, and that the not-being-sure was the engine, was the whole of it, was the truest thing he owned — and that it did not matter in the least, because the crowd was sure enough for the both of them, sure enough for the whole county, sure enough to raise a church and fill a plate and send a boy down a dry hole in the belief that a dead woman was tending the water for them from the far side of the salt.

He did not tell them what they were seeing. He let them tell themselves. That was the last thing she had taught him without meaning to teach it, the thing he had watched her do at the White Draw and over the shot boy and at the lip of the grave-well: you do not have to say the lie. You have only to hold the sign up in the light and be silent, and the frightened will build the lie themselves, brick by brick, out of their own need, and thank you for the bricks.

---

Persy Gault was there.

He had known she would be. She had come off her ruined quarter that fall with nothing left to hold her to it, the field salt-bloomed white in the low corner and the seed corn hoed into ground that took a rain and spat it, and she had come into Salt Fork the way the last of the belt came in, walking, with her black dress gone brown-red with dust and her split-and-mended hands, and she had found the one place in the county that still had a use for a woman who could believe past the end of her own life, which was the church. She sat near the front. She did not go down on her knees when the copper came up, because she had passed some while ago beyond the kind of belief that needs to fall down; hers had gone quiet and hard and settled, the way a thing settles that has cost everything it is going to cost. She watched the rods catch the light with her dust-pale eyes and her face did not change, and Rourke, who read faces for his living, could not read hers, and it was the one thing in the room that gave him pause.

Afterward she waited until the church had emptied but for the two of them and the boy, who had taken to sitting on the step below the pulpit the way a dog sits below a table, and she came up the aisle and stood before the plank and looked at the copper Rourke had laid back in its box but not yet covered.

"That's her rods," she said.

"It is."

"I dug a hole eleven foot on the word of them rods," Persy said, plainly, a farm woman's economy, no heat in it. "And it took a rain that spat on it, and my man's in the ground and my field's salt and I've not got a thing left in the world but a horse I give away and a road I walked in on." She was quiet a moment. The boy watched her from the step. "You going to send that boy down holes on them rods now."

"The county will send him," Rourke said. "I only carry the name."

"You carry it," Persy agreed. "You carry it like a man carries a full bucket he never once dug the well for." And Rourke, who had been called a great many things by a great many people and had let all of it slide off the coat, felt that one go in under the coat, because it was true, and because she said it without any wish to wound, which was worse. "She never found me any water," Persy said. "I know it. She told me so herself, plain, the last morning, stammering, the way she got when she meant a thing all the way down. *I never found you any water.* Said it to my face." She looked at the copper. "And I'll go on believing anyhow, because I'd sooner have had the three years with a reason in them than a wet season with none. That's mine. I earned that. Ain't nobody's to take, not even hers." She lifted her split hand and let it hover a moment over the rods without touching them, the way you hold a hand over a stove to feel is it hot, and then she took the hand back. "But you," she said to Rourke, "you never earned a thing you're spending. You're drinking a well another woman dug and telling folks you witched it up. She'd have known you for what you are inside a breath. She knew everybody for what they was. It was the only mercy in her — she never once pretended a soul was better than they was, not even her own self."

Then she turned and went out of the church into the dry white afternoon, upright, unhurried, and Rourke stood at the pulpit with the dead woman's copper open in the box before him and did not, for a moment, know what he held, or what he was, and found, as he always found, that he did not want to know, and that the not-wanting was the truest thing he owned, and that it would carry a church further than any answer, and that Persy Gault had told him so, and had been the only one in eleven hundred to see it, and would be back in her pew on Sunday all the same.

---

The boy on the step got up when the woman was gone.

He crossed to the pulpit on his silent feet and looked into the box at the copper, close, the way a boy looks at a thing he means to have, and Rourke watched him look, and thought of the other boy, the mute one from the boom, the one they said the woman had suffered to follow her, and wondered idly and without much caring where that one had got to — south, someone had said, sent south with a fist of silver ahead of the rope, though no one could say by whom, and the tale had already begun to say the woman had sent her disciple out to carry the water-word into Texas, which was better than the truth and so would be the thing remembered. It did not matter. This boy would do as well. Any boy would do. Any body would do. That was the marvel of the thing, the marvel Rourke turned in the dark now the way she had once turned her arithmetic: the woman had been the least necessary part of it. She had been a place for the belief to land, and then she had walked off into the salt, and the belief had looked around and found the ground still there and landed again, on a church, on a boy, on a pair of tin rods filed bright by a hand a year dead, and it had not so much as staggered.

"You want them," Rourke said to the boy. Not a question.

The boy looked at him.

"Not yet," Rourke said, and closed the box, and set the cloth over it, and slid it behind the pulpit into the dark where it would wait, wrapped, until the day the county needed it to come out again, which would be the next dry Sunday, or the one after, there being no shortage of dry Sundays in a county that had decided the drought was ending and was wrong. "You'll want them slow," he told the boy. "When it comes to you. You raise it slow. You never tell them yet. You make them wait." He heard his own voice drop into the cadence, her cadence, the church's cadence, the one the whole burnt county had been trained across two generations to obey, and he did not stop it, because it was his now, it was the county's now, it was no one's now and everyone's, loosed from the woman who never believed it and set running through the dry air like a grass fire that had forgotten the match that lit it. "So that when you speak," Rourke said, "it lands like weather."

The boy said nothing. He looked at the covered box a long moment with his watchful stray's eyes. Then he went and sat back down on the step below the pulpit and folded his burnt hands and waited, easy, patient, the way a boy waits who has understood the one thing there is to understand — that the waiting is the trade, and that he is good at it, and that no one had to teach him, and that out past the church wall and the square and the busted town and the dead river, out west where the salt lay white and blind under the iron sky and gave nothing back and kept whatever it kept forever, dry, and did not say, there was no woman anywhere in the county at all, and the whole of Cheyne County, on its knees or on its feet, digging its dry holes and filling its heavy plate, had gone on without her and would not have known how to stop.


---

# CHAPTER 30 · Movement V — The Mercy

### "Lands Like Weather"

*Cheyne County, three summers on from the year the water was said to come back — another dry year, the country having learned by then that a dry year is only the name for a year, and that the wet ones are the strangers.*
*Rain twice since the spring: an inch in April that ran off the baked ground and made nothing, a hard hour in June that filled the low places for a day. The Rueda a bed of cracked plate ford to county line, wet nowhere, the same road it had been. Hauled water back to twenty cents the barrel and the dray owner's boy running it now, the dray owner dead of the flux. The count that mattered was still Kyle's, though Kyle was three years in the churchyard and a nephew ran the Clarion: nineteen hundred souls on the subscription list of the Salt Fork church, in a county that could not, by any honest census, hold that many living people. They came from off the county now. They came for the walk.*

---

The boy who was not Tobe was a man now, or near it, and he had the copper.

He came out onto the Furlong flat a little after sunup with the crowd already gathered ahead of him, the way it gathered now before he ever arrived, having learned that the arriving was part of the thing and that a body did well to be there before it. He walked from the wagon-road on his silent feet, seventeen or eighteen by whatever count a stray keeps of himself, wire-thin still and burnt the color of the ground still, and the crowd opened for him the way water opens for a stone, closing behind, and he did not look at any of them. He looked at the ground.

He carried the copper across his shoulder in a canteen strap, the two rods with the ends filed bright a lifetime and more ago by a hand nobody living had held. Rourke had given them to him the winter Rourke died, in the church, at night, the way a thing like that is done — had lifted the box from behind the pulpit-plank and set it on the plank and opened it and taken out the rods and put them in the boy's two hands and said, *You'll want them slow. You never tell them yet. You make them wait.* The boy had already known it. He had known it before Rourke, before the church, before the town; he had picked it up out of the county's air the way a child picks up a limp off a limping father, and Rourke had only put the metal into hands that already understood the trade. The boy had watched Rourke die three days later of nothing anyone could name, an old road-preacher going out dry, and he had preached the man's grave himself, without a word, standing at the lip of the hole and letting the crowd wait on his silence, and the collection plate had come back heavier for the buried preacher than it had ever come back for the living one, because that was the arithmetic of the place, and the boy was good at the arithmetic, and no one had taught him.

He stopped at the middle of the flat where a stake stood driven with a red rag on it, put there before light by men who had ridden the ground the night before deciding where the walk would begin, because the walk had a shape now, a liturgy, and the beginning of it was a red rag on a stake and the whole county knew that too.

He set down nothing. He raised the copper.

---

> **ASSAY.**
> Furlong flat, N belt, the old wagon-road half a mile E. Ground: gray crust,
> red seam under, gyp faint on the throw where the stake was driven. Caliche
> shelf runs shallow here, tilts wrong, sheds in-country away from the low
> corner — same cap that took the last four holes on this quarter. Grama gone
> to straw three years. Mesquite green on the rise, false, rooted deep past
> anything a well reaches. Salt cedar in flood down the dead draw, feathered,
> drinking the poison forty feet down no shovel touches. No dove-line at dusk;
> the belt has said so a season. No phoebe. Ants gone off the fresh-turned
> ground by the stake, trenching NW, off it entire, the way they go off ground
> that will not keep them. No damp on the deep throw of the last hole, forty
> paces N, abandoned at twenty-two feet, dry to the bottom. Water does not lay
> here. Did not ever lay here. No damp. No tell. Nothing turned. The ground
> unread.

---

He walked it slow.

He walked the copper up over ground no one on the flat could read. The boy could not read it; the boy read the crowd, and raised the rod where they leaned. There was no one on the Furlong flat that morning who could read the ground the old way, and there had not been for three years, and it made no difference to any soul out there, because the ground was not the thing they had come to consult. They had come for the walk.

He raised the rods the way she had raised them, the way the story said she had raised them, the way you would lift a dying man's head to help him drink, and the crowd came up onto its toes behind him the way a field of grass leans before the wind you cannot yet feel. There were perhaps two hundred of them on the flat, which was small for a walk now; a summer walk near town would draw four times that, but this was the belt, and the belt was thin ground with thin people on it, come out from their doomed quarters and their dry holes and the roads they walked in on, and they leaned on the boy's slowness the way the whole burnt country had learned across two generations now to lean, hats coming off, though the morning did not warrant it.

Among them, near the front, in a black dress gone brown-red with dust, stood Persy Gault.

She was old now, or the country had made her old, which came to the same face. She had come back to Cheyne County the way the ruined come back to the ground that ruined them, having found no other ground would have her, and she lived in the town on the charity of the church, which was to say she lived on the strangers' money the boy's silence pulled out of them, though no one would have said it so plainly. She stood in the front the way she had always stood in the front, and she did not go up on her toes with the rest, and she did not take her eyes off the boy, and her face did not change, because she had gone some years ago past the kind of belief that leans. Hers had settled. It sat in her hard and quiet the way a thing sits that has cost everything it is going to cost.

The boy passed her once, close, and she watched the rods go by her at the height of her own chest, the filed ends throwing the low sun, and she lifted one split-and-mended hand a few inches from her side and let it hover a moment in the air where the copper had been, the way you hold a hand over a stove to feel is it hot, and then she took the hand back. She did not touch them. She never touched them. There were people at the walks now who would have crawled to touch them, who touched the boy's sleeve as he passed and touched the ground where he had stood and carried the dirt home in twists of cloth, and Persy Gault had watched the whole of it come up out of the county like salt out of dry ground and had never once put her hand to the copper, and if the boy had known why he might have been afraid of her, but the boy did not know why, and was not afraid of anything on the flat but the one thing every man on it was afraid of, which was that the walk might mean nothing at all.

---

The boy had come out of nothing, which was the best country a prophet can come out of.

He did not remember a mother. He remembered a wagon and then no wagon, a road and then a town at the end of the road, and a crowd standing off a hole the way a crowd stands off a thing it has decided is holy, and he had come to the crowd the way a stray comes to a fire, because a crowd is the one thing that will feed a stray without asking whose he is. He had cranked a windlass over a dry hole for a widow who fed him, and a man had said, *he come out of the west and pointed here and he won't say, same as she wouldn't,* and the word had settled on him like a coat before he understood there was a coat being put on him, and by the time he understood he had learned that the coat was warm and that a body who wears it does not go hungry in a county that cannot haul water for less than twenty cents the barrel. He had never seen the woman. He had walked into her legend the way a man walks into a house another man built, and found it furnished, and lived in it, and it fit him because it had been built to fit no one in particular, built to fit the leaning, built to fit whatever body stood at the lip of a hole and had the sense to be silent — and the boy had that sense the way some men are born with a true eye or a straight arm, born to it, and it had cost him nothing to find, which was the whole horror of the thing, though no one on the flat would have called it a horror. They called it a gift. They called it hers, come back out of the west in a stray boy's hands, and the boy let them call it that, because it fed him, and because he had learned, off the county's own talk, that you never told them yet.

He had a wife now, on a quarter north of town the church had bought him, and a child coming, and a name of his own he no longer used because the county had given him a better one. He was going to grow old inside the coat. He knew it and did not mind it. It was a good coat and there was no other going in the county for a boy who had come out of nothing, and a man does not walk out of the one house that will have him back into the country that has no water in it and no name.

---

He held the pronouncement.

He held it past the point where the theater needed it held, held it into the place where the holding stopped being a tool and became the thing itself, and the crowd strained against the silence the way the whole county strained against the withholding sky, and a woman near the back said low, *is there water, is there water there, does he say,* and the boy did not answer, and his not-answering ran through the two hundred like a current in a wire, and the last of the hats came off.

He raised the right rod higher. He had wired it to his wrist that morning in the dark, the wire biting, the way the story said she had wired hers to a hand that would not close — though the boy's hand closed fine, the boy's hand was whole, and he wound the wire to it anyway because the wire was in the trade now, the wire was scripture, a thing done because it had always been done, the truth of a woman's ruined hand gone down into ritual and lost, so that a boy with two good hands lashed a rod to a wrist that could have held it, out of reverence for a wound he had never seen and could not have named. The wire bit him. He let it bite. That was in the trade too.

He stopped over the low corner where the salt cedar stood green in the dead draw, and he lowered the rods, slow, and the crowd went down into a silence so whole a man could hear the dry mesquite tick in the heat, and the boy stood with his head bent and the sun on the filed copper and he did not say water and he did not say no water and he did not say anything at all, and the two hundred read the silence, and built the water out of it, brick by brick, out of their own thirst, and thanked him for the bricks.

A man came forward with a shovel. There was always a man with a shovel. This one was a stranger to the county, come up from the south on the talk, a big slow-handed man with a family in a wagon at the edge of the flat and no ground of his own anywhere in the world, which was the only kind of man who dug the last hole, the man with nothing behind him but the hole. He set the shovel to the earth where the boy had stood and he looked up at the boy with his whole life in his face, and the boy looked back at him and did not warn him, because the boy did not know there was anything to warn him of, the boy could not read the gyp under the crust or the caliche tilting wrong or the ants gone off the ground; the boy read only that the man needed the water to be there, and gave him the silence to build it in, and it was enough. It was always enough.

Persy Gault watched the man set his shovel to the salt-shed ground and she knew what the boy did not, because she had dug that same hole herself, eleven foot into a playa that took a rain and spat it, and had come up with nothing but the memory of water, and had gone on believing anyhow. Her face did not change. She had learned the one thing there was to learn, which the boy had learned too and which the whole county had built a church on: that the water was never the thing. The waiting was the thing. She had told a dead woman so on a west road three summers gone, had put the lines in the woman's broken hand and folded the dead fingers over them and said *you give me the waiting, and a reason is worth more than a rain,* and she believed it still, and would believe it into the ground, and the man with the shovel would dig his family's hole on the strength of a silence, and it would go to caliche and then to gyp and then to nothing the way the last four had, and the man would decide he had dug on the wrong day, or lacked faith, or that the Lord had withheld to try him, and the faith would not so much as stagger, because there was no failure left in the county that the county had not already learned to eat.

---

The boy did not stay to watch the digging.

He never stayed. That was in the trade — the finder gives the ground and goes, and the going is part of the giving, a man who watches his own miracle put to the shovel being a man who might be there when it comes up dry, and the story had learned to move the finder along before the ground could answer. He wound the copper back into the canteen strap and he turned and walked out through the crowd toward the wagon-road, and they opened for him and closed behind, reaching, some of them, touching his sleeve, and one old man went down on his knees in the boy's track and pressed both hands flat to the dirt where the boy had walked, and the boy did not look at any of them.

He passed Persy Gault going out.

She was the only one who did not reach for him. She stood in the parting crowd with her hands at her sides and she looked at him the way she had looked at the dead woman's rods in the church three years gone, close and quiet and unafraid, and as he came level with her she said, not loud, in a farm woman's economy, few words wasted, "You never found that man any water."

The boy stopped.

He looked at her. It was the first thing anyone had said to him all morning that was not a prayer or a plea, and it went into him the way a true word goes in, by the way it cost him, and for a moment the whole of it stood open behind his burnt watchful eyes — that he knew it, that he had always known it, that the copper was tube and the wire was theater and the water was never once his to find or fail to find, that he stood at the lip of other men's holes and gave them a silence to drown in and took their strangers' money and their bent knees and buried a preacher and would bury this county too, hole by hole, and that there was nothing under any of it, nothing at all, only the wanting, only the ground, only the leaning-in of the frightened toward a thing that would tell them.

He did not say any of it. He had never said anything. He looked at Persy Gault a long moment with the copper on his shoulder and the sun in it, and she looked back at him and did not flinch, the way another woman had once not flinched at a wild dog of a boy at the lip of a hole in the dirt, and something passed between them on the flat that had no words in it because both of them had put words down years ago as tools too dangerous to keep — the knowledge, held between the two people on the whole flat who held it, that it was a lie, and that the lie was fireproof, and that neither of them could stop it now, and that neither of them would try.

Then the boy went on. He walked out to the wagon-road on his silent feet with the copper catching the light, and behind him the shovel bit the salt-shed ground once, twice, and the man with nothing behind him but the hole began to dig, and the crowd came off its knees and up onto its toes again, leaning toward the sound.

---

Persy Gault stood on the flat until the boy was gone up the wagon-road and small.

She turned then and looked west, the way she always looked west now, out past the belt and the town and the dead river to where the flats went white and blind under the iron sky and gave nothing back, out to the salt where a wagon had once stood pointed at the one country there was nothing in, and a horse had gone to bone in the traces, and a woman's boot-track had walked out into the white and lost itself where the crust took no print. She looked a long time. She looked the way you wait on a thing, patient, with the whole of a life folded into the waiting and nothing at the end of it, and out there the salt lay the way it had always lain, white as a lie about snow, holding whatever it held, forever, dry, and did not say.

There was no woman anywhere in the country. There had not been for three years. The wagon was long since hauled off for its lumber and the copper was on a stray boy's shoulder and the name was a church and a subscription list and a thing men said at the pump, loosed clean off the woman who never believed it and never wanted it and had walked out into the salt to be rid of it, and it had looked around at her going and found the ground still there, patient, and landed again, on a boy, on a silence, on a shovel biting dry dirt, and it had not staggered, and it would not stagger, because it had never needed her at all — she had only been a place for it to fall, and the country was full of places, and the country was patient, and it was going to be a dry year, and another after it, and the ground does not tire of being asked.

Behind her the shovel bit the dirt again. Somebody near the hole said, low, so the ring had to strain for it, *he never told them yet — you never tell them yet — you make them wait,* and the words went out over the flat in the old cadence, the county's cadence, no one's now and everyone's, and the crowd leaned, and the sun climbed, and the man dug down toward the gyp and the caliche and the nothing, believing it was water, so that when the word came at last it would land like weather.

Persy Gault turned her back on the west and started the long walk into town, and did not look again at the salt, because she had learned from the woman who taught her everything that looking back was the door of all doors, and she had a wall to keep, and the waiting to do, and she was good at it now. She had been learnt good.
